A Runaway Reef

The year 1866 was marked by a bizarre event, an unexplained and altogether inexplicable phenomenon that no one, surely, has forgotten. To say nothing of the rumors that stirred up the populations of the ports and inflamed the public mind far inland across whole continents, it was the seafaring men above all who were shaken. Merchants and shipowners, sea captains, skippers and masters throughout Europe and America, naval officers of every country, and, in their wake, the governments of the various states on both continents — all of them took the matter with the utmost seriousness.

The fact was that, for some time now, several ships had met with "an enormous thing" out on the open sea: a long object, tapered like a spindle, sometimes glowing with a strange light, and vastly larger and faster than any whale.

The details concerning this apparition, entered in the various ships' logs, agreed rather closely on the structure of the object — or the creature — in question, on the unheard-of speed of its movements, on the astonishing power of its locomotion, and on the peculiar vitality with which it seemed endowed. If it was a member of the whale family, then it exceeded in bulk every specimen science had catalogued up to that time. Neither Cuvier, nor Lacépède, nor Monsieur Duméril, nor Monsieur de Quatrefages would have granted the existence of such a monster — not, at least, without having seen it, seen it with their own learned eyes.

Taking the average of the observations made on the various occasions — rejecting the timid estimates that gave the object a length of two hundred feet, and dismissing the wild exaggerations that made it a mile wide and three miles long — one could nonetheless assert that this phenomenal being far surpassed any dimensions admitted so far by the ichthyologists — assuming it existed at all.

And exist it did; the fact in itself could no longer be denied, and given the human brain's incurable fondness for the marvelous, one can well imagine the excitement this supernatural apparition stirred up all around the world. As for filing it away quietly among the fables, that idea had to be given up for good.

For on July 20, 1866, the steamer Governor Higginson, of the Calcutta and Burnach Steam Navigation Company, had encountered this moving mass five miles off the eastern coast of Australia. Captain Baker at first believed he was looking at some uncharted reef; he was even preparing to fix its exact position when two columns of water, hurled up by the inexplicable object, shot hissing a hundred and fifty feet into the air. So unless this reef happened to be subject to the intermittent eruptions of a geyser, the Governor Higginson had well and truly come face to face with some aquatic mammal, unknown until then, which spouted from its blowholes columns of water mixed with air and steam.

The same phenomenon was observed on July 23 of that same year, in the waters of the Pacific, by the Cristobal Colon, of the West India and Pacific Steam Navigation Company. Clearly, then, this extraordinary whale could travel from one place to another with astonishing speed, since within an interval of just three days the Governor Higginson and the Cristobal Colon had observed it at two points on the map separated by a distance of more than seven hundred nautical leagues. Two weeks later, and two thousand leagues from there, the Helvetia, of the Compagnie Nationale, and the Shannon, of the Royal Mail line, steaming in opposite directions through that stretch of the Atlantic that lies between the United States and Europe, each in turn signaled the monster at 42°15′ north latitude and 60°35′ longitude west of the Greenwich meridian. From this simultaneous sighting, the creature's length was reckoned at a minimum of more than three hundred and fifty English feet, since both the Shannon and the Helvetia were smaller than it, even though they measured a hundred meters from stem to stern. Now, the largest whales — those that frequent the waters around the Aleutian Islands, the Kulammak and the Umgullick — have never exceeded a length of fifty-six meters, if indeed they ever reach it.

These reports arriving one on top of another, along with fresh observations made aboard the transatlantic liner Pereire, a collision between the Etna, of the Inman line, and the monster, an official report drawn up by the officers of the French frigate Normandie, and a very sober sighting recorded by the staff of Commodore Fitz-James aboard the Lord Clyde — all of this stirred public opinion to its depths. In the lighter-hearted countries, people made a joke of the whole thing, but the serious, practical countries — England, America, Germany — were gravely concerned.

Everywhere in the great cities the monster became the fashion of the hour; it was sung about in the cafés, jeered at in the newspapers, and acted out on the stage. The tabloids found in it a fine opportunity to lay eggs of every color. In the papers, short as ever on copy, one saw the reappearance of every imaginary and gigantic creature you could name, from the white whale — the terrible "Moby Dick" of the far northern regions — to the monstrous Kraken, whose tentacles can wrap themselves around a five-hundred-ton vessel and drag it down into the depths of the ocean. They even dusted off the official records of ancient times: the opinions of Aristotle and Pliny, who allowed the existence of such monsters; then the Norwegian tales of Bishop Pontoppidan; the accounts of Paul Heggede; and finally the reports of Mr. Harrington, whose good faith cannot be doubted when he swears that, aboard the Castilian in 1857, he saw that enormous sea serpent which until then had frequented no waters but those of the old Constitutionnel.

Then broke out the endless quarrel between the believers and the skeptics, waged in the learned societies and the scientific journals alike. The "monster question" set minds ablaze. Journalists who make a profession of science, locked in combat with those who make a profession of wit, spilled floods of ink during that memorable campaign — and a few of them even spilled two or three drops of blood, for from the sea serpent they passed straight on to the most offensive personal attacks.

For six months the war dragged on, with fortunes shifting on both sides. To the weighty articles of the Geographical Institute of Brazil, the Royal Academy of Sciences in Berlin, the British Association, the Smithsonian Institution in Washington; to the discussions in The Indian Archipelago, in Abbé Moigno's Cosmos, in Petermann's Mittheilungen; to the science columns of the great newspapers of France and abroad — the popular press fired back with an inexhaustible verve. Twisting a saying of Linnaeus that the monster's opponents had quoted, its witty writers maintained that "nature does not make fools," and they begged their contemporaries not to give nature the lie by admitting the existence of Krakens, sea serpents, "Moby Dicks," and other ravings of delirious sailors. Finally, in an article in a much-feared satirical paper, its most popular writer, mounting the attack over them all, went at the monster like Hippolytus, dealt it one last blow, and finished it off amid a burst of universal laughter. Wit had defeated science.

Through the first months of 1867, the question seemed to be buried, and it did not look likely to rise again — when new facts were brought to the public's attention. This time it was no longer a scientific puzzle to be solved, but a real and serious danger to be avoided. The question took on a wholly different aspect. The monster became once more an islet, a rock, a reef — but a runaway reef, one you could neither pin down nor lay hold of.

On March 5, 1867, the Moravian, of the Montreal Ocean Company, finding herself during the night at 27°30′ latitude and 72°15′ longitude, struck with her starboard quarter a rock that no chart marked in those waters. Under the combined thrust of the wind and her four hundred horsepower, she was making thirteen knots. There is no doubt that, but for the superior quality of her hull, the Moravian would have been split open by the blow and gone down with the two hundred and thirty-seven passengers she was bringing back from Canada.

The accident had happened around five in the morning, just as day was beginning to break. The watch officers rushed to the stern of the ship. They examined the ocean with the most scrupulous attention. They saw nothing — nothing but a strong eddy breaking about three cable-lengths off, as though the sheets of water had been violently churned. The position was noted down exactly, and the Moravian continued on her way with no apparent damage. Had she struck a submerged rock, or some enormous piece of wreckage? No one could say; but when her hull was examined in dry dock, it was found that part of the keel had been broken.

This incident, extremely serious in itself, might perhaps have been forgotten like so many others, had it not, three weeks later, repeated itself under conditions exactly the same. Only this time, thanks to the nationality of the ship that was the new victim, and thanks to the reputation of the company to which she belonged, the event caused an enormous stir all over the world.

No one is unaware of the name of the famous English shipowner Cunard. In 1840 this shrewd businessman founded a postal service between Liverpool and Halifax, with three wooden paddle-wheel ships of four hundred horsepower and eleven hundred and sixty-two tons' burden. Eight years later, the company's fleet grew by four ships of six hundred and fifty horsepower and eighteen hundred and twenty tons, and two years after that by two more vessels of still greater power and tonnage. In 1853, the Cunard company — its contract for carrying the mails having just been renewed — added one after another to its fleet the Arabia, the Persia, the China, the Scotia, the Java, and the Russia, all ships of the first rank, and the largest that, after the Great Eastern, had ever plowed the seas. And so, by 1867, the company owned twelve ships, eight of them paddle-wheelers and four screw-driven.

If I give these very brief details, it is so that everyone may grasp just how important this maritime transport company is, known the world over for its intelligent management. No transoceanic shipping enterprise has been run with more skill; no business has been crowned with more success. In twenty-six years, Cunard ships have crossed the Atlantic two thousand times, and never once has a voyage been missed, never a delay occurred, never a letter, a man, or a vessel been lost. That is why passengers, despite the powerful competition France offers, still choose the Cunard line over any other, as is plain from a survey of the official records of recent years. Having said this, no one will be surprised at the stir caused by the accident that befell one of its finest steamers.

On April 13, 1867, with the sea calm and the breeze manageable, the Scotia lay at 15°12′ longitude and 45°37′ latitude. She was making thirteen knots and forty-three hundredths under the push of her thousand horsepower. Her paddle wheels beat the sea with perfect regularity. Her draft at the time was six meters seventy centimeters, and her displacement six thousand six hundred and twenty-four cubic meters.

At seventeen minutes past four in the afternoon, while the passengers were gathered for lunch in the grand saloon, a shock — a slight one, all things considered — struck the Scotia's hull, on the quarter and a little abaft the port paddle wheel.

The Scotia had not struck; she had been struck, and by a cutting or piercing instrument rather than a blunt one. The impact had seemed so light that no one aboard would have worried about it, but for the cry of the men down in the hold, who came scrambling up onto the deck shouting:

"We're sinking! We're sinking!"

At first the passengers were badly frightened; but Captain Anderson hastened to reassure them. In fact, the danger could not be imminent. The Scotia, divided into seven compartments by watertight bulkheads, could shrug off any single leak with impunity.

Captain Anderson went down at once into the hold. He found that the sea had flooded the fifth compartment, and the speed of the flooding proved the leak was a large one. Very fortunately, that compartment did not house the boilers, or the fires would have gone out on the spot.

Captain Anderson had the ship stopped immediately, and one of the sailors dove down to inspect the damage. A few moments later they confirmed the existence of a hole two meters wide in the steamer's hull. Such a leak could not be plugged, and the Scotia, her paddle wheels half submerged, had to continue her voyage that way. She was then three hundred miles from Cape Clear, and after three days of delay that gave Liverpool a serious fright, she entered the company's docks.

The engineers then set about examining the Scotia, which was placed in dry dock. They could not believe their eyes. Two and a half meters below the waterline gaped a clean tear, in the shape of an isosceles triangle. The break in the metal plating was of perfect sharpness — it could not have been cut more cleanly with a punch press. So the piercing tool that had made it must have been tempered to an uncommon hardness — and, after being launched with prodigious force and thus driving through a plate four centimeters thick, it had had to withdraw itself by a backward motion that was truly inexplicable.

Such was this latest incident, and its result was to fire up public opinion all over again. From this moment on, in fact, every maritime disaster that had no clearly determined cause was laid at the monster's door. This fantastic animal was made to shoulder the blame for all these shipwrecks, whose number is unfortunately considerable; for out of the three thousand ships whose loss is recorded every year at the Bureau Veritas, the number of steamships and sailing vessels presumed lost with all hands for want of news comes to no fewer than two hundred!

And so it was the "monster" that, justly or unjustly, was accused of their disappearance; and, thanks to it, communication between the various continents growing more and more dangerous, the public spoke out and demanded, in no uncertain terms, that the seas be rid at last, and at any price, of this formidable creature.

For and Against

At the time these events took place, I was on my way home from a scientific expedition into the Badlands of Nebraska, in the United States. In my capacity as assistant professor at the Museum of Natural History in Paris, I had been attached to the expedition by the French government. After six months in Nebraska, laden with precious collections, I reached New York toward the end of March. My departure for France was set for the first days of May. In the meantime, then, I was busy sorting and classifying my mineralogical, botanical, and zoological treasures, when the incident of the Scotia occurred.

I was thoroughly up to date on the question of the hour — and how could I not have been? I had read and reread every American and European newspaper without getting any closer to the truth. The mystery intrigued me. Unable to form an opinion, I drifted from one extreme to the other. That there was something out there could not be doubted, and the skeptics were cordially invited to lay a finger on the wound in the Scotia's side.

By the time I arrived in New York, the question was at fever pitch. The hypothesis of a floating island, an uncharted reef, championed by a few not especially competent minds, had been thoroughly abandoned. And indeed — unless this reef had an engine in its belly, how could it move about with such prodigious speed?

Dismissed for the same reason was the idea of a floating hull, an enormous piece of wreckage — again on account of the speed of its movements.

That left two possible solutions to the puzzle, and they created two very distinct camps of believers: on one side, those who held out for a monster of colossal strength; on the other, those who held out for a "submarine" boat of extraordinary driving power.

Now, this second hypothesis, admissible after all, could not survive the inquiries pursued in both the Old World and the New. That some private individual should have such a machine at his disposal was hardly likely. Where and when had he had it built, and how could he have kept its construction a secret?

Only a government could possibly own such an engine of destruction, and in these disastrous times, when men rack their brains to multiply the power of their weapons of war, it was conceivable that some state might be testing this formidable device without the others' knowledge. After the chassepot rifles, the torpedoes; after the torpedoes, the underwater rams; and then — the recoil. At least, so I hope.

But the theory of a war machine collapsed too, before the governments' formal denials. Since a matter of public interest was at stake — for transoceanic communications were suffering — the good faith of the governments could not be questioned. Besides, how could one accept that the construction of such a submarine boat had escaped the public eye? Keeping a secret under such circumstances is very difficult for a private individual, and certainly impossible for a state whose every act is doggedly watched by rival powers.

And so, after inquiries carried out in England, France, Russia, Prussia, Spain, Italy, America, and even Turkey, the hypothesis of an underwater Monitor was definitively rejected.

Upon my arrival in New York, several people had done me the honor of consulting me about the phenomenon in question. I had published in France a two-volume quarto work entitled The Mysteries of the Great Ocean Depths. This book, particularly well received in scholarly circles, had made me something of a specialist in that rather obscure branch of natural history. My opinion was sought. So long as I could deny the facts, I took refuge in flat denial. But soon, pinned against the wall, I had to explain myself in no uncertain terms. And indeed, "the honorable Pierre Aronnax, professor at the Paris Museum," was called upon by the New York Herald to formulate some opinion or other.

So I complied. I spoke because I could no longer keep silent. I discussed the question from every angle, politically and scientifically, and here I give an extract from a well-stuffed article I published in the issue of April 30.

"And so," I wrote, "having examined the various hypotheses one by one, and every other supposition being rejected, we are necessarily driven to admit the existence of a marine animal of tremendous power.

"The great depths of the ocean are entirely unknown to us. The sounding line has never reached them. What goes on in those remote abysses? What creatures inhabit, and can inhabit, twelve or fifteen miles beneath the surface of the waters? What is the makeup of these animals? We can scarcely even guess.

"Yet the solution to the problem put before me may take the form of a dilemma.

"Either we know every variety of creature that populates our planet, or we do not.

"If we do not know them all — if nature still keeps secrets from us in matters of ichthyology — then nothing is more reasonable than to admit the existence of fish, or cetaceans, of new species or even new genera, of an essentially 'bottom-dwelling' constitution, that inhabit the strata beyond the reach of the sounding line, and that some event or other — a whim, a caprice, if you like — brings up at long intervals toward the upper level of the ocean.

"If, on the contrary, we do know every living species, then we must necessarily look for the animal in question among the marine creatures already catalogued, and in that case I would be inclined to admit the existence of a giant narwhal.

"The common narwhal, or sea unicorn, often reaches a length of sixty feet. Multiply that dimension by five, even by ten; give this cetacean a strength proportional to its size; enlarge its offensive weapons — and you have the animal we are looking for. It will have the proportions determined by the officers of the Shannon, the instrument required by the puncturing of the Scotia, and the power needed to breach a steamer's hull.

"For the narwhal is armed with a sort of ivory sword — a halberd, as certain naturalists put it. It is a great tooth as hard as steel. Some of these teeth have been found buried in the bodies of whales, which the narwhal always attacks successfully. Others have been wrenched — not without difficulty — from the hulls of ships that they had pierced clean through, the way a drill bores through a barrel. The museum of the Paris Faculty of Medicine possesses one of these tusks, two meters and twenty-five centimeters long and forty-eight centimeters wide at its base!

"Well then! Suppose the weapon ten times stronger, and the animal ten times more powerful; launch it at a speed of twenty miles an hour; multiply its mass by its velocity, and you obtain an impact capable of producing the catastrophe in question.

"So, until further information comes to light, I would cast my vote for a sea unicorn of colossal dimensions, armed no longer with a mere halberd, but with a genuine ram like the ironclad frigates or the war 'rams,' whose bulk and driving power it would combine.

"That is how this inexplicable phenomenon could be explained — unless there is nothing there at all, in spite of everything that has been glimpsed, seen, felt, and sensed — which is also possible!"

Those last words were a piece of cowardice on my part; but up to a point I wanted to protect my professorial dignity, and not lay myself too open to the mockery of the Americans, who laugh heartily when they do laugh. I was leaving myself an escape hatch. Deep down, I admitted the existence of the "monster."

My article was hotly debated, which won it a wide echo. It rallied a fair number of supporters. The solution it proposed, moreover, left free rein to the imagination. The human mind delights in these grandiose conceptions of supernatural beings. And the sea is precisely their best vehicle — the one element in which such giants (beside whom the land animals, elephants or rhinoceroses, are mere dwarves) can be produced and can grow. The liquid masses carry the largest known species of mammals, and perhaps they harbor mollusks of incomparable size, crustaceans frightful to behold — lobsters a hundred meters long, say, or crabs weighing two hundred tons! Why not, indeed? In former ages, the land animals, contemporaries of the geological epochs — the quadrupeds, the quadrumanes, the reptiles, the birds — were built on gigantic patterns. The Creator had cast them in a colossal mold, which time has gradually reduced. Why should the sea, in its unknown depths, not have preserved these vast specimens of the life of another age — the sea that never changes, while the earth's core is almost ceaselessly transformed? Why should it not hide in its bosom the last varieties of these titanic species, whose years are centuries and whose centuries are millennia?

But I let myself be carried away into daydreams that are no longer mine to entertain! Enough of these fancies, which time has turned, for me, into terrible realities. I repeat: opinion had by then made up its mind about the nature of the phenomenon, and the public accepted without dispute the existence of a prodigious being that had nothing in common with the fabled sea serpents.

But while some saw in it only a purely scientific problem to be solved, others — more practical, especially in America and England — were of a mind to purge the ocean of this fearsome monster, in order to make the sea lanes safe again. The industrial and commercial papers treated the question chiefly from this angle. The Shipping and Mercantile Gazette, the Lloyd, the Paquebot, the Revue maritime et coloniale — every sheet devoted to the insurance companies, which were threatening to raise their premium rates — were unanimous on this point.

Public opinion having declared itself, the states of the Union were the first to act. Preparations were made in New York for an expedition to hunt down the narwhal. A fast frigate, the Abraham Lincoln, made ready to put to sea as soon as possible. The arsenals were thrown open to Commander Farragut, who pressed briskly ahead with the arming of his frigate.

And, precisely as always happens, the very moment it was decided to give chase to the monster, the monster ceased to appear. For two months no one heard a word of it. Not a single ship crossed its path. It was as if this Unicorn had gotten wind of the plots being hatched against it. There had been so much talk of it — even over the transatlantic cable! And so the wits maintained that this sly creature had intercepted some passing telegram and was now turning it to its own advantage.

So there was the frigate, fitted out for a long cruise and equipped with formidable fishing gear, and no one knew where to point it. Impatience was mounting, when, on July 2, word came that a steamer of the San Francisco to Shanghai line had sighted the animal again three weeks earlier, in the northern seas of the Pacific.

The excitement this news caused was extreme. Commander Farragut was not granted twenty-four hours' respite. His provisions were aboard. His bunkers were bursting with coal. Not a man was missing from the crew rolls. He had only to light his furnaces, build up steam, and cast off! He would not have been forgiven half a day's delay. But then, Commander Farragut asked for nothing better than to be off.

Three hours before the Abraham Lincoln left the Brooklyn pier, I received a letter worded as follows:

Professor Aronnax, of the Paris Museum, Fifth Avenue Hotel.

New York.

"Sir,

If you would care to join the expedition of the Abraham Lincoln, the government of the Union will be pleased to see France represented by you in this undertaking. Commander Farragut has a cabin at your disposal.

Very cordially yours, J. B. HOBSON, Secretary of the Navy."

As Master Pleases

Three seconds before J.-B. Hobson's letter arrived, I no more dreamed of chasing the Unicorn than of attempting the Northwest Passage. Three seconds after reading the letter from the honorable Secretary of the Navy, I understood at last that my true calling, the single purpose of my life, was to hunt down this troubling monster and rid the world of it.

And yet I had just come back from a grueling expedition, worn out and hungry for rest. I longed for nothing but to see my own country again, my friends, my little lodging at the Jardin des Plantes, my dear and precious collections! But nothing could hold me back. I forgot everything — the fatigue, the friends, the collections — and without another moment's thought I accepted the American government's offer.

"Besides," I told myself, "every road leads back to Europe, and the Unicorn will be kind enough to draw me toward the coasts of France! The worthy creature will let itself be caught in European waters — purely for my personal convenience — and I mean to bring back no less than half a meter of its ivory halberd for the Museum of Natural History."

But in the meantime, I would have to go looking for this narwhal in the northern Pacific — which, as a way of getting home to France, meant taking the road to the antipodes.

"Conseil!" I called, impatience in my voice.

Conseil was my manservant. A devoted young fellow who came with me on all my travels; a good-hearted Fleming I was fond of, and who returned the feeling in full — a man phlegmatic by nature, methodical on principle, dutiful out of habit, seldom rattled by whatever surprises life sprang on him, very clever with his hands, ready for any task, and, in spite of his name, one who never gave advice — not even when nobody asked him for any.

From rubbing shoulders with the scientists of our little world at the Jardin des Plantes, Conseil had ended up picking up a thing or two. In him I had a genuine specialist, thoroughly grounded in natural-history classification, who could scramble up and down the whole ladder of branches, groups, classes, subclasses, orders, families, genera, subgenera, species, and varieties with the agility of an acrobat. But his science stopped right there. Classifying was his life, and he knew nothing beyond it. Deeply versed in the theory of classification, and hardly at all in its practice, he could not, I believe, have told a sperm whale from a right whale! And yet, what a fine and worthy young fellow!

For ten years now, Conseil had followed me wherever science dragged me. Never once a word from him about the length of a journey or how tired it left him. Never an objection to buckling up his suitcase for whatever country you cared to name — China or the Congo, however far off it lay. Off he went, there as here, and asked for nothing more. On top of that, he enjoyed splendid health that defied every illness; solid muscles, but no nerves — not the least trace of nerves, morally speaking, that is.

The young man was thirty years old, and his age stood to his master's as fifteen to twenty. I hope I may be forgiven for admitting, in this roundabout way, that I was forty.

Conseil had just one flaw. A rabid stickler for form, he never spoke to me except in the third person — to the point of being downright irritating.

"Conseil!" I said again, my hands already flying feverishly through my preparations for departure.

I had no doubt whatever about this devoted young man. As a rule I never asked whether it suited him or not to come along on my travels; but this time we were talking about an expedition that might drag on indefinitely, a hazardous undertaking, in pursuit of an animal that could send a frigate to the bottom like a walnut shell! There was matter for reflection here, even for the most unflappable man alive. What would Conseil say?

"Conseil!" I cried a third time.

Conseil appeared.

"Master called?" he said as he came in.

"Yes, my boy. Get me ready, get yourself ready. We leave in two hours."

"Just as Master pleases," Conseil answered calmly.

"Not a moment to lose. Pack into my trunk all my traveling gear, clothes, shirts, socks — don't bother counting them, just as many as you can manage — and be quick about it!"

"And Master's collections?" Conseil observed.

"We'll deal with those later."

"What! The archaeotherium, the hyracotherium, the oreodons, the cheiropotamus, and Master's other carcasses?"

"They'll be kept at the hotel."

"And Master's live babirusa?"

"It'll be fed while we're away. In fact, I'll leave orders to have our whole menagerie shipped off to France."

"So we're not going back to Paris?" Conseil asked.

"Yes... certainly..." I answered evasively, "only by way of a small detour."

"Whatever detour Master pleases."

"Oh, it's a trifling one! A slightly less direct route, that's all. We're taking passage aboard the Abraham Lincoln."

"Just as it suits Master," Conseil answered placidly.

"You see, my friend, it's about the monster... the famous narwhal... We're going to rid the seas of it! The author of a two-volume quarto work on The Mysteries of the Great Ocean Depths can hardly excuse himself from sailing with Commander Farragut. A glorious mission, but — a dangerous one too! There's no knowing where we're bound! These creatures can be very unpredictable! But we'll go all the same! We have a commander who doesn't scare easily!"

"Whatever Master does, I'll do," Conseil answered.

"And think it over carefully! Because I don't want to hide a thing from you. This is one of those voyages a man doesn't always come back from!"

"Just as Master pleases."

A quarter of an hour later, our trunks were ready. Conseil had done it in a flash, and I was certain nothing was missing, for the young man classified shirts and coats every bit as well as birds or mammals.

The hotel elevator set us down in the great vestibule of the mezzanine. I went down the few steps that led to the ground floor. I settled my bill at that vast counter forever besieged by a considerable crowd. I left orders to ship my bales of stuffed animals and dried plants to Paris (France). I opened a line of credit sufficient to keep the babirusa fed, and, with Conseil at my heels, I jumped into a carriage.

The vehicle — twenty francs the trip — went down Broadway as far as Union Square, followed Fourth Avenue to its junction with Bowery Street, turned into Catherine Street, and stopped at Pier 34. There the Catherine ferryboat carried us — men, horses, and carriage — over to Brooklyn, that great annex of New York on the left bank of the East River, and in a few minutes we reached the wharf beside which the Abraham Lincoln was belching torrents of black smoke from its two funnels.

Our baggage was hauled aboard the frigate's deck at once. I hurried on board. I asked for Commander Farragut. One of the sailors led me up to the poop deck, where I found myself face to face with a fine-looking officer who held out his hand to me.

"Monsieur Pierre Aronnax?" he said.

"The very man," I answered. "Commander Farragut?"

"In person. Welcome aboard, Professor. Your cabin is waiting for you."

I bowed, and leaving the commander to the business of getting under way, I had myself shown to the cabin set aside for me.

The Abraham Lincoln had been perfectly chosen and fitted out for its new assignment. It was a fast frigate, equipped with superheating apparatus that let it drive its steam pressure up to seven atmospheres. Under that pressure the Abraham Lincoln reached an average speed of eighteen and three-tenths miles an hour — a considerable pace, yet still not enough to keep up with the gigantic cetacean.

The interior arrangements of the frigate matched its nautical qualities. I was very pleased with my cabin, set in the stern, opening onto the officers' wardroom.

"We'll be comfortable here," I said to Conseil.

"As comfortable, if Master doesn't mind my saying so," Conseil replied, "as a hermit crab in the shell of a whelk."

I left Conseil to stow our trunks properly and went back up on deck to watch the preparations for getting under way.

Just then Commander Farragut was ordering the last moorings cast off that still held the Abraham Lincoln to the Brooklyn pier. And so, a quarter of an hour's delay — even less — and the frigate would have sailed without me, and I would have missed this extraordinary, supernatural, altogether improbable expedition, whose truthful account may yet find a few skeptics.

But Commander Farragut had no intention of losing a single day, or a single hour, in reaching the waters where the animal had just been sighted. He sent for his engineer.

"Are we up to pressure?" he asked.

"Yes, sir," the engineer answered.

"Go ahead," cried Commander Farragut.

At this order, relayed to the engine room by compressed-air devices, the mechanics set the starting wheel in motion. Steam whistled as it rushed into the half-open valves. The long horizontal pistons groaned and shoved the connecting rods of the shaft. The blades of the propeller beat the waves with mounting speed, and the Abraham Lincoln moved off majestically amid a hundred or so ferryboats and tenders loaded with spectators, forming an escort in its honor.

The wharves of Brooklyn and all that part of New York bordering the East River were packed with the curious. Three cheers, torn from five hundred thousand throats, burst out one after another. Thousands of handkerchiefs waved above the dense mass and saluted the Abraham Lincoln until it reached the waters of the Hudson, at the tip of that long peninsula that forms the city of New York.

Then the frigate, following along the New Jersey side the admirable right bank of the river, all lined with villas, passed between the forts, which saluted it with their heaviest guns. The Abraham Lincoln answered by lowering and raising three times the American flag, its thirty-nine stars blazing at the mizzen peak; then, altering course to take the buoyed channel that curves around into the inner bay formed by the point of Sandy Hook, it skimmed that sandy spit where a few thousand spectators cheered it one more time.

The procession of boats and tenders kept following the frigate, and left it only when they came abreast of the lightship whose two beacons mark the entrance to the New York channels.

Three o'clock was striking just then. The pilot climbed down into his boat and rejoined the little schooner waiting for him to leeward. The fires were stoked; the propeller beat the waves faster; the frigate ran along the low yellow coast of Long Island, and at eight o'clock in the evening, having lost the lights of Fire Island to the northwest, it steamed at full speed out onto the dark waters of the Atlantic.

Ned Land

Commander Farragut was a fine sailor, worthy of the frigate he commanded. Ship and man were one; he was her very soul. On the question of the cetacean not a shadow of doubt crossed his mind, and he would not allow the animal's existence to be argued aboard his vessel. He believed in it the way certain devout old women believe in the Leviathan — by faith, not by reason. The monster existed; he would rid the seas of it; he had sworn as much. He was a kind of Knight of Rhodes, a Dieudonné de Gozon marching out to meet the serpent that plagued his island. Either Commander Farragut would kill the narwhal, or the narwhal would kill Commander Farragut. There was no middle ground.

The ship's officers shared their chief's opinion. You should have heard them talk, debate, argue, calculate the various odds of an encounter, and scan the vast reach of the ocean. More than one volunteered for a watch up in the topgallant crosstrees who, under any other circumstances, would have cursed the chore. As long as the sun traced its daily arc, the masts were crowded with sailors who couldn't hold still — the planks of the deck fairly scorched their feet. And yet the Abraham Lincoln had not yet cut through the suspect waters of the Pacific with her prow.

As for the crew, they asked nothing better than to meet the unicorn, harpoon it, haul it aboard, and carve it up. They watched the sea with scrupulous attention. Besides, Commander Farragut had mentioned a certain sum of two thousand dollars, set aside for whoever — cabin boy or sailor, mate or officer — should be the first to sight the animal. I leave you to imagine how hard the eyes worked aboard the Abraham Lincoln.

For my own part, I was not to be outdone by the others, and I yielded to no one my share of the daily watching. The frigate would have had a hundred reasons to call herself the Argus. Alone among us all, Conseil met the question that so consumed the rest of us with sheer indifference, striking a jarring note against the general enthusiasm on board.

I've said that Commander Farragut had carefully outfitted his ship with every device suited to fishing up the gigantic cetacean. A whaler could not have been better armed. We had every known engine of the trade, from the hand-thrown harpoon to the barbed arrows of blunderbusses and the explosive shells of duck guns. Up on the forecastle stretched a perfected cannon, breech-loading, very thick in the walls and very narrow in the bore, whose model is due to appear at the Universal Exposition of 1867. This precious instrument, of American make, could throw a four-kilogram conical shell an average distance of sixteen kilometers without the least strain.

So the Abraham Lincoln lacked no means of destruction. But she had something better still. She had Ned Land, the king of the harpooners.

Ned Land was a Canadian, with a rare gift in his hands and no equal in his perilous trade. Skill and cool nerve, daring and cunning — he possessed these qualities to a superior degree, and it took an unusually crafty whale, or a singularly sly sperm whale, to escape a thrust of his harpoon.

Ned Land was about forty. He was a tall man — over six English feet — powerfully built, grave in manner, not much of a talker, at times violent, and thoroughly furious when crossed. His person drew your attention, above all the force of his gaze, which gave a striking accent to his whole face.

I think Commander Farragut had done wisely in signing this man aboard. For eye and arm, he was worth the whole crew put together. I can't compare him to anything better than a powerful telescope that was at the same time a cannon always ready to fire.

To say Canadian is to say Frenchman, and, reserved as Ned Land was, I have to admit he took a certain liking to me. My nationality drew him, no doubt. It was a chance for him to speak, and for me to hear, that old language of Rabelais still in use in a few Canadian provinces. The harpooner's family came originally from Quebec, and had already produced a whole tribe of bold fishermen back when that city belonged to France.

Little by little, Ned developed a taste for talking, and I loved to hear the tales of his adventures in the polar seas. He told the story of his fishing and his battles with a great natural poetry. His account took on an epic shape, and I felt I was listening to some Canadian Homer, singing the Iliad of the far northern regions.

I'm sketching this bold companion for you now as I know him at present. For we have become old friends, bound by that unshakable friendship that is born and cemented in the most terrifying trials. Ah, brave Ned! I ask nothing more than to live another hundred years, so I can remember you all the longer!

And now — what was Ned Land's opinion on the question of the sea monster? I have to admit he scarcely believed in the unicorn, and that, alone on board, he did not share the general conviction. He even avoided the subject, which one day I felt I had to take up with him.

On a magnificent evening — the 30th of July, that is, three weeks after our departure — the frigate lay off Cape Blanco, thirty miles to leeward of the Patagonian coast. We had crossed the Tropic of Capricorn, and the Strait of Magellan opened less than seven hundred miles to the south. Within a week the Abraham Lincoln would be furrowing the waves of the Pacific.

Seated on the poop deck, Ned Land and I chatted about this and that, gazing at that mysterious sea whose depths have so far remained beyond the reach of human eyes. Quite naturally I steered the conversation around to the giant unicorn, and weighed the various chances of success or failure for our expedition. Then, seeing that Ned let me talk without saying much of anything, I pressed him more directly.

"Come now, Ned," I asked him, "how can you not be convinced of the existence of the cetacean we're chasing? Do you have some particular reason for being so skeptical?"

The harpooner looked at me for a few moments before answering, struck his broad forehead with his hand — a habitual gesture of his — closed his eyes as if to gather his thoughts, and said at last:

"Maybe I do, Professor Aronnax."

"And yet, Ned — you, a whaler by profession, you who are on familiar terms with the great marine mammals, you whose imagination ought to accept the notion of enormous cetaceans easily enough — you of all people should be the last to doubt in a case like this!"

"That's just where you're wrong, Professor," Ned answered. "Let the common crowd believe in extraordinary comets crossing space, or in antediluvian monsters peopling the inside of the globe — fine, let it pass. But neither the astronomer nor the geologist admits such fairy tales. Same with the whaler. I've chased plenty of cetaceans, I've harpooned a great many, I've killed several, but powerful and well-armed as they were, neither their tails nor their tusks could ever have dented the sheet-iron plates of a steamer."

"Even so, Ned, people cite ships that a narwhal's tooth has run clean through, from one side to the other."

"Wooden ships, maybe," the Canadian answered. "Though even that I've never seen. So, until I have proof to the contrary, I deny that whales, sperm whales, or unicorns can produce any such effect."

"Listen to me, Ned—"

"No, Professor, no. Anything you like except that. A giant octopus, perhaps?"

"Even less likely, Ned. The octopus is only a mollusk, and the very word tells you how little firmness there is in its flesh. Even at five hundred feet long, the octopus, which does not belong to the vertebrate branch at all, is completely harmless to ships like the Scotia or the Abraham Lincoln. So the exploits of Krakens and other monsters of that sort have to be filed away among the fables."

"Then, Professor," Ned Land went on, in a fairly mocking tone, "you persist in accepting the existence of a huge cetacean...?"

"Yes, Ned, I say it again, with a conviction that rests on the logic of the facts. I believe in the existence of a mammal, powerfully built, belonging to the vertebrate branch, like the whales, the sperm whales, or the dolphins, and equipped with a horny tusk of extreme penetrating force."

"Hmph!" said the harpooner, shaking his head with the air of a man who refuses to let himself be convinced.

"Consider, my worthy Canadian," I went on, "that if such an animal exists, if it inhabits the depths of the ocean, if it frequents the liquid layers some miles below the surface of the waters, then it must necessarily possess a body whose strength defies all comparison."

"And why such a powerful body?" Ned asked.

"Because it takes an incalculable force to hold oneself in the deep layers and resist their pressure."

"Really?" said Ned, watching me with a wink.

"Really, and a few figures will prove it to you easily enough."

"Oh, figures!" Ned shot back. "You can make figures say whatever you want!"

"In business, Ned, but not in mathematics. Listen to me. Let's say the pressure of one atmosphere is represented by the pressure of a column of water thirty-two feet high. In reality the column would be a bit shorter, since we're dealing with seawater, whose density is greater than that of fresh water. Well then, when you dive, Ned, for every thirty-two feet of water above you, your body bears a pressure equal to that of the atmosphere — that is, so many kilograms for every square centimeter of its surface. It follows that at three hundred and twenty feet this pressure is ten atmospheres, a hundred atmospheres at three thousand two hundred feet, and a thousand atmospheres at thirty-two thousand feet — roughly two and a half leagues down. Which amounts to saying that if you could reach that depth in the ocean, every square centimeter of your body's surface would undergo a pressure of a thousand kilograms. Now, my good Ned, do you know how many square centimeters of surface you have?"

"I haven't the faintest idea, Professor Aronnax."

"About seventeen thousand."

"As many as that?"

"And since in reality atmospheric pressure is a little more than the weight of one kilogram per square centimeter, your seventeen thousand square centimeters are, at this very moment, bearing a pressure of seventeen thousand five hundred and sixty-eight kilograms."

"Without my noticing it?"

"Without your noticing it. And if you're not crushed by such a pressure, it's because the air penetrates the inside of your body with an equal pressure. Hence a perfect balance between the inward push and the outward push, which cancel each other out, and that lets you bear it without any trouble. But in the water, it's another matter."

"Yes, I understand," Ned answered, grown more attentive, "because the water surrounds me and doesn't penetrate me."

"Exactly, Ned. So, at thirty-two feet below the surface of the sea, you'd undergo a pressure of seventeen thousand five hundred and sixty-eight kilograms; at three hundred and twenty feet, ten times that pressure, or a hundred and seventy-five thousand six hundred and eighty kilograms; at three thousand two hundred feet, a hundred times that pressure, or seventeen hundred and fifty-six thousand eight hundred kilograms; and at thirty-two thousand feet, finally, a thousand times that pressure, or seventeen million five hundred and sixty-eight thousand kilograms — that is to say, you'd be flattened as if they'd pulled you out from between the plates of a hydraulic press!"

"The devil!" said Ned.

"Well then, my worthy harpooner, if vertebrates several hundred meters long, and thick in proportion, hold themselves at such depths — creatures whose surface is measured in millions of square centimeters — then the push they undergo has to be reckoned in billions of kilograms. Calculate, then, what the resistance of their bony framework must be, and the power of their whole organism, to withstand such pressures!"

"They'd have to be built," Ned Land answered, "out of eight-inch iron plate, like the ironclad frigates."

"Just as you say, Ned. And think, then, of the havoc such a mass could wreak, launched with the speed of an express train against a ship's hull."

"Yes... true enough... maybe," the Canadian answered, shaken by these figures but unwilling to give in.

"Well, have I convinced you?"

"You've convinced me of one thing, Professor — that if such animals exist at the bottom of the sea, they'd necessarily have to be as strong as you say."

"But if they don't exist, you stubborn harpooner, how do you explain the accident that happened to the Scotia?"

"It's maybe...," said Ned, hesitating.

"Go on!"

"Because... it just isn't true!" the Canadian answered, unknowingly reproducing a famous reply of Arago's.

But that answer proved nothing but the harpooner's obstinacy. That day I pressed him no further. The Scotia's accident could not be denied. The hole existed so plainly that it had had to be plugged, and I don't think the existence of a hole can be demonstrated more categorically than that. Now, that hole had not made itself, and since it had not been produced by underwater rocks or underwater machines, it was necessarily due to the piercing tool of some animal.

Now, in my opinion — and for all the reasons deduced above — that animal belonged to the branch of vertebrates, to the class of mammals, to the group of the fish-shaped, and finally to the order of cetaceans. As for the family in which it ranked — whale, sperm whale, or dolphin — as for the genus it belonged to, as for the species in which it ought to be classed, that was a question to be cleared up later on. To settle it, one would have to dissect this unknown monster; to dissect it, catch it; to catch it, harpoon it — which was Ned Land's affair; to harpoon it, sight it — which was the crew's affair; and to sight it, meet it — which was the affair of chance.

At a Venture

For a while the Abraham Lincoln's voyage passed without incident. But one occasion arose that threw Ned Land's marvelous skill into sharp relief and showed just how much confidence we ought to place in him.

Off the Falklands, on June 30, the frigate spoke with some American whalers, and we learned that none of them had seen anything of the narwhal. But one of them, the captain of the Monroe, knowing that Ned Land was aboard the Abraham Lincoln, asked for his help in hunting a whale that had come into view. Commander Farragut, eager to see Ned Land at work, gave him leave to go across to the Monroe. And fortune served our Canadian so well that, instead of one whale, he harpooned two at a single double stroke — striking one clean through the heart and taking the other after a chase of only a few minutes!

There's no doubt about it: if the monster ever finds itself on the wrong end of Ned Land's harpoon, I wouldn't bet on the monster.

The frigate ran down the southeastern coast of America at a prodigious clip. By July 3 we lay off the mouth of the Strait of Magellan, level with Cape Vírgenes. But Commander Farragut had no wish to take that winding passage, and maneuvered instead to round Cape Horn.

The crew backed him unanimously. And indeed — was it likely we'd meet the narwhal in that cramped strait? A good many of the sailors swore the monster couldn't get through it, "that it was too big to fit!"

On July 6, around three in the afternoon, the Abraham Lincoln, fifteen miles to the south, rounded that solitary islet, that lost rock at the very tip of the American continent, to which Dutch sailors gave the name of their home town — Cape Horn. The course was set to the northwest, and the next day the frigate's propeller was at last churning the waters of the Pacific.

"Keep your eyes peeled! Keep your eyes peeled!" the sailors of the Abraham Lincoln kept telling one another.

And peel them they did, enormously. Eyes and spyglasses — a little dazzled, it's true, by the prospect of two thousand dollars — never rested for a moment. Day and night we watched the surface of the ocean, and the night-sighted ones, whose ability to see in the dark boosted their chances by fifty percent, were well placed to win the prize.

As for me, though the lure of money held little appeal, I was hardly the least attentive man aboard. Giving only a few minutes to meals, a few hours to sleep, indifferent to sun or rain, I no longer left the deck. Now leaning over the bulwarks of the forecastle, now propped against the after rail, I devoured with a greedy eye the cottony wake that whitened the sea as far as it could reach. And how many times I shared the thrill of the officers and crew when some capricious whale lifted its blackish back above the waves! In an instant the frigate's deck would fill. The hatchways would spew a torrent of sailors and officers. Each one, chest heaving, eyes clouded, watched the passage of the great mammal. I looked, I looked until I wore out my retina, until I nearly went blind, while Conseil, forever phlegmatic, would say to me in his calm way:

"If Master would be so good as to open his eyes a little less wide, Master would see a great deal more!"

But it was all wasted feeling! The Abraham Lincoln would change course, run down the sighted animal — an ordinary whale or a common sperm whale — which soon vanished amid a chorus of curses!

The weather, though, held fair. The voyage was going on under the best of conditions. It was then the bad season in the southern hemisphere, for July in that zone corresponds to our European January; but the sea stayed calm, and let itself be scanned easily over a wide sweep.

Ned Land still showed the most stubborn skepticism; he even made a point of not examining the surface of the waves outside of his watch — at least when no whale was in view. And yet his marvelous powers of vision would have done us great service. But eight hours out of twelve, that pigheaded Canadian read or slept in his cabin. A hundred times I reproached him for his indifference.

"Bah!" he'd answer. "There's nothing out there, Professor Aronnax, and even if there were some animal, what chance do we have of spotting it? Aren't we just running along at a venture? People say this creature nobody can find was sighted again in the high seas of the Pacific — fine, I'll grant that — but two months have already gone by since that encounter, and going by the temperament of your narwhal, it doesn't care to sit and molder for long in the same waters! It's gifted with a prodigious knack for getting around. Now, you know better than I do, Professor, that nature does nothing without a reason, and she wouldn't give an animal that's slow by nature the power to move fast if it had no need to use it. So, if the beast exists, it's already far away!"

To that I had no answer. Plainly we were groping in the dark. But what other way was there to go about it? And so our chances were quite limited. Still, no one yet doubted our success, and not a sailor aboard would have bet against the narwhal and its next appearance.

On July 20 we cut the Tropic of Capricorn at 105° longitude, and on the 27th of the same month we crossed the equator at the hundred-and-tenth meridian. That fix taken, the frigate held a more decided course to the west and struck into the central seas of the Pacific.

Commander Farragut reasoned, rightly, that it was better to keep to deep water and stay well clear of the continents and islands whose approach the animal had always seemed to avoid — "no doubt because there wasn't enough water there for it!" as the boatswain put it. So the frigate stood off from the Pomotou group, the Marquesas, and the Sandwich Islands, cut the Tropic of Cancer at 132° longitude, and steered for the seas of China.

We were at last on the stage of the monster's latest antics! And to tell the truth, life aboard had become unlivable. Hearts pounded dreadfully, laying up incurable aneurysms for the future. The whole crew labored under a nervous overexcitement I couldn't begin to describe. No one ate, no one slept. Twenty times a day a misjudgment, an optical illusion of some sailor perched on the crosstrees, caused unbearable anguish, and these emotions, repeated twenty times over, kept us in a state of agitation too violent not to bring on a reaction before long.

And indeed, the reaction was not slow in coming. For three months — three months in which every day lasted a century! — the Abraham Lincoln plowed all the northern seas of the Pacific, chasing after every whale that was sighted, veering off course abruptly, swinging suddenly from one tack to the other, stopping dead, forcing or reversing its steam again and again at the risk of throwing its engine off its bearings, and it left not a single point unexplored from the shores of Japan to the American coast. And nothing! Nothing but the immensity of the empty waves! Nothing that looked like a gigantic narwhal, or a submarine islet, or the wreck of a foundered ship, or a fleeing reef, or anything whatever of the supernatural!

So the reaction set in. Discouragement took hold of everyone's mind first, and opened a breach for disbelief. A new sentiment sprang up aboard, made of three-tenths shame against seven-tenths fury. We had been "utter fools" to let ourselves be taken in by a fantasy — but even more furious than foolish! The mountains of arguments piled up over a year came crashing down all at once, and each man thought of nothing now but making up, at meals or in sleep, for the time he'd so stupidly sacrificed.

With the natural fickleness of the human mind, we flung ourselves from one excess to the other. The warmest champions of the enterprise inevitably became its most ardent detractors. The reaction rose up from the depths of the ship, from the stokers' quarters clear to the officers' wardroom, and certainly, without a very particular stubbornness on Commander Farragut's part, the frigate would have finally turned her head back to the south.

Still, this fruitless search could not go on much longer. The Abraham Lincoln had nothing to reproach herself with, having done everything to succeed. Never did the crew of an American naval vessel show more patience and more zeal; the failure could not be laid at their door; there was nothing left but to turn back.

A representation to that effect was made to the commander. The commander held firm. The sailors made no secret of their discontent, and the service suffered for it. I don't mean to say there was mutiny aboard, but after a reasonable spell of obstinacy, Commander Farragut — like Columbus before him — asked for three days' patience. If within a delay of three days the monster had not appeared, the helmsman would give three turns of the wheel, and the Abraham Lincoln would make for European seas.

This promise was given on November 2. Its first effect was to revive the flagging spirits of the crew. The ocean was watched with fresh attention. Every man wanted to give it that last look in which all one's memory is summed up. The spyglasses went to work with a feverish energy. It was a supreme challenge flung at the giant narwhal, and the creature could not in reason excuse itself from answering that summons "to appear!"

Two days went by. The Abraham Lincoln kept under low steam. We tried a thousand tricks to rouse the attention or stir the apathy of the animal, in case it happened to be found in these waters. Enormous quarters of bacon were streamed astern — to the great satisfaction of the sharks, I must admit. The boats fanned out in every direction around the Abraham Lincoln while she lay to, and left not a point of sea unexplored. But the evening of November 4 arrived without this undersea mystery being unveiled.

The next day, November 5, at noon, the final deadline ran out. After the fix was taken, Commander Farragut, faithful to his promise, was to give the course to the southeast and abandon for good the northern regions of the Pacific.

The frigate then lay at 31°15′ north latitude and 136°42′ east longitude. The lands of Japan were less than two hundred miles off to leeward. Night was coming on. Eight bells had just struck. Great clouds veiled the disk of the moon, then in its first quarter. The sea heaved peacefully under the frigate's stem.

At that moment I was leaning at the bow, on the starboard bulwark. Conseil, posted beside me, gazed straight ahead. The crew, perched in the rigging, scanned the horizon as it narrowed and darkened little by little. The officers, armed with their night glasses, searched the growing gloom. Now and then the somber ocean glittered under a ray the moon shot between the fringe of two clouds. Then every trace of light died away in the darkness.

Watching Conseil, I noticed that the good fellow was feeling, ever so slightly, the general influence. At least I thought so. Perhaps, and for the first time, his nerves were quivering under the touch of a feeling of curiosity.

"Come now, Conseil," I said to him, "here's a last chance to pocket two thousand dollars."

"If Master will permit me to say so," Conseil replied, "I never counted on that prize, and the government of the Union could have promised a hundred thousand dollars — it wouldn't have been any the poorer for it."

"You're right, Conseil. It's a foolish business after all, and one we threw ourselves into too lightly. So much time lost, so much wasted feeling! Six months ago we'd already be back in France —"

"In Master's little apartment," Conseil answered, "in Master's Museum! And I'd already have classified Master's fossils! And Master's babirusa would be settled in its cage at the Jardin des Plantes, drawing every curious soul in the capital!"

"Just so, Conseil, and — not to mention, I imagine — that they'll all be laughing at us!"

"Quite so," Conseil answered tranquilly. "I do think they'll laugh at Master. And — must I say it...?"

"You must say it, Conseil."

"Well then, Master will have no more than he deserves!"

"Really!"

"When one has the honor of being a scholar like Master, one doesn't lay oneself open to —"

Conseil couldn't finish his compliment. In the midst of the general silence, a voice had just made itself heard. It was the voice of Ned Land, and Ned Land was shouting:

"Ahoy! The thing in question, to leeward, off our beam!"

At Full Steam

At that shout the whole crew rushed toward the harpooner — commander, officers, mates, sailors, ship's boys, right down to the engineers who abandoned their engine and the stokers who left their furnaces. The order to stop had been given, and the frigate was now only coasting on her own momentum.

The darkness was deep just then, and good as the Canadian's eyes were, I wondered how he had seen anything, and what it was he could have seen. My heart was pounding fit to burst.

But Ned Land had not been mistaken, and all of us made out the object he was pointing to with his hand.

Two cable-lengths off the Abraham Lincoln's starboard quarter, the sea seemed to be lit from below. This was no mere trick of phosphorescence — there could be no mistaking it. The monster, submerged a few fathoms beneath the surface, was throwing off that intense but unexplained glow which several captains had described in their reports. This magnificent radiance had to be produced by some agent of tremendous illuminating power. The luminous patch traced across the sea an immense, elongated oval, at the center of which glowed a burning core whose unbearable brilliance faded off by successive degrees toward the edges.

"It's nothing but a cluster of phosphorescent molecules," cried one of the officers.

"No, sir," I replied with conviction. "Piddocks and salps never give off a light that powerful. This glow is electrical in nature... Besides — look, look! It's moving! It's coming forward, going back! It's charging straight at us!"

A general cry went up from the frigate.

"Silence!" ordered Commander Farragut. "Helm hard to windward! Engine astern!"

The sailors sprang to the wheel, the engineers to their engine. The steam was reversed at once, and the Abraham Lincoln, swinging to port, described a half-circle.

"Helm amidships! Engine ahead!" shouted Commander Farragut.

These orders were carried out, and the frigate pulled rapidly away from the glowing core.

Let me correct that. She meant to pull away, but the supernatural animal closed the distance at twice her speed.

We were breathless. Astonishment, far more than fear, held us silent and motionless. The animal ran us down as though it were a game. It circled the frigate, which was then making fourteen knots, and wrapped her in its electric sheets like a luminous dust. Then it drew off two or three miles, leaving behind a phosphorescent wake like the swirling clouds of steam an express locomotive flings out behind it. All at once, from the dark edge of the horizon, where it had gone to gather speed, the monster shot suddenly toward the Abraham Lincoln with terrifying swiftness, stopped short twenty feet from her wales, and went dark — not by sinking beneath the water, since its brilliance did not fade by degrees, but abruptly, as if the source of that dazzling outpouring had suddenly run dry. Then it reappeared on the other side of the ship, whether it had rounded her or slipped beneath her keel. At any instant a collision might occur that would be fatal to us.

Meanwhile, I was surprised at the frigate's maneuvers. She was fleeing, not attacking. She was being chased — she who ought to have been the hunter — and I said as much to Commander Farragut. His face, ordinarily so impassive, wore a look of indefinable bewilderment.

"Professor Aronnax," he answered me, "I don't know what formidable creature I'm dealing with, and I won't rashly risk my frigate in the middle of all this darkness. Besides, how do you attack something unknown, how do you defend against it? Let's wait for daylight, and then the roles will be reversed."

"You've no more doubt, Commander, as to the nature of the animal?"

"None, sir. It's obviously a gigantic narwhal — but an electric narwhal as well."

"Then perhaps," I added, "there's no more coming near it than there is coming near an electric eel or a torpedo ray."

"Just so," the commander replied. "And if it carries a thunderbolt's power inside it, then it's surely the most terrible animal that ever came from the hand of the Creator. That is why, sir, I intend to keep my guard up."

The whole crew stayed on their feet all through the night. No one dreamed of sleeping. The Abraham Lincoln, unable to match the creature for speed, had eased off and was holding under low steam. For its part the narwhal, imitating the frigate, let itself be rocked at the pleasure of the swell, and seemed determined not to abandon the field of battle.

Toward midnight, however, it disappeared — or, to put it more exactly, it "went out" like a big glowworm. Had it fled? That was to be feared, not hoped for. But at seven minutes to one in the morning came a deafening whistle, like the sound of a column of water driven up with extreme violence.

Commander Farragut, Ned Land, and I were on the poop deck just then, peering hungrily into the deep darkness.

"Ned Land," asked the commander, "you've often heard whales bellow?"

"Often, sir. But never a whale like this, one whose sight has earned me two thousand dollars."

"Quite right, you've a claim to the bounty. But tell me — isn't that the noise cetaceans make when they blow water out through their blowholes?"

"The same noise, sir, but this one is beyond all comparison louder. So there's no mistaking it. That's a cetacean out there in our waters, sure enough. With your permission, sir," the harpooner added, "we'll have a word or two with it tomorrow at daybreak."

"If it's in the mood to hear you out, Master Land," I said, in a tone of little conviction.

"Just let me get within four harpoon-lengths of it," the Canadian shot back, "and it'll have to hear me out!"

"But to get near it," the commander went on, "I'll have to put a whaleboat at your disposal?"

"No doubt, sir."

"That means gambling with my men's lives?"

"And with mine!" the harpooner answered simply.

Toward two in the morning the glowing core reappeared, no less intense, five miles to windward of the Abraham Lincoln. Despite the distance, despite the noise of wind and sea, we could distinctly hear the animal's formidable tail-strokes and even its panting breath. It seemed that when the enormous narwhal came up to breathe at the surface of the ocean, the air rushed into its lungs the way steam rushes into the vast cylinders of a two-thousand-horsepower engine.

"Hm!" I thought. "A whale with the strength of a cavalry regiment — that would be a fine whale indeed!"

We stayed on the alert until daybreak and made ready for battle. The fishing gear was laid out along the bulwarks. The first mate had them load those blunderbuss guns that can fire a harpoon a mile, and long duck-guns with explosive shells whose wound is mortal even to the mightiest animals. Ned Land had contented himself with sharpening his harpoon — a terrible weapon in his hand.

At six o'clock dawn began to break, and with the first gleams of daylight the narwhal's electric glow vanished. At seven the day had come up well enough, but a very thick morning mist shrank the horizon, and the best spyglasses could not pierce it. Hence disappointment and anger.

I hauled myself up to the mizzen crosstrees. A few officers had already perched themselves at the mastheads.

At eight the mist rolled heavily off the water, and its thick coils lifted little by little. The horizon widened and cleared all at once.

Suddenly, just as the evening before, Ned Land's voice rang out.

"The thing in question, off the port quarter!" cried the harpooner.

Every eye turned toward the point he indicated.

There, a mile and a half from the frigate, a long blackish body rose a meter above the waves. Its tail, lashing furiously, kicked up a considerable churn. Never did a caudal fin beat the sea with such power. An immense wake, dazzlingly white, marked the animal's passage and traced a long, sweeping curve.

The frigate drew near the cetacean. I studied it with a perfectly clear mind. The reports of the Shannon and the Helvetia had somewhat exaggerated its dimensions, and I put its length at only two hundred and fifty feet. As for its girth, I could gauge it only with difficulty; but on the whole the animal struck me as admirably proportioned in all three dimensions.

While I was observing this phenomenal creature, two jets of steam and water shot from its blowholes and rose to a height of forty meters, which settled the matter of how it breathed. From this I concluded definitively that it belonged to the branch of the vertebrates, the class of mammals, the subclass of monodelphians, the group of the fish-shaped, the order of cetaceans, family... Here I could not yet pronounce. The order of cetaceans comprises three families: the baleen whales, the sperm whales, and the dolphins — and it is among the last of these that the narwhals are classed. Each of these families divides into several genera, each genus into species, each species into varieties. Variety, species, genus, and family still eluded me, but I had no doubt of completing my classification with the help of heaven and Commander Farragut.

The crew was waiting impatiently for their chief's orders. He, after attentively studying the animal, sent for the engineer. The engineer came running.

"Sir," said the commander, "do you have your pressure up?"

"Yes, sir," the engineer answered.

"Good. Stoke your fires, and full steam ahead!"

Three cheers greeted this order. The hour of the fight had struck. A few moments later the frigate's two funnels were vomiting torrents of black smoke, and the deck shuddered under the trembling of the boilers.

Driven forward by her powerful propeller, the Abraham Lincoln headed straight at the animal. It let her come up to within half a cable-length, indifferently; then, disdaining to dive, it put on a little turn of flight and merely kept its distance.

This pursuit went on for about three quarters of an hour, without the frigate gaining a fathom on the cetacean. It was plain, then, that at this rate she would never overtake it.

Commander Farragut furiously twisted the thick tuft of hair that sprouted beneath his chin.

"Ned Land?" he called.

The Canadian came to the order.

"Well, Master Land," the commander asked, "do you still advise me to put my boats over the side?"

"No, sir," Ned Land answered, "because that beast won't be taken unless it chooses to be."

"Then what do we do?"

"Force the steam, if you can, sir. As for me — with your permission, that is — I'll go and post myself under the bobstays of the bowsprit, and if we come within harpoon-range, I harpoon."

"Go to it, Ned," Commander Farragut answered. "Engineer!" he shouted. "Get your pressure up."

Ned Land went off to his post. The fires were pushed harder; the propeller turned forty-three revolutions a minute, and the steam hissed out through the valves. The log was heaved, and it showed that the Abraham Lincoln was making eighteen and a half miles an hour.

But the cursed animal was also cruising along at eighteen and a half miles an hour.

For another hour the frigate held to that pace without gaining a single fathom. It was humiliating for one of the fastest ships in the American navy. A dull rage ran through the crew. The sailors cursed the monster, which for its part disdained to answer them. Commander Farragut no longer contented himself with twisting his little beard; he was biting it.

The engineer was called once again.

"You've reached your maximum pressure?" the commander asked him.

"Yes, sir," the engineer answered.

"And your valves are loaded?"

"To six and a half atmospheres."

"Load them to ten atmospheres."

There was an American order if ever there was one. They'd have done no better on the Mississippi to outrun a "competitor"!

"Conseil," I said to my good servant, who was standing near me, "do you realize we're probably going to blow up?"

"Just as Master pleases!" answered Conseil.

Well — I'll admit it — this was a chance I was not sorry to run.

The valves were loaded. The coal was shoveled into the furnaces. The ventilators sent torrents of air over the blazing fires. The Abraham Lincoln's speed climbed. Her masts trembled down to their very steps, and the swirling smoke could barely find a way out through the too-narrow funnels.

The log was heaved a second time.

"Well, helmsman?" asked Commander Farragut.

"Nineteen and three-tenths miles, sir."

"Stoke the fires harder."

The engineer obeyed. The pressure gauge read ten atmospheres. But the cetacean "got its steam up" too, no doubt, for without the least strain it went right on making its nineteen and three-tenths miles.

What a chase! No, I cannot describe the emotion that set my whole being trembling. Ned Land held his post, harpoon in hand. Several times the animal let itself be approached.

"We're gaining on it! We're gaining on it!" cried the Canadian.

Then, just as he was getting ready to strike, the cetacean would slip away at a speed I cannot reckon at less than thirty miles an hour. And even at our top speed, didn't it take the liberty of mocking the frigate by circling clean around her! A howl of fury broke from every chest.

By noon we were no further along than we had been at eight in the morning.

Commander Farragut then resolved to try more direct means.

"Ah!" he said. "So this animal goes faster than the Abraham Lincoln! Well then — we'll see whether it can outrun her conical shells. Master-at-arms, men to the forward gun."

The forecastle gun was loaded and trained at once. The shot went off, but the shell passed a few feet over the cetacean, which was holding at half a mile.

"Someone with a better aim!" cried the commander. "And five hundred dollars to whoever pierces that infernal beast!"

An old gunner with a gray beard — I can see him still — calm-eyed, cold of face, stepped up to his gun, brought it into position, and took a long aim. A heavy detonation burst out, mingled with the cheers of the crew.

The shell found its mark; it struck the animal, but not squarely, and glancing off its rounded surface, went to lose itself two miles out to sea.

"Well now!" said the old gunner, seething. "That rogue's plated with six-inch armor!"

"Damnation!" cried Commander Farragut.

The chase began again, and Commander Farragut, leaning toward me, said:

"I'll hound that animal until my frigate blows apart!"

"Yes," I answered, "and you'll be right to!"

We could hope the animal would wear itself out, that it wouldn't be as indifferent to fatigue as a steam engine. But nothing of the kind. The hours went by without its giving any sign of exhaustion.

Still, it must be said to the Abraham Lincoln's credit that she battled on with tireless tenacity. I reckon at no less than five hundred kilometers the distance she covered during that ill-fated day of November 6! But night came and wrapped the heaving ocean in its shadows.

At that moment I believed our expedition was over, and that we would never see the fantastic animal again. I was mistaken.

At ten fifty in the evening, the electric glow reappeared, three miles to windward of the frigate, as pure and as intense as during the previous night.

The narwhal seemed motionless. Perhaps, worn out by its day, it was sleeping, giving itself up to the swaying of the waves? Here was a chance that Commander Farragut resolved to seize.

He gave his orders. The Abraham Lincoln was held under low steam and advanced cautiously so as not to wake its adversary. It is not unusual to come upon whales fast asleep in the open ocean, and attack them then with success, and Ned Land had harpooned more than one in its sleep. The Canadian went back to take up his post under the bobstays of the bowsprit.

The frigate crept up without a sound, stopped two cable-lengths from the animal, and coasted on her own way. No one aboard breathed anymore. A deep silence reigned over the deck. We were not a hundred feet from the burning core, whose glow grew and dazzled our eyes.

At that moment, leaning over the rail of the forecastle, I saw below me Ned Land, clinging with one hand to the martingale stay, brandishing his terrible harpoon with the other. Scarcely twenty feet separated him from the motionless animal.

All at once his arm shot out violently, and the harpoon was hurled. I heard the ringing clang of the weapon, which seemed to have struck some hard body.

The electric glow went out suddenly, and two enormous waterspouts crashed down onto the frigate's deck, running like a torrent from bow to stern, knocking men off their feet, snapping the lashings of the spare spars.

A frightful shock followed, and, flung over the rail before I had time to catch hold of anything, I was hurled into the sea.

A Whale of Unknown Species
