Startled as I was by that sudden plunge, I nonetheless kept a very clear impression of my sensations.

At first I was dragged down to a depth of about twenty feet. I am a strong swimmer — without claiming to rival Byron and Edgar Poe, who are masters of the art — and the fall did not make me lose my head. Two vigorous kicks brought me back to the surface.

My first concern was to look for the frigate. Had the crew noticed my disappearance? Had the Abraham Lincoln come about? Was Commander Farragut lowering a boat? Could I dare hope to be saved?

The darkness was deep. I caught a glimpse of a black mass vanishing away to the east, its running lights dying out in the distance. It was the frigate. I felt that I was lost.

"Help! Help me!" I shouted, swimming toward the Abraham Lincoln with desperate strokes.

My clothes weighed me down. The water plastered them to my body; they paralyzed my movements. I was sinking! I was suffocating!

"Help!"

That was the last cry I flung out. My mouth filled with water. I struggled, dragged down into the abyss —

Suddenly my clothes were seized by a powerful hand, I felt myself hauled violently back to the surface, and I heard — yes, I heard — these words spoken close to my ear:

"If Master would be so very kind as to lean on my shoulder, Master would swim a great deal more comfortably."

I grabbed the arm of my faithful Conseil with one hand.

"You!" I said. "You!"

"Myself," Conseil answered, "and at Master's orders."

"That shock threw you overboard at the same time it threw me?"

"Not at all. But being in Master's service, I followed Master."

The good fellow found that the most natural thing in the world!

"And the frigate?" I asked.

"The frigate!" Conseil answered, rolling onto his back. "I think Master would do well not to count too much on her."

"What do you mean?"

"I mean that just as I was throwing myself into the sea, I heard the men at the helm cry out, 'The propeller and the rudder are broken —'"

"Broken?"

"Yes! Broken by the monster's tooth. That's the only damage the Abraham Lincoln suffered, I believe. But it's an awkward circumstance for us — she can no longer steer."

"Then we're lost!"

"Perhaps," Conseil replied calmly. "Still, we have a few hours ahead of us, and a great deal can be done in a few hours."

Conseil's unshakable composure put fresh heart into me. I swam more vigorously; but hampered by my clothes, which gripped me like a cloak of lead, I found it extremely hard to keep myself up. Conseil noticed.

"Will Master permit me to make an incision?" he said.

And slipping an open knife under my clothes, he slit them from top to bottom with one quick stroke. Then he swiftly stripped them off me, while I swam for us both.

In my turn I did the same service for Conseil, and we went on "navigating" side by side.

Even so, our situation was no less terrible. Perhaps our disappearance had not been noticed; and even if it had, the frigate — stripped of her rudder — could not beat back upwind toward us. We could count on nothing but her boats.

Conseil reasoned this out coldly and made his plan accordingly. An astonishing nature! This phlegmatic fellow was as much at home here as in his own room.

So it was decided that, since our only chance of rescue lay in being picked up by the Abraham Lincoln's boats, we had to arrange things so as to wait for them as long as we possibly could. I resolved to divide our efforts, so as not to exhaust them both at once, and here is what we agreed on: while one of us lay stretched on his back, motionless, arms crossed and legs extended, the other would swim and push him forward. This towing job was not to last more than ten minutes, and by relieving each other in this way, we could stay afloat for several hours — perhaps until daybreak.

A slim chance! But hope is so deeply rooted in the human heart! Besides, there were two of us. And finally — I state this plainly, improbable as it may seem — even if I tried to crush every illusion in myself, even if I wanted to "give up hope," I could not do it!

The collision between the frigate and the whale had happened at around eleven o'clock at night. I therefore reckoned on eight hours of swimming until sunrise — an operation strictly feasible, so long as we relieved each other. The sea, fairly calm, tired us little. Now and then I tried to pierce those thick shadows, broken only by the phosphorescence stirred up by our own movements. I watched the luminous ripples that broke across my hand, their shimmering surface flecked with livid patches. It was as if we were plunged in a bath of quicksilver.

Toward one in the morning I was seized with extreme exhaustion. My limbs stiffened under the grip of violent cramps. Conseil had to hold me up, and the whole care of keeping us alive fell on him alone. Before long I heard the poor fellow panting; his breath came short and quick. I understood that he could not hold out much longer.

"Leave me! Leave me!" I told him.

"Abandon Master? Never!" he replied. "I fully intend to drown before he does!"

At that moment the moon broke through the fringes of a great cloud that the wind was driving eastward. The surface of the sea flashed under its light. That kindly gleam revived our strength. My head lifted. My eyes swept every point of the horizon. I spotted the frigate. She was five miles from us, no more than a dark blur, barely to be made out. But of boats — not one!

I wanted to cry out. What good would it do, at such a distance? My swollen lips let no sound pass. Conseil managed to get a few words out, and I heard him call again and again:

"Over here! Over here!"

Suspending our movements for an instant, we listened. And — perhaps it was one of those hummings with which the blood, pressed hard, fills the ear — but it seemed to me that a cry answered Conseil's.

"Did you hear that?" I whispered.

"Yes! Yes!"

And Conseil flung another desperate call into the dark.

This time there was no possible mistake! A human voice was answering ours! Was it the voice of some poor wretch abandoned in the middle of the ocean, some other victim of the blow the ship had taken? Or was one of the frigate's boats hailing us out of the shadows?

Conseil made a supreme effort, and, bracing himself on my shoulder while I resisted in one last convulsion, he raised himself half out of the water and dropped back exhausted.

"What did you see?"

"I saw —" he murmured, "I saw — but let's not talk — let's save all our strength!"

What had he seen? Then, I don't know why, the thought of the monster came into my mind for the first time! But that voice, though? The days are past when a Jonah takes refuge in the belly of a whale!

Still, Conseil went on towing me. Now and then he raised his head, looked ahead, and let out a hail of recognition, answered by a voice coming nearer and nearer. I could barely hear it. My strength was at its end; my fingers were spreading apart; my hand no longer gave me any hold; my mouth, convulsively open, was filling with salt water; the cold was creeping over me. I raised my head one last time, then I went under —

At that instant a hard body struck me. I clung to it. Then I felt myself being pulled up, brought back to the surface, my chest emptying of water — and I fainted.

I certainly came to quickly enough, thanks to the vigorous rubbing that worked up and down my body. I half opened my eyes.

"Conseil!" I murmured.

"Master rang?" answered Conseil.

At that moment, by the last light of the moon as it sank toward the horizon, I made out a face that was not Conseil's, and that I recognized at once.

"Ned!" I cried.

"In person, sir — and running after his bonus!" the Canadian replied.

"You were thrown overboard when the frigate was struck?"

"Yes, Professor, but with better luck than you: I managed to set foot almost at once on a floating island."

"An island?"

"Or, to put it more exactly, on our giant narwhal."

"Explain yourself, Ned."

"Only I soon understood why my harpoon couldn't bite into it and just glanced off, blunted, against its hide."

"Why, Ned? Why?"

"Because that beast, Professor, is made of sheet steel!"

Here I must gather my wits, refresh my memory, check my own assertions.

The Canadian's last words had produced a sudden upheaval in my brain. I hauled myself quickly up to the top of the creature — or object — half awash beneath us, which was serving as our refuge. I tested it with my foot. It was plainly a hard body, impenetrable, and not that soft substance that makes up the bulk of the great sea mammals.

But that hard body might be a bony shell, like those of the antediluvian animals, and I would be entitled to classify the monster among the amphibious reptiles, such as turtles or alligators.

Well — no! The blackish back that held me up was smooth, polished, not overlapping like scales. Struck, it rang with a metallic sound, and — incredible as it was — it seemed, I say, to be made of bolted plates.

There was no room for doubt! The animal, the monster, the natural phenomenon that had puzzled the entire scientific world, that had thrown into confusion and led astray the imaginations of sailors in both hemispheres — I had to admit it — was a still more astonishing phenomenon: a phenomenon made by human hands.

The discovery of the most fabulous, the most mythological creature in existence would not have staggered my reason to the same degree. That something prodigious should come from the Creator is simple enough. But to find, all at once, right before your eyes, the impossible mysteriously and humanly accomplished — that was enough to confound the mind!

Yet there was no hesitating. We were stretched out on the back of a kind of underwater boat, which, as far as I could judge, had the shape of an immense steel fish. Ned Land's opinion on that point was already settled. Conseil and I could only fall in with it.

"But then," I said, "this contraption must hold within it some mechanism of locomotion, and a crew to work it?"

"Obviously," the harpooner replied, "and yet, in the three hours I've lived on this floating island, it hasn't given a single sign of life."

"The boat hasn't moved at all?"

"No, Professor Aronnax. It lets itself be rocked by the swell, but it doesn't budge."

"And yet we know beyond any doubt that it's capable of great speed. Now, since it takes an engine to produce that speed, and an engineer to run the engine, I conclude — that we are saved."

"Hmph," said Ned Land in a guarded tone.

At that very moment, as if to prove my argument, a churning broke out at the stern of the strange machine — whose propeller was plainly a screw — and it began to move. We barely had time to grab hold of its upper part, which rose about eighty centimeters above the water. Very luckily its speed was not excessive.

"As long as it sails on the level," muttered Ned Land, "I have nothing to say. But if it takes a notion to dive, I wouldn't give two dollars for my hide!"

Less than that, the Canadian might have said. It was becoming urgent, then, to make contact with whatever beings were shut up inside this machine. I searched its surface for an opening, a hatch — "a manhole," to use the technical term; but the lines of bolts, firmly hammered down along the seams of the plating, were clean and uniform.

What was more, the moon then disappeared and left us in deep darkness. We had to wait for daylight to work out some way of getting inside this underwater boat.

So our safety depended entirely on the whim of the mysterious helmsmen who were steering this craft — and if they dove, we were lost! Barring that, I had no doubt we could open some communication with them. And indeed, unless they manufactured their own air, they necessarily had to come back to the surface of the ocean from time to time to renew their supply of breathable molecules. Hence there had to be some opening putting the inside of the boat in contact with the atmosphere.

As for any hope of being saved by Commander Farragut, that had to be given up completely. We were being carried westward, and I estimated that our speed, relatively moderate, reached twelve miles an hour. The screw beat the waves with a mathematical regularity, sometimes breaking the surface and sending the phosphorescent water spurting up to a great height.

Toward four in the morning the craft picked up speed. We could barely hold on against this dizzying rush whenever the waves struck us full force. Fortunately Ned's hand fell on a large mooring ring fixed to the top of the steel back, and we managed to hang on to it firmly.

At last the long night wore away. My incomplete recollection won't let me set down all its impressions. Only one detail comes back to me. During certain lulls in the sea and the wind, I thought I heard, several times, vague sounds — a kind of fleeting harmony produced by distant chords. What, then, was the mystery of this underwater navigation that the whole world was vainly trying to explain? What beings lived aboard this strange boat? What mechanical force let it travel at such a prodigious speed?

Day broke. The morning mists wrapped us round, but they were not slow to tear apart. I was about to begin a careful examination of the hull, which formed at its upper part a kind of horizontal platform, when I felt it start to sink, little by little.

"Hey! A thousand devils!" cried Ned Land, stamping his foot on the ringing steel. "Open up, you inhospitable sailors!"

But it was hard to make yourself heard over the deafening beat of the propeller. Fortunately, the sinking motion stopped.

Suddenly a noise of ironwork violently shoved came from inside the boat. A plate lifted, a man appeared, let out a strange cry, and instantly vanished.

A few moments later, eight burly fellows, their faces veiled, appeared in silence and dragged us down into their formidable machine.

Mobilis in Mobili

The abduction had been so brutally carried out, and with such lightning speed, that neither my companions nor I had had time to collect ourselves. I don't know what they felt as they were shoved into this floating prison, but for my own part a quick shudder ran cold across my skin. Who were we dealing with? Some new breed of pirate, no doubt, plundering the sea in their own fashion.

The narrow hatch had scarcely closed above me when a deep darkness swallowed me whole. My eyes, still soaked with the light outside, could make out nothing. I felt my bare feet grip the rungs of an iron ladder. Ned Land and Conseil, seized just as roughly, came down after me. At the foot of the ladder a door swung open, then shut again immediately behind us with a resounding clang.

We were alone. Where? I couldn't have said, could scarcely even imagine. Everything was black — black so utter that, even after a few minutes, my eyes had not yet caught one of those vague glimmers that drift through the deepest of nights.

Meanwhile Ned Land, furious at being handled this way, gave free rein to his indignation.

"A thousand devils!" he burst out. "These people could give the Caledonians lessons in hospitality! All that's left is for them to turn out to be cannibals. I wouldn't be surprised if they did — but I'm telling you now, no one eats me without hearing me object!"

"Easy, Ned, easy," Conseil answered calmly. "Don't work yourself up before the hour. We're not in the roasting pan just yet."

"In the roasting pan, no," the Canadian shot back, "but in the oven for certain. It's dark enough. Lucky for me my bowie knife hasn't left my side, and I can still see well enough to use it. The first of these bandits who lays a hand on me—"

"Don't get riled, Ned," I said to the harpooner, "and don't compromise us with pointless violence. For all we know, they're listening. Let's try instead to find out where we are."

I felt my way forward. After five steps I came up against an iron wall of bolted plates. Turning back, I bumped into a wooden table, and set around it were several stools. The floor of this prison was hidden beneath a thick mat of phormium that muffled the sound of footsteps. The bare walls betrayed no trace of a door or a window. Conseil, making the same tour in the opposite direction, met up with me, and we came back to the middle of this cabin, which must have measured twenty feet long by ten feet wide. As for its height, Ned Land, tall as he was, couldn't reach it.

A full half hour had already crept by with no change in our situation when our eyes passed abruptly from utter darkness to the most violent light. Our prison lit up all at once — that is, it filled with a luminous substance so intense that at first I couldn't bear its brilliance. By its whiteness, by its intensity, I recognized that electric glow which produced around the submarine boat a kind of magnificent phosphorescence. After shutting my eyes involuntarily, I opened them again and saw that the light streamed from a frosted half-globe curving out of the ceiling of the cabin.

"At last! Now we can see!" cried Ned Land, who stood on the defensive, knife in hand.

"Yes," I answered, risking the play on words, "but our situation is as dark as ever."

"Let Master be patient," said the imperturbable Conseil.

The cabin's sudden lighting had let me examine it down to the smallest detail. It held nothing but the table and the five stools. The invisible door must have been hermetically sealed. Not a sound reached our ears. Everything seemed dead inside this boat. Was it moving? Holding at the surface of the ocean? Sinking into the depths? I couldn't guess.

Still, the luminous globe hadn't been lit for nothing, so I hoped the crew would not be long in showing themselves. When you mean to forget people, you don't light up their dungeon.

I was not mistaken. The rattle of a bolt was heard, the door opened, and two men appeared.

One was short, powerfully muscled, broad-shouldered, sturdy of limb, with a strong head, a thick mane of black hair, a heavy mustache, and a quick, penetrating gaze — his whole person stamped with that southern liveliness that in France marks the people of Provence. Diderot claimed, and rightly, that a man's gestures are metaphorical, and this little man was surely living proof of it. You sensed that in his ordinary speech he must lavish personifications, metonymies, and hypallages. Not that I was ever in a position to confirm it, for in my presence he always used a peculiar and utterly incomprehensible tongue.

The second stranger deserves a more detailed description. A disciple of Gratiolet or Engel could have read his face like an open book. I recognized his dominant qualities without hesitation. Self-confidence — for his head rose nobly on the arch formed by the line of his shoulders, and his black eyes looked out with cold assurance. Composure — for his skin, pale rather than ruddy, betokened calmness of blood. Energy — shown by the swift contraction of his brow muscles. And courage, in the end — for his deep breathing told of a great vital force.

I will add that this man was proud, that his firm, calm gaze seemed to reflect lofty thoughts, and that from the whole of him — from the harmony of expression in the movements of his body and face — there resulted, by the physiognomists' reckoning, an unquestionable frankness.

I felt myself "involuntarily" reassured in his presence, and I took it as a good omen for our interview.

Whether this man was thirty-five or fifty, I couldn't have said. He was tall, with a broad forehead, a straight nose, a clearly drawn mouth, magnificent teeth, and fine, elongated hands — eminently "psychic," to borrow a term from chirognomy, that is, worthy of serving a high and passionate soul. This man was certainly the most admirable specimen I had ever met. One particular detail: his eyes, set a little wide apart, could take in nearly a quarter of the horizon at once. This faculty — I verified it later — was matched by a power of vision even keener than Ned Land's. When this stranger fixed his gaze on an object, the line of his brows knit together, his broad eyelids drew close so as to hem in the pupils and narrow the field of sight, and he looked! What a look! How it magnified objects shrunk by distance! How it pierced you to the soul! How it saw through those sheets of water so opaque to our eyes, and read into the deepest reaches of the sea!

The two strangers wore berets made of sea-otter fur and sealskin sea boots, and their clothes, of a peculiar fabric, hung loose at the waist and left great freedom of movement.

The taller of the two — clearly the master of the ship — examined us with the closest attention, without saying a word. Then, turning to his companion, he conversed with him in a language I couldn't place. It was a sonorous, harmonious, supple tongue, whose vowels seemed to carry a widely varied accent.

The other replied with a nod of the head and added two or three perfectly incomprehensible words. Then his eyes seemed to question me directly.

I answered, in good French, that I did not understand his language. But he seemed not to understand mine either, and the situation grew rather awkward.

"Let Master tell our story anyway," said Conseil. "Perhaps these gentlemen will catch a word or two."

I began again the tale of our adventures, pronouncing each syllable clearly and leaving out no detail. I gave our names and stations; then I formally introduced Professor Aronnax, his servant Conseil, and Ned Land, master harpooner.

The man with the gentle, calm eyes listened to me quietly, politely even, with remarkable attention. But nothing in his face showed that he had understood my story. When I had finished, he did not speak a single word.

There remained the resource of speaking English. Perhaps we could make ourselves understood in that nearly universal tongue. I knew it, along with German, well enough to read fluently, though not to speak correctly. And here, above all, we needed to make ourselves understood.

"Come, your turn," I said to the harpooner. "It's up to you, Master Land — pull out of your bag the finest English an Anglo-Saxon ever spoke, and try for better luck than I had."

Ned did not need to be asked twice, and he began my story over in a version I could follow well enough. The substance was the same, but the form differed. Carried away by his temper, the Canadian threw a good deal of heat into it. He complained violently of being imprisoned in defiance of all human rights, demanded to know by what law they held him this way, invoked habeas corpus, threatened to prosecute those who were confining him unlawfully, thrashed about, gesticulated, shouted, and finally made clear by an expressive gesture that we were dying of hunger.

Which was perfectly true, though we had all but forgotten it.

To his great astonishment, the harpooner seemed to have been no more intelligible than I. Our visitors didn't so much as blink. It was plain they understood neither the language of Arago nor that of Faraday.

Thoroughly at a loss, having vainly exhausted our philological resources, I no longer knew what course to take, when Conseil said to me:

"If Master will permit, I'll tell the whole thing in German."

"What! You know German?" I exclaimed.

"Like a Fleming, if it won't displease Master."

"On the contrary, it pleases me very much. Go ahead, my boy."

And Conseil, in his tranquil voice, recounted for the third time the various turns of our story. But despite the narrator's elegant phrasing and fine accent, German met with no more success.

At last, driven to the wall, I gathered up everything left to me from my early studies and undertook to tell our adventures in Latin. Cicero would have stopped his ears and packed me off to the kitchen, but I managed to get through it all the same. Same negative result.

This last attempt having definitively failed, the two strangers exchanged a few words in their incomprehensible language and withdrew, without so much as offering us one of those reassuring gestures that pass current in every country on earth. The door closed again.

"It's an outrage!" cried Ned Land, exploding for the twentieth time. "What! We speak to these rascals in French, English, German, Latin, and not one of them has the courtesy to answer!"

"Easy, Ned," I said to the seething harpooner. "Anger won't get us anywhere."

"But do you realize, Professor," our irascible companion went on, "that a man could starve to death outright in this iron cage?"

"Bah," said Conseil philosophically, "we can hold out a good while yet."

"My friends," I said, "we mustn't despair. We've been in worse spots than this. Do me the favor of waiting before you form an opinion of this boat's commander and crew."

"My opinion's already made," Ned Land retorted. "They're rascals—"

"Fine. And from what country?"

"The country of rascals!"

"My good Ned, that country isn't yet marked clearly enough on the map, and I admit the nationality of these two strangers is hard to pin down. Not English, not French, not German — that's all one can say for certain. Still, I'd be tempted to grant that this commander and his second officer were born in low latitudes. There's something southern about them. But whether they're Spanish, Turkish, Arab, or Indian, their physical type won't let me decide. As for their language, it's absolutely incomprehensible."

"That's the trouble with not knowing every language," Conseil answered, "or the drawback of not having a single universal one!"

"Which would do no good at all!" Ned Land replied. "Don't you see these people have a language of their own, a language invented to drive to despair honest men who are asking for their dinner? But in every country on earth, opening your mouth, working your jaws, snapping your teeth and lips — isn't that plain enough on its own? Doesn't it mean the same in Quebec as in the Pomotous, in Paris as at the antipodes: I'm hungry! Give me something to eat!"

"Oh," said Conseil, "there are some natures so unintelligent!"

As he spoke those words, the door opened. A steward came in. He was bringing us clothes — jackets and sea trousers of a fabric whose nature I couldn't identify. I hurried to put them on, and my companions did the same.

Meanwhile the steward — mute, perhaps deaf — had set the table and laid three places.

"Now that's something serious," said Conseil, "and it looks promising."

"Bah," answered the resentful harpooner, "what the devil do you expect to eat here? Turtle liver, shark fillet, dogfish steak!"

"We'll soon see!" said Conseil.

The dishes, covered with their silver domes, were set out symmetrically on the cloth, and we took our places at the table. Decidedly, we were dealing with civilized people, and but for the electric light flooding over us, I could have believed myself in the dining room of the Adelphi Hotel in Liverpool, or the Grand Hotel in Paris. I must say, though, that bread and wine were entirely absent. The water was fresh and clear, but it was water — which was not to Ned Land's taste. Among the dishes served to us I recognized various fish delicately prepared; but of certain others, excellent though they were, I couldn't venture an opinion, and I couldn't even have said to which kingdom, vegetable or animal, their contents belonged. As for the table service, it was elegant and in perfect taste. Each utensil — spoon, fork, knife, plate — bore a letter encircled by a motto in exergue, and here is the exact facsimile:

Mobilis in Mobili — moving within the moving element! The motto applied precisely to this underwater vessel, provided you translated the preposition in as "within" and not "upon." The letter N was doubtless the initial of the name of that enigmatic figure who reigned in command at the bottom of the seas!

Ned and Conseil weren't given to so much reflection. They were devouring their meal, and I was not slow to follow their example. I was reassured, moreover, about our fate, and it seemed clear to me that our hosts did not mean to let us die of starvation.

But everything comes to an end here below, everything passes — even the hunger of men who haven't eaten in fifteen hours. Our appetites satisfied, the need for sleep made itself imperiously felt. A natural enough reaction after the endless night through which we had struggled against death.

"My word, I could sleep well," said Conseil.

"And I'm asleep already!" answered Ned Land.

My two companions stretched out on the cabin's mat and were soon plunged into a deep sleep.

For my part, I gave in less easily to that violent need for sleep. Too many thoughts crowded my mind, too many unanswerable questions pressed upon me, too many images held my eyelids half open. Where were we? What strange power was carrying us off? I felt — or rather thought I felt — the vessel sinking toward the sea's most remote layers. Violent nightmares beset me. In those mysterious refuges I glimpsed a whole world of unknown animals, and this submarine boat seemed one of their own kind — alive, moving, formidable like them! Then my brain quieted, my imagination melted into a vague drowsiness, and I soon fell into a heavy, dismal sleep.

Ned Land's Tempers

How long that sleep lasted, I have no idea; but it must have been a long one, for it left us fully rested from our exhaustion. I was the first to wake. My companions hadn't stirred yet, and lay stretched out in their corner like two inert masses.

I had barely risen from that passably hard bunk when I felt my head clear and my mind sharpen. I set about examining our cell all over again.

Nothing about its interior arrangements had changed. The prison was still a prison, and the prisoners were still prisoners. Only, the steward had taken advantage of our sleep to clear the table. So nothing pointed to any imminent change in our situation, and I began to wonder in all seriousness whether we were fated to spend the rest of our days in this cage.

That prospect struck me as all the more unpleasant because, although my brain was free of the previous night's obsessions, I now felt a curious tightness in my chest. My breathing was coming with difficulty. The heavy air was no longer enough for my lungs to work with. Roomy as the cell was, it was plain that we had used up most of the oxygen it held. In fact, each man consumes in one hour the oxygen contained in a hundred liters of air, and that air, by then loaded with a nearly equal quantity of carbon dioxide, becomes unbreathable.

It was therefore urgent to renew the atmosphere of our prison — and no doubt the atmosphere of the underwater boat as well.

That raised a question in my mind. How did the commander of this floating dwelling manage it? Did he obtain air by chemical means, driving off with heat the oxygen locked in potassium chlorate and absorbing the carbon dioxide with caustic potash? In that case he would have had to keep up some contact with the continents in order to procure the materials for the process. Did he simply store air under high pressure in tanks and then release it as his crew required? Perhaps. Or — a more convenient method, more economical, and therefore more likely — did he content himself with rising to the surface of the sea to breathe, like a cetacean, renewing his supply of atmosphere every twenty-four hours? However he did it, whatever the method, it seemed to me only prudent that he employ it without delay.

Indeed, I was already reduced to quickening my breaths to draw out what little oxygen the cell still held when, all at once, I was refreshed by a current of pure air, fragrant with a salty tang. It was the sea breeze itself, bracing and heavy with iodine! I opened my mouth wide, and my lungs drank in the fresh molecules. At the same moment I felt a swaying, a gentle roll of no great amplitude but perfectly unmistakable. The boat — the monster of sheet iron — had evidently just come up to the surface of the ocean to breathe, whale-fashion. So the ship's ventilation method was now perfectly understood.

Once I had filled my chest with that pure air, I looked for the duct — the "airway," if you like — that let this reviving flow reach us, and I soon found it. Above the door opened a ventilation hole that let through a cool column of air, renewing the cell's depleted atmosphere.

I had gotten that far in my observations when Ned and Conseil woke almost at the same instant, roused by that revitalizing draft. They rubbed their eyes, stretched their arms, and were on their feet in a moment.

"Did Master sleep well?" Conseil asked me, with his everyday courtesy.

"Very well, my good fellow," I answered. "And you, Ned Land?"

"Like a log, Professor. But unless I'm mistaken, I could swear I'm breathing a sea breeze?"

A sailor couldn't be wrong about that, and I told the Canadian what had happened while he slept.

"Well now!" he said. "That explains those bellowing noises we heard whenever the so-called narwhal came in sight of the Abraham Lincoln."

"Exactly, Ned. It was the thing breathing!"

"Only, Professor Aronnax, I've no idea what time it is — unless it happens to be dinnertime?"

"Dinnertime, my worthy harpooner? Say breakfast time, at the very least, for we've certainly reached the day after yesterday."

"Which proves," Conseil put in, "that we've had twenty-four hours' sleep."

"That's my reckoning," I answered.

"I won't argue the point," Ned Land replied. "But dinner or breakfast, the steward will be welcome whichever one he brings."

"Both," said Conseil.

"Just so," said the Canadian. "We've a right to two meals, and for my part I'll do justice to the pair of them."

"All right, Ned, let's wait," I answered. "It's clear these strangers don't mean to let us starve — otherwise last night's supper would make no sense."

"Unless they're fattening us up!" Ned shot back.

"I object," I answered. "We haven't fallen into the hands of cannibals!"

"Once is no habit," the Canadian replied gravely. "Who's to say these people haven't gone a long time without fresh meat? And in that case, three sound, well-built specimens like the Professor here, his servant, and me—"

"Get those ideas out of your head, Ned," I told the harpooner, "and above all, don't let them set you off against our hosts. That would only make matters worse."

"In any case," said the harpooner, "I've got the devil's own hunger, and dinner or breakfast, the meal's slow in coming!"

"Ned," I replied, "we have to conform to the ship's schedule, and I suspect our stomachs are running fast against the cook's dinner bell."

"Well then, we'll set them right," Conseil said calmly.

"There you are, friend Conseil, that's you all over," the impatient Canadian retorted. "You never waste your temper or your nerves! Always calm! You'd be capable of saying your thanks before you'd said grace, and starving to death rather than complaining!"

"What good would it do?" asked Conseil.

"It would do the good of complaining! That's already something. And if these pirates — I say pirates out of respect, and so as not to cross the Professor, who forbids me to call them cannibals — if these pirates imagine they're going to keep me shut up in this cage where I'm suffocating, without hearing the kind of oaths I season my rages with, they're mistaken! Come now, Professor Aronnax, speak frankly. Do you really think they'll hold us long in this iron box?"

"To tell the truth, I know no more about it than you do, Ned."

"But come, what's your guess?"

"My guess is that chance has made us masters of an important secret. Now, the crew of this underwater boat has an interest in keeping that secret, and if that interest outweighs the lives of three men, then I believe our existence is very much in danger. If not, then at the first opportunity the monster that swallowed us will hand us back to the world our own kind lives in."

"Unless it enrolls us among its crew," said Conseil, "and keeps us that way—"

"Right up until," Ned Land replied, "some frigate faster or cleverer than the Abraham Lincoln captures this nest of cutthroats and sends its crew — and us along with it — to breathe our last from the end of its mainyard."

"Well reasoned, Ned," I replied. "But so far, as far as I know, no one has made us any such offer. No use, then, debating what course to take should the occasion arise. I say again: let's wait, let's take our cue from circumstances, and let's do nothing, since there's nothing to be done."

"On the contrary, Professor!" answered the harpooner, who wouldn't let it go. "We've got to do something."

"Do what, Ned?"

"Escape."

"Escaping from a prison on land is often difficult enough, but from an underwater prison strikes me as flat-out impossible."

"Come on, friend Ned," Conseil asked, "what's your answer to Master's objection? I can't believe an American is ever at the end of his resources!"

The harpooner, plainly embarrassed, said nothing. Flight, given the circumstances chance had thrown us into, was absolutely impossible. But a Canadian is half a Frenchman, and Ned Land made that plain enough by his reply.

"So, Professor Aronnax," he went on after a few moments' thought, "you can't guess what people ought to do when they can't break out of their prison?"

"No, my friend."

"Simple enough. They fix things so as to stay in it."

"Good grief!" said Conseil. "Better to be inside than above or below!"

"But only after throwing out the jailers, the turnkeys, and the guards," Ned Land added.

"What, Ned? You'd seriously think of seizing this vessel?"

"Very seriously," answered the Canadian.

"It's impossible."

"Why so, Professor? Some lucky chance might turn up, and I don't see what could stop us from taking advantage of it. If there aren't more than a score of men aboard this machine, they won't make two Frenchmen and a Canadian back down, I should think!"

Better to accept the harpooner's proposition than to argue it. So I contented myself with answering:

"Let's wait and see how things develop, Ned, and we'll see. But until then, I beg you, hold your impatience in check. We can only act by cunning, and you won't bring lucky chances into being by flying off the handle. Promise me, then, that you'll accept the situation without too much anger."

"I promise you, Professor," Ned Land answered, in a tone that was anything but reassuring. "Not a violent word will pass my lips, not a brutal gesture will give me away — not even if the meals aren't served with all the regularity a man could wish."

"I have your word, Ned," I said to the Canadian.

Then the conversation lapsed, and each of us fell to thinking on his own. For my part, I'll admit that, despite the harpooner's assurances, I kept no illusions. I put no stock in those lucky chances Ned Land had spoken of. To be handled so surely, the underwater boat required a large crew, and consequently, in the event of a fight, we would be up against too strong a force. Besides, before anything else we would have to be free, and we weren't. I couldn't see any way at all of escaping this cell of sheet iron, sealed so tightly shut. And if this strange commander had a secret to keep — which seemed at the very least probable — he wouldn't let us move about freely aboard his ship. Now, would he rid himself of us by violence, or would he set us down some day on some corner of dry land? That was the unknown. All these hypotheses struck me as extremely plausible, and one had to be a harpooner to hope for the return of one's freedom.

I understood, moreover, that Ned Land's mood was souring as the thoughts crowded in on his brain. Little by little I could hear the curses rumbling in the back of his throat, and I saw his gestures turn menacing again. He would get up, pace like a wild beast in a cage, kick and punch at the walls. Meanwhile time was passing, hunger was making itself cruelly felt, and this time the steward did not appear. And it was leaving us castaways forgotten too long, if anyone really did have good intentions toward us.

Ned Land, tormented by the pangs of his sturdy stomach, was working himself up more and more, and, for all his promise, I genuinely feared an explosion the moment he came face to face with one of the men aboard.

For two more hours, Ned Land's fury mounted. The Canadian called out, he shouted, but in vain. The iron walls were deaf. I couldn't hear so much as a sound inside this boat, which seemed to lie dead. It wasn't moving, for I would surely have felt the hull shudder under the drive of the propeller. Plunged, no doubt, into the depths of the waters, it no longer belonged to the earth. That whole gloomy silence was terrifying.

As for how long we might be left abandoned, isolated at the bottom of this cell, I didn't dare guess. The hopes I had conceived after our meeting with the ship's commander were fading little by little. The gentleness of that man's gaze, the generous cast of his features, the nobility of his bearing — all of it was vanishing from my memory. I saw this enigmatic figure again as he must necessarily be: pitiless, cruel. I felt him to be outside humanity, closed to any feeling of mercy, an implacable enemy of his own kind, toward whom he must have sworn an undying hatred.

But this man — was he really going to let us die of starvation, shut up in this narrow prison, given over to those horrible temptations that savage hunger drives one to? That frightful thought took on a terrible intensity in my mind, and with my imagination feeding it, I felt myself seized by a senseless dread. Conseil stayed calm. Ned Land roared.

At that moment, a sound was heard outside.

Footsteps rang on the metal floor. The locks were worked, the door swung open, and the steward appeared.

Before I could make a move to stop him, the Canadian had hurled himself on the poor man; he had knocked him down; he had him by the throat. The steward was choking under that powerful grip.

Conseil was already trying to pry the harpooner's half-suffocated victim out of his hands, and I was about to join my efforts to his, when I was suddenly nailed to the spot by these words, spoken in French:

"Calm yourself, Ned Land, and you, Professor, be so good as to hear me out!"

The Man of the Seas

It was the commander of the vessel who had spoken.

At those words, Ned Land sprang to his feet. The steward, nearly throttled, staggered out at a sign from his master; and such was the commander's authority aboard his own ship that not a single gesture betrayed the resentment this man must surely have felt toward the Canadian. Conseil, curious in spite of himself, and I, dumbfounded, waited in silence for the scene to reach its end.

The commander, leaning against the corner of the table, arms folded, studied us with deep attention. Was he hesitating to speak? Did he regret those words he had just let slip in French? One might well have thought so.

After a few moments of a silence none of us dreamed of breaking, he said, in a calm and penetrating voice:

"Gentlemen, I speak French, English, German, and Latin equally well. I could therefore have answered you at our very first meeting; but I wanted to know you first, and to reflect afterward. Your four accounts, absolutely alike at bottom, confirmed for me the identity of your persons. I now know that chance has brought before me Monsieur Pierre Aronnax, professor of natural history at the Museum of Paris, charged with a scientific mission abroad; Conseil, his servant; and Ned Land, a Canadian by birth, harpooner aboard the frigate Abraham Lincoln of the United States Navy."

I bowed in a gesture of assent. The commander had not put a question to me. So there was no reply to make. The man expressed himself with perfect ease, without the slightest accent. His phrasing was clear, his words exact, his fluency remarkable. And yet I did not "feel" in him a fellow countryman.

He went on in these terms:

"You have doubtless found, sir, that I was slow in paying you this second visit. It is because, once your identity was established, I wanted to weigh carefully what course to take with you. I hesitated a great deal. The most unfortunate of circumstances has brought you into the presence of a man who has broken with humankind. You came to trouble my existence—"

"Not by intention," I said.

"Not by intention?" the stranger answered, raising his voice a little. "Is it by no intention that the Abraham Lincoln hunts me across every sea? Is it by no intention that you took passage aboard that frigate? Is it by no intention that your cannonballs bounced off the hull of my ship? Is it by no intention that Master Ned Land struck me with his harpoon?"

I caught a contained irritation in these words. But to these recriminations I had a perfectly natural answer to make, and I made it.

"Sir," I said, "you are surely unaware of the debates that have taken place about you in America and in Europe. You do not know that various accidents, caused by collisions with your underwater craft, have stirred public opinion on both continents. I will spare you the countless hypotheses by which people tried to explain the inexplicable phenomenon whose secret you alone possessed. But you must understand that, in pursuing you across the high seas of the Pacific, the Abraham Lincoln believed it was chasing some powerful sea monster, one the ocean had to be rid of at any cost."

A half smile relaxed the commander's lips; then, in a calmer tone:

"Professor Aronnax," he replied, "would you dare to claim that your frigate would not have pursued and cannonaded an underwater boat just as readily as a monster?"

The question left me at a loss, for Commander Farragut would certainly not have hesitated. He would have believed it his duty to destroy a craft of this kind exactly as he would a gigantic narwhal.

"You understand, then, sir," the stranger went on, "that I have the right to treat you as enemies."

I made no answer, and with good reason. What was the use of arguing a proposition like that, when force can shatter the best of arguments?

"I hesitated for a long time," the commander resumed. "Nothing obliged me to offer you my hospitality. If I were to part with you, I had no interest in ever seeing you again. I would set you back down on the platform of this ship that had served you as a refuge. I would sink beneath the seas, and forget that you had ever existed. Was that not my right?"

"It might have been the right of a savage," I answered, "but not that of a civilized man."

"Professor," the commander shot back sharply, "I am not what you call a civilized man! I have broken with the whole of society for reasons that I alone have the right to judge. I therefore obey none of its rules, and I urge you never to invoke them in my presence again!"

This was said flatly. A flash of anger and scorn had lit the stranger's eyes, and in this man's life I glimpsed a formidable past. Not only had he placed himself outside human laws, he had made himself independent, free in the strictest sense of the word, beyond all reach! For who would dare pursue him to the bottom of the seas, when at their surface he foiled every effort mounted against him? What ship could withstand a collision with his underwater ram? What armor plating, however thick, could bear the blows of his spur? No man on earth could call him to account for his deeds. God—if he believed in one; his conscience—if he had one; these were the only judges he could depend on.

These reflections flashed swiftly through my mind while the strange personage fell silent, absorbed and as though withdrawn into himself. I regarded him with a dread mingled with fascination, much, no doubt, as Oedipus regarded the Sphinx.

After a rather long silence, the commander spoke again.

"I hesitated, then," he said, "but I concluded that my own interest could be reconciled with that natural pity to which every human being has a claim. You will remain aboard my ship, since fate has cast you here. You will be free aboard her, and in exchange for that freedom—a wholly relative freedom, I admit—I will impose only a single condition on you. Your word that you will submit to it will be enough for me."

"Speak, sir," I answered. "I take it this condition is one an honest man can accept?"

"Yes, sir, and here it is. It is possible that certain unforeseen events may oblige me to confine you to your cabins for some hours or some days, as the case may be. Wishing never to resort to violence, I expect from you in that case—more even than in any other—a passive obedience. In acting so, I shield you from all responsibility, I clear you entirely, for it falls to me to make it impossible for you to see what must not be seen. Do you accept this condition?"

So things went on aboard that were at the very least peculiar—things not to be seen by people who had not placed themselves outside the laws of society! Among the surprises the future held in store for me, this would not prove the least.

"We accept," I answered. "Only, I will ask your permission, sir, to put one question to you—just one."

"Speak, sir."

"You said that we would be free aboard your ship?"

"Entirely."

"Then I will ask what you understand by that freedom."

"Why, the freedom to come and go, to see, even to observe everything that happens here—save in a few grave circumstances—the freedom, in short, that my companions and I enjoy ourselves."

It was clear that we did not understand each other.

"Forgive me, sir," I resumed, "but such freedom is only the freedom every prisoner has to pace his cell! It cannot be enough for us."

"And yet it will have to be enough!"

"What! We must give up ever again seeing our homeland, our friends, our families?"

"Yes, sir. But giving up that unbearable yoke of the land, which men take to be freedom—perhaps that is not so painful as you suppose!"

"For one thing," cried Ned Land, "I will never give my word not to try to escape!"

"I am not asking you for your word, Master Land," the commander answered coldly.

"Sir," I said, carried away in spite of myself, "you are abusing your position over us! This is cruelty!"

"No, sir, it is mercy! You are my prisoners of war! I keep you, when with a single word I could plunge you back into the depths of the ocean! You attacked me! You came to surprise a secret that no man in the world must uncover—the secret of my whole existence! And you think I am going to send you back to that dry land that must know me no longer? Never! In holding you, it is not you I am keeping—it is myself!"

These words showed a resolve on the commander's part against which no argument would prevail.

"So, sir," I resumed, "you are simply giving us the choice between life and death?"

"Simply that."

"My friends," I said, "to a question put in such terms there is nothing to answer. But no word binds us to the master of this ship."

"No word, sir," the stranger replied.

Then, in a gentler voice, he went on:

"Now, allow me to finish what I have to say to you. I know you, Professor Aronnax. You, if not your companions, will perhaps have less cause to complain of the chance that has bound you to my fate. Among the books that serve my favorite studies you will find that work you published on the great ocean depths. I have often read it. You have carried your work as far as land-bound science allowed. But you do not know everything, you have not seen everything. Let me tell you, then, Professor, that you will not regret the time spent aboard my ship. You are about to travel through the land of wonders. Astonishment, amazement, will very likely be the habitual condition of your mind. You will not easily grow jaded with the spectacle offered ceaselessly to your eyes. In a new tour of the underwater world—the last, perhaps, who knows?—I mean to see again all that I have been able to study at the bottom of these seas I have crossed so many times, and you shall be my companion in study. From this day forward, you enter a new element; you will see what no man has yet seen—for my crew and I no longer count ourselves among the living—and our planet, thanks to me, is going to yield up to you its last secrets."

I cannot deny it: these words of the commander made a great impression on me. He had caught me by my weakness, and I forgot, for a moment, that the contemplation of these sublime things could not be worth the freedom I had lost. Besides, I was counting on the future to settle that grave question. So I contented myself with answering:

"Sir, though you have broken with humanity, I want to believe that you have not renounced all human feeling. We are castaways charitably taken in aboard your ship; we will not forget it. As for me, I do not deny that if the interest of science could absorb even the need for freedom, what our meeting promises would offer me great compensations."

I thought the commander was going to hold out his hand to seal our pact. He did nothing of the sort. I regretted it for his sake.

"One last question," I said, just as this inexplicable being seemed about to withdraw.

"Speak, Professor."

"By what name am I to call you?"

"Sir," the commander replied, "to you I am nothing but Captain Nemo, and you and your companions are nothing to me but the passengers of the Nautilus."

Captain Nemo called out. A steward appeared. The captain gave him his orders in that strange language I could not identify. Then, turning to the Canadian and Conseil:

"A meal is waiting for you in your cabin," he told them. "Kindly follow this man."

"I won't say no to that!" replied the harpooner.

At last he and Conseil left the cell where they had been shut up for more than thirty hours.

"And now, Professor Aronnax, our lunch is ready. Allow me to lead the way."

"At your service, Captain."

I followed Captain Nemo, and the moment I stepped through the door I entered a sort of corridor lit by electricity, like the gangways of a ship. After a walk of some ten meters, a second door opened before me.

I then entered a dining room, decorated and furnished in severe good taste. Tall oak sideboards, inlaid with ebony ornaments, rose at either end of the room, and on their shelves, cut in undulating lines, gleamed earthenware, porcelain, and glassware of inestimable value. The flat silver plate shone under rays poured down from a luminous ceiling, whose fine paintings softened and filtered the glare.

At the center of the room stood a table richly laid. Captain Nemo pointed out the place I was to occupy.

"Sit down," he said, "and eat like a man who must be starving."

The lunch consisted of a number of dishes whose contents the sea alone had supplied, and of a few whose nature and origin I could not guess. I will admit that it was good, though with a peculiar flavor that I grew used to easily. These various foods struck me as rich in phosphorus, and I concluded they must be of marine origin.

Captain Nemo watched me. I asked him nothing, but he read my thoughts and answered, of his own accord, the questions I was burning to put to him.

"Most of these dishes are unknown to you," he said. "Yet you may partake of them without fear. They are wholesome and nourishing. Long ago I gave up the foods of the land, and I am none the worse for it. My crew, who are a hardy lot, eat no differently than I do."

"So," I said, "all these foods are products of the sea?"

"Yes, Professor, the sea provides for all my needs. Sometimes I set my nets adrift and haul them back in, ready to burst. Sometimes I go hunting in the very midst of this element that seems beyond man's reach, and run down the game that lodges in my underwater forests. My flocks, like those of old Neptune's shepherd, graze the vast prairies of the ocean without fear. I have there a great estate that I work myself, and that is forever sown anew by the hand of the Creator of all things."

I looked at Captain Nemo with a certain astonishment, and answered him:

"I understand perfectly, sir, that your nets furnish excellent fish for your table; I understand less well that you should hunt aquatic game in your underwater forests; but I no longer understand at all how a scrap of meat, however small, could figure in your menu."

"And indeed, sir," Captain Nemo answered me, "I never make use of the flesh of land animals."

"This, however," I said, pointing to a dish where a few slices of fillet still remained.

"What you take to be meat, Professor, is nothing but fillet of sea turtle. Here, too, are some dolphin livers you would mistake for a pork stew. My cook is a skillful hand, who excels at preserving these varied products of the ocean. Taste all these dishes. Here is a preserve of sea cucumbers that a Malay would declare without rival in the world; here is a cream whose milk was furnished by the udders of whales, and its sugar by the great kelp of the North Sea; and finally, allow me to offer you some preserves of sea anemones, as good as those made from the most delicious fruits."

And I tasted, more out of curiosity than appetite, while Captain Nemo enchanted me with his improbable tales.

"But this sea, Professor Aronnax," he said to me, "this prodigious, inexhaustible nurse, does not merely feed me; it clothes me as well. The fabrics that cover you are woven from the byssus of certain shellfish; they are dyed with the purple of the ancients, shaded with violet tints that I extract from the sea hares of the Mediterranean. The perfumes you will find on the washstand of your cabin are the product of distilling marine plants. Your bed is made of the softest eelgrass of the ocean. Your pen will be a whale's baleen, your ink the liquor secreted by the cuttlefish or the squid. Everything now comes to me from the sea, as everything will one day return to it!"

"You love the sea, Captain."

"Yes! I love it! The sea is everything! It covers seven tenths of the globe. Its breath is pure and healthful. It is the vast desert where man is never alone, for he feels life quivering at his side. The sea is only the vehicle of a supernatural and prodigious existence; it is nothing but movement and love; it is the Living Infinite, as one of your poets put it. And indeed, Professor, nature reveals herself there through her three kingdoms—mineral, vegetable, animal. The last of these is broadly represented in it by the four groups of zoophytes, by three classes of articulates, by five classes of mollusks, by three classes of vertebrates—the mammals, the reptiles, and those countless legions of fish, an endless order of animals numbering more than thirteen thousand species, of which only a tenth belong to fresh water. The sea is the vast reservoir of nature. It was by the sea that the globe began, so to speak, and who knows but that it will end by it too! There lies supreme tranquility. The sea does not belong to despots. On its surface they may still exercise their iniquitous rights, do battle, devour one another, carry across it every terrestrial horror. But thirty feet below its surface, their power ceases, their influence dies out, their might disappears! Ah, sir—live, live in the bosom of the seas! There alone is independence! There I acknowledge no masters! There I am free!"

Captain Nemo fell silent all at once, in the midst of this enthusiasm that had come pouring out of him. Had he let himself be carried beyond his usual reserve? Had he said too much? For a few moments he paced back and forth, greatly agitated. Then his nerves grew calm, his face resumed its accustomed coldness, and, turning toward me:

"Now, Professor," he said, "if you wish to visit the Nautilus, I am at your service."

The Nautilus

Captain Nemo rose. I followed him. A double door at the far end of the room swung open, and I stepped into a chamber the same size as the one I had just left.

It was a library. Tall bookcases of black rosewood, inlaid with copper, held on their broad shelves a great many volumes bound alike. They ran the whole curve of the room and ended along the bottom in wide divans, upholstered in brown leather, that offered the most comfortable of curves. Light reading-stands on rollers, which could be pushed away or drawn near at will, let you rest your open book on them. At the center stood a large table strewn with pamphlets, among which a few already old newspapers showed. Electric light flooded this whole harmonious ensemble, falling from four frosted globes half sunk in the scrollwork of the ceiling. I gazed with real wonder at this room so ingeniously fitted out, and I could scarcely believe my eyes.

"Captain Nemo," I said to my host, who had just stretched out on a divan, "here is a library that would do honor to more than one palace on the continents, and I'm truly amazed to think that it can follow you down into the deepest seas."

"Where would one find more solitude, more silence, Professor?" Captain Nemo answered. "Does your study at the Museum offer you as complete a peace?"

"No, Captain, and I must add that mine is a poor thing beside yours. You must have six or seven thousand volumes here—"

"Twelve thousand, Professor Aronnax. They are the only ties that still bind me to the land. But the world ended for me the day my Nautilus first dove beneath the waves. That day I bought my last books, my last pamphlets, my last newspapers, and ever since I have chosen to believe that humanity has neither thought nor written another word. These books, Professor, are at your disposal in any case, and you may use them freely."

I thanked Captain Nemo and went over to the library shelves. Books of science, of philosophy, of literature, written in every language, were there in abundance; but I did not see a single work of political economy—those, it seemed, were strictly banned from the ship. One curious detail: all these books were shelved together without distinction, whatever language they happened to be written in, and this mingling proved that the captain of the Nautilus must read fluently any volume his hand happened to pull down.

Among these works I noticed the masterpieces of the old masters and the new—that is, everything most beautiful that humanity has produced in history, poetry, fiction, and science, from Homer to Victor Hugo, from Xenophon to Michelet, from Rabelais to Madame Sand. But science, above all, was what this library had been built to hold. Books of mechanics, ballistics, hydrography, meteorology, geography, geology, and the rest took up a place no less important than the works of natural history, and I understood that they formed the captain's chief study. There I saw all of Humboldt, all of Arago, the work of Foucault, of Henri Sainte-Claire Deville, of Chasles, of Milne-Edwards, of Quatrefages, of Tyndall, of Faraday, of Berthelot, of Father Secchi, of Petermann, of Commander Maury, of Agassiz, and more. The memoirs of the Academy of Sciences, the bulletins of the various geographical societies, and—in a place of honor—the two volumes that had perhaps earned me this relatively charitable welcome from Captain Nemo. Among the works of Joseph Bertrand, his book titled The Founders of Astronomy even gave me a firm date: since I knew it had appeared in the course of 1865, I could conclude that the Nautilus had been fitted out no earlier than that. So Captain Nemo had begun his underwater existence three years ago at the most. I hoped, moreover, that still more recent works might let me pin down the date exactly; but I had time to make that search, and I did not want to hold up our stroll through the wonders of the Nautilus any longer.

"Captain," I said, "I thank you for putting this library at my disposal. There are treasures of science here, and I'll make good use of them."

"This room is not only a library," said Captain Nemo. "It's also a smoking room."

"A smoking room?" I exclaimed. "So one smokes on board?"

"Certainly."

"Then, Captain, I'm forced to conclude that you've kept up your dealings with Havana."

"None at all," the captain replied. "Take this cigar, Professor Aronnax, and though it doesn't come from Havana, you'll be pleased with it if you know your tobacco."

I took the cigar he offered me. Its shape recalled that of a Havana londres, but it seemed to be made of leaves of gold. I lit it at a little brazier resting on an elegant bronze stand, and drew my first puffs with all the pleasure of a connoisseur who hasn't smoked in two days.

"It's excellent," I said, "but it isn't tobacco."

"No," the captain answered. "This tobacco comes neither from Havana nor from the East. It's a kind of seaweed, rich in nicotine, that the sea supplies me—not without some stinginess. Do you still miss your londres cigars, Professor?"

"Captain, from this day on I scorn them."

"Then smoke as you please, and don't fret over where these cigars come from. No tax office has passed judgment on them, but they're none the worse for that, I imagine."

"On the contrary."

At that moment Captain Nemo opened a door facing the one by which I had entered the library, and I passed into an immense salon, splendidly lit.

It was a vast rectangle with cut corners, ten meters long, six wide, five high. A luminous ceiling, decorated with light arabesques, spread a clear, soft daylight over all the wonders heaped up in this museum. For it truly was a museum, in which an intelligent and lavish hand had gathered every treasure of nature and of art, with that artist's jumble that marks a painter's studio.

Some thirty masterworks, framed alike and separated by dazzling displays of arms, adorned the walls, which were hung with tapestries of a severe design. There I saw canvases of the highest value, most of which I had admired in the private collections of Europe and at the painting exhibitions. The various schools of the old masters were represented by a madonna of Raphael, a virgin of Leonardo da Vinci, a nymph of Correggio, a woman of Titian, an adoration of Veronese, an assumption of Murillo, a portrait by Holbein, a monk by Velázquez, a martyr by Ribera, a village festival by Rubens, two Flemish landscapes by Teniers, three little genre pieces by Gerard Dou, Metsu, and Paul Potter, two canvases by Géricault and Prud'hon, and a few seascapes by Backhuysen and Vernet. Among the works of modern painting were pictures signed Delacroix, Ingres, Decamps, Troyon, Meissonier, Daubigny, and others; and a few admirable scale copies of marble or bronze statues, modeled after the finest works of antiquity, stood on their pedestals in the corners of this magnificent museum. The stunned amazement the commander of the Nautilus had predicted was already beginning to take hold of my mind.

"Professor," said this strange man just then, "you'll forgive the informality with which I receive you, and the disorder that reigns in this salon."

"Captain," I answered, "without trying to learn who you are, may I be allowed to recognize in you an artist?"

"An amateur, at most, Professor. I once loved to collect these beautiful works created by the hand of man. I was an avid seeker, a tireless hunter, and I managed to gather a few pieces of great value. They are my last mementos of that land which is dead to me. In my eyes, your modern artists are already ancients; they have two or three thousand years of existence behind them, and I blur them together in my mind. The masters have no age."

"And these musicians?" I said, pointing to scores by Weber, Rossini, Mozart, Beethoven, Haydn, Meyerbeer, Hérold, Wagner, Auber, Gounod, and many others, scattered over a large-model piano-organ that filled one of the salon's panels.

"These musicians," Captain Nemo replied, "are contemporaries of Orpheus, for the differences of chronology fade away in the memory of the dead—and I am dead, Professor, as thoroughly dead as those friends of yours who rest six feet underground!"

Captain Nemo fell silent and seemed lost in a deep reverie. I studied him with keen emotion, silently reading the strangeness of his features. Leaning on the corner of a precious mosaic table, he no longer saw me; he had forgotten my presence.

I respected his meditation and went on reviewing the curiosities that enriched this salon.

Alongside the works of art, the natural rarities held a very important place. They consisted mainly of plants, shells, and other products of the Ocean, which must have been Captain Nemo's own personal finds. In the middle of the salon, a jet of water, lit by electricity, fell back into a basin made from a single giant clam. This shell, furnished by the largest of the headless mollusks, measured around six meters in circumference along its delicately scalloped rim; so it surpassed in size even those splendid giant clams the Republic of Venice gave to Francis I, and from which the church of Saint-Sulpice in Paris made two gigantic holy-water fonts.

Around this basin, under elegant glass cases fixed with copper frames, were classified and labeled the most precious products of the sea that had ever been offered to a naturalist's gaze. You can imagine my professor's delight.

The branch of the zoophytes offered very curious specimens of its two groups, the polyps and the echinoderms. In the first group there were organ-pipe corals, sea fans spread out like fans, soft sponges of Syria, sea shrubs of the Moluccas, sea pens, an admirable sea rod from the waters of Norway, various sea plumes, soft corals, a whole series of those madrepores that my teacher Milne-Edwards has so shrewdly sorted into sections, and among which I noticed lovely flabellinas, oculina corals from Réunion Island, the "chariot of Neptune" of the Antilles, superb varieties of coral—in short, every species of those curious coral-builders whose colonies form entire islands that will one day become continents. Among the echinoderms, remarkable for their spiny casing, the sea stars, the starfish, the sea lilies, the feather stars, the brittle stars, the sea urchins, the sea cucumbers, and the rest represented a complete collection of that group.

A conchologist of nervous temperament would surely have swooned before other, more numerous cases in which the specimens of the mollusk branch were arranged. There I saw a collection of inestimable value that I would not have time to describe in full. Among these treasures I'll cite, just for the record: the elegant royal hammer shell of the Indian Ocean, whose regular white spots stood out vividly against a red and brown ground; an imperial spondylus of vivid colors, bristling all over with spines, a rare specimen in the museums of Europe, whose value I put at twenty thousand francs; a common hammer shell from the seas of New Holland, hard to come by; exotic cockles from Senegal, fragile white shells with double valves that a breath would have scattered like a soap bubble; several varieties of the watering-pot shells of Java, sorts of chalky tubes fringed with leafy folds and much fought over by collectors; a whole series of top shells, some greenish-yellow, fished from American seas, others reddish-brown, partial to the waters of New Holland, some of these from the Gulf of Mexico and remarkable for their overlapping scale-like shell, others sun shells found in the southern seas, and last, the rarest of all, the magnificent New Zealand spur shell; then some admirable sulfur-yellow tellins, precious species of Cytherea and Venus clams, the trellised sundial shell from the coasts of Tranquebar, the marbled turban with its resplendent mother-of-pearl, the green parrot shells of the China seas, the almost unknown cone of the genus Coenodullus, every variety of cowrie that serves as money in India and Africa, the "Glory of the Sea," the most precious shell of the East Indies; and finally periwinkles, dolphin shells, turret shells, violet snails, egg cowries, volutes, olives, miters, helmet shells, murexes, whelks, harp shells, rock shells, tritons, cerith shells, spindle shells, conchs, wing shells, limpets, sea butterflies, sea angels—delicate, fragile shells that science has christened with its most charming names.

Set apart, in special compartments, were unrolled strings of pearls of the greatest beauty, which the electric light pricked with points of fire: pink pearls torn from the pinna shells of the Red Sea; green pearls from the iris abalone; yellow, blue, and black pearls, curious products of the various mollusks of every ocean and of certain mussels from the rivers of the North; and finally several specimens of inestimable price, distilled by the rarest of pearl oysters. Some of these pearls were bigger than a pigeon's egg; they were worth as much as, and more than, the one the traveler Tavernier sold for three million to the Shah of Persia, and they outshone that other pearl of the Imam of Muscat, which I had believed had no rival in the world.

So putting a figure on the value of this collection was, so to speak, impossible. Captain Nemo must have spent millions to acquire these various specimens, and I was wondering what source he could be drawing on to satisfy his collector's whims like this, when I was interrupted by these words:

"You're examining my shells, Professor. Indeed, they may interest a naturalist; but for me they hold one further charm, for I gathered every one of them with my own hand, and there is not a sea on the globe that has escaped my searches."

"I understand, Captain—I understand that joy of walking among such riches. You are one of those who have built their own treasure with their own hands. No museum in Europe possesses such a collection of the Ocean's products. But if I exhaust my admiration on it, what will I have left for the vessel that carries it! I have no wish to pry into secrets that are yours. Still, I confess that this Nautilus, the motive power it holds within itself, the machinery that lets it be handled, the mighty agent that drives it—all of that excites my curiosity in the highest degree. I see instruments hanging on the walls of this salon whose purpose is unknown to me. May I ask—?"

"Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo answered, "I told you that you would be free aboard my ship, and so no part of the Nautilus is off-limits to you. You may visit it in detail, and I'll be glad to be your guide."

"I don't know how to thank you, Captain, but I won't abuse your kindness. I'll only ask what these instruments of physics are meant for—"

"Professor, these same instruments are in my own room, and it's there that I'll have the pleasure of explaining their use to you. But first, come see the cabin that has been set aside for you. You need to know how you'll be housed aboard the Nautilus."

I followed Captain Nemo, who led me through one of the doors cut into each canted corner of the salon and back into the ship's passageways. He took me forward, and there I found, not a cabin, but an elegant room, with a bed, a washstand, and various other furnishings.

I could only thank my host.

"Your room adjoins mine," he said, opening a door, "and mine opens onto the salon we have just left."

I entered the captain's room. It had a severe, almost monastic look. An iron cot, a work table, a few washing furnishings. The whole lit by a dim half-light. Nothing comfortable. The strict necessities, and no more.

Captain Nemo pointed me to a seat.

"Please sit down," he said.

I sat, and he began to speak in these terms:

Everything by Electricity

"Professor," said Captain Nemo, pointing to the instruments hanging on the walls of his cabin, "here are the devices the navigation of the Nautilus requires. In here, as in the salon, I have them always before my eyes, and they tell me my position and my exact heading in the middle of the Ocean. Some of them you'll recognize: the thermometer, which gives the temperature inside the Nautilus; the barometer, which weighs the pressure of the air and predicts changes in the weather; the hygrometer, which marks the degree of dryness in the atmosphere; the storm-glass, whose mixture, as it breaks down, announces the coming of a storm; the compass, which steers my course; the sextant, which by the height of the sun tells me my latitude; the chronometers, which allow me to calculate my longitude; and finally the day and night glasses, which serve me to scan every point of the horizon when the Nautilus has risen back to the surface of the waves."

"These are the ordinary instruments of any navigator," I answered, "and I know how they're used. But here are others that no doubt answer to the particular demands of the Nautilus. This dial I see, with a needle traveling across it — isn't that a pressure gauge?"

"A pressure gauge, exactly. Put in contact with the water, whose external pressure it registers, it tells me thereby the depth at which my craft is holding."

"And these soundings of a new kind?"

"They're thermometric soundings that report back the temperature of the various layers of water."

"And these other instruments, whose use I can't guess?"

"Here, Professor, I must give you a few explanations," said Captain Nemo. "Kindly hear me out."

He kept silent for a few moments, then he said:

"There is a powerful agent, obedient, quick, easy, one that bends to every use and reigns as master aboard my ship. Everything is done by it. It lights me, it warms me, it is the soul of my mechanical apparatus. This agent is electricity."

"Electricity!" I exclaimed, rather taken aback.

"Yes, Professor."

"And yet, Captain, you command an extreme swiftness of movement that sits poorly with the power of electricity. So far, its dynamic force has remained very limited, capable of producing only small amounts of power!"

"Professor," replied Captain Nemo, "my electricity is not everybody's electricity, and that is all I'll permit myself to tell you about it."

"I won't press you, Captain, and I'll content myself with being thoroughly astonished at such a result. One question only, however — which you needn't answer if it's indiscreet. The elements you use to produce this marvelous agent must wear out quickly. The zinc, for instance — how do you replace it, since you no longer have any communication with the land?"

"Your question will have its answer," replied Captain Nemo. "Let me tell you, first of all, that there exist at the bottom of the seas mines of zinc, iron, silver, and gold whose working would very certainly be practicable. But I've borrowed nothing from these metals of the earth; I wanted to ask the sea itself, and nothing else, for the means to produce my electricity."

"The sea?"

"Yes, Professor, and the means were not lacking. I could, in fact, by setting up a circuit between wires sunk to different depths, have obtained electricity from the difference in the temperatures they were exposed to; but I preferred to use a more practical system."

"And that is?"

"You know the composition of seawater. In every thousand grams you find ninety-six and a half hundredths water, and about two and two-thirds hundredths sodium chloride; then, in small quantities, chlorides of magnesium and potassium, bromide of magnesium, sulfate of magnesia, and the sulfate and carbonate of lime. You see, then, that sodium chloride is present in a considerable proportion. Now, it is this sodium that I extract from seawater, and out of which I compose my elements."

"The sodium?"

"Yes, Professor. Mixed with mercury, it forms an amalgam that takes the place of zinc in the Bunsen cells. The mercury never wears out. Only the sodium is consumed, and the sea itself supplies it to me. I'll tell you, besides, that sodium batteries must be regarded as the most powerful of all, and that their electromotive force is double that of zinc batteries."

"I quite understand, Captain, the excellence of sodium in the conditions you find yourself in. The sea contains it. Good. But you still have to manufacture it — to extract it, in a word.
