And how do you do that? Your batteries could obviously serve for the extraction; but, if I'm not mistaken, the sodium the electrical apparatus would consume would exceed the quantity extracted. It would turn out, then, that you'd use up more producing it than you'd ever produce!"

"And so, Professor, I don't extract it with the battery; I simply use the heat of coal from the earth."

"From the earth?" I said, pressing him.

"Say sea coal, if you like," replied Captain Nemo.

"And you can work undersea coal mines?"

"Professor Aronnax, you'll see me at work. I ask you only for a little patience, since you have time to be patient. Remember just this: I owe everything to the Ocean; it produces the electricity, and the electricity gives the Nautilus heat, light, motion — life, in a word."

"But not the air you breathe?"

"Oh, I could manufacture the air I need, but there's no point, since I go back up to the surface of the sea whenever I please. Still, if electricity doesn't supply me with breathable air, it does at least work powerful pumps that store it away in special tanks, and that lets me prolong my stay in the deep layers as long as I want, whenever the need arises."

"Captain," I answered, "I can only admire you. You've evidently found what men will no doubt find one day — the true dynamic power of electricity."

"I don't know whether they'll find it," replied Captain Nemo coldly. "Be that as it may, you already know the first application I've made of this precious agent. It's electricity that lights us, with an evenness, a steadiness, that the light of the sun doesn't have. Now, look at this clock; it's electric, and it runs with a regularity that defies the best chronometers. I've divided it into twenty-four hours, like the Italian clocks, for to me there is neither night nor day, neither sun nor moon, but only this artificial light that I carry with me to the bottom of the seas! Look — right now, it's ten in the morning."

"Exactly."

"Another application of electricity. This dial, hung here before our eyes, serves to indicate the speed of the Nautilus. An electric wire connects it to the log's propeller, and its needle shows me the craft's actual pace. And there, at this moment, we're running at a moderate speed of fifteen miles an hour."

"It's marvelous," I answered, "and I see very well, Captain, that you were right to make use of this agent, which is destined to replace wind, water, and steam."

"We're not finished, Professor Aronnax," said Captain Nemo, rising. "If you'll follow me, we'll visit the stern of the Nautilus."

In fact, I already knew the whole forward part of this underwater boat, whose exact layout, going from the center toward the ram, was as follows: the dining room, five meters long, separated from the library by a watertight bulkhead — that is, one that water couldn't get through; the library, five meters; the grand salon, ten meters, separated from the captain's cabin by a second watertight bulkhead; the said captain's cabin, five meters; mine, two and a half meters; and finally an air reservoir, seven and a half meters, that extended all the way to the bow. Total, thirty-five meters in length. The watertight bulkheads were pierced with doors that shut hermetically by means of rubber gaskets, and they guaranteed complete safety aboard the Nautilus in the event that a leak sprang somewhere.

I followed Captain Nemo through the gangways running along the sides, and came to the center of the ship. There I found a sort of well that opened between two watertight bulkheads. An iron ladder, bolted to the wall, led up to its upper end. I asked the captain what this ladder was for.

"It leads to the dinghy," he answered.

"What! You have a dinghy?" I replied, rather astonished.

"Of course. An excellent little boat, light and unsinkable, that serves for excursions and fishing."

"But then, when you want to embark, you're forced to come back to the surface of the sea?"

"Not at all. This dinghy sits against the upper part of the Nautilus's hull and occupies a cavity built to receive it. It's completely decked over, absolutely watertight, and held in place by strong bolts. This ladder leads to a manhole cut through the hull of the Nautilus, which matches a similar hole cut in the side of the dinghy. It's through this double opening that I let myself into the boat. They close one — the Nautilus's; I close the other — the dinghy's — by means of pressure screws; I release the bolts, and the boat shoots up to the surface of the sea with prodigious speed. Then I open the deck hatch, kept carefully shut until then, I step the mast, I hoist my sail or take up my oars, and off I go."

"But how do you get back aboard?"

"I don't come back, Professor Aronnax — it's the Nautilus that comes back."

"At your command!"

"At my command. An electric wire connects me to it. I send a telegram, and that's enough."

"Indeed," I said, intoxicated by these marvels, "nothing could be simpler!"

After passing the stairwell that led up to the platform, I saw a cabin two meters long, in which Conseil and Ned Land, delighted with their meal, were busy devouring it with a hearty appetite. Then a door opened onto the galley, three meters long, set between the ship's spacious storerooms.

There, electricity — more energetic and more obedient than gas itself — did all the work of cooking. The wires, running in under the stoves, delivered to spongy plates of platinum a heat that was distributed and held steady and even. It also heated distilling apparatus that, through evaporation, furnished excellent drinking water. Next to this galley opened a bathroom, comfortably fitted out, whose taps supplied cold or hot water at will.

After the galley came the crew's quarters, five meters long. But the door was shut, and I couldn't see how it was arranged — which might have told me how many men it took to run the Nautilus.

At the far end rose a fourth watertight bulkhead that separated these quarters from the engine room. A door opened, and I found myself in the compartment where Captain Nemo — a first-rate engineer, beyond any doubt — had installed his machinery for locomotion.

This engine room, brightly lit, measured no less than twenty meters in length. It was naturally divided into two parts: the first held the elements that produced the electricity, the second the mechanism that transmitted the motion to the propeller.

I was struck at first by the sui generis odor that filled the compartment. Captain Nemo noticed my reaction.

"These," he told me, "are a few emissions of gas produced by the use of sodium; but it's only a slight inconvenience. Besides, every morning we purify the ship by ventilating it in the open air."

Meanwhile, I was examining the machinery of the Nautilus with an interest easy to imagine.

"You see," Captain Nemo told me, "I use Bunsen cells, not Ruhmkorff cells. Those would have been powerless. The Bunsen cells are few in number, but strong and large, which works better, as experience has shown. The electricity produced travels to the stern, where it acts through large electromagnets on a special system of levers and gearing that transmits the motion to the propeller shaft. The propeller, six meters in diameter with a pitch of seven and a half meters, can turn as fast as a hundred and twenty revolutions per second."

"And you get, then?"

"A speed of fifty miles an hour."

There was a mystery here, but I didn't press to learn it. How could electricity act with such power? Where did this almost unlimited force take its origin? Was it in its excessive tension, obtained by coils of some new kind? Was it in its transmission, which an unknown system of levers could amplify to infinity? That was what I couldn't grasp.

"Captain Nemo," I said, "I take note of the results and I don't try to explain them. I saw the Nautilus maneuvering in front of the Abraham Lincoln, and I know what to make of its speed. But moving isn't enough. You have to see where you're going! You have to be able to steer right, left, up, down! How do you reach the great depths, where you meet a growing resistance measured in hundreds of atmospheres? How do you rise back to the surface of the Ocean? And how, finally, do you hold yourself at whatever level suits you? Am I being indiscreet in asking you?"

"Not at all, Professor," the captain answered me, after a slight hesitation, "since you must never leave this underwater boat. Come into the salon. It's our real study, and there you'll learn everything you need to know about the Nautilus!"

Some Figures

A moment later we were seated on a divan in the lounge, cigars between our lips. The captain laid before me a set of drawings that gave the plan, section, and elevation of the Nautilus. Then he began his description in these words:

"Here, Professor Aronnax, are the various dimensions of the vessel that carries you. It's a very elongated cylinder with conical ends. It closely resembles the shape of a cigar — a shape already adopted in London for several craft of the same kind. The length of this cylinder, from tip to tip, is exactly seventy meters, and its beam, at its widest point, is eight meters. So it isn't built quite to the ratio of one to ten, like your fast steamers, but its lines are long enough and its run prolonged enough that the displaced water slips away easily and puts up no resistance to its passage.

"These two dimensions let you work out, by a simple calculation, the surface area and the volume of the Nautilus. Its surface comes to 1,011.45 square meters; its volume, 1,500.2 cubic meters — which is to say that, fully submerged, it displaces or weighs 1,500.2 cubic meters, or tons.

"When I drew up the plans for a vessel meant to travel underwater, I wanted it, when floating in equilibrium, to sit nine-tenths submerged and to show only one-tenth above the surface. Under those conditions, then, it had to displace only nine-tenths of its volume — that is, 1,356.48 cubic meters, weighing that same number of tons. So in building it to the dimensions I just gave you, I couldn't exceed that weight.

"The Nautilus is made of two hulls, one inside the other, joined by T-shaped iron beams that give it tremendous rigidity. Thanks to this cellular arrangement, it stands up to pressure like a solid block, as though it were cast in one piece. Its plating can't give way; it holds together by its own cohesion and not by the grip of rivets, and the uniformity of its construction — the result of a perfect fit between materials — lets it defy the most violent seas.

"Both hulls are built of sheet steel, whose density relative to water is 7.8. The inner hull is no less than five centimeters thick and weighs 394.96 tons. The outer shell, together with the keel — fifty centimeters high and twenty-five wide, weighing 62 tons on its own — plus the engine, the ballast, the various fittings and furnishings, the interior bulkheads and bracing, all come to 961.62 tons; add that to the 394.96 tons, and you get the required total of 1,356.48 tons. Is that clear?"

"It's clear," I answered.

"So," the captain went on, "when the Nautilus is afloat under these conditions, one-tenth of it rides above the water. Now, I've fitted it with tanks whose capacity equals that one-tenth — a volume of 150.72 tons — and if I fill them with water, the vessel then displaces, or weighs, 1,507 tons, and is completely submerged. That's exactly what happens, Professor. These tanks are set in the lower parts of the Nautilus. I open the valves, they fill, the vessel sinks, and comes to rest just level with the surface of the water."

"Fine, Captain, but here we come to the real difficulty. That you can bring the ship level with the surface of the ocean, I understand. But lower down, diving beneath that surface, won't your underwater machine meet a pressure — and therefore suffer an upward thrust — that has to be reckoned at one atmosphere for every thirty feet of water, or about one kilogram per square centimeter?"

"Precisely, Professor."

"Then unless you fill the Nautilus entirely, I don't see how you can drive it down into the heart of the waters."

"Professor," Captain Nemo replied, "you mustn't confuse statics with dynamics, or you lay yourself open to serious errors. It takes very little effort to reach the ocean's lower regions, because bodies have a tendency to become 'sinkers.' Follow my reasoning."

"I'm listening, Captain."

"When I set out to determine the added weight the Nautilus would need in order to submerge, all I had to concern myself with was the reduction in volume that seawater undergoes as its layers grow deeper and deeper."

"That's obvious," I answered.

"Now, if water isn't absolutely incompressible, it is at least very slightly compressible. In fact, according to the most recent calculations, this reduction amounts to only 436 ten-millionths per atmosphere, or per thirty feet of depth. If I mean to go down a thousand meters, I then allow for the reduction in volume under a pressure equal to that of a column of water a thousand meters high — that is, under a pressure of a hundred atmospheres. This reduction would then be 436 hundred-thousandths. So I'd have to increase the weight until the vessel weighs 1,513.77 tons instead of 1,507.2 tons. The increase, consequently, would come to only 6.57 tons."

"Only that?"

"Only that, Professor Aronnax, and the calculation is easy to check. Now, I have supplementary tanks capable of taking on a hundred tons of water. So I can descend to considerable depths. When I want to rise again and come level with the surface, I need only expel that water, emptying all the tanks completely if I want the Nautilus to ride one-tenth of its total capacity above the waves."

Against reasoning backed by figures like these, I had nothing to object.

"I accept your calculations, Captain," I answered, "and I'd be churlish to dispute them, since experience proves them right every day. But now I sense a genuine difficulty ahead of us."

"Which is, Professor?"

"When you're at a depth of a thousand meters, the walls of the Nautilus bear a pressure of a hundred atmospheres. If, at that moment, you want to empty the supplementary tanks in order to lighten your vessel and rise to the surface, your pumps have to overcome that pressure of a hundred atmospheres — which comes to a hundred kilograms per square centimeter. That calls for a power —"

"That electricity alone could give me," Captain Nemo cut in. "I repeat, Professor: the dynamic power of my machines is very nearly infinite. The pumps of the Nautilus have prodigious force — as you must have seen when their columns of water came pouring like a torrent over the Abraham Lincoln. Besides, I only use the supplementary tanks to reach middling depths of fifteen hundred to two thousand meters, and even then only to spare my equipment. So when the whim takes me to visit the depths of the ocean, two or three leagues below its surface, I use maneuvers that take longer but are no less reliable."

"Which are, Captain?" I asked.

"That brings me naturally to telling you how the Nautilus is handled."

"I can't wait to hear it."

"To steer this vessel to starboard or to port — to maneuver it, in short, along a horizontal plane — I use an ordinary broad-bladed rudder, mounted on the stern-post and worked by a wheel and tackle. But I can also drive the Nautilus up and down, in a vertical plane, by means of two inclined planes attached to its sides at the waterline — movable planes, able to take any position, worked from inside by powerful levers. Kept parallel to the vessel, these planes hold it moving horizontally. Tilted, the Nautilus — depending on the angle set and driven by the thrust of its propeller — either dives along a diagonal as long as I please, or climbs along that same diagonal. And if I want to return to the surface faster still, I throw in the propeller, and the pressure of the water sends the Nautilus rising straight up like a balloon that, swollen with hydrogen, shoots quickly into the air."

"Bravo, Captain!" I cried. "But how can the helmsman follow the course you set for him in the middle of the waters?"

"The helmsman is stationed in a glass-walled cage that projects from the upper part of the Nautilus's hull, fitted with lens-shaped panes."

"Panes able to withstand such pressures?"

"Perfectly. Crystal, fragile under a sharp blow, nonetheless offers considerable resistance. In experiments with fishing by electric light, carried out in 1864 in the northern seas, plates of this material, only seven millimeters thick, were seen to withstand a pressure of sixteen atmospheres, all the while letting through powerful heat rays that spread the warmth unevenly across them. Now, the panes I use are no less than twenty-one centimeters thick at their center — thirty times that thickness."

"Granted, Captain Nemo — but after all, to see, you need light to drive back the darkness, and I wonder how, in the blackness of the waters —"

"Behind the helmsman's cage is mounted a powerful electric reflector, whose beams light up the sea for half a mile around."

"Ah, bravo — three times bravo, Captain! Now I understand that phosphorescence of the so-called narwhal that so puzzled the scientists! And on that subject, let me ask you: was the collision of the Nautilus with the Scotia, which caused such a stir, the result of a chance encounter?"

"Purely by chance, Professor. I was cruising two meters below the surface when the shock occurred. I saw, moreover, that it had done no serious harm."

"None, sir. But as for your encounter with the Abraham Lincoln?"

"Professor, I'm sorry for one of the finest ships of that gallant American navy — but I was under attack, and I had to defend myself! I contented myself, though, with putting the frigate out of any condition to harm me; she'll have no trouble repairing her damage at the nearest port."

"Ah, Captain," I exclaimed with conviction, "your Nautilus really is a marvelous vessel!"

"Yes, Professor," Captain Nemo answered with genuine emotion, "and I love it as the flesh of my flesh! If aboard one of your ships, exposed to all the hazards of the ocean, everything is danger — if on that sea the first impression is the sense of the abyss, as the Dutchman Jansen so aptly put it — then aboard the Nautilus, and beneath its waters, a man's heart has nothing left to fear. No warping to dread, for the double hull of this vessel has the rigidity of iron; no rigging to be worn out by pitch and roll; no sails for the wind to carry off; no boilers for the steam to burst; no fire to fear, since this craft is made of sheet metal and not of wood; no coal to run out of, since electricity is its driving power; no collision to fear, since it sails alone in the deep waters; no storm to brave, since a few meters below the surface it finds absolute calm! There, Professor. There is the ship of ships! And if it's true that the engineer has more confidence in a vessel than the builder, and the builder more than the captain himself, then you can understand with what abandon I trust myself to my Nautilus — since I am at once its captain, its builder, and its engineer!"

Captain Nemo spoke with a compelling eloquence. The fire in his eyes, the passion in his gestures, transfigured him. Yes! He loved his ship the way a father loves his child.

But a question — an indiscreet one, perhaps — arose naturally, and I couldn't keep myself from putting it to him.

"You're an engineer, then, Captain Nemo?"

"Yes, Professor," he answered. "I studied in London, in Paris, in New York, back when I was a dweller on the continents of the earth."

"But how were you able to build this admirable Nautilus in secret?"

"Each of its pieces, Professor Aronnax, reached me from a different point on the globe, under a disguised destination. Its keel was forged at Le Creusot, its propeller shaft at Pen & Co. of London, the sheet-iron plates of its hull at Leard's of Liverpool, its propeller at Scott's of Glasgow. Its tanks were made by Cail & Co. of Paris, its engine by Krupp in Prussia, its ram in the workshops of Motala in Sweden, its precision instruments at Hart Brothers of New York, and so on — and each of these suppliers received my plans under a different name."

"But," I went on, "once these pieces were made, they had to be assembled, fitted together?"

"Professor, I'd set up my workshops on a deserted islet, in the open ocean. There, my workmen — that is, my brave companions, whom I've trained and formed — and I finished our Nautilus. Then, once the work was done, fire destroyed every trace of our passage on that islet, which I'd have blown up if I could have."

"Then I may assume the cost of building this craft was enormous?"

"Professor Aronnax, an iron ship costs eleven hundred and twenty-five francs per ton. Now, the Nautilus gauges fifteen hundred. So it came to 1,687,000 francs — two million counting its fittings, or four or five million with the works of art and the collections it holds."

"One last question, Captain Nemo."

"Go ahead, Professor."

"You're rich, then?"

"Infinitely rich, sir — I could pay off France's ten billion of debt without the slightest inconvenience!"

I stared hard at the strange man who spoke to me this way. Was he trading on my credulity? The future would tell.

The Black Current

The portion of the globe covered by water has been reckoned at three million eight hundred thirty-two thousand five hundred fifty-eight square myriameters — more than thirty-eight million hectares. This liquid mass comprises two billion two hundred fifty million cubic miles, and would form a sphere sixty leagues in diameter weighing three quintillion tons. To grasp that number, you have to tell yourself that a quintillion is to a billion what a billion is to one — that is, there are as many billions in a quintillion as there are single units in a billion. And this mass of water is roughly the quantity that all the rivers of the earth would pour out over the span of forty thousand years.

During the geological ages, the age of fire was followed by the age of water. In the beginning the ocean was universal. Then, little by little, in the Silurian period, mountain peaks appeared, islands emerged and vanished again under partial floods, showed themselves once more, fused together, formed continents, and at last the land settled geographically into the shape we see today. The solid had wrested from the liquid thirty-seven million six hundred fifty-seven thousand square miles — twelve thousand nine hundred sixteen million hectares.

The configuration of the continents lets us divide the waters into five great parts: the Arctic Ocean, the Antarctic Ocean, the Indian Ocean, the Atlantic Ocean, and the Pacific Ocean.

The Pacific runs from north to south between the two polar circles, and from west to east between Asia and America across a stretch of a hundred and forty-five degrees of longitude. It is the most peaceful of the seas: its currents broad and slow, its tides moderate, its rainfall abundant. Such was the ocean that fate had first summoned me to travel — and under the strangest conditions imaginable.

"Professor," Captain Nemo said to me, "if you don't mind, we're going to fix our exact position and set the point of departure for this voyage. It's a quarter to noon. I'll bring us back up to the surface."

The captain pressed an electric bell three times. The pumps began driving the water out of the ballast tanks; on the pressure gauge the needle marked, through its shifting readings, the Nautilus's rise, then came to a stop.

"We've arrived," said the captain.

I made my way to the central stairway that led up to the platform. I climbed the metal steps and, through the open hatches, came out onto the upper part of the Nautilus.

The platform stood barely eighty centimeters above the water. The bow and stern of the Nautilus tapered into that spindle shape which had, quite rightly, earned it the comparison to a long cigar. I noticed that its sheet-metal plates, slightly overlapping, resembled the scales that clothe the bodies of the great land reptiles. So I understood perfectly well why, even through the best telescopes, this vessel had always been taken for a marine animal.

Toward the middle of the platform, the dinghy, half sunk into the hull of the ship, formed a slight swelling. Fore and aft rose two cages of modest height, with sloping walls, partly closed by thick lens-shaped panes of glass: one meant for the helmsman who steered the Nautilus, the other for the powerful electric beacon that lit its way.

The sea was magnificent, the sky clear. The long craft scarcely felt the wide swells of the ocean. A light breeze out of the east ruffled the surface of the water. The horizon, swept clear of mist, was perfect for observation.

There was nothing in sight. Not a reef, not an islet. No more Abraham Lincoln. Nothing but the empty vastness.

Sextant in hand, Captain Nemo took the altitude of the sun, which would give him his latitude. He waited a few minutes for the star to come and just graze the rim of the horizon. While he sighted, not one of his muscles so much as twitched, and the instrument could not have held stiller in a hand of marble.

"Noon," he said. "Professor, whenever you're ready."

I cast one last look at that faintly yellowish sea off the Japanese coast, and went back down to the grand salon.

There the captain worked out his position, calculated his longitude by chronometer, and checked it against earlier observations of hour angles. Then he said to me:

"Professor Aronnax, we are at a hundred thirty-seven degrees and fifteen minutes of longitude west —"

"West of which meridian?" I asked eagerly, hoping the captain's answer might give me a clue to his nationality.

"Sir," he replied, "I have several chronometers set to the meridians of Paris, Greenwich, and Washington. But in your honor I'll use the one for Paris."

The answer told me nothing. I bowed, and the commander went on:

"Thirty-seven degrees and fifteen minutes of longitude west of the Paris meridian, and thirty degrees and seven minutes of north latitude — that is, some three hundred miles off the coast of Japan. Today, the eighth of November, at noon, our voyage of exploration beneath the waters begins."

"God keep us," I answered.

"And now, Professor," the captain added, "I'll leave you to your studies. I've set our course east-northeast at a depth of fifty meters. Here are some large-scale charts where you can follow it. The salon is at your disposal, and I ask your leave to withdraw."

Captain Nemo bowed to me. I was left alone, lost in my thoughts. Every one of them turned on this commander of the Nautilus. Would I ever learn what nation this strange man belonged to — this man who boasted of belonging to none? What had provoked the hatred he had sworn against all humanity, this hatred that perhaps was hunting for some terrible revenge? Was he one of those unrecognized scholars, one of those geniuses "who had been dealt some great sorrow," as Conseil put it — a modern Galileo — or one of those men of science, like the American Maury, whose career had been broken by political upheavals? I still couldn't say. Chance had just flung me aboard his ship; he held my life in his hands; and he received me coldly, but with courtesy. Only, he had never once taken the hand I held out to him. He had never once offered me his own.

For a full hour I sat sunk in these reflections, trying to pierce a mystery that fascinated me. Then my gaze fixed on the vast planisphere spread across the table, and I laid my finger on the very spot where the observed longitude and latitude crossed.

The sea has its rivers just as the continents do. They are particular currents, recognizable by their temperature and their color, and the most remarkable of them is known as the Gulf Stream. Science has charted the courses of five principal currents around the globe: one in the North Atlantic, a second in the South Atlantic, a third in the North Pacific, a fourth in the South Pacific, and a fifth in the southern Indian Ocean. It is even probable that a sixth current once existed in the northern Indian Ocean, back when the Caspian and Aral Seas, joined to the great lakes of Asia, formed a single unbroken sheet of water.

Now, at the point marked on the planisphere, there unrolled one of these currents — the Kuro-Shivo of the Japanese, the Black River — which, issuing from the Bay of Bengal where the perpendicular rays of the tropical sun warm it, crosses the Strait of Malacca, runs along the coast of Asia, and curves through the North Pacific as far as the Aleutian Islands, carrying with it drifting trunks of camphor trees and other native products, and slicing through the ocean's waves with the pure indigo of its warm waters. This was the current the Nautilus was about to travel. I followed it with my eye, watched it lose itself in the immensity of the Pacific, and felt myself being carried along with it — when Ned Land and Conseil appeared in the salon doorway.

My two good companions stood petrified at the sight of the wonders heaped up before their eyes.

"Where are we? Where are we?" cried the Canadian. "In the museum at Quebec?"

"If Master will allow," Conseil put in, "it looks more like the Hôtel du Sommerard!"

"My friends," I answered, motioning them in, "you're neither in Canada nor in France, but very much aboard the Nautilus, fifty meters below the surface of the sea."

"We'll have to take Master's word for it, since Master says so," Conseil replied. "But frankly, this salon is enough to astonish even a Fleming like me."

"Be astonished, my friend, and look — for a classifier of your caliber, there's plenty of work here."

I hardly needed to encourage Conseil. Bent over the display cases, the good fellow was already murmuring words in the language of the naturalists: class of Gastropods, family of Buccinoids, genus of Cowries, species Cypraea madagascariensis, and so on.

Meanwhile Ned Land, no great conchologist, was questioning me about my meeting with Captain Nemo. Had I found out who he was, where he came from, where he was going, into what depths he was dragging us? In short, a thousand questions I hadn't the time to answer.

I told him everything I knew, or rather everything I didn't know, and asked him what he'd heard or seen on his side.

"Seen nothing, heard nothing!" the Canadian replied. "I haven't even caught sight of this ship's crew. You don't suppose they're electric too, do you?"

"Electric!"

"Upon my word, you'd almost be tempted to believe it. But you, Professor Aronnax," asked Ned Land, who still had his fixed idea, "can't you tell me how many men are aboard? Ten, twenty, fifty, a hundred?"

"I couldn't tell you, Master Land. And besides, take my word for it — drop this notion of seizing the Nautilus or escaping from it, at least for now. This ship is one of the masterpieces of modern industry, and I'd have hated never to see it! Plenty of people would accept the situation we're in, if only for the chance to walk through these marvels. So keep calm, and let's try to see what's going on around us."

"See!" cried the harpooner. "But there's nothing to see, there'll never be anything to see out of this iron prison! We're sailing, we're navigating, blind..."

Ned Land was just uttering these last words when darkness fell all at once — utter, absolute darkness. The luminous ceiling went out, and so abruptly that my eyes felt a stab of pain, the same you get when you pass the other way, from deep gloom into the most dazzling light.

We stood mute, not stirring, not knowing what surprise, pleasant or unpleasant, awaited us. But a sliding sound made itself heard. It was as if panels were being worked along the flanks of the Nautilus.

"This is the end of the end!" said Ned Land.

"Order of the Hydromedusae!" murmured Conseil.

Suddenly light returned on either side of the salon, through two oblong openings. The liquid masses appeared, brilliantly lit by the electric glow. Two panes of crystal separated us from the sea. At first I shuddered at the thought that this fragile wall might shatter; but strong copper frames held it in place and gave it an all but infinite resistance.

The sea was distinctly visible for a mile in every direction around the Nautilus. What a sight! What pen could describe it? Who could paint the play of light through those transparent sheets, the softness of its gradations fading down to the ocean's lower and upper reaches?

Everyone knows how transparent the sea is. Its clarity is known to surpass that of spring water. The mineral and organic substances it holds in suspension actually increase its transparency. In certain parts of the ocean, in the Antilles, a hundred forty-five meters of water let you make out the sandy bottom with astonishing sharpness, and the penetrating power of the sun's rays seems to stop only at a depth of three hundred meters. But in this fluid medium the Nautilus was crossing, the electric brilliance was being produced within the very heart of the waves. It was no longer luminous water — it was liquid light.

If one accepts the hypothesis of Ehrenberg, who believes in a phosphorescent illumination of the ocean depths, then nature has certainly reserved for the creatures of the sea one of its most prodigious spectacles — and I could judge it here by the thousand plays of this light. On either side I had a window opening onto those unexplored abysses. The darkness of the salon set off the brightness outside, and we gazed as though this pure crystal were the glass of some immense aquarium.

The Nautilus seemed not to be moving. That was because we had no landmarks. Now and then, though, the lines of water, split by its ram, streamed past our eyes at tremendous speed.

Marveling, we leaned on our elbows before those windows, and none of us had yet broken this silence of amazement when Conseil said:

"You wanted to see, friend Ned — well, now you see!"

"Amazing! Amazing!" the Canadian kept saying — for, forgetting his rage and his plans of escape, he had fallen under an irresistible spell. "A man would come from the far ends of the earth to admire a sight like this!"

"Ah!" I exclaimed. "Now I understand this man's life! He's made himself a world apart that holds its most astonishing marvels in reserve for him!"

"But the fish?" the Canadian objected. "I don't see any fish!"

"What does it matter to you, friend Ned," Conseil answered, "since you don't know them anyway?"

"Me! A fisherman!" cried Ned Land.

And on that subject an argument broke out between the two friends, for they both knew fish — but each in a very different way.

Everyone knows that fish form the fourth and last class of the vertebrate branch. They have been very properly defined as "vertebrates with double circulation and cold blood, breathing through gills and destined to live in water." They fall into two distinct series: the series of bony fish — those whose spine is made of bony vertebrae — and the cartilaginous fish, those whose spine is made of cartilaginous vertebrae.

The Canadian may perhaps have known this distinction, but Conseil knew a great deal more, and now, bound to Ned in friendship, he couldn't allow himself to seem less learned than his friend. So he said to him:

"Friend Ned, you're a slayer of fish, a very skillful fisherman. You've caught a great number of these interesting animals. But I'd wager you don't know how they're classified."

"I do," the harpooner replied gravely. "They're classified into fish you eat and fish you don't!"

"That's a glutton's distinction," Conseil answered. "But tell me — do you know the difference between bony fish and cartilaginous fish?"

"I might, Conseil."

"And the subdivisions of those two great classes?"

"I haven't the faintest idea," the Canadian admitted.

"Well then, friend Ned, listen and remember! The bony fish are subdivided into six orders. First: the acanthopterygians, whose upper jaw is complete and mobile, and whose gills take the shape of a comb. This order includes fifteen families — that is, three-quarters of all known fish. Type: the common perch."

"Fairly good eating," said Ned Land.

"Second," Conseil went on, "the abdominals, which have their ventral fins slung under the abdomen and behind the pectorals, without being attached to the shoulder bones — an order that divides into five families and takes in the greater part of the freshwater fish. Type: the carp, the pike."

"Bah!" said the Canadian with a certain scorn. "Freshwater fish!"

"Third," said Conseil, "the subbrachians, whose ventral fins are attached beneath the pectorals and hung directly from the shoulder bones. This order contains four families. Type: plaice, dab, turbot, brill, sole, and so on."

"Excellent! Excellent!" cried the harpooner, who would consider fish only from the standpoint of the dinner table.

"Fourth," Conseil resumed, unruffled, "the apodes, with an elongated body, without ventral fins, clothed in a thick and often slimy skin — an order comprising just one family. Type: the eel, the electric eel."

"Mediocre! Mediocre!" answered Ned Land.

"Fifth," said Conseil, "the lophobranchs, which have complete and free jaws, but whose gills are formed of little tufts arranged in pairs along the branchial arches. This order counts only one family. Type: the seahorses, the sea dragons."

"Poor! Poor!" the harpooner shot back.

"Sixth and last," said Conseil, "the plectognaths, whose maxillary bone is fixed to the side of the intermaxillary that forms the jaw, and whose palatine arch is locked by suture to the skull, which makes it immovable — an order that lacks true ventral fins and is made up of two families. Types: the pufferfish, the sunfish."

"Fish fit to dishonor a stewpot!" cried the Canadian.

"Have you followed all that, friend Ned?" asked the learned Conseil.

"Not in the least, friend Conseil," the harpooner replied. "But go right on, because you're very interesting."

"As for the cartilaginous fish," Conseil continued imperturbably, "they comprise only three orders."

"So much the better," said Ned.

"First, the cyclostomes, whose jaws are fused into a single mobile ring and whose gills open through numerous holes — an order comprising only one family. Type: the lamprey."

"An acquired taste," Ned Land replied.

"Second, the selacians, with gills like those of the cyclostomes, but with a mobile lower jaw. This order, the most important of the class, includes two families. Types: the ray and the sharks."

"What!" cried Ned. "Rays and sharks in the same order! Well, friend Conseil, for the rays' sake I wouldn't advise putting them together in the same tank!"

"Third," Conseil answered, "the sturgeons, whose gills open, as usual, through a single slit fitted with a gill cover — an order that includes four genera. Type: the sturgeon."

"Ah, friend Conseil, you saved the best for last — in my opinion, anyway. And is that all?"

"Yes, my good Ned," Conseil replied, "and mind you, once you know all that, you still know nothing, for the families subdivide into genera, subgenera, species, varieties..."

"Well, friend Conseil," said the harpooner, leaning over the pane of the panel, "here come some varieties now!"

"Yes! Fish!" cried Conseil. "You'd think we were standing in front of an aquarium!"

"No," I said, "because an aquarium is only a cage, and those fish are as free as birds in the air."

"Well then, friend Conseil, name them, go on, name them!" said Ned Land.

"Me?" Conseil replied. "I'm not able to! That's Master's business!"

And indeed, the worthy fellow — a rabid classifier — was no naturalist, and I doubt he could have told a tuna from a bonito. In a word, the opposite of the Canadian, who named all these fish without a moment's hesitation.

"A triggerfish," I said.

"And a Chinese triggerfish!" answered Ned Land.

"Genus Balistes, family of the Sclerodermi, order of the Plectognaths," murmured Conseil.

Decidedly, between the two of them, Ned and Conseil would have made one distinguished naturalist.

The Canadian hadn't been mistaken. A troop of triggerfish, with compressed bodies and grainy skin, armed with a spine on the dorsal fin, were frolicking around the Nautilus, flicking the four rows of prickles that bristle along each side of their tails. Nothing could be more admirable than their covering — gray above, white below — its spots of gold glittering in the dark swirl of the waves. Among them undulated rays, like a sheet let loose to the winds, and among these I spotted, to my great joy, that Chinese ray, yellowish on its upper part, soft pink beneath the belly, and fitted with three spines behind its eye — a rare species, and one whose very existence was doubtful in the time of Lacépède, who had never seen it except in a collection of Japanese drawings.

For two hours a whole aquatic army escorted the Nautilus. Amid their games and their leaps, as they vied with one another in beauty, brilliance, and speed, I made out the green wrasse; the surmullet, marked with a double black stripe; the eleotris goby, with rounded tail, white in color and flecked with violet on the back; the Japanese mackerel, that admirable scomber of these seas, its body blue and its head silver; brilliant azurors whose name alone dispenses with any description; striped sea breams, their fins variegated in blue and yellow; banded sea breams, set off by a black band on the tail; girdled sea breams, elegantly corseted in their six sashes; snipefish, true flute-mouthed trumpetfish, some specimens of which reached a meter in length; Japanese salamanders; snake-eels of the moray kind, long six-foot serpents with small, lively eyes and a wide mouth bristling with teeth — and so on.

Our admiration held always at its highest pitch. Our exclamations never ran dry. Ned named the fish, Conseil classified them, and I stood enraptured before the liveliness of their movements and the beauty of their forms. Never before had I been given the chance to catch these animals alive and free in their natural element.

I will not list all the varieties that passed thus before our dazzled eyes, this whole collection of the seas of Japan and China. The fish came running, more numerous than birds in the air, drawn no doubt by the dazzling focus of the electric light.

Suddenly the salon filled with light again. The metal panels closed. The enchanting vision vanished. But for a long while I went on dreaming, until the moment my gaze fixed on the instruments hung along the walls. The compass still showed our heading as north-northeast, the pressure gauge indicated a pressure of five atmospheres, corresponding to a depth of fifty meters, and the electric log gave a speed of fifteen miles an hour.

I waited for Captain Nemo. But he didn't appear. The clock read five.

Ned Land and Conseil went back to their cabin. I returned to my own room. My dinner was waiting there, ready. It consisted of a turtle soup made from the most delicate hawksbills, a red mullet with white, slightly flaky flesh — its liver, prepared separately, made a delicious dish — and fillets of that meat of the emperor holocanthus, whose flavor struck me as superior to salmon's.

I spent the evening reading, writing, thinking. Then, as sleep crept over me, I stretched out on my bed of eelgrass and fell into a deep sleep, while the Nautilus slid along through the swift current of the Black River.

An Invitation by Letter

The next day, November 9, I woke only after a long twelve-hour sleep. Conseil came, as was his habit, to ask "how Master had passed the night" and to offer his services. He had left his friend the Canadian sleeping like a man who had never done anything else his whole life.

I let the good fellow chatter away to his heart's content without answering him much. My mind was on Captain Nemo's absence during our session the day before, and I hoped to see him again today.

I was soon back in my clothes of byssus. Their material had prompted more than one comment from Conseil. I explained to him that they were woven from the glossy, silky filaments that anchor the "fan mussels" to the rocks — a kind of shell very common along the shores of the Mediterranean. In the old days these threads were made into fine fabrics, stockings, gloves, for they were at once very soft and very warm. So the Nautilus's crew could clothe itself cheaply, without asking a thing of the cotton fields, the sheep, or the silkworms of the land.

Once dressed, I made my way to the grand salon. It was deserted.

I plunged into the study of those treasures of conchology heaped beneath the glass cases. I also went digging through the great herbariums, filled with the rarest marine plants, which, though dried, still kept their wonderful colors. Among these precious hydrophytes I noticed whorled cladostephus, peacock's-tail padinas, grape-leaved caulerpas, grain-bearing callithamnions, delicate scarlet-tinted ceramiums, agars spread out like fans, acetabularias resembling the caps of very flattened mushrooms, and long classed among the zoophytes — in short, a whole series of wracks and kelps.

The entire day went by without my being honored by a visit from Captain Nemo. The panels of the salon did not open. Perhaps they didn't want to let us grow jaded on these beautiful things.

The Nautilus held its course east-northeast, its speed at twelve miles, its depth between fifty and sixty meters.

The next day, November 10, the same neglect, the same solitude. I saw no one from the crew. Ned and Conseil spent the greater part of the day with me. They were astonished by the captain's inexplicable absence. Was this strange man ill? Did he mean to change his plans concerning us?

After all, as Conseil pointed out, we enjoyed complete liberty; we were fed delicately and abundantly. Our host was keeping to the terms of his agreement. We had nothing to complain of, and besides, the very strangeness of our fate held such splendid compensations in store that we had no right, as yet, to accuse him of anything.

That day I began the journal of these adventures — which is what has allowed me to recount them with the most scrupulous accuracy — and, a curious detail, I wrote it on paper made from marine eelgrass.

On November 11, early in the morning, the fresh air spreading through the interior of the Nautilus told me we had returned to the surface of the ocean to renew our supply of oxygen. I headed for the central stairway and climbed up onto the platform.

It was six o'clock. I found the weather overcast, the sea gray but calm. Scarcely a swell. Captain Nemo, whom I hoped to meet up there — would he come? I saw no one but the helmsman, penned in his glass cage. Seated on the ridge formed by the hull of the dinghy, I breathed in the salt air with delight.

Little by little the mist burned off under the sun's rays. The radiant star came flooding up over the eastern horizon. The sea caught fire beneath its gaze like a trail of gunpowder. The clouds, scattered high overhead, took on vivid, exquisitely shaded tones, and countless "cat's tongues" of cloud warned of wind for the whole day.

But what did the wind matter to a Nautilus that not even storms could frighten?

So I was admiring that joyous sunrise — so cheerful, so invigorating — when I heard someone climbing up toward the platform.

I was getting ready to greet Captain Nemo, but it was his second-in-command — whom I had already seen during the captain's first visit — who appeared. He advanced onto the platform and did not seem to notice my presence. His powerful telescope at his eyes, he scanned every point of the horizon with the closest attention. Then, this examination done, he came up to the panel and pronounced a sentence whose exact words are these. I've remembered it, because every morning it was repeated under identical conditions. It ran as follows:

"Nautron respoc lorni virch."

What it meant, I couldn't say.

These words spoken, the second-in-command went back down. I supposed the Nautilus was about to resume its underwater voyage. So I went back to the panel, and through the passageways I returned to my cabin.

Five days passed like this, with no change in the situation. Every morning I climbed up onto the platform. The same sentence was pronounced by the same man. Captain Nemo did not appear.

I had made up my mind that I would not see him again, when, on November 16, coming back into my cabin with Ned and Conseil, I found on the table a note addressed to me.

I opened it with an impatient hand. It was written in a frank, clear hand, though a little Gothic, calling to mind the German styles of lettering.

The note was worded as follows:

Professor Aronnax, aboard the Nautilus.

November 16, 1867.

Captain Nemo invites Professor Aronnax to a hunting party that will take place tomorrow morning in his forests on Crespo Island. He hopes that nothing will prevent the Professor from attending, and he will be glad to see the Professor's companions join him.

The commander of the Nautilus, Captain NEMO.

"A hunt!" cried Ned.

"And in his forests on Crespo Island!" added Conseil.

"So he does go ashore, then, this odd character of ours?" Ned Land went on.

"That seems clearly stated," I said, rereading the letter.

"Well then! We have to accept," the Canadian replied. "Once we're on solid ground, we'll figure out what to do. Besides, I won't be sorry to sink my teeth into a few slabs of fresh venison."

Without trying to reconcile the plain contradiction between Captain Nemo's evident loathing for continents and islands and his invitation to go hunting in a forest, I simply answered:

"Let's first see what this Crespo Island is."

I consulted the planisphere, and at 32°40′ north latitude and 167°50′ west longitude I found a small island, sighted in 1801 by Captain Crespo, which the old Spanish charts called Rocca de la Plata — that is, "Silver Rock." So we were about eighteen hundred miles from our starting point, and the Nautilus's slightly altered course was bringing it back toward the southeast.

I showed my companions that little rock lost in the middle of the North Pacific.

"If Captain Nemo does go ashore now and then," I told them, "he at least chooses islands that are absolutely deserted!"

Ned Land shook his head without answering; then he and Conseil left me. After a supper served to me by the mute, impassive steward, I fell asleep, though not without some uneasiness.

The next day, November 17, when I awoke, I felt that the Nautilus was completely motionless. I dressed quickly and went into the grand salon.

Captain Nemo was there. He was waiting for me; he rose, bowed, and asked whether it suited me to come along with him.

Since he made no allusion whatever to his absence over these eight days, I refrained from mentioning it, and simply replied that my companions and I were ready to follow him.

"Only, sir," I added, "I'll take the liberty of putting a question to you."

"Put it, Professor Aronnax, and if I can answer it, I will."

"Well then, Captain, how does it happen that you, who have broken off all connection with the land, own forests on Crespo Island?"

"Professor," the captain answered me, "the forests I own ask the sun for neither light nor warmth. No lions, no tigers, no panthers, no quadruped of any kind sets foot in them. They are known to me alone. They grow for me alone. They are not land forests at all, but undersea forests."

"Undersea forests!" I exclaimed.

"Yes, Professor."

"And you're offering to take me there?"

"Precisely."

"On foot?"

"On foot, and dry-shod at that."

"Hunting?"

"Hunting."

"Rifle in hand?"

"Rifle in hand."

I looked at the commander of the Nautilus in a way that was hardly flattering to him.

"Decidedly, his mind is diseased," I thought. "He's had a fit that's lasted eight days, and that's lasting still. What a shame! I liked him better strange than mad!"

This thought could be read plainly on my face, but Captain Nemo merely invited me to follow him, and I followed like a man resigned to anything.

We came to the dining room, where breakfast was already served.

"Professor Aronnax," the captain said to me, "I'll ask you to share my breakfast without ceremony. We'll talk while we eat. But though I've promised you a walk in the forest, I've made no pledge to have you come across a restaurant there. So eat like a man who probably won't dine until very late."

I did justice to the meal. It was made up of various fish and slices of holothurians — those excellent zoophytes — dressed with very appetizing seaweeds, such as Porphyria laciniata and Laurentia primafetida. The drink was clear water, to which, following the captain's example, I added a few drops of a fermented liquor, extracted, in the Kamchatkan fashion, from the alga known as "palmate Rhodymenia."

Captain Nemo ate, at first, without saying a single word. Then he said to me:

"Professor, when I proposed that you come hunt in my forests of Crespo, you thought me at odds with myself. When I told you it was a matter of undersea forests, you thought me mad. Professor, one must never judge men lightly."

"But, Captain, believe me that—"

"Kindly hear me out, and you'll see whether you have any grounds to accuse me of madness or of contradiction."

"I'm listening."

"Professor, you know as well as I do that a man can live underwater provided he carries his own supply of breathable air with him. In underwater work, the laborer, dressed in a waterproof suit with his head sealed inside a metal capsule, receives air from the outside by means of force pumps and flow regulators."

"That's the diving-suit apparatus," I said.

"Just so, but under those conditions the man isn't free. He's tethered to the pump that sends him air through a rubber hose — a veritable chain riveting him to the land — and if we had to be held to the Nautilus like that, we couldn't go far."

"And the means of being free?" I asked.

"It's to use the Rouquayrol-Denayrouze apparatus, devised by two of your countrymen, but which I've perfected for my own use, and which will let you venture into these new physiological conditions without your organs suffering the least harm. It consists of a tank of thick sheet metal, in which I store air under a pressure of fifty atmospheres. This tank is fastened to the back by straps, like a soldier's pack. Its upper part forms a box out of which the air, held in by a bellows mechanism, can escape only at its normal pressure. In the Rouquayrol apparatus as it is ordinarily used, two rubber tubes run from this box to a kind of mask that seals the operator's nose and mouth; one serves to bring in the air breathed in, the other to let out the air breathed out, and the tongue closes off one or the other according to the needs of respiration. But I, who face considerable pressures at the bottom of the seas, have had to enclose my head, like the head of a diver, in a copper sphere, and it is to this sphere that the two intake and exhaust tubes lead."

"Perfect, Captain Nemo, but the air you carry must run out fast, and once it holds no more than fifteen percent oxygen, it becomes unbreathable."

"No doubt, but as I told you, Professor Aronnax, the pumps of the Nautilus let me store it under considerable pressure, and under those conditions the tank of the apparatus can supply breathable air for nine or ten hours."

"I have no further objection to make," I replied. "I'll only ask you, Captain, how you can light your way at the bottom of the ocean."

"With the Ruhmkorff apparatus, Professor Aronnax. If the first is carried on the back, the second is fastened to the belt. It consists of a Bunsen battery that I activate not with potassium bichromate, but with sodium. An induction coil gathers the electricity produced and directs it toward a specially designed lantern. In this lantern there is a glass coil containing only a residue of carbon dioxide gas. When the apparatus is working, this gas becomes luminous, giving off a whitish, continuous light. Thus equipped, I breathe and I see."

"Captain Nemo, to every one of my objections you give such crushing answers that I no longer dare doubt. Still, though I'm quite forced to accept the Rouquayrol and Ruhmkorff apparatus, I ask leave to hold some reservations about the rifle you mean to arm me with."

"But it isn't a powder rifle," the captain replied.

"An air rifle, then?"

"No doubt. How would you have me manufacture powder aboard my ship, having neither saltpeter, nor sulfur, nor charcoal?"

"Besides," I said, "to fire underwater, in a medium eight hundred fifty-five times denser than air, you'd have to overcome a considerable resistance."

"That would be no reason not to. There exist certain guns, perfected after Fulton by the Englishmen Philip Coles and Burley, by the Frenchman Furcy, by the Italian Landi, which are fitted with a special sealing system and can fire under these conditions. But I repeat, having no powder, I've replaced it with high-pressure air, which the pumps of the Nautilus supply me in abundance."

"But this air must run out quickly."

"Well, don't I have my Rouquayrol tank, which can furnish it at need? All it takes is a suitable tap. Besides, Professor Aronnax, you'll see for yourself that during these undersea hunts one uses up no great quantity of air or of bullets."

"Still, it seems to me that in this half-darkness, in the midst of a liquid so dense compared to the atmosphere, the shots can't carry far and are hardly likely to be deadly."

"Sir, with this rifle, on the contrary, every shot is deadly, and the instant an animal is hit, however lightly, it falls as if struck by lightning."

"Why?"

"Because what this rifle fires are not ordinary bullets, but little glass capsules — invented by the Austrian chemist Leniebroek — of which I have a considerable stock. These glass capsules, sheathed in a steel casing and weighted with a lead base, are true little Leyden jars, in which electricity is forced to a very high tension. At the slightest impact they discharge, and the animal, however powerful it may be, drops dead. I'll add that these capsules are no bigger than number-four shot, and that the charge of an ordinary rifle could hold ten of them."

"I'll argue no more," I replied, rising from the table, "and I have nothing left to do but take up my rifle. Wherever you go, I'll go."

Captain Nemo led me toward the stern of the Nautilus, and, passing by the cabin of Ned and Conseil, I called my two companions, who followed us at once.

Then we came to a cell located alongside, near the engine room, in which we were to put on our walking gear.

A Walk Across the Plains

This cell was, strictly speaking, the arsenal and the changing room of the Nautilus. A dozen diving suits hung along the wall, waiting for anyone who cared to go walking.

When Ned Land laid eyes on them, he made no secret of how little he wanted to climb into one.

"But my good Ned," I said to him, "the forests of Crespo Island are nothing more than underwater forests!"

"Terrific," grumbled the harpooner, watching his dreams of fresh meat evaporate. "And you, Professor Aronnax, you're going to squeeze yourself into one of those getups?"

"I'm afraid I must, Master Ned."

"Suit yourself, Professor," he answered with a shrug. "But as for me, unless somebody forces me into it, I'll never set foot inside one of those things."

"No one is going to force you, Master Ned," said Captain Nemo.

"And Conseil means to risk it?" Ned asked.

"I go wherever Master goes," Conseil replied.

At a word from the captain, two of the crew came to help us into these heavy waterproof garments, made of seamless rubber and built to withstand tremendous pressures. They were like a suit of armor at once supple and unyielding. Each formed a pair of trousers and a jacket. The trousers ended in thick boots weighted with heavy lead soles. The fabric of the jacket was reinforced by strips of copper that plated the chest like a cuirass, shielding it against the crush of the water while leaving the lungs free to work; the sleeves ended in soft, flexible gloves that in no way hampered the movements of the hand.

There was, as you can see, a world of difference between these perfected diving suits and the shapeless contraptions — the cork breastplates, the leather overjackets, the sea-coats, the airtight chests, and the rest — that had been invented and touted in the eighteenth century.

Captain Nemo, one of his companions — a sort of Hercules who must have possessed prodigious strength — Conseil, and I were soon dressed in our diving suits. All that remained was to seat our heads inside their metal spheres. But before we came to that, I asked the captain's permission to examine the guns that had been set aside for us.

One of the Nautilus's men handed me an ordinary-looking gun whose butt, made of sheet steel and hollow inside, was of fairly large size. It served as a reservoir for compressed air, which a valve, worked by a trigger, released into the metal barrel. A magazine hollowed out within the thickness of the butt held some twenty electric bullets that, by means of a spring, loaded themselves automatically into the barrel. The moment one shot was fired, the next was ready to go.

"Captain Nemo," I said, "this weapon is perfect, and easy to handle. I ask nothing better than to try it out. But how are we to reach the bottom of the sea?"

"At this moment, Professor, the Nautilus is grounded in ten meters of water, and we have only to set out."

"But how will we get out?"

"You are about to see."

Captain Nemo thrust his head into the spherical helmet. Conseil and I did the same — though not before hearing the Canadian fling us an ironic "Happy hunting!" The top of our suits ended in a threaded copper collar onto which the metal helmet screwed. Three portholes, protected by thick glass, let us see in every direction simply by turning our heads inside the sphere. As soon as the helmet was in place, the Rouquayrol apparatus strapped to our backs began to work, and for my part I breathed easily.

With the Ruhmkorff lamp hanging at my belt and the gun in my hand, I was ready to set out. But to be honest, imprisoned in these heavy garments and nailed to the deck by my lead soles, I would have found it impossible to take a single step.

But this had been anticipated, for I felt myself being pushed into a small chamber next to the changing room. My companions, towed along in the same way, followed after me. I heard a door fitted with valves close behind us, and a deep darkness enveloped us.

After a few minutes, a sharp hissing reached my ear. I felt a distinct sensation of cold rise from my feet to my chest. Clearly, from inside the vessel someone had opened a tap and let in the water from outside, which flooded over us and soon filled the chamber. A second door, cut in the side of the Nautilus, then swung open. A dim light shone on us. An instant later, our feet were treading the floor of the sea.

And now, how could I ever retrace the impressions this walk beneath the waters left with me? Words are powerless to tell of such marvels! When the painter's own brush cannot capture the effects peculiar to the liquid element, how could the pen ever hope to reproduce them?

Captain Nemo walked ahead, and his companion followed us a few paces behind. Conseil and I kept close together, as though an exchange of words had been possible through our metal shells. Already I no longer felt the weight of my clothing, my boots, my air reservoir, or the heavy sphere in the middle of which my head rattled like an almond in its shell. Plunged in the water, all these objects lost a portion of their weight equal to that of the liquid they displaced, and I found myself the happy beneficiary of that law of physics discovered by Archimedes. I was no longer an inert mass; I had a relatively great freedom of movement.

The light, which lit up the ground to thirty feet below the surface of the ocean, astonished me by its power. The sun's rays passed easily through this watery mass and dissolved its color. I could make out objects clearly at a distance of a hundred meters. Beyond that, the depths shaded off into the fine gradations of ultramarine, then turned blue in the far distance, and faded away into a vague darkness. Truly, this water surrounding me was only a kind of air, denser than the earth's atmosphere but nearly as transparent. Above me I could see the calm surface of the sea.

We walked on fine, smooth sand, not rippled like that of the beaches, which keep the imprint of the swell. This dazzling carpet, a true mirror, hurled back the sun's rays with a startling intensity. Hence the immense glare that penetrated every molecule of the water. Will I be believed if I claim that at this depth of thirty feet I could see as if in broad daylight?

For a quarter of an hour I trod that blazing sand, strewn with an impalpable dust of seashells. The hull of the Nautilus, stretched out like a long reef, was gradually disappearing, but its beacon, once night fell in the midst of the waters, would help guide us back on board by casting its rays with perfect clearness. This is an effect hard to grasp for anyone who has only seen such whitish sheets of light on land. There, the dust with which the air is saturated gives them the look of a luminous fog; but on the sea, as beneath it, these electric beams travel with an incomparable purity.

Meanwhile we kept walking on, and the vast plain of sand seemed to stretch on without end. With my hand I parted the liquid curtains that closed again behind me, and the track of my footsteps vanished at once under the pressure of the water.

Before long, the shapes of a few objects, barely sketched in the distance, took form before my eyes. I recognized magnificent foregrounds of rocks carpeted with zoophytes of the finest kind, and I was struck at once by an effect peculiar to this element.

It was then ten in the morning. The sun's rays struck the surface of the waves at a rather oblique angle, and at the touch of their light — broken up by refraction as though through a prism — flowers, rocks, seedlings, shells, and polyps were tinged along their edges with the seven colors of the solar spectrum. It was a marvel, a feast for the eyes, this tangle of colored tones, a veritable kaleidoscope of green, yellow, orange, violet, indigo, and blue — in a word, the whole palette of a mad colorist! How I longed to share with Conseil the vivid sensations flooding into my brain, and to vie with him in cries of admiration! How I wished I knew, like Captain Nemo and his companion, how to trade my thoughts by means of agreed-upon signs! And so, for want of anything better, I talked to myself, I shouted inside the copper box that capped my head, spending on empty words perhaps more air than I ought.

Faced with this splendid spectacle, Conseil had stopped short, as I had. Plainly the worthy fellow, in the presence of these specimens of zoophytes and mollusks, was classifying, forever classifying. Polyps and echinoderms abounded on the ground. The various isis corals, the cornularians that live in isolation, tufts of virgin oculina — known long ago as "white coral" — the fungia bristling in the shape of mushrooms, the anemones clinging by their muscular disks, all made up a bed of flowers, enameled with porpitae decked in their collar of azure tentacles, with starfish that studded the sand, and with warty astrophyton — fine lace embroidered by the hands of the water-nymphs, whose festoons swayed to the faint ripples stirred up by our walking. It was a real sorrow to me to crush beneath my steps the brilliant specimens of mollusks that littered the ground by the thousand — the concentric scallops, the hammer shells, the donax clams (true leaping shells), the top shells, the red helmet shells, the angel-wing stromb shells, the sea hares, and so many other products of this inexhaustible ocean. But we had to keep walking, and on we went, while overhead there sailed troops of Portuguese men-of-war trailing their ultramarine tentacles behind them, jellyfish whose opaline or soft-pink umbrellas, fringed with a band of azure, sheltered us from the sun's rays, and pelagia panopyra that, in the dark, would have sown our path with phosphorescent gleams!

All these marvels I glimpsed within the space of a quarter of a mile, scarcely pausing, and following Captain Nemo, who beckoned me on with a gesture. Before long the nature of the ground changed. To the plain of sand there succeeded a layer of viscous mud that the Americans call "ooze," composed solely of siliceous or calcareous shells. Then we crossed a meadow of seaweed, open-sea plants that the waters had not yet torn away, and whose growth was rampant. These close-woven lawns, soft to the foot, could have rivaled the most luxurious carpets ever woven by human hands. But even as the greenery spread out beneath our steps, it did not desert our heads. A light arbor of marine plants, ranked in that exuberant family of the algae — of which more than two thousand species are known — interwove at the surface of the waters. I saw floating there long ribbons of fucus, some globular, others tubular; laurencia and cladostephus with their delicate fronds; palmate rhodymenia resembling the fans of a cactus. I noticed that the green plants kept nearer the surface of the sea, while the red ones held to a middle depth, leaving to the black or brown hydrophytes the task of forming the gardens and flower beds of the ocean's deepest layers.

These algae are truly a wonder of creation, one of the marvels of the world's flora. This one family produces at once the smallest and the largest plants on the globe. For just as forty thousand of these all-but-invisible seedlings have been counted in a space of five square millimeters, so too specimens of fucus have been gathered whose length exceeded five hundred meters.

We had left the Nautilus about an hour and a half earlier. It was nearly noon. I could tell by the perpendicular fall of the sun's rays, which no longer refracted. The magic of the colors faded away little by little, and the shades of emerald and sapphire were wiped from our firmament. We walked with a steady tread that rang out against the ground with an astonishing intensity. The faintest sounds carried with a speed the ear is not used to on land. Water, in fact, is a better conductor of sound than air, and it travels through water four times faster.

Just then the ground fell away in a marked slope. The light took on a uniform tint. We reached a depth of a hundred meters, where we came under a pressure of ten atmospheres. But my diving suit was built in such a way that I suffered nothing at all from this pressure. I felt only a certain stiffness in the joints of my fingers, and even that discomfort was not long in disappearing. As for the fatigue that ought to have come with this two-hour walk in a harness I was so little used to, there was none. My movements, helped along by the water, came with a surprising ease.

Arrived at this depth of three hundred feet, I could still perceive the sun's rays, but faintly. Their intense brilliance had given way to a reddish twilight, a halfway state between day and night. Still, we could see well enough to make our way, and there was no need yet to switch on the Ruhmkorff apparatus.

At that moment Captain Nemo stopped. He waited until I had rejoined him, and with a finger he pointed out to me some dark masses that stood out in the shadows a short distance off.

"That is the forest of Crespo Island," I thought — and I was not mistaken.

An Underwater Forest

We had reached at last the edge of this forest — surely one of the finest in all Captain Nemo's vast domain. He regarded it as his own, claiming over it the very rights the first men held in the first days of the world. And who, after all, would have disputed his ownership of this underwater estate? What bolder pioneer would ever have come, axe in hand, to clear its shadowy thickets?

This forest was made up of great tree-like plants, and the moment we passed beneath its towering arches, my eyes were struck at once by a singular arrangement of their branches — an arrangement I had never observed until then.

Not one of the grasses carpeting the ground, not one of the boughs bristling on the shrubs, crept along the bottom, or curved over, or spread out in a horizontal plane. Every one of them climbed toward the surface of the ocean. There was no filament, no ribbon, however slender, that did not hold itself straight as an iron rod. The wracks and the sea-vines developed along a rigid, perpendicular line, obeying the density of the element that had produced them. Motionless too: when I pushed them aside with my hand, these plants sprang back at once to their original position. This was the realm of the vertical.

Soon I grew used to this strange arrangement, and to the relative gloom that wrapped around us. The forest floor was strewn with sharp blocks of stone, hard to avoid. The undersea flora struck me as remarkably complete here, richer even than it would have been in the arctic or tropical zones, where its species are fewer. But for a few minutes I kept mixing up the kingdoms in spite of myself, mistaking zoophytes for water plants, animals for vegetables. And who would not have made the same mistake? Fauna and flora touch so closely in this underwater world!

I noticed that all these products of the plant kingdom held to the ground only by the shallowest of grips. Without roots, indifferent to the solid body — sand, shell, shard, or pebble — that supported them, they asked of it only a foothold, not the source of their life. These plants proceed from nothing but themselves, and the principle of their existence lies in the water that holds them up and feeds them. Most of them, instead of leaves, put out little blades of the most fanciful shapes, confined to a narrow range of colors that ran only to pink, carmine, green, olive, tawny, and brown. Here I saw again — not dried out this time, as in the Nautilus's specimens — peacock's-tail algae spread into fans that seemed to beg for a breeze, scarlet ceramium weeds, tangleweeds stretching out their young, edible shoots, thread-like sea-otters' cabbage rippling and unfurling to a height of fifteen meters, clusters of acetabularia whose stalks grow upward from the crown, and any number of other open-sea plants, all of them flowerless. "A curious anomaly," as one witty naturalist put it, "a strange element indeed, where the animal kingdom flowers and the plant kingdom does not!"

Among these various shrubs, as tall as the trees of the temperate zones, and under their damp shade, gathered real bushes of living flowers, hedges of zoophytes on which bloomed brain corals zebra-striped with twisting furrows, yellowish cup-corals with translucent tentacles, and turf-like tufts of zoanthids. And to complete the illusion, the flying gurnards darted from branch to branch like a swarm of hummingbirds, while yellow lepisacanths with bristling jaws and sharp scales, along with sea-robins and boarfish, rose up under our feet like a flock of flushed snipe.

Toward one o'clock, Captain Nemo gave the signal to halt. For my own part I was glad enough of it, and we stretched out beneath a bower of winged kelp, whose long tapering strands stood up like arrows.

That moment of rest struck me as delicious. All we lacked was the charm of conversation. But it was impossible to speak, impossible to answer. I only brought my big copper head close to Conseil's. I saw the good fellow's eyes shining with contentment, and, as a sign of his satisfaction, he wriggled inside his shell with the most comical air in the world.

After four hours of this walk, I was astonished to feel no violent hunger. What accounted for this state of my stomach, I couldn't say. But in exchange, I felt an overwhelming urge to sleep, as happens to every diver. And so my eyes soon closed behind their thick glass, and I sank into an irresistible drowsiness that the movement of walking alone had been holding off until then. Captain Nemo and his sturdy companion, stretched out in that limpid crystal, set us the example of sleep.

How long I stayed sunk in that slumber I couldn't judge; but when I woke, it seemed to me the sun was sinking toward the horizon. Captain Nemo was already on his feet, and I was beginning to stretch my limbs, when an unexpected apparition brought me abruptly upright.

A few steps off, a monstrous sea-spider, a full meter high, was watching me with its squinting eyes, poised to spring. Thick as my diving suit was — thick enough to protect me from the creature's bite — I couldn't hold back a shudder of horror. Conseil and the sailor from the Nautilus woke at that moment. Captain Nemo pointed the hideous crustacean out to his companion, who felled it at once with a blow from the butt of his rifle, and I saw the monster's horrible legs writhe in terrible convulsions.

This encounter set me thinking that other, more fearsome animals must haunt these dark depths, and that my diving suit would be no protection against their attacks. I hadn't given it a thought until then, and I resolved to stay on my guard. I supposed, moreover, that this halt marked the end of our walk; but I was wrong, and instead of turning back to the Nautilus, Captain Nemo pressed on with his daring excursion.

The ground kept dropping away, and its slope, growing steeper, led us to greater depths. It must have been around three o'clock when we reached a narrow valley, cut between high sheer walls, lying at a depth of a hundred and fifty meters. Thanks to the perfection of our equipment, we thus went ninety meters beyond the limit that nature had seemed until now to impose on the undersea excursions of man.

I say a hundred and fifty meters, though no instrument allowed me to gauge the distance. But I knew that even in the clearest seas the sun's rays could penetrate no deeper. And, sure enough, the darkness now became profound. Not an object was visible ten steps off. So I was groping my way forward when I suddenly saw a fairly vivid white light shine out. Captain Nemo had just switched on his electric apparatus. His companion did the same. Conseil and I followed their lead. By turning a screw, I established the connection between the coil and the glass spiral, and the sea, lit by our four lanterns, glowed within a radius of twenty-five meters.

Captain Nemo went on plunging deeper into the dark depths of the forest, whose shrubs were thinning out more and more. I noticed that plant life was disappearing faster than animal life. The open-sea plants had already forsaken the ground, which had turned barren, while a prodigious number of animals — zoophytes, articulates, mollusks, and fish — still swarmed over it.

As I walked, it occurred to me that the light of our Ruhmkorff apparatus was bound to draw in some of the inhabitants of these dark strata. But if any did approach us, they at least kept a distance regrettable for hunters. Several times I saw Captain Nemo stop and bring his gun to his shoulder; then, after a few moments' observation, he would straighten up and resume his march.

At last, toward four o'clock or so, this marvelous excursion came to an end. A wall of superb rock reared up before us, an imposing mass — a heaping of gigantic blocks, an enormous granite cliff hollowed with dark grottoes, but offering no practicable slope to climb. These were the coastal cliffs of Crespo Island. This was dry land.
