Captain Nemo stopped short. A gesture from him brought us all to a halt, and however much I longed to scale that rampart, I had to stop. Here the domains of Captain Nemo ended. He would not go beyond them. Past this point lay that portion of the globe he was never to set foot on again.

The return began. Captain Nemo had taken the lead of his little troop once more, moving forward without a moment's hesitation. I thought I could tell that we weren't taking the same path back to the Nautilus. This new route, very steep and therefore very trying, brought us quickly nearer the surface of the sea. Still, this return to the upper layers wasn't so sudden that the decompression came too fast — which might have brought on grave disorders in our systems and caused those internal injuries so fatal to divers. Very quickly the light reappeared and grew, and, since the sun was already low on the horizon, refraction once again fringed every object with a spectral ring.

At a depth of ten meters, we walked in the midst of a swarm of little fish of every kind, more numerous than the birds of the air, and more nimble too; but no aquatic game worth a shot had yet offered itself to our eyes.

At that moment I saw the captain's weapon snap to his shoulder and track some moving object through the bushes. The shot went off, I heard a faint whistle, and an animal dropped, struck down, a few steps away.

It was a magnificent sea otter, an enhydra, the only quadruped that lives exclusively in the sea. This otter, a meter and a half long, must have been of very great value. Its coat, chestnut-brown above and silvered below, made one of those admirable furs so sought after in the Russian and Chinese markets; the fineness and sheen of its fur guaranteed it a value of at least two thousand francs. I greatly admired this curious mammal, with its rounded head trimmed with short ears, its round eyes, its white whiskers like a cat's, its webbed and clawed feet, and its bushy tail. Hunted and harried by the fishermen, this precious carnivore is becoming extremely rare, and it has taken refuge mainly in the northern reaches of the Pacific, where its species will most likely soon die out altogether.

Captain Nemo's companion came to pick up the animal, hoisted it onto his shoulder, and we set off again.

For an hour, a plain of sand unrolled before our steps. Often it rose to within less than two meters of the surface of the water. I could then see our own image, sharply reflected, sketched upside down, and above us there appeared an identical troop, reproducing our movements and our gestures, in every point exactly like us, except that it walked head-down and feet-up.

Another effect worth noting: the passing of thick clouds that formed and vanished swiftly. But on reflection I understood that these so-called clouds were due only to the varying thickness of the long ground-swells, and I could even make out the foamy "whitecaps" that their breaking crests multiplied across the water. There was nothing, right down to the shadows of the great birds passing over our heads, whose quick grazing of the sea's surface I did not catch.

On this occasion I witnessed one of the finest shots that ever set a hunter's nerves tingling. A great bird of wide wingspan, plainly visible, came gliding closer. Captain Nemo's companion took aim and fired the instant it was only a few meters above the waves. The creature dropped, struck down, and its fall carried it within reach of the deft hunter, who seized it. It was an albatross of the finest kind, an admirable specimen of the open-sea birds.

Our march had not been interrupted by this incident. For two hours we followed sometimes sandy plains, sometimes meadows of seaweed that were very hard going. Frankly, I could go no further when I caught sight of a dim glow that broke the darkness of the waters half a mile off. It was the beacon of the Nautilus. Within twenty minutes we would be aboard, and there I would breathe easy again, for it seemed to me that my tank was now supplying only air very poor in oxygen. But I had reckoned without an encounter that delayed our arrival somewhat.

I had fallen some twenty steps behind when I saw Captain Nemo come hurrying back toward me. With his powerful hand, he pressed me down to the ground, while his companion did the same to Conseil. At first I didn't know what to make of this abrupt assault, but I was reassured to see the captain lie down beside me and stay motionless.

So there I was, stretched out on the bottom, sheltered exactly beneath a bush of seaweed, when, raising my head, I saw enormous masses go noisily past, casting off phosphorescent gleams.

My blood froze in my veins! I had recognized the formidable sharks that threatened us. It was a pair of tintorea sharks, terrible creatures with enormous tails and dull, glassy stares, that distill a phosphorescent matter through holes pierced around their snouts. Monstrous fireflies, that grind a whole man to pulp in their iron jaws! I don't know whether Conseil was busy classifying them, but for my own part I studied their silvered bellies, their fearsome maws bristling with teeth, from a rather unscientific point of view — more as a victim than as a naturalist.

Very luckily, these voracious animals see poorly. They passed without noticing us, grazing us with their brownish fins, and we escaped, as if by a miracle, a danger surely greater than meeting a tiger in the depths of a forest.

Half an hour later, guided by the electric trail, we reached the Nautilus. The outer door had been left open, and Captain Nemo closed it again the moment we were back in the first cell. Then he pressed a button. I heard the pumps working inside the vessel, I felt the water sinking around me, and in a few moments the cell was completely emptied. The inner door then opened, and we passed into the changing room.

There, our diving suits were stripped off, not without some trouble, and, thoroughly worn out, faint with hunger and sleep, I made my way back to my cabin, filled with wonder at this astonishing excursion to the bottom of the seas.

Four Thousand Leagues Under the Pacific

The next morning, November 18, I had fully recovered from the exhaustion of the day before, and I climbed up onto the platform just as the Nautilus's first officer was pronouncing his daily phrase. It occurred to me then that it must have to do with the state of the sea — or rather that it meant: "We have nothing in sight."

And indeed, the ocean was deserted. Not a sail on the horizon. The heights of Crespo Island had vanished during the night. The sea drank in all the colors of the spectrum except the blue, reflecting that in every direction and taking on a magnificent shade of indigo. A watered-silk pattern of broad bands rippled regularly across the swelling waves.

I was admiring this splendid face of the ocean when Captain Nemo appeared. He seemed not to notice my presence, and began a series of astronomical observations. Then, his work finished, he went and leaned his elbows on the housing of the beacon-lamp, and his gaze lost itself over the surface of the sea.

Meanwhile some twenty of the Nautilus's sailors — all of them vigorous, well-built men — had come up onto the platform. They had come to haul in the nets that had been trailing behind us through the night. These sailors clearly belonged to different nations, though the European type was marked in every one of them. I recognized, beyond any doubt, Irishmen, Frenchmen, a few Slavs, a Greek or a Cretan. Still, these men were sparing with their words, and among themselves they used only that strange tongue whose origin I could not so much as guess at. So I had to give up questioning them.

The nets were hauled aboard. They were a kind of trawl, like those used off the coast of Normandy — vast pouches held half-open by a floating spar and by a chain threaded through the lower meshes. These pouches, dragged along on their iron footropes, swept the ocean floor and gathered up everything in their path. That day they brought up some curious samples from these fish-rich waters: anglerfish, whose comical movements have earned them the nickname of clowns; black commersons, fitted with their antennae; wavy triggerfish ringed with red bands; crescent puffers, whose venom is extremely subtle; a few olive-colored lampreys; long-snouted grenadiers covered in silvery scales; cutlassfish, whose electric power equals that of the electric eel and the torpedo ray; scaly featherbacks with brown transverse stripes; greenish cod; several varieties of gobies; and so on — and finally a few fish of larger proportions: a jack with a prominent head, a meter long; several fine bonito mackerel splashed with blue and silver; and three magnificent tuna whose swiftness had not been enough to save them from the trawl.

I reckoned that this cast of the net brought up more than a thousand pounds of fish. It was a fine haul, but no surprise. These nets stay in tow for hours, and they cage up an entire aquatic world in their prison of twine. So we would have no shortage of provisions, and excellent ones at that — supplies the speed of the Nautilus and the lure of its electric light could renew endlessly.

These various products of the sea were at once lowered through the hatch toward the storerooms, some destined to be eaten fresh, others to be preserved.

With the fishing done and the air supply renewed, I supposed the Nautilus was about to resume its underwater excursion, and I was getting ready to go back to my cabin, when Captain Nemo turned to me and said, without any preamble:

"Look at this ocean, Professor — isn't it endowed with a life of its own? Doesn't it have its rages and its tendernesses? Yesterday it fell asleep as we did, and now here it is, waking after a peaceful night!"

Not a good morning, not a good evening! You would have thought this strange man was simply carrying on a conversation with me that had already begun.

"Look," he went on, "it wakes beneath the sun's caresses! It's about to live its daytime life all over again. It makes for a fascinating study, to follow the workings of its organism. It has a pulse, it has arteries, it has its spasms — and I say that clever man Maury was right when he discovered in it a circulation as real as the circulation of the blood in animals."

It was clear that Captain Nemo expected no answer from me, and it seemed pointless to lavish on him the "Of courses," the "Certainlys," and the "You're quite rights." He was speaking rather to himself, taking long pauses between his sentences. It was a meditation aloud.

"Yes," he said, "the ocean has a true circulation, and to bring it about, the Creator of all things had only to multiply within it heat, salt, and microscopic animals. Heat, in fact, creates different densities, which give rise to currents and countercurrents. Evaporation — nil in the hyperborean regions, very active in the equatorial zones — sets up a permanent exchange between the tropical waters and the polar waters. What's more, I have caught these currents flowing top to bottom and bottom to top, and they make up the true respiration of the ocean. I have seen the molecule of seawater, warmed at the surface, sink back down toward the depths, reach its maximum density at two degrees below zero, then, cooling further, grow lighter and rise again. At the poles you'll see the consequences of this phenomenon, and you'll understand why, by this law of provident nature, freezing can never occur anywhere but at the surface of the water!"

While Captain Nemo was finishing his sentence, I was saying to myself: "The pole! Does this bold character mean to take us all the way there?"

But the captain had fallen silent, contemplating this element he had studied so completely and so ceaselessly. Then, resuming:

"Salts," he said, "are present in the sea in considerable quantity, Professor, and if you removed all of those it holds in solution, you would make a mass of four and a half million cubic leagues, which, spread out over the globe, would form a layer more than ten meters high. And don't imagine that the presence of these salts is due to some caprice of nature. No. They make the marine waters less liable to evaporation, and keep the winds from carrying off too great a quantity of vapor — which, condensing again, would flood the temperate zones. An immense role, the role of a stabilizer in the general economy of the globe!"

Captain Nemo stopped, even stood up, took a few steps along the platform, and came back to me:

"As for the infusoria," he resumed, "as for those billions of microscopic animals, which exist by the million in a single droplet, and of which it takes eight hundred thousand to weigh a milligram — their role is no less important. They absorb the marine salts, they assimilate the solid elements of the water, and, true makers of limestone continents, they manufacture corals and madrepores! And so the drop of water, stripped of its mineral nourishment, grows lighter, rises again to the surface, absorbs there the salts left behind by evaporation, grows heavier, sinks back down, and brings the microscopic animals fresh elements to absorb. Hence a double current, ascending and descending — and always motion, always life! Life, more intense than on the continents, more exuberant, more infinite, blossoming in every part of this ocean — an element of death for man, so they have said, but an element of life for myriads of animals, and for me!"

When Captain Nemo spoke like this, he was transfigured, and he stirred in me an extraordinary emotion.

"And so," he added, "there is where true existence lies! And I could well conceive of the founding of nautical cities, of clusters of underwater houses that, like the Nautilus, would come up to breathe each morning at the surface of the seas — free cities, if ever there were any, independent towns! And yet — who knows whether some despot..."

Captain Nemo finished his sentence with a violent gesture. Then, addressing me directly, as if to drive off a grim thought:

"Professor Aronnax," he asked, "do you know the depth of the ocean?"

"I know, at least, Captain, what the principal soundings have taught us."

"Could you cite them for me, so that I might check them if need be?"

"Here are a few," I answered, "that come back to me. If I'm not mistaken, an average depth of eight thousand two hundred meters has been found in the north Atlantic, and of two thousand five hundred meters in the Mediterranean. The most remarkable soundings were taken in the south Atlantic, near the thirty-fifth parallel, and they gave twelve thousand meters, fourteen thousand and ninety-one meters, and fifteen thousand one hundred and forty-nine meters. All in all, it's estimated that if the sea bottom were leveled off, its average depth would be about seven kilometers."

"Good, Professor," Captain Nemo replied. "We'll show you better than that, I hope. As for the average depth of this part of the Pacific, I'll tell you that it is only four thousand meters."

That said, Captain Nemo made for the hatch and disappeared down the ladder. I followed him, and went back into the grand salon. The propeller began to turn at once, and the log registered a speed of twenty miles an hour.

Over the days and weeks that followed, Captain Nemo was very sparing with his visits. I saw him only at rare intervals. His first officer regularly took the ship's position, which I would find marked on the chart, so that I could trace the Nautilus's course exactly.

Conseil and Land spent long hours with me. Conseil had told his friend all the wonders of our undersea walk, and the Canadian was sorry not to have come along. But I hoped the chance to visit the oceanic forests would present itself again.

Almost every day, for a few hours, the panels of the salon would open, and our eyes never tired of probing the mysteries of the underwater world.

The Nautilus's general heading was southeast, and it kept between a hundred and a hundred and fifty meters deep. One day, though, on some whim or other, drawn down at an angle by its inclined planes, it reached the layers of water lying at two thousand meters. The thermometer read a temperature of 4.25 degrees centigrade — a temperature that, at this depth, seems common to all latitudes.

On November 26, at three in the morning, the Nautilus crossed the Tropic of Cancer at 172° longitude. On the 27th, it passed in sight of the Sandwich Islands, where the illustrious Cook met his death on February 14, 1779. By then we had covered four thousand eight hundred and sixty leagues since our starting point. That morning, when I came up onto the platform, I made out, two miles to leeward, Hawaii, the largest of the seven islands that make up this archipelago. I distinguished clearly its cultivated fringe, the various mountain chains that run parallel to the coast, and its volcanoes, dominated by Mauna Kea, which rises five thousand meters above sea level. Among other samples from these waters, the nets brought up peacock sea-fans — flattened polyps of a graceful shape, peculiar to this part of the ocean.

The Nautilus held to its southeast heading. It crossed the Equator on December 1, at 142° longitude, and on the 4th of the same month, after a swift passage marked by no incident, we came within sight of the Marquesas group. I sighted, three miles off, at 8°57′ south latitude and 139°32′ west longitude, Martin Point on Nuku Hiva, the chief island of this group, which belongs to France. I saw only the wooded mountains outlined against the horizon, for Captain Nemo did not care to close with land. There the nets brought up fine specimens of fish: dolphinfish with azure fins and gold tails, whose flesh has no rival in the world; hologymnosus, all but scaleless yet of an exquisite taste; bony-jawed cardinalfish; and yellowish frigate mackerel worthy of the bonito — all fish deserving to be entered in the ship's larder.

After leaving these charming islands under the protection of the French flag, from December 4 to 11 the Nautilus covered about two thousand miles. This stretch of sailing was marked by an encounter with an immense host of squid, curious mollusks very close kin to the cuttlefish. French fishermen call them encornets; they belong to the class of cephalopods and to the family of the dibranchiates, which also takes in the cuttlefish and the argonauts. These animals were studied with particular care by the naturalists of antiquity, and they furnished many a metaphor to the orators of the Agora, as well as an excellent dish at the tables of wealthy citizens — if we are to believe Athenaeus, a Greek physician who lived before Galen.

It was during the night of December 9 to 10 that the Nautilus met this army of mollusks, which are especially nocturnal. You could count them by the million. They were migrating from the temperate zones toward the warmer ones, following the same route as the herrings and sardines. We watched them through the thick crystal panes, swimming backward with extreme speed, propelling themselves by means of their locomotor tube, chasing fish and mollusks, eating the small ones, eaten by the big ones, and thrashing in indescribable confusion the ten arms that nature has planted on their heads like a mane of pneumatic serpents. For all its speed, the Nautilus sailed for several hours in the midst of this troop of animals, and its nets brought up an uncountable number of them, among which I recognized the nine species that d'Orbigny has classified for the Pacific Ocean.

As you can see, throughout this crossing the sea lavished its most marvelous spectacles on us without pause. It varied them endlessly. It changed its scenery and its staging for the pleasure of our eyes, and we were called not only to contemplate the works of the Creator in the midst of the liquid element, but also to penetrate the most formidable mysteries of the ocean.

During the day of December 11, I was busy reading in the grand salon. Ned Land and Conseil were watching the luminous waters through the half-open panels. The Nautilus lay motionless. Its tanks filled, it held at a depth of a thousand meters — a scarcely inhabited region of the oceans, where only the large fish made rare appearances.

Just then I was reading a charming book by Jean Macé, The Servants of the Stomach, and savoring its ingenious lessons, when Conseil broke off my reading.

"Would Master care to come here a moment?" he said in a peculiar voice.

"What is it, Conseil?"

"Let Master have a look."

I got up, went and leaned my elbows before the pane, and looked.

There, in the full glare of the electric light, an enormous blackish mass hung motionless in the midst of the waters. I studied it carefully, trying to make out the nature of this gigantic cetacean. But a thought suddenly crossed my mind.

"A ship!" I cried.

"Yes," answered the Canadian, "a disabled vessel that's gone straight to the bottom!"

Ned Land was not mistaken. We were in the presence of a ship whose cut shrouds still hung from their chainplates. Its hull seemed to be in good condition, and its wreck was at most a few hours old. Three stumps of masts, sheared off two feet above the deck, showed that the vessel, in trouble, had been forced to sacrifice its rigging. But, lying over on its side, it had filled with water, and it still listed to port. A sorry sight, this hulk lost beneath the waves — yet sorrier still the view of its deck, where a few corpses, lashed by ropes, still lay! I counted four of them — four men, one of whom stood upright at the helm — and then a woman, half emerged through the skylight of the poop deck, holding a child in her arms. This woman was young. In the vivid glare of the Nautilus's lights, I could make out her features, which the water had not yet begun to disfigure. In one last effort, she had lifted her child above her head — poor little creature, whose arms were locked around its mother's neck! The attitude of the four sailors struck me as terrifying, twisted as they were in convulsive movements, making one final effort to tear free of the ropes that bound them to the ship. Alone, calmer, his face clear and grave, his graying hair plastered to his forehead, his hand clenched on the wheel, the helmsman seemed still to be steering his shipwrecked three-master through the depths of the ocean!

What a scene! We stood mute, our hearts pounding, before this shipwreck caught in the act and, so to speak, photographed in its final minute! And already I could see, their eyes ablaze, enormous sharks advancing, drawn by this bait of human flesh!

Meanwhile the Nautilus, maneuvering, turned around the sunken ship, and for an instant I was able to read on its stern board:

Florida, Sunderland.

Vanikoro

That terrible spectacle was only the first in a whole series of maritime disasters that the Nautilus was to encounter along its course. Ever since it had begun following more frequented waters, we often caught sight of wrecked hulls rotting away between the surface and the deep, and, lower down, cannons, cannonballs, anchors, chains, and a thousand other iron objects being devoured by rust.

Meanwhile, still carried along by the Nautilus — where we lived as though cut off from the world — on December 11 we came within sight of the Pomotou archipelago, Bougainville's old "dangerous group," which stretches across a span of five hundred leagues from east-southeast to west-northwest, between 13°30′ and 23°50′ south latitude, and 125°30′ and 151°30′ west longitude, from Ducie Island to Lazareff Island. The archipelago covers an area of three hundred and seventy square leagues and is made up of some sixty clusters of islands, among which one notes the Gambier group, over which France has imposed its protectorate. These islands are coral formations. A slow but ceaseless upheaval, driven by the labor of the polyps, will one day link them all together. Then this new island will later fuse with the neighboring archipelagos, and a fifth continent will stretch from New Zealand and New Caledonia all the way to the Marquesas.

The day I laid out this theory before Captain Nemo, he answered me coldly:

"It isn't new continents the earth needs, but new men!"

The chances of its navigation had led the Nautilus straight toward Clermont-Tonnerre Island, one of the most curious of the group, discovered in 1822 by Captain Bell of the Minerve. I was able, then, to study the madrepore system to which the islands of this ocean owe their existence.

Madrepores — which one must be careful not to confuse with corals — have a tissue coated in a calcareous crust, and the variations in its structure led Monsieur Milne-Edwards, my illustrious teacher, to classify them into five sections. The tiny animalcules that secrete this coral stone live by the billions at the bottom of their cells. It is their calcareous deposits that become rocks, reefs, islets, islands. In one place they form a circular ring surrounding a lagoon or small inner lake, which openings connect with the sea. In another they take the shape of barrier reefs like those along the coasts of New Caledonia and various islands of the Pomotou. Elsewhere, as at Réunion and Mauritius, they raise up fringing reefs — high, sheer walls, alongside which the ocean plunges to considerable depths.

Skirting the sheer flanks of Clermont-Tonnerre Island by only a few cable-lengths, I marveled at the gigantic work accomplished by these microscopic laborers. These walls were specifically the work of the madrepores known as millepores, porites, astraea, and meandrina. These polyps develop above all in the churning surface layers of the sea, and so it is by their upper part that they begin these substructures, which sink little by little along with the debris of secretions that support them. Such, at least, is the theory of Monsieur Darwin, who explains the formation of atolls this way — a theory superior, in my view, to the one that gives the madrepore works a foundation of mountaintops or volcanoes submerged a few feet below sea level.

I could observe these curious walls at very close range, for the sounding line marked more than three hundred meters of depth right at their base, and our electric sheets set the brilliant limestone glittering.

Answering a question Conseil put to me about how long it took these colossal barriers to grow, I astonished him considerably by telling him that scientists reckoned that growth at one-eighth of an inch per century.

"So then," he said to me, "to raise these walls, it took...?"

"A hundred and ninety-two thousand years, my good Conseil, which stretches the biblical days out rather a lot. Besides, the formation of coal — that is, the mineralization of forests swallowed up by floods — required a far greater span of time still. But I would add that the days of the Bible are only epochs, and not the interval that passes between two sunrises, because, according to the Bible itself, the sun does not date from the first day of creation."

When the Nautilus rose back to the surface of the ocean, I could take in the whole spread of this island of Clermont-Tonnerre, low and wooded. Its madrepore rocks had plainly been made fertile by waterspouts and storms. One day some seed, torn from the neighboring lands by a hurricane, fell onto the calcareous layers, mixed with the decomposed remains of fish and sea plants that formed the vegetable soil. A coconut, driven by the waves, arrived on this new coast. The germ took root. The tree, growing tall, held back the water vapor. A stream was born. Vegetation gained ground little by little. A few animalcules, worms, insects, landed on trunks wrenched from the windward islands. Turtles came to lay their eggs. Birds nested in the young trees. In this way animal life developed, and, drawn by the greenery and the fertility, man appeared. And so were formed these islands, the immense works of microscopic animals.

Toward evening, Clermont-Tonnerre melted into the distance, and the Nautilus's course shifted noticeably. After touching the Tropic of Capricorn at the one hundred and thirty-fifth degree of longitude, it headed west-northwest, working its way back up the whole intertropical zone. Though the summer sun was lavish with its rays, we suffered no discomfort from the heat, for thirty or forty meters below the surface the temperature never rose above ten or twelve degrees.

On December 15 we left to the east the alluring Society archipelago and graceful Tahiti, the queen of the Pacific. That morning I glimpsed, a few miles to leeward, the lofty peaks of that island. Its waters supplied the tables aboard with excellent fish — mackerel, bonito, albacore, and varieties of a sea serpent called munerophis.

The Nautilus had covered eight thousand one hundred miles. Nine thousand seven hundred and twenty miles were logged when it passed between the Tonga-Tabou archipelago, where the crews of the Argo, the Port-au-Prince, and the Duke-of-Portland met their end, and the Navigators' archipelago, where Captain de Langle, La Pérouse's friend, was killed. Then it came within sight of the Viti archipelago, where the savages massacred the sailors of the Union and Captain Bureau, of Nantes, commander of the Aimable-Joséphine.

This archipelago, which extends over a span of a hundred leagues from north to south and ninety leagues from east to west, lies between 6° and 2° south latitude and 174° and 179° west longitude. It is made up of a number of islands, islets, and shoals, among which one notes the islands of Viti-Levou, Vanoua-Levou, and Kandubon.

It was Tasman who discovered this group in 1643 — the very year Torricelli invented the barometer and Louis XIV ascended the throne. I leave you to decide which of these events was of greater use to humanity. Then came Cook in 1714, d'Entrecasteaux in 1793, and finally Dumont d'Urville, in 1827, who untangled the whole geographical chaos of this archipelago. The Nautilus drew near the bay of Wailea, the scene of the terrible adventures of that Captain Dillon who was the first to shed light on the mystery of La Pérouse's shipwreck.

This bay, dredged several times over, yielded excellent oysters in abundance. We ate them immoderately, having opened them at our very table, following the precept of Seneca. These mollusks belonged to the species known as ostrea lamellosa, which is very common in Corsica. This Wailea bank must have been enormous, and certainly, were it not for the many causes of destruction, these accumulations would end up filling in the bays, since as many as two million eggs are counted in a single individual.

And if Master Ned Land had no cause to regret his gluttony on this occasion, it is because the oyster is the one dish that never brings on indigestion. Indeed, it takes no fewer than sixteen dozen of these headless mollusks to furnish the three hundred and fifteen grams of nitrogenous matter needed for one man's daily nourishment.

On December 25 the Nautilus was sailing through the middle of the New Hebrides archipelago, which Quiros discovered in 1606, Bougainville explored in 1768, and Cook gave its present name in 1773. This group is made up chiefly of nine large islands and forms a band a hundred and twenty leagues long from north-northwest to south-southeast, lying between 15° and 2° south latitude and between 164° and 168° longitude. We passed fairly close to the island of Aurou, which, at the moment of the midday observations, appeared to me as a mass of green woods crowned by a peak of great height.

That day was Christmas, and Ned Land seemed to me to miss keenly the celebration of "Christmas," the true family festival that Protestants keep with a fervor bordering on fanaticism.

I hadn't seen Captain Nemo for a week or so when, on the morning of the 27th, he came into the grand salon with the air, as always, of a man who had left you five minutes before. I was busy tracing the Nautilus's route on the planisphere. The captain came over, laid a finger on a point on the chart, and spoke this single word:

"Vanikoro."

The name was magic. It was the name of the islets on which La Pérouse's ships had been lost. I sprang to my feet.

"The Nautilus is taking us to Vanikoro?" I asked.

"Yes, Professor," the captain replied.

"And I'll be able to visit those famous islands where the Boussole and the Astrolabe were wrecked?"

"If it would please you, Professor."

"When will we reach Vanikoro?"

"We are there now, Professor."

Followed by Captain Nemo, I climbed up onto the platform, and from there my eyes swept eagerly across the horizon.

To the northeast rose two volcanic islands of unequal size, ringed by a coral reef that measured forty miles around. We were facing the island of Vanikoro proper, to which Dumont d'Urville gave the name Île de la Recherche — Search Island — and we lay directly before the little harbor of Vanou, situated at 16°4′ south latitude and 164°32′ east longitude. The land seemed carpeted in green from the beach up to the interior summits, over which towered Mount Kapogo, four hundred and seventy-six fathoms high.

The Nautilus, after clearing the outer belt of rocks through a narrow pass, found itself inside the breakers, where the sea ran thirty to forty fathoms deep. Under the verdant shade of the mangroves I made out a few natives, who showed extreme surprise at our approach. In this long, blackish body advancing at water level, did they not see some formidable sea creature they had every reason to distrust?

Just then Captain Nemo asked me what I knew of La Pérouse's shipwreck.

"What everyone knows, Captain," I answered him.

"And could you tell me what everyone knows?" he asked, in a slightly ironic tone.

"Very easily."

I recounted for him what the latest work of Dumont d'Urville had brought to light — work of which here is a very brief summary.

La Pérouse and his second-in-command, Captain de Langle, were sent by Louis XVI in 1785 to carry out a voyage of circumnavigation. They sailed aboard the corvettes the Boussole and the Astrolabe, which were never seen again.

In 1791, the French government, rightly troubled by the fate of the two corvettes, fitted out two large storeships, the Recherche and the Espérance, which left Brest on September 28 under the command of Bruni d'Entrecasteaux. Two months later, word came through the deposition of a certain Bowen, commander of the Albermale, that the wreckage of shipwrecked vessels had been seen off the coasts of New Georgia. But d'Entrecasteaux, unaware of this communication — which was uncertain enough, at any rate — steered for the Admiralty Islands, named in a report by Captain Hunter as the site of La Pérouse's shipwreck.

His searches came to nothing. The Espérance and the Recherche even passed straight in front of Vanikoro without stopping, and, all in all, the voyage was a most unhappy one, for it cost the lives of d'Entrecasteaux, two of his officers, and several sailors of his crew.

It was an old hand of the Pacific, Captain Dillon, who was the first to find indisputable traces of the shipwrecked men. On May 15, 1824, his ship, the Saint-Patrick, passed near the island of Tikopia, one of the New Hebrides. There, a lascar who came alongside in a canoe sold him a silver sword hilt bearing the imprint of characters engraved with a graver. This lascar claimed, moreover, that six years earlier, during a stay at Vanikoro, he had seen two Europeans who belonged to ships that had run aground years before on the island's reefs.

Dillon guessed that these must be La Pérouse's ships, whose disappearance had stirred the whole world. He tried to reach Vanikoro, where, according to the lascar, a great deal of wreckage from the disaster was to be found; but the winds and currents kept him from it.

Dillon returned to Calcutta. There he managed to interest the Asiatic Society and the East India Company in his discovery. A ship, which was given the name Recherche, was placed at his disposal, and he set out on January 23, 1827, accompanied by a French agent.

The Recherche, after putting in at several points in the Pacific, anchored off Vanikoro on July 7, 1827, in that same harbor of Vanou where the Nautilus was floating at this very moment.

There, it gathered up numerous remains of the wreck — iron implements, anchors, pulley straps, swivel guns, an eighteen-pound cannonball, fragments of astronomical instruments, a piece of taffrail, and a bronze bell bearing the inscription "Bazin made me," the mark of the foundry at the Brest Arsenal around 1785. Doubt was no longer possible.

Dillon, rounding out his information, stayed at the site of the disaster until the month of October. Then he left Vanikoro, headed for New Zealand, anchored at Calcutta on April 7, 1828, and returned to France, where he was very warmly received by Charles X.

But at that same time, Dumont d'Urville, without any knowledge of Dillon's work, had already set out to search elsewhere for the scene of the shipwreck. And in fact, word had come through the reports of a whaler that medals and a cross of Saint-Louis were in the hands of the natives of the Louisiade and New Caledonia.

Dumont d'Urville, commanding the Astrolabe, had therefore put to sea, and, two months after Dillon had just left Vanikoro, he was anchoring off Hobart Town. There he learned of the results Dillon had obtained, and, on top of that, he heard that a certain James Hobbs, second officer of the Union, of Calcutta, having gone ashore on an island situated at 8°18′ south latitude and 156°30′ east longitude, had noticed iron bars and red cloth in use among the natives of those parts.

Dumont d'Urville, rather perplexed, and unsure whether he ought to put any faith in these accounts carried by newspapers of little credibility, nevertheless resolved to launch himself onto Dillon's trail.

On February 10, 1828, the Astrolabe presented itself off Tikopia, took on as guide and interpreter a deserter settled on the island, made for Vanikoro, came within sight of it on February 12, skirted its reefs until the 14th, and only on the 20th anchored inside the barrier, in the harbor of Vanou.

On the 23rd, several of the officers made a circuit of the island and brought back a few unimportant pieces of debris. The natives, adopting a policy of denials and evasions, refused to lead them to the site of the disaster. This very shady conduct suggested that they had mistreated the shipwrecked men, and, indeed, they seemed to fear that Dumont d'Urville had come to avenge La Pérouse and his ill-fated companions.

Nevertheless, on the 26th, won over by gifts and understanding that they had no reprisals to fear, they led the second officer, Monsieur Jacquinot, to the scene of the wreck.

There, under three or four fathoms of water, between the Pacou and Vanou reefs, lay anchors, cannons, and pigs of iron and lead, encrusted in calcareous concretions. The Astrolabe's launch and whaleboat were directed to this spot, and, not without long and exhausting labor, their crews managed to haul up an anchor weighing eighteen hundred pounds, a cast-iron eight-pounder, a pig of lead, and two copper swivel guns.

Questioning the natives, Dumont d'Urville also learned that La Pérouse, after losing his two ships on the island's reefs, had built a smaller vessel in which he went off to wreck a second time... Where? No one knew.

The commander of the Astrolabe then had a cenotaph raised, under a clump of mangroves, to the memory of the famous navigator and his companions. It was a simple four-sided pyramid, set on a base of coral, into which went no ironwork that might tempt the greed of the natives.

Then Dumont d'Urville wished to leave; but his crews were sapped by the fevers of that unhealthy coast, and, very ill himself, he was unable to set sail until March 17.

Meanwhile the French government, fearing that Dumont d'Urville might not be aware of Dillon's work, had sent to Vanikoro the corvette the Bayonnaise, commanded by Legoarant de Tromelin, who was on station off the west coast of America. The Bayonnaise anchored off Vanikoro a few months after the Astrolabe's departure, found no new document, but established that the savages had respected La Pérouse's memorial.

Such is the substance of the account I gave Captain Nemo.

"So then," he said to me, "it's still not known where this third vessel — the one the shipwrecked men built on the island of Vanikoro — went to perish?"

"It is not known."

Captain Nemo said nothing in reply, and signaled me to follow him to the grand salon. The Nautilus sank a few meters beneath the waves, and the panels slid open.

I rushed to the window, and there, beneath the coral incrustations, sheathed in fungia, siphonules, alcyons, and caryophyllia, through myriads of charming fish — rainbow wrasse, glyphisodons, pompherids, snappers, and squirrelfish — I recognized certain remains the dredges had not been able to tear loose: iron stirrups, anchors, cannons, cannonballs, the fittings of a capstan, a stem-post — all objects come from the wrecked ships and now upholstered in living flowers.

And while I gazed at these forlorn wrecks, Captain Nemo said to me in a grave voice:

"Commander La Pérouse set out on December 7, 1785, with his ships the Boussole and the Astrolabe. He first anchored at Botany Bay, visited the Friendly archipelago and New Caledonia, made for Santa Cruz, and put in at Namouka, one of the islands of the Hapaï group. Then his ships arrived at the uncharted reefs of Vanikoro. The Boussole, which was in the lead, ran onto the southern coast. The Astrolabe came to her aid and grounded in the same way. The first ship was destroyed almost at once. The second, wedged fast to leeward, held out a few days. The natives gave the castaways a fairly good welcome. They settled on the island and built a smaller vessel out of the debris of the two large ones. A few sailors stayed at Vanikoro of their own free will.

"The rest, weakened and ill, set off with La Pérouse. They steered for the Solomon Islands, and they perished, ship and all, on the western coast of the group's main island, between Capes Deception and Satisfaction!"

"And how do you know this?" I cried.

"Here is what I found on the very site of that last wreck!"

Captain Nemo showed me a tin box, stamped with the arms of France and entirely corroded by the salt water. He opened it, and I saw a bundle of yellowed papers, still legible.

They were the very instructions of the Minister of the Navy to Commander La Pérouse, annotated in the margins in the hand of Louis XVI!

"Ah! It's a fine death for a sailor!" said Captain Nemo then. "A coral tomb is a peaceful grave, and may heaven grant that my companions and I never have any other!"

The Torres Strait

During the night of December 27th to 28th, the Nautilus left the waters of Vanikoro behind at a headlong speed. Her course was southwest, and in three days she covered the seven hundred and fifty leagues that separate the La Pérouse group from the southeastern point of Papua.

Early on the morning of January 1, 1863, Conseil joined me on the platform.

"Master," said the good fellow, "will Master permit me to wish him a happy New Year?"

"Of course, Conseil — exactly as if I were back in Paris, in my study at the Botanical Gardens. I accept your good wishes and I thank you for them. Only, I'd like to ask what you mean by 'a happy New Year,' given the circumstances we find ourselves in. Do you mean the year that will bring an end to our imprisonment, or the year that will see this strange voyage go on?"

"Well now," Conseil answered, "I hardly know what to tell Master. It's certain we're seeing curious things, and that for two months now we haven't had a moment to be bored. The latest wonder is always the most astonishing, and if this keeps up, I honestly don't know how it will all end. It's my opinion we'll never come across an opportunity like this again."

"Never, Conseil."

"Besides, Captain Nemo, who certainly lives up to his Latin name, is no more of a bother than if he didn't exist at all."

"Just as you say, Conseil."

"So I think, if Master doesn't mind my saying so, that a happy year would be one that let us see everything —"

"See everything, Conseil? That might take a while. But what does Ned Land think?"

"Ned Land thinks exactly the opposite of me," Conseil replied. "He's a practical mind and a demanding stomach. Watching fish, and forever eating them, isn't enough for him. The lack of wine, of bread, of meat hardly suits a worthy Saxon who's used to his beefsteaks, and whom a bit of brandy or gin, taken in moderation, doesn't frighten in the least!"

"For my part, Conseil, that isn't what troubles me, and I get along very well on the ship's fare."

"So do I," Conseil answered. "Which is why I think as much about staying as Master Land does about running off. So if the year that's beginning isn't a good one for me, it will be for him, and the other way around. That way there'll always be somebody satisfied. And so, to conclude, I wish Master whatever will please Master."

"Thank you, Conseil. Only I'll ask you to put off the question of New Year's gifts to a later date, and for now to accept a good firm handshake in their place. It's all I have on me."

"Master has never been so generous," Conseil replied.

And with that the good fellow went off.

By January 2 we had logged eleven thousand three hundred and forty miles — that is, five thousand two hundred and fifty leagues — since our starting point in the seas of Japan. Ahead of the Nautilus's ram stretched the dangerous waters of the Coral Sea, off the northeastern coast of Australia. Our boat ran along, a few miles off, that formidable barrier on which Cook's ships nearly came to grief on June 10, 1770. The vessel Cook was aboard struck a rock, and if she didn't sink it was thanks to this piece of luck: the chunk of coral, torn loose by the impact, stayed lodged in the gaping hull.

I would dearly have loved to visit that reef, three hundred and sixty leagues long, against which the always-heavy sea broke with a stupendous violence, like rolling thunder. But just then the Nautilus's diving planes were carrying us down to a great depth, and I could see nothing of those high coral walls. I had to content myself with the various specimens of fish our nets brought up. Among others I noticed albacore, a kind of mackerel as big as tuna, with bluish flanks striped by transverse bands that vanish when the animal dies. These fish traveled with us in shoals and furnished our table with extremely delicate flesh. We also caught a great number of green sea breams, half a decimeter long, tasting like gilthead, and flying pyrapedes, true submarine swallows that on dark nights streak the air and the water by turns with their phosphorescent gleams. Among the mollusks and zoophytes, I found in the meshes of the trawl various kinds of alcyonarians, sea urchins, hammer shells, spur shells, sundial shells, cerith shells, and sea butterflies. The flora was represented by beautiful floating seaweeds, tangleweed and giant kelp, steeped in the mucilage that oozed through their pores, and among them I gathered a magnificent Nemastoma Geliniaroide, which was filed away among the natural curiosities of the ship's museum.

Two days after crossing the Coral Sea, on January 4, we sighted the coast of Papua. On this occasion Captain Nemo informed me that he intended to reach the Indian Ocean by way of the Torres Strait. That was all he said. Ned was glad to see that this route brought him closer to European seas.

The Torres Strait is reckoned no less dangerous for the reefs that bristle across it than for the savage inhabitants who frequent its shores. It separates the great island of Papua — also called New Guinea — from New Holland.

Papua is four hundred leagues long by a hundred and thirty leagues wide, with an area of forty thousand geographical leagues. It lies in latitude between 0°19′ and 10°2′ south, and in longitude between 128°23′ and 146°15′. At noon, while the first officer was taking the sun's altitude, I caught sight of the summits of the Arfak Mountains, rising in terraces and crowned with sharp peaks.

This land, discovered in 1511 by the Portuguese Francisco Serrano, was visited in turn by Don José de Menesès in 1526, by Grijalva in 1527, by the Spanish general Alvar de Saavedra in 1528, by Juigo Ortez in 1545, by the Dutchman Shouten in 1616, by Nicolas Sruick in 1753, by Tasman, Dampier, Fumel, Carteret, Edwards, Bougainville, Cook, Forrest, and Mac Cluer, by d'Entrecasteaux in 1792, by Duperrey in 1823, and by Dumont d'Urville in 1827. "It is the heartland of the black peoples who occupy all of Malaysia," Monsieur de Rienzi has said — and I little suspected that the hazards of this passage were about to bring me face to face with the formidable Andamenese.

So the Nautilus presented herself at the mouth of the most dangerous strait on the globe — the one the boldest navigators scarcely dare to run — the strait that Luis Vaez de Torres braved coming back from the southern seas into Melanesia, and in which, in 1840, the stranded corvettes of Dumont d'Urville came within a hair of being lost with all hands. The Nautilus herself, superior to every peril of the sea, was nonetheless about to make the acquaintance of its coral reefs.

The Torres Strait is about thirty-four leagues wide, but it is choked with a countless swarm of islands, islets, breakers, and rocks that make navigating it all but impossible. Accordingly, Captain Nemo took every precaution the crossing required. The Nautilus, floating just at the surface, advanced at a moderate pace. Her propeller, like the tail of some great sea creature, beat the waves slowly.

Taking advantage of this, my two companions and I settled ourselves on the platform, which was as deserted as ever. In front of us rose the pilothouse, and unless I was much mistaken, Captain Nemo himself must be in there, steering his own Nautilus.

Before me I had the excellent charts of the Torres Strait, surveyed and drawn up by the hydrographic engineer Vincendon Dumoulin and the ensign Coupvent-Desbois — now an admiral — who were both on Dumont d'Urville's staff during his last voyage of circumnavigation. These, along with Captain King's, are the best charts for untangling the snarl of this narrow passage, and I studied them with the most scrupulous attention.

All around the Nautilus the sea boiled furiously. The current, running from southeast to northwest at a speed of two and a half miles an hour, broke over the coral heads that jutted up here and there.

"That's an ugly sea!" Ned Land said to me.

"Detestable, indeed," I answered, "and not at all the thing for a vessel like the Nautilus."

"That damned captain," the Canadian went on, "had better be very sure of his course, because I can see clumps of coral out there that would smash his hull into a thousand pieces if it so much as grazed them!"

The situation was perilous, no question — yet the Nautilus seemed to glide as if by magic through those raging reefs. She didn't follow exactly the track of the Astrolabe and the Zélée that had proved fatal to Dumont d'Urville. She bore farther north, skirted Murray Island, and came back to the southwest, toward the Cumberland passage. I thought she was going to head straight into it when, standing off again to the northwest, she made her way through a great many little-known islands and islets, toward Tound Island and the Bad Channel.

I was already wondering whether Captain Nemo, reckless to the point of madness, meant to drive his ship into that passage where Dumont d'Urville's two corvettes had run aground — when, altering course a second time and cutting straight west, he steered for Gueboroar Island.

It was then three in the afternoon. The swell broke high, the tide being nearly full. The Nautilus drew near this island, which I can still picture with its remarkable fringe of pandanus. We ranged along it at less than two miles.

Suddenly a shock threw me off my feet. The Nautilus had just struck a reef, and she lay motionless, listing slightly to port.

When I got up, I saw Captain Nemo and his first officer on the platform. They were examining the ship's position, exchanging a few words in their incomprehensible tongue.

Here is how things stood. Two miles off to starboard lay Gueboroar Island, its coastline curving from north to west like an enormous arm. To the south and east a few coral heads were already showing, laid bare by the ebb. We had run hard aground at full tide, and in one of those seas where the tides are feeble — an awkward circumstance for refloating the Nautilus. Still, the ship had suffered no damage at all, so solidly was her hull put together. But though she could neither sink nor split open, she ran a very real risk of being pinned forever on those reefs — and if so, that would be the end of Captain Nemo's underwater vessel.

I was turning all this over in my mind when the captain, cool and calm, master of himself as always, showing neither agitation nor annoyance, came up to me.

"An accident?" I asked.

"No — an incident," he answered.

"An incident," I shot back, "that may well force you to become once more a dweller on the very lands you flee!"

Captain Nemo looked at me strangely and made a negative gesture. It told me clearly enough that nothing would ever compel him to set foot on a continent again. Then he said:

"Besides, Professor Aronnax, the Nautilus is not lost. She will carry you yet through the wonders of the ocean. Our voyage is only just beginning, and I have no wish to deprive myself so soon of the honor of your company."

"All the same, Captain Nemo," I resumed, letting the ironic turn of the phrase pass, "the Nautilus has run aground at high water. Now, the tides aren't strong in the Pacific, and if you can't lighten the Nautilus — which strikes me as impossible — I don't see how she can be refloated."

"The tides aren't strong in the Pacific — you're right there, Professor," Captain Nemo replied. "But in the Torres Strait you still get a difference of a meter and a half between high and low water. Today is January 4, and in five days the moon will be full. Now, I'll be very much surprised if that obliging satellite doesn't raise these masses of water high enough, and do me a service I care to owe to no one but it alone."

With that, Captain Nemo, followed by his first officer, went back down inside the Nautilus. As for the ship, she no longer stirred, lying as motionless as if the coral polyps had already walled her in with their indestructible cement.

"Well, Professor?" said Ned Land, coming over to me after the captain had gone.

"Well, friend Ned, we'll wait quietly for the tide of the 9th, since it seems the moon will be kind enough to set us afloat again."

"Just like that?"

"Just like that."

"And this captain isn't going to drop anchors off the shoals, put the engine on the chains, and do everything he can to haul her off?"

"Since the tide will be enough!" Conseil put in simply.

The Canadian looked at Conseil, then shrugged his shoulders. It was the sailor in him talking.

"Professor," he said, "you can take my word for it when I tell you this hunk of iron will never sail again, on or under the sea. It's fit for nothing but selling by the pound. So I think the time has come to give Captain Nemo the slip."

"Friend Ned," I answered, "I don't despair of this gallant Nautilus the way you do, and in four days we'll know just where we stand on the Pacific tides. Besides, the advice to make a run for it might be timely if we were within sight of the coasts of England or Provence — but off Papua it's another matter, and there'll always be time enough to come to that extremity if the Nautilus fails to right herself, which I would regard as a grave turn of events."

"But couldn't we at least get a taste of that ground over there?" Ned Land went on. "There's an island. On the island, there are trees. Under the trees, land animals — the kind that carry cutlets and roast beef around on them, and that I'd gladly sink a few teeth into."

"Here friend Ned is right," said Conseil, "and I side with him. Couldn't Master persuade his friend Captain Nemo to set us ashore, if only so that we don't lose the habit of treading the solid parts of our planet underfoot?"

"I can ask him," I answered, "but he'll refuse."

"Let Master take the chance," said Conseil, "and we'll find out just how obliging the captain is."

To my great surprise, Captain Nemo granted the permission I asked of him, and he did it very graciously and readily, without even requiring me to promise to come back aboard. But a flight across the wilds of New Guinea would have been extremely dangerous, and I would not have advised Ned Land to attempt it. Better to be a prisoner aboard the Nautilus than to fall into the hands of the natives of Papua.

The dinghy was placed at our disposal for the next morning. I didn't try to find out whether Captain Nemo would come with us. I even supposed that no member of the crew would be assigned to us, and that Ned Land alone would be charged with handling the boat. In any case, the shore was two miles off at most, and it was mere child's play for the Canadian to steer that light craft between the lines of reefs so fatal to great ships.

The next day, January 5, the dinghy was lifted out of its housing, its decking removed, and launched into the sea from the top of the platform. Two men were enough for the operation. The oars were already in the boat, and we had only to take our places.

At eight o'clock, armed with rifles and axes, we pushed off from the Nautilus. The sea was fairly calm. A light breeze blew off the land. Conseil and I took the oars and pulled vigorously, while Ned steered through the narrow gaps the breakers left between them. The boat handled well and skimmed along swiftly.

Ned Land couldn't contain his joy. He was a prisoner escaped from his prison, and he gave hardly a thought to the fact that he would have to go back into it.

"Meat!" he kept saying. "So we're going to eat meat — and what meat! Real game! No bread, though, more's the pity! I'm not saying fish isn't a fine thing, but you mustn't overdo it, and a chunk of fresh venison, grilled over glowing coals, will be a pleasant change from our usual fare."

"Glutton!" Conseil retorted. "He's making my mouth water."

"It remains to be seen," I said, "whether these forests are full of game, and whether the game isn't of such a size that it might hunt the hunter instead."

"Fine, Professor Aronnax!" answered the Canadian, whose teeth seemed as sharp as the edge of an axe. "But I'll eat tiger — sirloin of tiger — if there's no other four-footed animal on this island."

"Friend Ned is a worry," said Conseil.

"Whatever it is," Ned Land went on, "any animal on four legs without feathers, or on two legs with them, will get a salute from my first shot."

"Good!" I said. "There go Master Land's rash ways, starting up again!"

"Don't be afraid, Professor Aronnax," the Canadian replied, "and pull hard! I don't ask twenty-five minutes to serve you up a dish of my own making."

At half past eight, the Nautilus's dinghy ran gently aground on a beach of sand, having safely cleared the coral ring that encircled the island of Gueboroar.

A Few Days Ashore

Setting foot on land made a strong impression on me. Ned Land tested the ground with his foot, as though to take possession of it. Yet it had been only two months since we had become, in Captain Nemo's phrase, the "passengers of the Nautilus" — which is to say, in plain fact, the prisoners of her commander.

In a few minutes we were within gunshot of the shore. The soil was almost entirely made of coral, but certain beds of dried-up torrents, strewn with fragments of granite, showed that the island dated from some primordial formation. The whole horizon lay hidden behind a curtain of magnificent forest. Enormous trees, some of them reaching two hundred feet, were linked one to another by garlands of vines — real natural hammocks, rocked by a light breeze. There were mimosas, ficus, casuarinas, teaks, hibiscus, pandanus, palms, all mingled in profusion, and beneath the shelter of their green vault, at the foot of their giant trunks, grew orchids, legumes, and ferns.

But without so much as a glance at these fine specimens of Papuan flora, the Canadian abandoned the pleasant for the useful. He spotted a coconut palm, knocked down a few of its fruit, split them open, and we drank their milk and ate their meat with a satisfaction that was a standing protest against the ordinary fare of the Nautilus.

"Excellent!" said Ned Land.

"Exquisite!" answered Conseil.

"And I don't imagine," said the Canadian, "that your Nemo would object to our bringing a cargo of coconuts aboard his ship?"

"I don't suppose he would," I replied, "but he'll never want to taste them!"

"His loss!" said Conseil.

"And our gain!" Ned Land shot back. "That leaves more for us."

"Just one word, Master Land," I said to the harpooner, who was getting ready to lay waste to another coconut palm. "Coconuts are a fine thing, but before we fill the dinghy with them, it seems sensible to find out whether the island doesn't produce some other substance just as useful. Fresh vegetables would be very welcome in the Nautilus's pantry."

"Master is right," answered Conseil, "and I propose we set aside three spaces in our boat — one for fruit, one for vegetables, and the third for game, of which I haven't yet glimpsed the slightest specimen."

"Conseil, we mustn't despair of anything," answered the Canadian.

"Let's press on with our excursion, then," I said, "but let's keep our eyes open. The island may look uninhabited, but it might still harbor a few individuals who'd be less particular than we are about the kind of game they'll eat!"

"Ho! ho!" went Ned Land, with a very meaningful working of his jaw.

"Really, Ned!" cried Conseil.

"On my word," the Canadian shot back, "I'm beginning to understand the charms of cannibalism!"

"Ned! Ned! What are you saying!" retorted Conseil. "You, a cannibal! Why, I'll never be safe near you again, and I share your cabin! Am I to wake up one morning half devoured?"

"Conseil, my friend, I'm very fond of you, but not fond enough to eat you when there's no need for it."

"I don't trust it," answered Conseil. "To the hunt! We absolutely must bring down some game to satisfy this cannibal, or one of these mornings Master will find nothing left but a few scraps of servant to wait on him."

While this banter passed back and forth, we made our way beneath the dark vaults of the forest, and for two hours we roamed it in every direction.

Chance served this search for edible plants exactly as we could have wished, and one of the most useful products of the tropical zones furnished us with a precious foodstuff that was missing aboard ship. I mean the breadfruit tree, very plentiful on Gueboroar Island, and I noticed there above all that seedless variety which in Malay goes by the name of "rima."

This tree stood apart from the others by a straight trunk forty feet high. Its crown, gracefully rounded and formed of large many-lobed leaves, marked it out plainly enough to a naturalist's eye as that artocarpus which has been so happily naturalized in the Mascarene Islands. Out of its mass of greenery hung large globular fruit, a decimeter across, their outer skin covered with roughnesses arranged in a hexagonal pattern — a useful plant with which nature has favored those regions that lack wheat, and which, requiring no cultivation, bears fruit for eight months of the year.

Ned Land knew these fruit well. He had already eaten them on his many voyages, and he knew how to prepare their edible flesh. So the sight of them fired his appetite, and he could hold out no longer.

"Master," he said to me, "let me die if I don't get a taste of this breadfruit paste!"

"Taste away, Ned, my friend, taste to your heart's content. We're here to run experiments — let's run them."

"It won't take long," answered the Canadian.

And, armed with a lens, he lit a fire of dead wood that crackled away merrily. Meanwhile Conseil and I picked out the best of the artocarpus fruit. Some had not yet reached a sufficient degree of ripeness, and their thick skin covered a white pulp that was not very fibrous. Others, in great number, yellowish and gelatinous, were only waiting to be gathered.

These fruit contained no pit. Conseil brought a dozen of them to Ned Land, who cut them into thick slices and laid them on a bed of coals, all the while repeating:

"You'll see, Master, how good this bread is!"

"Especially when you've gone without it for so long," said Conseil.

"It isn't even bread anymore," added the Canadian. "It's a delicate pastry. You've never eaten it, Master?"

"No, Ned."

"Well then, get ready to take in something truly succulent. If you don't come back for more, my name's not the king of harpooners!"

After a few minutes, the part of the fruit exposed to the fire was completely charred. Inside, a white paste had appeared, a sort of tender crumb whose flavor recalled that of an artichoke.

I have to admit, this bread was excellent, and I ate it with great pleasure.

"Unfortunately," I said, "such a paste won't keep fresh, so it seems pointless to lay in a store of it for the ship."

"Come now, Master!" cried Ned Land. "There you speak like a naturalist, but I'm going to act like a baker. Conseil, gather up a harvest of these fruit that we'll take back on our way home."

"And how will you prepare them?" I asked the Canadian.

"By making a fermented paste out of their pulp that will keep indefinitely without spoiling. When I want to use it, I'll bake it in the galley aboard ship, and in spite of a slightly sour flavor, you'll find it excellent."

"Then, Master Ned, I see there's nothing lacking in this bread..."

"There is, Professor," answered the Canadian. "It lacks a bit of fruit, or at the very least a few vegetables! Let's go find the fruit and the vegetables."

Once our harvest was finished, we set out to complete this "terrestrial" dinner.

Our searches were not in vain, and by around midday we had laid in an ample supply of bananas. These delicious products of the torrid zone ripen all year round, and the Malays, who have given them the name "pisang," eat them without cooking. Along with the bananas, we gathered enormous jackfruit with a very pronounced taste, savory mangoes, and pineapples of unbelievable size. But this harvest took up a large part of our time — which, for that matter, there was no cause to regret.

Conseil kept his eye on Ned the whole while. The harpooner walked ahead, and as he strolled through the forest, he gleaned with a sure hand excellent fruit that were meant to round out his stores.

"So then," Conseil asked, "you're not missing anything now, Ned my friend?"

"Hmph!" went the Canadian.

"What! You're complaining?"

"All this greenery can't make up a meal," answered Ned. "It's the end of a meal, it's dessert. But the soup? The roast?"

"Indeed," I said, "Ned promised us cutlets that are looking rather doubtful to me."

"Professor," answered the Canadian, "the hunt isn't just unfinished — it hasn't even begun. Patience! We'll manage to come across some creature of feather or fur sooner or later, and if not in this spot, then in another..."

"And if not today, then tomorrow," added Conseil, "for we mustn't wander too far. I'd even propose that we head back to the dinghy."

"What! Already!" cried Ned.

"We ought to be back before nightfall," I said.

"But what time is it, then?" asked the Canadian.

"Two o'clock, at least," answered Conseil.

"How time flies on solid ground!" sighed Master Ned Land with a note of regret.

"Let's move," answered Conseil.

So we made our way back through the forest, rounding out our harvest with a raid on some palm cabbages that had to be gathered from the tops of the trees, some small beans I recognized as the Malays' "abrou," and some yams of superior quality.

We were loaded down when we reached the dinghy. Even so, Ned Land didn't consider his supply sufficient yet. But luck was on his side. Just as we were about to board, he spotted several trees, twenty-five to thirty feet tall, belonging to the palm family. These trees, as valuable as the artocarpus, are rightly counted among the most useful products of Malaysia.

They were sago palms, plants that grow without cultivation, propagating themselves, like mulberry trees, through their shoots and their seeds.

Ned Land knew the way to handle these trees. He took up his axe, and wielding it with great vigor, he soon had two or three sago palms lying on the ground, their ripeness betrayed by the white dust that powdered their fronds.

I watched him work more with the eyes of a naturalist than with the eyes of a hungry man. He began by stripping from each trunk a band of bark an inch thick, which covered a network of long fibers knotted in an inextricable tangle, held together by a sort of gummy flour. This flour was sago, an edible substance that serves above all to feed the populations of Melanesia.

For the moment Ned Land was content to cut these trunks into pieces, as he would have done with firewood, saving for later the job of extracting the flour, straining it through a cloth to separate it from its fibrous ligaments, evaporating its moisture in the sun, and letting it harden in molds.

At last, at five in the evening, loaded down with all our riches, we left the shore of the island, and half an hour later we drew alongside the Nautilus. No one appeared at our arrival. The enormous cylinder of sheet iron seemed deserted. Once the provisions were aboard, I went down to my cabin. I found my supper waiting for me. I ate, and then I fell asleep.

The next day, January 6, nothing new aboard. Not a sound inside, not a sign of life. The dinghy had stayed alongside, in the very spot where we had left it. We resolved to return to Gueboroar Island. Ned Land hoped to have better luck than the day before from a hunter's point of view, and wanted to explore another part of the forest.

At sunrise we were on our way. The boat, borne along by the tide that ran toward land, reached the island in no time.

We landed, and thinking it best to trust the Canadian's instinct, we followed Ned Land, whose long legs threatened to leave us behind.

Ned Land worked his way up the coast toward the west, then, wading across a few torrent beds, he reached the high plain bordered by admirable forests. A few kingfishers prowled along the watercourses, but they wouldn't let us come near. Their wariness proved to me that these birds knew what to expect from bipeds of our kind, and I concluded that, even if the island wasn't inhabited, at least human beings frequented it.

After crossing a fairly lush meadow, we came to the edge of a small wood alive with the song and flight of a great many birds.

"They're still only birds," said Conseil.

"But some of them are edible!" answered the harpooner.

"Not so, Ned my friend," retorted Conseil, "for I see nothing there but ordinary parrots."

"Conseil, my friend," Ned answered gravely, "the parrot is the pheasant of a man with nothing else to eat."

"And I'll add," I said, "that this bird, properly prepared, is well worth the fork's trouble."

And in fact, beneath the thick foliage of that wood, a whole world of parrots fluttered from branch to branch, wanting only a more careful education to speak the language of men. For the moment they chattered away in the company of parakeets of every color, of solemn cockatoos that seemed to be meditating on some philosophical problem, while lories of a brilliant red flashed past like scraps of bunting carried off by the breeze, amid loud-flying hornbills, Papuan parrots painted in the finest shades of azure, and a whole variety of charming birds, though most of them barely fit to eat.

Yet one bird peculiar to these lands, one that has never crossed beyond the limits of the Aru Islands and the Papuan islands, was missing from this collection. But fate had it in store for me to admire it before long.

After passing through a copse of no great thickness, we came again upon a plain choked with brush. There I saw rising into the air some magnificent birds whose long feathers were arranged so as to oblige them to fly against the wind. Their undulating flight, the grace of their aerial curves, the shimmer of their colors, drew and charmed the eye. I had no trouble recognizing them.

"Birds of paradise!" I cried.

"Order of passerines, section of clystomores," answered Conseil.

"Family of partridges?" asked Ned Land.

"I don't think so, Master Land. Still, I'm counting on your skill to catch one of these charming products of tropical nature!"

"We'll give it a try, Professor, though I'm more used to handling the harpoon than the gun."

The Malays, who carry on a great trade in these birds with the Chinese, have various means of taking them that we couldn't make use of. Sometimes they set snares at the tops of the tall trees the birds of paradise prefer to live in. Sometimes they capture them with a sticky birdlime that paralyzes their movements. They even go so far as to poison the springs where these birds are in the habit of drinking. As for us, we were reduced to shooting them on the wing, which left us little chance of hitting them. And in fact we wasted a good part of our ammunition to no purpose.

Toward eleven in the morning we had crossed the first range of mountains that form the center of the island, and we still hadn't killed a thing. Hunger spurred us on. The hunters had counted on the yield of their hunt, and they'd been wrong. Very luckily, Conseil, to his own great surprise, brought off a double shot and secured our lunch. He knocked down a white pigeon and a wood pigeon, which, briskly plucked and hung on a skewer, roasted before a blazing fire of dead wood. While these interesting creatures cooked, Ned prepared some artocarpus fruit. Then the pigeon and the wood pigeon were devoured down to the bone and declared excellent. The nutmeg they habitually gorge on scents their flesh and makes it delicious eating.

"It's as if the fatted hens fed on truffles," said Conseil.

"And now, Ned, what are you still missing?" I asked the Canadian.

"Some four-legged game, Professor," answered Ned Land. "All these pigeons are nothing but appetizers and little amusements for the mouth. So until I've killed an animal with cutlets on it, I won't be satisfied!"

"Nor I, Ned, if I don't catch a bird of paradise."

"Let's keep hunting, then," answered Conseil, "but working our way back toward the sea. We've reached the first slopes of the mountains, and I think it's better to regain the region of the forests."

That was sensible advice, and it was followed. After an hour's march we had reached a veritable forest of sago palms. A few harmless snakes fled from beneath our feet. The birds of paradise slipped away at our approach, and I was truly despairing of ever reaching them, when Conseil, who was walking ahead, suddenly stooped, let out a cry of triumph, and came back to me carrying a magnificent bird of paradise.

"Ah! Bravo, Conseil!" I cried.

"Master is very kind," answered Conseil.

"No, no, my boy. You've pulled off a masterstroke. To take one of these birds alive, and to take it by hand!"

"If Master will examine it closely, he'll see that I deserve no great credit."

"And why is that, Conseil?"

"Because this bird is as drunk as a quail."

"Drunk?"

"Yes, Master, drunk on the nutmegs it was gobbling under the nutmeg tree where I caught it. Look, Ned my friend, look at the monstrous effects of intemperance!"

"A thousand devils!" the Canadian shot back. "For the little gin I've drunk these past two months, you've no call to hold it against me!"

Meanwhile I was examining the curious bird. Conseil was not mistaken. The bird of paradise, intoxicated by the heady juice, was reduced to helplessness. It couldn't fly. It could barely walk. But that worried me little, and I let it sleep off its nutmegs.

This bird belonged to the most beautiful of the eight species found in Papua and the neighboring islands. It was the "great emerald" bird of paradise, one of the rarest. It measured three decimeters in length. Its head was relatively small, its eyes, set near the opening of the beak, small as well. But it offered an admirable assembly of shades — beak yellow, feet and claws brown, wings hazel with crimson tips, head and back of the neck pale yellow, throat emerald, belly and breast maroon. Two horny, downy filaments rose above its tail, which was drawn out by long, very light feathers of admirable delicacy, and they completed the ensemble of this marvelous bird, which the natives have poetically named the "sun bird."

I longed dearly to be able to bring back to Paris this superb specimen of the birds of paradise, to make a gift of it to the Jardin des Plantes, which does not possess a single living one.

"Is it really so rare, then?" asked the Canadian, in the tone of a hunter who rates game very low from the artistic point of view.

"Very rare, my good companion, and above all very hard to take alive. Even dead, these birds are still the object of an important trade. So the natives have hit on the idea of manufacturing them, the way one manufactures pearls or diamonds."

"What!" cried Conseil. "People make fake birds of paradise?"

"Yes, Conseil."

"And does Master know the natives' method?"

"Perfectly. During the east monsoon, the birds of paradise shed those magnificent feathers that surround their tail, which naturalists have named the subalar feathers. It's these feathers that the counterfeiters in birds collect, and cleverly fit onto some poor parakeet previously mutilated for the purpose. Then they dye the seam, they varnish the bird, and they ship off to the museums and collectors of Europe these products of their peculiar industry."

"Good!" said Ned Land. "If it isn't the bird, it's still its feathers, and as long as the object isn't meant to be eaten, I don't see much harm in it!"

But if my own desires were satisfied by possession of this bird of paradise, those of the Canadian hunter were not yet. Luckily, toward two o'clock, Ned Land brought down a magnificent forest hog, of the kind the natives call "bari-outang." The animal came just in time to supply us with real quadruped meat, and it was warmly welcomed. Ned Land was very proud of his shot. The hog, struck by the electric bullet, had dropped stone dead.

The Canadian skinned it and gutted it cleanly, after taking out half a dozen cutlets meant to furnish a grill for the evening meal. Then the hunt resumed, and it was to be marked yet again by the exploits of Ned and Conseil.

For in fact the two friends, beating the bushes, flushed a troop of kangaroos, which fled off bounding on their elastic legs. But these animals didn't flee so fast that the electric charge couldn't stop them in their course.

"Ah, Professor!" cried Ned Land, seized with a hunter's frenzy, "what excellent game, stewed above all! What a stock of provisions for the Nautilus! Two! Three! Five down! And to think we'll devour all this meat, and those fools aboard won't get a scrap of it!"

I believe that, in his excess of joy, if the Canadian hadn't talked so much, he'd have massacred the whole herd! But he contented himself with a dozen of these interesting marsupials, which form the first order of the aplacental mammals — so Conseil informed us.
