These animals were small in size. It was one of those "kangaroo-rabbit" species that usually den in the hollows of trees, and whose speed is extreme; but if they're only middling in size, they furnish, at least, the most prized meat.

We were very pleased with the results of our hunt. Merry Ned proposed to return the next day to this enchanted island, which he meant to strip of every one of its edible quadrupeds. But he was reckoning without the events to come.

At six in the evening we had regained the beach. Our dinghy was aground in its usual spot. The Nautilus, like a long reef, emerged from the waves two miles off the shore.

Ned Land, without further delay, busied himself with the great affair of dinner. He understood this whole business of cookery admirably. The "bari-outang" cutlets, grilled over the coals, soon spread a delicious smell that perfumed the air!...

But I notice I'm following in the Canadian's footsteps. Here I am, in raptures over a grill of fresh pork! Let me be forgiven, as I forgave Master Land, and for the same reasons!

At last the dinner was excellent. Two wood pigeons rounded out this extraordinary menu. The sago paste, the artocarpus bread, a few mangoes, half a dozen pineapples, and the fermented liquor of certain coconuts put us in high spirits. I even suspect that the wits of my worthy companions were not as clear as one might have wished.

"What if we didn't go back to the Nautilus tonight?" said Conseil.

"What if we never went back?" added Ned Land.

At that moment a stone came falling at our feet and cut short the harpooner's proposal.

Captain Nemo's Thunderbolt

We had been looking toward the forest, without getting up, my hand frozen halfway to my mouth, Ned Land's completing its errand.

"A stone doesn't fall out of the sky," said Conseil, "unless it has earned the name of a meteorite."

A second stone, carefully rounded, which knocked a savory pigeon thigh clean out of Conseil's hand, lent still more weight to his observation.

All three of us on our feet, rifles to our shoulders, we were ready to answer any attack.

"Are those monkeys?" cried Ned Land.

"More or less," answered Conseil. "They're savages."

"To the boat!" I said, heading for the sea.

Retreat was the only choice, for a good twenty natives, armed with bows and slings, were emerging at the edge of a thicket that hid the horizon to our right, barely a hundred paces off.

Our dinghy lay grounded about twenty meters from us.

The savages came on, not running, but making the most hostile displays imaginable. Stones and arrows rained down.

Ned Land had refused to leave his provisions behind, and despite the imminent danger, his pig on one side, his kangaroos on the other, he made off at a fair clip.

In two minutes we were on the beach. Loading the dinghy with the provisions and weapons, shoving it into the sea, setting the two oars — all of it was the work of an instant. We hadn't gained two cable-lengths when a hundred savages, howling and gesturing, waded into the water up to their waists. I watched to see whether their appearance would draw a few of the Nautilus's men onto the platform. But no. The enormous machine, lying out in the deeper water, remained utterly deserted.

Twenty minutes later we were climbing aboard. The hatches were open. After making the dinghy fast, we went back inside the Nautilus.

I went down to the salon, from which a few chords were drifting. Captain Nemo was there, bent over his organ, lost in a musical trance.

"Captain!" I said to him.

He didn't hear me.

"Captain!" I said again, touching him with my hand.

He shuddered, and turning around:

"Ah! it's you, Professor?" he said to me. "Well! did you have good hunting? did you botanize with success?"

"Yes, Captain," I answered, "but unfortunately we've brought back a troop of bipeds whose nearness strikes me as troubling."

"What bipeds?"

"Savages."

"Savages!" answered Captain Nemo in an ironic tone. "And you're surprised, Professor, that having set foot on one of the lands of this globe, you should find savages there? Savages — where are there not any? And besides, are they any worse than the others, these people you call savages?"

"But, Captain—"

"For my part, sir, I've met them everywhere."

"Well," I answered, "if you'd rather not receive any aboard the Nautilus, you'd do well to take a few precautions."

"Set your mind at ease, Professor, there's nothing here to worry about."

"But these natives are numerous."

"How many did you count?"

"A hundred, at least."

"Professor Aronnax," answered Captain Nemo, his fingers settling back onto the keys of the organ, "if every last one of the natives of Papua were gathered on that beach, the Nautilus would have nothing to fear from their attacks!"

The captain's fingers were running over the keyboard now, and I noticed that he struck only the black keys, which gave his melodies an essentially Scottish color. Soon he had forgotten I was there, sunk in a reverie I no longer tried to break.

I went back up onto the platform. Night had already fallen, for at that low latitude the sun sets quickly and without any twilight. I could no longer make out Gueboroar Island except as a blur. But numerous fires, lit along the beach, showed that the natives had no thought of leaving it.

I stayed there alone for several hours, now musing on these natives — but without any real fear of them, for the captain's imperturbable confidence was catching — now forgetting them altogether to admire the splendors of this tropical night. My memory flew off toward France, following those zodiacal stars that would be shining over it in a few hours. The moon blazed among the constellations at the zenith. I thought then that this faithful and obliging satellite would come back the day after tomorrow, to this same spot, to lift these waters and tear the Nautilus free of its bed of coral. Toward midnight, seeing that all was quiet on the darkened waves as well as beneath the trees along the shore, I returned to my cabin and fell peacefully asleep.

The night passed without mishap. The Papuans were no doubt frightened off by the mere sight of the monster stranded in the bay, for the hatches, left open, would have offered them easy access to the interior of the Nautilus.

At six in the morning — January 8 — I went back up onto the platform. The shadows of dawn were lifting. Soon the island showed itself through the scattering mists — its beaches first, then its summits.

The natives were still there, more numerous than the day before — five or six hundred, perhaps. A few, taking advantage of the low tide, had come out onto the heads of coral, less than two cable-lengths from the Nautilus. I could make them out easily. They were true Papuans, of athletic build, men of a fine race, with broad, high foreheads, big but not flattened noses, and white teeth. Their woolly hair, dyed red, stood out against a body black and glistening like that of the Nubians. From the lobes of their ears, cut and stretched, hung strings of bone beads. These men were generally naked. Among them I noticed a few women, dressed from hip to knee in a true crinoline of grasses held up by a plant-fiber belt. Some of the chiefs had adorned their necks with a crescent and with necklaces of red and white glass beads. Almost all of them, armed with bows, arrows, and shields, carried at their shoulder a kind of net holding those rounded stones that their slings launch with such skill.

One of these chiefs, come fairly close to the Nautilus, was examining it with attention. He must have been a "mado" of high rank, for he draped himself in a mat of banana leaves, notched at the edges and set off with brilliant colors.

I could easily have brought this native down, standing as he was within short range; but I thought it better to wait for genuinely hostile moves. Between Europeans and savages, it is only right that the Europeans answer an attack and never start one.

All through the low tide these natives prowled near the Nautilus, but they did not grow loud. I heard them often repeat the word "assai," and from their gestures I understood that they were inviting me to come ashore — an invitation I felt I ought to decline.

So that day the dinghy did not leave the ship, much to the displeasure of Master Land, who could not complete his provisions. The resourceful Canadian spent his time preparing the meats and flours he had brought back from Gueboroar Island. As for the savages, they returned to shore around eleven in the morning, as soon as the heads of coral began to disappear under the rising tide. But I saw their numbers swell considerably on the beach. It seemed likely they were coming from the neighboring islands, or from Papua proper. Still, I had not glimpsed a single native canoe.

Having nothing better to do, I thought of dredging these beautiful clear waters, which showed shells, zoophytes, and sea plants in abundance. This was, moreover, the last day the Nautilus would spend in these waters — provided, that is, that it floated free on tomorrow's high tide, according to Captain Nemo's promise.

So I called Conseil, who brought me a small light dredge, rather like the ones used to fish for oysters.

"And those savages?" Conseil asked me. "With all respect to Master, they don't strike me as very wicked!"

"They're cannibals all the same, my boy."

"One can be a cannibal and a decent fellow," answered Conseil, "just as one can be a glutton and an honest man. The one doesn't rule out the other."

"Fine, Conseil, I grant you they're honest cannibals, and that they devour their prisoners honestly. But since I have no wish to be devoured, even honestly, I'll keep on my guard, for the commander of the Nautilus seems to be taking no precautions at all. And now, to work."

For two hours our dredging was actively carried on, but without bringing up anything rare. The dredge filled with Midas' ears, harp shells, melania snails, and in particular the finest hammer shells I had seen up to that day. We also took a few sea cucumbers, some pearl oysters, and a dozen little turtles that were set aside for the ship's larder.

But just when I was least expecting it, I laid my hand on a marvel — I should say on a natural deformity, very rare to come upon. Conseil had just cast the dredge, and his apparatus was coming back up loaded with various fairly ordinary shells, when all at once he saw me plunge my arm quickly into the net, draw out a shell, and let loose the cry of a conchologist — that is, the most piercing cry a human throat can produce.

"Eh! what's the matter with Master?" asked Conseil, very surprised. "Has Master been bitten?"

"No, my boy, and yet I would gladly have paid for my discovery with a finger!"

"What discovery?"

"This shell," I said, holding up the object of my triumph.

"But that's simply a porphyry olive, of the genus Oliva, order Pectinibranchia, class Gastropoda, phylum Mollusca—"

"Yes, Conseil, but instead of coiling from right to left, this olive turns from left to right!"

"Is it possible!" cried Conseil.

"Yes, my boy, it's a left-handed shell!"

"A left-handed shell!" repeated Conseil, his heart pounding.

"Look at its spire!"

"Ah! Master may believe me," said Conseil, taking the precious shell in a trembling hand, "but I've never felt an emotion like it!"

And there was reason to be moved! Everyone knows, as the naturalists have pointed out, that right-handedness is a law of nature. The stars and their satellites, in their movements of revolution and rotation, move from right to left. Man uses his right hand more often than his left, and consequently his tools and instruments, stairways, locks, watch springs, and so on, are designed to be worked from right to left. Now, nature has generally followed this same law for the coiling of her shells. They are all right-handed, with rare exceptions, and when, by some chance, their spire runs left-handed, collectors will pay for them their weight in gold.

Conseil and I were thus lost in the contemplation of our treasure, and I was already promising myself to enrich the Museum with it, when a stone, unluckily flung by a native, came and shattered the precious object in Conseil's hand.

I let out a cry of despair! Conseil threw himself on my rifle and took aim at a savage who was swinging his sling ten meters from him. I tried to stop him, but his shot went off and broke the bracelet of amulets that hung from the native's arm.

"Conseil!" I cried, "Conseil!"

"What of it! Doesn't Master see that this cannibal started the attack?"

"A shell isn't worth a man's life!" I told him.

"Ah! the wretch!" cried Conseil. "I'd sooner he had broken my shoulder!"

Conseil was sincere, but I didn't share his view. Meanwhile the situation had changed in the last few moments, and we hadn't noticed. A good twenty canoes now surrounded the Nautilus. These canoes, hollowed out of tree trunks, long, narrow, well built for speed, were kept steady by a double bamboo outrigger that floated on the surface of the water. They were handled by skillful half-naked paddlers, and I did not watch them advance without misgivings.

It was plain that these Papuans had already had dealings with Europeans, and that they knew their ships. But this long cylinder of iron stretched out in the bay, without masts, without a funnel — what were they to make of it? Nothing good, for at first they had kept a respectful distance. But seeing it motionless, they were little by little regaining confidence and trying to grow familiar with it. Now it was precisely this familiarity that had to be prevented. Our weapons, which fired without any report, could have only a middling effect on these natives, who respect nothing but noisy contrivances. Lightning without the rolling of thunder would frighten men very little, though the danger lies in the flash, not in the sound.

At that moment the canoes drew closer to the Nautilus, and a cloud of arrows came down on it.

"The devil! it's hailing," said Conseil, "and maybe a poisoned hail!"

"We must warn Captain Nemo," I said, going back in through the hatch.

I went down to the salon. I found no one there. I ventured to knock at the door that opened onto the captain's stateroom.

An "Enter" answered me. I went in, and found Captain Nemo absorbed in a calculation in which x's and other algebraic signs were not lacking.

"Am I disturbing you?" I said, out of politeness.

"Indeed you are, Professor Aronnax," the captain answered me, "but I imagine you've had serious reasons for wanting to see me?"

"Very serious ones. The natives' canoes have surrounded us, and in a few minutes we'll certainly be assailed by several hundred savages."

"Ah!" said Captain Nemo calmly. "They've come in their canoes?"

"Yes, sir."

"Well, sir, then all we need do is close the hatches."

"Precisely, and I came to tell you—"

"Nothing simpler," said Captain Nemo.

And, pressing an electric button, he transmitted an order to the crew's quarters.

"There, it's done, sir," he said to me after a few moments. "The dinghy is in place, and the hatches are closed. You don't fear, I suppose, that these gentlemen will stave in walls that the shells of your frigate couldn't dent?"

"No, Captain, but there's still one danger."

"What danger, sir?"

"It's that tomorrow, at about this same hour, we'll have to reopen the hatches to renew the air of the Nautilus—"

"No doubt about it, sir, since our vessel breathes after the manner of the cetaceans."

"Now, if at that moment the Papuans are occupying the platform, I don't see how you'll be able to keep them from coming in."

"Then, sir, you suppose they'll climb aboard?"

"I'm certain of it."

"Well, sir, let them climb aboard. I see no reason to prevent them. At bottom they're poor devils, these Papuans, and I don't want my visit to Gueboroar Island to cost the life of a single one of these unfortunate people!"

That said, I was about to withdraw; but Captain Nemo held me back and invited me to sit down beside him. He questioned me with interest about our excursions ashore, about our hunting, and seemed not to understand that craving for meat which so consumed the Canadian. Then the conversation touched on various subjects, and without being any more communicative, Captain Nemo showed himself more amiable.

Among other things, we came to speak of the Nautilus's situation, grounded as it was precisely in this strait where Dumont d'Urville had nearly been lost. Then, on that subject:

"He was one of your great seamen," the captain said to me, "one of your most intelligent navigators, that d'Urville! He's your Captain Cook, you Frenchmen. Poor, ill-fated man of science! To have braved the ice fields of the South Pole, the coral reefs of Oceania, the cannibals of the Pacific, only to perish miserably in a railway train! If that energetic man was able to reflect during the last seconds of his life, imagine what his final thoughts must have been!"

Speaking this way, Captain Nemo seemed moved, and I credit him for that emotion.

Then, chart in hand, we went back over the French navigator's labors, his voyages of circumnavigation, his twofold attempt at the South Pole that led to the discovery of the Adélie and Louis-Philippe lands, and finally his hydrographic surveys of the principal islands of Oceania.

"What your d'Urville did on the surface of the seas," Captain Nemo said to me, "I have done inside the Ocean, and more easily, more completely than he. The Astrolabe and the Zélée, tossed endlessly by the storms, could never be worth the Nautilus — a quiet study, and truly settled, in the midst of the waters!"

"Still, Captain," I said, "there's one point of resemblance between Dumont d'Urville's corvettes and the Nautilus."

"What point, sir?"

"It's that the Nautilus has run aground just as they did!"

"The Nautilus has not run aground, sir," Captain Nemo answered me coldly. "The Nautilus is made to rest on the bed of the seas, and the painful labors, the maneuvers that refloating his corvettes forced on d'Urville, I shall not undertake. The Astrolabe and the Zélée nearly perished, but my Nautilus is in no danger. Tomorrow, on the day appointed, at the hour appointed, the tide will lift it peacefully, and it will resume its voyage across the seas."

"Captain," I said, "I don't doubt—"

"Tomorrow," added Captain Nemo, rising, "tomorrow, at two forty in the afternoon, the Nautilus will float free and leave the Torres Strait without any damage."

These words spoken in a very clipped tone, Captain Nemo bowed slightly. This was his way of dismissing me, and I went back to my room.

There I found Conseil, who was eager to know the outcome of my interview with the captain.

"My boy," I answered, "when I made a show of believing that his Nautilus was threatened by the natives of Papua, the captain answered me very ironically. So I have only one thing to say: trust him, and go sleep in peace."

"Master has no need of my services?"

"No, my friend. What is Ned Land doing?"

"Begging Master's pardon," answered Conseil, "but friend Ned is cooking up a kangaroo pâté that will be a wonder!"

I was left alone; I lay down, but I slept rather badly. I could hear the noise of the savages, who were stamping on the platform and letting out deafening cries. The night passed like that, without the crew ever stirring from its usual inertia. It worried no more about the presence of these cannibals than the soldiers of an armored fort worry about the ants running over its plating.

At six in the morning I got up. The hatches had not been opened. So the air had not been renewed inside, but the tanks, charged against just such an occasion, worked in good time and released a few cubic meters of oxygen into the impoverished atmosphere of the Nautilus.

I worked in my room until noon, without having seen Captain Nemo even for a moment. No preparations for departure seemed to be under way aboard.

I waited a while longer, then made my way to the grand salon. The clock read half past two. In ten minutes the tide would reach its maximum height, and, if Captain Nemo had not made a rash promise, the Nautilus would be freed at once. Otherwise, many months would pass before it could leave its bed of coral.

But some warning shudders soon made themselves felt in the vessel's hull. I heard the calcareous roughness of the coral bottom grating along her plating.

At thirty-five minutes past two, Captain Nemo appeared in the salon.

"We're about to leave," he said.

"Ah!" I said.

"I've given the order to open the hatches."

"And the Papuans?"

"The Papuans?" answered Captain Nemo, giving a slight shrug of his shoulders.

"Won't they get inside the Nautilus?"

"And how?"

"By climbing through the hatches you've had opened."

"Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo answered calmly, "one does not enter through the hatches of the Nautilus that way, even when they are open."

I looked at the captain.

"You don't understand?" he said to me.

"Not in the least."

"Well then! come and you'll see."

I made for the central stairway. There Ned Land and Conseil, very intrigued, were watching a few of the crew open the hatches, while cries of rage and appalling howls resounded outside.

The covers were thrown back on the outside. Twenty hideous faces appeared. But the first of these natives to lay a hand on the stair rail was flung backward by some invisible force I couldn't name, and fled, letting out horrible cries and leaping about wildly.

Ten of his companions came after him. Ten met the same fate.

Conseil was in ecstasy. Ned Land, carried away by his violent instincts, rushed onto the stairway. But the moment he seized the rail with both hands, he too was thrown down.

"A thousand devils!" he cried. "I've been struck by lightning!"

That word explained everything to me. It was no longer a rail, but a metal cable, fully charged with the ship's electricity, running up to the platform. Anyone who touched it felt a tremendous shock — and that shock would have been fatal, had Captain Nemo sent the whole current of his apparatus into this conductor! One could truly say that between himself and his attackers he had stretched an electric net that no one could cross unharmed.

Meanwhile the terrified Papuans had beaten a retreat, wild with fear. And we, half laughing, were comforting and rubbing down the wretched Ned Land, who was swearing like a man possessed.

But at that instant the Nautilus, lifted by the last swells of the tide, left its bed of coral at that fortieth minute exactly fixed by the captain. Its propeller beat the waters with a majestic slowness. Its speed built up little by little, and, sailing on the surface of the Ocean, it left behind, safe and sound, the dangerous passages of the Torres Strait.

Ægri Somnia

The next day, January 10, the Nautilus resumed its course between two waters, but at a remarkable speed that I could not put at less than thirty-five miles an hour. Its propeller turned so fast that I could neither follow nor count its revolutions.

When I reflected that this marvelous electric agent, having given the Nautilus its motion, its heat, and its light, also protected the ship against attacks from outside and transformed it into a sacred ark that no profaner could touch without being struck down by lightning, my admiration knew no bounds — and from the machine it climbed at once to the engineer who had created it.

We were heading due west, and on January 11 we rounded Cape Wessel, situated at 135° longitude and 10° north latitude, which forms the eastern tip of the Gulf of Carpentaria. The reefs were still numerous, but more scattered, and marked on the chart with extreme precision. The Nautilus easily avoided the Money shoals to port and the Victoria reefs to starboard, placed at 130° longitude, along that tenth parallel we were following without deviation.

On January 13, Captain Nemo, having reached the Timor Sea, sighted the island of that name at 122° longitude. This island, whose area comes to sixteen hundred and twenty-five square leagues, is governed by rajahs. These princes call themselves sons of crocodiles — that is to say, sprung from the highest origin to which any human being can lay claim. And so their scaly ancestors swarm in the rivers of the island and are the objects of a particular veneration. People protect them, pamper them, flatter them, feed them; they offer them young girls to devour, and woe to the stranger who lays a hand on these sacred lizards.

But the Nautilus had no dealings with these ugly beasts. Timor was visible for only a moment, at noon, while the first officer took his bearings. And I caught no more than a glimpse of the little island of Rotti, part of the same group, whose women enjoy a well-established reputation for beauty in the Malay markets.

From this point, the course of the Nautilus, in latitude, bent away to the southwest. We were headed for the Indian Ocean. Where would Captain Nemo's whim carry us now? Would he sail back up toward the coasts of Asia? Would he draw near the shores of Europe? Unlikely resolutions for a man who fled the inhabited continents. Would he sail south, then? Would he go on to round the Cape of Good Hope, then Cape Horn, and push toward the Antarctic pole? Would he come back at last to his Pacific seas, where the Nautilus found its sailing easy and unhindered? The future would have to tell us.

After running along the reefs of Cartier, of Hibernia, of Seringapatam, of Scott — the last efforts of the solid element against the liquid one — by January 14 we were beyond all land. The speed of the Nautilus was strangely slackened, and, very capricious in its ways, sometimes it swam in the midst of the waters, and sometimes it floated on the surface.

During this stretch of the voyage, Captain Nemo carried out some interesting experiments on the various temperatures of the sea at different depths. Under ordinary conditions, such readings are obtained by means of fairly complicated instruments whose reports are dubious at best — whether thermometric sounding-lines, whose glass often shatters under the pressure of the water, or devices based on the varying resistance of metals to electric currents. The results obtained this way can never be checked with any real certainty. Captain Nemo, on the contrary, went to find this temperature himself, down in the depths of the sea, and his thermometer, put in contact with the various liquid strata, gave him the degree he sought immediately and reliably.

And so, whether by overloading his ballast tanks or by descending on a slant by means of his diving planes, the Nautilus reached, one after another, depths of three, four, five, seven, nine, and ten thousand meters — and the final result of these experiments was that the sea maintained a permanent temperature of four and a half degrees at a depth of a thousand meters, under every latitude.

I followed these experiments with the keenest interest. Captain Nemo brought a genuine passion to them. Often I asked myself to what end he made these observations. Was it for the good of his fellow men? That was hardly likely, for one day or another his work would perish with him in some unknown sea. Unless he meant to leave the results of his experiments to me. But that was to assume that my strange voyage would have an end — and that end I still could not see anywhere.

Be that as it may, Captain Nemo also shared with me various figures he had obtained, establishing the relative densities of the water in the principal seas of the globe. From this exchange I drew a personal lesson that had nothing to do with science.

It was during the morning of January 15. The captain, with whom I was strolling on the platform, asked me whether I knew the different densities that the waters of the sea present. I answered no, and added that science lacked rigorous observations on the subject.

"I have made them, those observations," he told me, "and I can vouch for their certainty.

"Very well," I answered, "but the Nautilus is a world apart, and the secrets of its scientists never reach the land."

"You are right, Professor," he said, after a few moments of silence. "It is a world apart. It is as foreign to the earth as the planets that accompany this globe around the sun, and the labors of the scientists of Saturn or Jupiter will never be known. Still, since chance has bound our two existences together, I can share with you the result of my observations."

"I am listening, Captain."

"You know, Professor, that seawater is denser than fresh water, but this density is not uniform. In fact, if I represent the density of fresh water by one, I find one and twenty-eight thousandths for the waters of the Atlantic, one and twenty-six thousandths for the waters of the Pacific, one and thirty thousandths for the waters of the Mediterranean —"

"Aha!" I thought. "So he ventures into the Mediterranean?"

"— one and eighteen thousandths for the waters of the Ionian Sea, and one and twenty-nine thousandths for the waters of the Adriatic."

Clearly the Nautilus did not shun the frequented seas of Europe, and I concluded from this that it would carry us back — perhaps before long — toward more civilized continents. I thought that Ned Land would learn of this detail with a very natural satisfaction.

For several days our time was taken up with experiments of every kind, bearing on the degrees of saltness of the waters at different depths, on their electrification, their coloration, their transparency — and in all these circumstances Captain Nemo displayed an ingenuity equaled only by his graciousness toward me. Then, for some days, I saw no more of him, and once again found myself as though isolated aboard his ship.

On January 16, the Nautilus seemed to fall asleep only a few meters below the surface of the waves. Its electric apparatus had stopped working, and its motionless propeller left it to drift at the mercy of the currents. I supposed the crew was busy with interior repairs, made necessary by the violence of the machine's mechanical movements.

My companions and I then witnessed a curious spectacle. The panels of the salon were open, and since the Nautilus's beacon was not lit, a vague darkness reigned in the midst of the waters.

The stormy sky, thick with heavy clouds, gave the upper layers of the ocean only an insufficient light.

I was observing the state of the sea under these conditions, and the biggest fish appeared to me only as barely sketched shadows, when the Nautilus suddenly found itself transported into full light. At first I thought the beacon had been relit, and was casting its electric brilliance through the liquid mass. I was mistaken, and after a quick look I recognized my error.

The Nautilus was floating in the midst of a phosphorescent layer that, in this darkness, became dazzling. It was produced by myriads of luminous animalcules, whose sparkle grew as they slid over the ship's metal hull. I caught, then, flashes in the midst of these luminous sheets, like streams of molten lead in a blazing furnace, or masses of metal heated white-hot — so that, by contrast, certain bright portions cast shadow in this fiery medium, from which every shadow ought to have been banished. No! this was no longer the calm radiance of our ordinary lighting. There was an uncommon vigor and movement in it. You could feel that this light was alive!

For in truth it was an infinite mass of pelagic infusoria, of miliary noctilucae, true globules of transparent jelly, furnished with a threadlike tentacle, of which as many as twenty-five thousand have been counted in thirty cubic centimeters of water. And their light was doubled again by the peculiar glow of the medusae, the starfish, the aurelia jellyfish, the date-shell pholads, and other phosphorescent zoophytes, steeped in the grease of organic matter decomposed by the sea, and perhaps in the mucus secreted by the fish.

For several hours the Nautilus floated in these gleaming waves, and our wonder grew as we watched the great sea animals frolic in them like salamanders. There, in the midst of that fire that does not burn, I saw porpoises — elegant, swift, tireless clowns of the seas — and swordfish three meters long, intelligent forerunners of hurricanes, whose formidable blades sometimes struck the salon's window. Then smaller fish appeared: varied triggerfish, leaping scomberoids, wolf-snouted filefish, and a hundred others that streaked the luminous atmosphere as they darted through it.

There was an enchantment in that dazzling spectacle! Perhaps some atmospheric condition was heightening the intensity of the phenomenon. Perhaps some storm was breaking loose on the surface of the waves. But at this depth of a few meters, the Nautilus felt nothing of its fury, and it rocked peacefully in the midst of the tranquil waters.

So we went on, endlessly charmed by some new marvel. Conseil observed and classified his zoophytes, his articulates, his mollusks, his fish. The days slipped by rapidly, and I no longer counted them. Ned, following his habit, tried to vary the fare on board. Like true snails, we had grown used to our shell, and I can affirm that it is an easy thing to become a perfect snail.

And so this existence seemed to us easy, natural, and we no longer imagined that a different sort of life existed on the surface of the terrestrial globe — when an event came to remind us of the strangeness of our situation.

On January 18, the Nautilus was at 105° longitude and 15° south latitude. The weather was threatening, the sea rough and heaving. A strong breeze was blowing from the east. The barometer, which had been falling for some days, announced an imminent battle of the elements.

I had climbed onto the platform just as the first officer was taking his measurements of hour angles. I waited, as usual, for the daily phrase to be spoken. But that day it was replaced by another phrase, no less incomprehensible. Almost at once I saw Captain Nemo appear, his eyes — fitted to a telescope — turned toward the horizon.

For a few minutes the captain stood motionless, without leaving the point framed within his lens. Then he lowered his glass and exchanged a dozen words with his first officer. The man seemed in the grip of an emotion he was vainly trying to contain. Captain Nemo, more master of himself, remained cold.

He appeared, moreover, to be raising certain objections, to which the officer replied with firm assurances. At least, that was how I understood it, from the difference in their tone and their gestures.

As for me, I had looked carefully in the direction they were observing, without seeing anything. Sky and water merged along a line of horizon of perfect clarity.

Meanwhile Captain Nemo paced from one end of the platform to the other, without looking at me — perhaps without seeing me. His step was firm, but less regular than usual. Sometimes he stopped, and, arms crossed on his chest, he watched the sea. What could he be searching for on that immense expanse? The Nautilus was then some hundreds of miles from the nearest coast.

The first officer had taken up his telescope again and was stubbornly questioning the horizon, coming and going, stamping his foot, contrasting with his chief by his nervous agitation.

In any case, this mystery would necessarily be cleared up, and before long — for on an order from Captain Nemo, the engine, increasing its propulsive power, drove the propeller into a faster rotation.

At that moment the first officer once more drew the captain's attention. Nemo broke off his pacing and pointed his telescope toward the indicated spot. He observed it a long while. For my part, seriously intrigued, I went down to the salon and brought back an excellent spyglass that I ordinarily used. Then, resting it on the cage of the beacon that jutted out at the front of the platform, I prepared to sweep the whole line of sky and sea.

But my eye had not yet been fixed to the eyepiece when the instrument was snatched violently from my hands.

I turned around. Captain Nemo stood before me — but I did not recognize him. His face was transfigured. His eye, gleaming with a somber fire, had retreated beneath his knitted brow. His teeth were half bared. His stiff body, his clenched fists, his head drawn down between his shoulders, bore witness to the violent hatred that breathed from his whole person. He did not move. My spyglass, fallen from his hand, had rolled to his feet.

Had I then, without meaning to, provoked this posture of rage? Did he imagine — this incomprehensible man — that I had surprised some secret forbidden to the guests of the Nautilus?

No! that hatred, I was not its object, for he was not looking at me, and his eye stayed obstinately fixed on that impenetrable point of the horizon.

At last Captain Nemo became master of himself again. His face, so profoundly altered, resumed its habitual calm. He addressed a few words to his first officer in the foreign language, then he turned back to me.

"Professor Aronnax," he said to me in a rather imperious tone, "I ask you to observe one of the pledges that bind you to me.

"What is it, Captain?"

"You must let yourself be confined, you and your companions, until the moment when I judge it fitting to give you back your freedom."

"You are the master," I answered, looking at him steadily. "But may I ask you a question?"

"None, sir."

At that word, I had nothing to argue, only to obey, since all resistance would have been impossible.

I went down to the cabin occupied by Ned Land and Conseil, and told them of the captain's decision. I leave it to be imagined how this news was received by the Canadian. In any case, there was no time for any explanation. Four members of the crew were waiting at the door, and they led us to that cell where we had spent our first night aboard the Nautilus.

Ned Land tried to protest, but the door closed on him for all answer.

"Will Master tell me what this means?" Conseil asked me.

I recounted to my companions what had happened. They were as astonished as I was, and no further along.

Meanwhile, I was plunged into an abyss of reflections, and the strange, apprehensive look on Captain Nemo's face would not leave my thoughts. I was incapable of linking two logical ideas together, and was losing myself in the most absurd hypotheses, when I was pulled from my mental strain by these words of Ned Land's:

"Well, look at that! Lunch is served!"

Indeed, the table had been laid. It was obvious that Captain Nemo had given this order at the same time that he had the Nautilus hurried along.

"Will Master permit me to make him a recommendation?" Conseil asked.

"Yes, my boy," I answered.

"Well then — let Master eat his lunch. It is the prudent thing, for we do not know what may happen."

"You're right, Conseil."

"Unfortunately," said Ned Land, "they've only given us the regular fare of the ship."

"Friend Ned," Conseil replied, "what would you say, then, if there had been no lunch at all?"

This reasoning cut the harpooner's grumbling short.

We sat down to table. The meal passed in near silence. I ate little. Conseil "forced himself," always out of prudence, and Ned Land, whatever his mood, did not miss a single bite. Then, lunch over, each of us settled into his corner.

At that moment the luminous globe that lit the cell went out and left us in deep darkness. Ned Land was not slow to fall asleep, and — what astonished me — Conseil too gave way to a heavy drowsiness. I was asking myself what could have brought on this imperious need for sleep in him, when I felt my own brain steeping in a thick torpor. My eyes, which I wanted to keep open, closed in spite of me. I was in the grip of a painful hallucination. Plainly, some sleeping drug had been mixed into the food we had just eaten. So prison was not enough to hide Captain Nemo's plans from us — sleep had to be added to it as well!

I heard then the panels closing again. The undulations of the sea, which had set up a slight rolling motion, ceased. Had the Nautilus, then, left the surface of the ocean? Had it gone back down into the motionless stratum of the waters?

I tried to resist sleep. It was impossible. My breathing weakened. I felt a deadly cold freeze my heavy, half-paralyzed limbs. My eyelids, true caps of lead, fell over my eyes. I could not lift them. A morbid sleep, full of hallucinations, took hold of my whole being. Then the visions faded, and left me in a total annihilation.

The Coral Kingdom

The next morning I woke with my head remarkably clear. To my great surprise, I was in my own room. My companions, no doubt, had been carried back to their cabin without noticing it any more than I had. What had happened during the night, they knew as little as I did myself, and to unravel that mystery I could count on nothing but the chances of the future.

Then I thought of leaving my room. Was I once more free, or still a prisoner? Entirely free. I opened the door, made my way along the passageways, and climbed the central staircase. The panels, shut the evening before, now stood open. I came out onto the platform.

Ned Land and Conseil were waiting for me there. I questioned them. They knew nothing. Sunk in a heavy sleep that had left them no memory at all, they had been very much astonished to find themselves back in their cabin.

As for the Nautilus, it seemed to us as calm and mysterious as ever. It floated on the surface of the waves at a moderate pace. Nothing on board appeared to have changed.

Ned Land, with those piercing eyes of his, studied the sea. It was empty. The Canadian could point to nothing new on the horizon — no sail, no land. A west wind blew loud and gusty, and long swells, their crests torn ragged by the wind, set the vessel rolling noticeably.

Once it had renewed its air, the Nautilus held to an average depth of fifteen meters, so as to be able to return quickly to the surface. This maneuver, contrary to habit, was carried out several times over the course of that day, the nineteenth of January. Each time, the first officer would climb up onto the platform, and the customary phrase would ring out through the interior of the ship.

As for Captain Nemo, he did not appear. Of the crew I saw no one but the impassive steward, who served me with his usual precision and his usual silence.

Toward two o'clock I was in the salon, busy organizing my notes, when the captain opened the door and came in. I greeted him. He returned an almost imperceptible nod, without a word to me. I went back to my work, hoping he might perhaps give me some explanation of the events that had marked the previous night. He did nothing of the sort. I watched him. His face struck me as exhausted; his reddened eyes had found no refreshment in sleep; his whole expression spoke of a deep sadness, a genuine grief. He paced back and forth, sat down and got up again, picked up a book at random and set it aside at once, consulted his instruments without taking his usual notes, and seemed unable to stay still for a single moment.

At last he came over to me and said:

"Are you a physician, Professor Aronnax?"

So little did I expect the question that I looked at him for some time without answering.

"Are you a physician?" he repeated. "Several of your colleagues studied medicine — Gratiolet, Moquin-Tandon, and others."

"That's true," I said. "I am a doctor; I served my residency in the hospitals. I practiced for several years before joining the Museum."

"Good, sir."

My answer had plainly satisfied Captain Nemo. But not knowing what he was driving at, I waited for further questions, meaning to answer as circumstances required.

"Professor Aronnax," the captain said, "would you consent to look after one of my men?"

"You have a sick man aboard?"

"Yes."

"I'm ready to follow you."

"Come."

I confess my heart was pounding. I don't know why I saw some connection between this man's illness and the events of the night before, and that mystery preoccupied me at least as much as the patient himself.

Captain Nemo led me to the after part of the Nautilus and showed me into a cabin next to the crew's quarters.

There, on a bed, lay a man of about forty, with strong features — a true Anglo-Saxon type.

I bent over him. This was not merely a sick man; this was a wounded one. His head, swathed in bloodstained cloths, rested on a doubled pillow. I unwound the bandages, and the wounded man, watching me with his wide, fixed eyes, let me do it without uttering a single complaint.

The wound was horrible. The skull, shattered by some blunt instrument, laid the brain bare, and the cerebral matter had suffered a deep bruising. Clots of blood had formed in the dissolving mass, which had taken on the color of wine dregs. There had been both contusion and concussion of the brain. The man's breathing was slow, and a few spasmodic twitches ran through the muscles of his face. Inflammation of the brain was complete, and it had brought on the paralysis of feeling and of movement.

I took the wounded man's pulse. It was intermittent. The extremities of the body were already growing cold, and I saw that death was coming on, without its seeming possible for me to hold it back. Having dressed the poor man's wound, I readjusted the bandages about his head and turned back to Captain Nemo.

"How did he get this wound?" I asked him.

"What does it matter?" the captain answered evasively. "A shock to the Nautilus broke one of the levers of the engine, and it struck this man. But your opinion of his condition?"

I hesitated to pronounce it.

"You may speak," the captain told me. "This man does not understand French."

I looked one last time at the wounded man, then answered:

"This man will be dead in two hours."

"Is there nothing that can save him?"

"Nothing."

Captain Nemo's hand clenched, and a few tears slid from those eyes I had not believed made for weeping.

For a few moments I went on watching the dying man, whose life was ebbing little by little. His pallor deepened still further under the electric glow that bathed his deathbed. I looked at that intelligent head, furrowed with premature wrinkles that misfortune, and perhaps misery, had carved there long ago. I searched for the secret of his life in the last words to escape his lips.

"You may withdraw, Professor Aronnax," said Captain Nemo.

I left the captain in the dying man's cabin and went back to my room, deeply shaken by the scene. All through the day I was troubled by grim forebodings. That night I slept badly, and between my frequently interrupted dreams I thought I heard distant sighs, like the chanting of a funeral dirge. Was it the prayer for the dead, murmured in that language I could not understand?

The next morning I went up on deck. Captain Nemo was there before me. As soon as he caught sight of me, he came over.

"Professor," he said, "would it suit you to make an underwater excursion today?"

"With my companions?" I asked.

"If they care to come."

"We are at your orders, Captain."

"Then be so good as to go and put on your diving suits."

Of the dying man, or the dead one, not a word was said. I rejoined Ned Land and Conseil and told them of Captain Nemo's proposal. Conseil hastened to accept, and this time the Canadian showed himself very willing to come along.

It was eight o'clock in the morning. By half past eight we were dressed for this new outing and fitted with the two devices for light and for breathing. The double door was opened, and, accompanied by Captain Nemo with a dozen crewmen behind him, we set foot at a depth of ten meters on the firm ground where the Nautilus rested.

A gentle slope led down to an uneven bottom some fifteen fathoms deep. This bottom was completely different from the one I had visited during my first excursion beneath the waters of the Pacific. Here there was no fine sand, no undersea meadows, no open-water forest. I recognized at once the marvelous region that Captain Nemo was that day doing us the honors of. It was the coral kingdom.

In the branch of the zoophytes and the class of the alcyonarians, one finds the order of the gorgonaceans, which contains the three groups of the gorgonians, the isidians, and the corallians. It is to this last that coral belongs — a curious substance that has been classified in turn among the mineral, vegetable, and animal kingdoms. A medicine to the ancients, a jewel to the moderns, it was not until 1694 that the Marseillais Peysonnel definitively assigned it to the animal kingdom.

Coral is a colony of tiny animals joined together on a brittle, stony framework called a polypary. These polyps have a single generating parent that produced them all by budding, and each possesses an existence of its own while sharing in the common life. It is, then, a kind of natural socialism. I was familiar with the latest work done on this strange zoophyte, which mineralizes even as it branches out like a tree — the naturalists' apt observation — and nothing could have been more fascinating to me than to visit one of these petrified forests that nature has planted at the bottom of the seas.

The Ruhmkorff lamps were switched on, and we followed a bank of coral still in the making, which, given time, will one day close off this whole portion of the Indian Ocean. The path was bordered by impenetrable thickets, tangles of shrubs covered with little star-shaped flowers whose rays were white. Only, contrary to the plants of dry land, these branchings, anchored to the rocks of the seafloor, all pointed downward from above.

The light produced a thousand charming effects as it played among these vividly colored boughs. I seemed to see those membranous, cylindrical tubes trembling under the undulation of the water. I was tempted to gather their fresh corollas, adorned with delicate tentacles, some newly opened, others scarcely born, while nimble little fish, quick-finned, brushed past them as they swam by, like flights of birds. But if my hand came near these living flowers, these animated sensitive plants, the alarm ran at once through the whole colony. The white corollas drew back into their red sheaths, the flowers vanished before my eyes, and the bush turned into a block of stony knobs.

Chance had set me there in the presence of the most precious specimens of this zoophyte. This coral was the equal of what is fished up in the Mediterranean, off the coasts of France, Italy, and Barbary. By its vivid tones it justified those poetic names the trade gives to its loveliest products — flower of blood, foam of blood. Coral sells for as much as five hundred francs a kilogram, and here the liquid depths covered the fortune of a whole world of coral-fishers. This precious material, often mingled with other polyparies, formed dense, inextricable masses called "macciota," on which I noticed some admirable specimens of pink coral.

But soon the bushes drew closer together, the tree-forms grew taller. Real petrified copses and long spans of fantastic architecture opened before our steps. Captain Nemo turned in under a dark gallery whose gentle downward slope led us to a depth of a hundred meters. The light from our glass coils produced magical effects at times, catching on the rough irregularities of these natural arches and on the hanging formations arranged like chandeliers, pricking them with points of fire. Among the coral shrubs I observed other polyps no less curious — melites, irises with jointed branches, then a few tufts of corallines, some green, some red, true algae encrusted in their calcareous salts, which the naturalists, after long debate, have definitively placed in the vegetable kingdom. But, as one thinker has remarked, "this is perhaps the very point where life obscurely lifts itself out of the sleep of stone, without yet detaching itself from that rough point of departure."

At last, after two hours of walking, we had reached a depth of about three hundred meters — that is, the extreme limit at which coral begins to form. But here it was no longer the isolated bush, nor the modest low-growing copse. It was the immense forest, the great mineral vegetation, the enormous petrified trees, joined by garlands of graceful plumarias — those vines of the sea — all decked out in shifting hues and reflections. We passed freely beneath their tall boughs, lost in the shadow of the waters, while at our feet the organ-pipe corals, the brain corals, the star corals, the mushroom corals, and the caryophylls formed a carpet of flowers strewn with dazzling gems.

What an indescribable spectacle! Ah, if only we could have shared what we felt! Why were we imprisoned under this mask of metal and glass? Why were words forbidden between one of us and another? If only we could have lived, at least, the life of those fish that people the liquid element — or better still, the life of those amphibious creatures that, for long hours at a stretch, can roam at their own whim through the double domain of the land and the waters!

Meanwhile Captain Nemo had come to a halt. My companions and I stopped our own march, and, turning around, I saw that his men were forming a semicircle about their chief. Looking more closely, I noticed that four of them carried on their shoulders an object of oblong shape.

We stood, at this spot, in the center of a vast clearing ringed by the tall tree-forms of the undersea forest. Our lamps cast over the space a kind of twilight glimmer that stretched the shadows out enormously across the ground. At the edge of the clearing the darkness closed in again, deep, and gathered only faint sparks caught here and there on the sharp ridges of the coral.

Ned Land and Conseil were beside me. We watched, and the thought came to me that I was about to witness a strange scene. Studying the ground, I saw that it was swollen at certain points into slight mounds crusted over with calcareous deposits, and laid out with a regularity that betrayed the hand of man.

In the middle of the clearing, on a pedestal of roughly heaped stones, rose a cross of coral, spreading its long arms that seemed made of petrified blood.

At a sign from Captain Nemo, one of his men stepped forward, and a few feet from the cross he began to dig a hole with a pick he unhooked from his belt.

I understood it all! This clearing was a cemetery, this hole a grave, that oblong object the body of the man who had died in the night! Captain Nemo and his people had come to bury their companion in this common resting place, at the bottom of that unreachable ocean!

No — never had my mind been so wrought upon! Never had more overwhelming ideas come flooding into my brain! I did not want to see what my eyes were seeing!

Meanwhile the grave was slowly deepening. Fish fled this way and that from their disturbed retreat. I could hear the iron of the pick ringing on the calcareous floor, striking sparks now and then as it met some flint lost at the bottom of the waters. The hole lengthened, widened, and soon it was deep enough to receive the body.

Then the bearers drew near. The body, wrapped in a cloth of white byssus, was lowered into its watery grave. Captain Nemo, his arms folded across his chest, and all the friends of the man who had loved them knelt in the attitude of prayer. My two companions and I had bowed reverently.

The grave was then covered over with the fragments torn from the ground, forming a slight rise above it.

When this was done, Captain Nemo and his men rose to their feet; then, drawing near the grave, all bent the knee once more, and all stretched out a hand in a sign of final farewell.

Then the funeral procession took the road back to the Nautilus, passing again beneath the arches of the forest, through the copses, along the coral bushes, and always climbing.

At last the ship's lights appeared. Their luminous trail guided us back to the Nautilus. By one o'clock we had returned.

As soon as I had changed my clothes, I went back up onto the platform, and, in the grip of a terrible obsession of thoughts, I sat down near the beacon.

Captain Nemo joined me. I rose and said to him:

"So, just as I foresaw, that man died in the night?"

"Yes, Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo answered.

"And he rests now beside his companions, in that coral cemetery?"

"Yes — forgotten by all, but not by us! We dig the grave, and the polyps take it upon themselves to seal our dead there for eternity!"

And burying his face abruptly in his clenched hands, the captain tried in vain to hold back a sob. Then he added:

"There lies our peaceful cemetery, a few hundred feet beneath the surface of the waves!"

"Your dead sleep there in peace, at least, Captain, beyond the reach of the sharks!"

"Yes, sir," Captain Nemo answered gravely, "beyond the reach of the sharks — and of men!"

Part Two

The Indian Ocean

Here begins the second part of this voyage under the seas. The first had ended with that moving scene in the coral cemetery, which left a deep impression on my mind. So this, then, was how Captain Nemo's whole life unfolded, in the heart of that immense sea — even his grave he had made ready in the most impenetrable of its depths. There, not one of the ocean's monsters would come to trouble the last sleep of those companions of the Nautilus, those friends, bound to one another in death as surely as in life! "Nor any man, either!" the captain had added.

Always that same wild, implacable distrust of human society!

For my part, I could no longer content myself with the guesses that satisfied Conseil. That worthy fellow persisted in seeing the commander of the Nautilus as nothing more than one of those unappreciated scholars who repay humanity's indifference with scorn — a misunderstood genius who, weary of the disappointments of the land, had been driven to take refuge in this inaccessible element where his instincts could work freely. But to my mind, that theory explained only one side of Captain Nemo.

For the mystery of that last night, when we had been chained up in sleep and a locked cell; the violent precaution the captain had taken in tearing from my eyes the telescope I was about to sweep across the horizon; the mortal wound of that crewman, caused by some inexplicable collision of the Nautilus — all of this was pushing me down a new road. No! Captain Nemo was not merely fleeing from mankind! His formidable machine served not only his instincts for freedom, but perhaps also the interests of some terrible reprisal — I could not say what.

At this moment nothing is clear to me; in this darkness I still catch only glimmers, and I must confine myself to writing, so to speak, at the dictation of events.

Besides, nothing binds us to Captain Nemo. He knows that escape from the Nautilus is impossible. We are not even prisoners on parole. No pledge of honor holds us. We are only captives, prisoners dressed up with the name of "guests" by a show of courtesy. Ned Land, however, has not given up hope of recovering his freedom. He is sure to seize the first chance that luck may offer him. And I would no doubt do the same. And yet it would not be without a kind of regret that I would carry away what the captain's generosity had allowed us to learn of the Nautilus's secrets. For, in the end, must one hate this man or admire him? Is he a victim or an executioner? And then, to be honest, I would like — before I leave him forever — I would like to have completed this underwater tour of the world, whose opening chapters are so magnificent. I would like to have observed the complete series of wonders heaped up beneath the seas of the globe. I would like to have seen what no man has yet seen, even if I had to pay with my life for this insatiable need to learn! What have I discovered so far? Nothing, or next to nothing, since we have crossed only six thousand leagues of the Pacific!

And yet I know very well that the Nautilus is drawing near to inhabited lands, and that if some chance of deliverance offered itself to us, it would be cruel to sacrifice my companions to my passion for the unknown. I would have to follow them — perhaps even to lead them. But would that chance ever come? The man robbed by force of his free will longs for it, that chance; but the scholar, the curious mind, dreads it.

That day, January 21, 1868, at noon, the first officer came to take the sun's altitude. I climbed onto the platform, lit a cigar, and watched him at work. It seemed clear to me that this man did not understand French, for several times I made remarks aloud that ought to have drawn from him some involuntary sign of attention, had he understood them; but he remained impassive and silent.

While he was taking his sightings with the sextant, one of the Nautilus's sailors — that powerful man who had gone with us on our first underwater excursion to Crespo Island — came to clean the glass of the beacon. I studied then the arrangement of this apparatus, whose power was multiplied a hundredfold by lens-shaped rings set like those of a lighthouse, which kept its light trained in the useful plane. The electric lamp was designed to yield its full illuminating power. Its light, in fact, was produced in a vacuum, which ensured both its steadiness and its intensity. This vacuum also spared the graphite points between which the luminous arc springs — an important economy for Captain Nemo, who could not have replaced them easily. But under these conditions their wear was almost imperceptible.

When the Nautilus prepared to resume its underwater course, I went back down to the salon. The panels closed again, and our heading was set due west.

We were now furrowing the waters of the Indian Ocean, a vast liquid plain covering five hundred and fifty million hectares, whose waters are so transparent that they make you dizzy if you lean out over their surface. The Nautilus generally floated there at depths between one and two hundred meters. So it went for several days. To anyone but me, gripped by an immense love of the sea, the hours would no doubt have seemed long and monotonous; but these daily strolls on the platform, where I steeped myself again in the invigorating air of the ocean, the spectacle of these rich waters through the salon's glass, the reading of the books in the library, the writing of my memoirs — all of it filled my time and left me not one moment of weariness or boredom.

Our health, all of us, kept up in a very satisfactory state. The shipboard diet suited us perfectly, and for my part I could easily have done without the variations that Ned Land, out of sheer protest, took pains to introduce to it. What is more, in this constant temperature there was not even a cold to fear. Besides, that madreporarian coral the Dendrophyllia — known in Provence as "sea fennel," of which a certain supply was kept on board — would have furnished, with the melting flesh of its polyps, an excellent paste against a cough.

For several days we saw a great many aquatic birds, web-footed sorts, gulls or seagulls. Some were cleverly killed, and, prepared in a certain way, they made a very acceptable dish of waterfowl. Among the great gliders, carried off to long distances from any land and resting on the waves from the fatigue of flight, I made out magnificent albatrosses with a discordant cry like the braying of a donkey, birds that belong to the family of the long-winged. The web-toed family was represented by swift frigate birds that nimbly fished the surface waters, and by numerous phaetons or tropicbirds — among others, that red-tailed tropicbird, big as a pigeon, whose white plumage is shaded with rosy tints that set off the black tone of its wings.

The Nautilus's nets brought up several sorts of sea turtles, of the hawksbill kind, with an arched back, whose shell is highly prized. These reptiles, which dive easily, can stay a long time underwater by closing the fleshy valve at the external opening of their nasal passage. Some of these hawksbills, when they were caught, were still asleep in their shells, safe from marine animals. The flesh of these turtles was generally mediocre, but their eggs made an excellent treat.

As for the fish, they never failed to rouse our admiration whenever, through the open panels, we surprised the secrets of their aquatic life. I noticed several species I had never before had the chance to observe.

I will name chiefly certain boxfish peculiar to the Red Sea, the Indian Ocean, and that part of the ocean bathing the coasts of equatorial America. These fish, like the turtles, the armadillos, the sea urchins, and the crustaceans, are protected by an armor that is neither chalky nor stony, but genuinely bony. Sometimes it takes the shape of a triangular solid, sometimes of a four-sided one. Among the triangular kinds I noted a few half a decimeter long, of wholesome flesh and exquisite taste, brown at the tail and yellow at the fins, whose acclimatization I would recommend even in fresh water, to which, for that matter, a certain number of saltwater fish grow accustomed easily. I will also mention four-sided boxfish topped along the back with four large tubercles; boxfish speckled with white points along the lower part of the body, which grow as tame as birds; trunkfish armed with spines formed by the prolongation of their bony crust, whose peculiar grunting has earned them the nickname "sea pigs"; and then "dromedaries" with big cone-shaped humps, whose flesh is hard and leathery.

I find further, in the daily notes kept by Conseil, certain fish of the pufferfish kind, peculiar to these seas: red-backed, white-breasted Spengler's puffers, distinguished by three longitudinal rows of filaments, and electric ones, seven inches long, decked out in the liveliest colors. Then, as specimens of other kinds: ovoids resembling a blackish-brown egg, striped with white bands and lacking a tail; diodons, true porcupines of the sea, armed with spines and able to swell up so as to form a ball bristling with darts; seahorses common to every ocean; flying sea moths, with an elongated snout, whose very broad pectoral fins, arranged like wings, allow them — if not to fly — at least to launch themselves into the air; spoon-tailed "pigeons," whose tail is covered with many scaly rings; long-jawed spiny eels, excellent fish twenty-five centimeters long and gleaming with the most agreeable colors; livid dragonets, whose head is rough; myriads of leaping blennies, striped with black, with long pectoral fins, gliding over the surface of the water with prodigious speed; delicious sailfin blennies, which can hoist their fins like so many sails spread to the favoring currents; splendid kurtus, on which nature has lavished yellow, sky blue, silver, and gold; trichopters, whose wings are formed of filaments; bullheads, forever smeared with mud, which produce a certain rustling sound; gurnards, whose liver is considered poison; bodians, which wear a movable blinder over the eyes; and finally trumpetfish, with a long tubular snout, true flycatchers of the ocean, armed with a gun that neither the Chassepot nor the Remington ever foresaw, and which kill insects by striking them with a single drop of water.

In the eighty-ninth genus of the fish classified by Lacépède — belonging to the second subclass of the bony fishes, marked by a gill cover and a branchial membrane — I noticed the scorpionfish, whose head is fitted out with spines and which possesses only a single dorsal fin; these animals are clad in or stripped of small scales, according to the subgenus to which they belong. The second subgenus gave us specimens of dactylopterus, three to four decimeters long, striped with yellow, but with a head of fantastic appearance. As for the first subgenus, it furnished several specimens of that bizarre fish justly nicknamed "sea toad," a fish with a large head, sometimes hollowed with deep cavities, sometimes swollen with protuberances; bristling with spines and strewn with tubercles, it carries irregular, hideous horns; its body and tail are studded with calluses; its stingers inflict dangerous wounds; it is repugnant and horrible.

From January 21 to 23 the Nautilus traveled at the rate of two hundred and fifty leagues per twenty-four hours, that is, five hundred and forty miles, or twenty-two miles an hour.

If we recognized the various kinds of fish as they passed, it was because they were drawn by the electric glare and tried to keep us company; most, outstripped by our speed, soon fell behind; a few, however, managed to hold their place for a while in the Nautilus's wake.

On the morning of the 24th, at 12°5′ south latitude and 94°33′ longitude, we sighted Keeling Island, a madreporic upheaval planted with magnificent coconut palms, which was visited by Mr. Darwin and Captain Fitz-Roy. The Nautilus skirted the steep shores of this deserted island at a short distance. Its dredges brought up numerous specimens of polyps and echinoderms, and curious shells of the mollusk branch. A few precious products of the dolphin-shell species enriched Captain Nemo's treasures, to which I added a punctiferous star coral, a kind of parasitic coral often fixed on a shell.

Soon Keeling Island vanished below the horizon, and our heading was set to the northwest, toward the tip of the Indian peninsula.

"Civilized lands," Ned Land said to me that day. "That'll be better than these Papuan islands, where you meet more savages than roebuck! On that Indian soil, Professor, there are roads, railways, English, French, and Hindu towns. You wouldn't go five miles without running into a countryman. Well now! Isn't it about time we gave Captain Nemo the slip?"

"No, Ned, no," I answered in a very firm tone. "Let it run, as you sailors say. The Nautilus is drawing near the inhabited continents. It's coming back toward Europe — let it take us there. Once we've reached our own seas, we'll see what prudence advises us to attempt. Besides, I hardly suppose Captain Nemo will allow us to go hunting on the coasts of Malabar or Coromandel the way we did in the forests of New Guinea."

"Well then, Professor — can't we do without his permission?"

I did not answer the Canadian. I had no wish to argue. At bottom, I was set on running the chances of the destiny that had thrown me aboard the Nautilus all the way to the end.

From Keeling Island on, our pace generally slowed. It also grew more erratic and often carried us to great depths. Several times we made use of the inclined planes, which interior levers could set at an angle to the waterline. We went down that way to depths of two and three kilometers, but without ever having verified the great deeps of this Indian sea, which soundings of thirteen thousand meters have not been able to reach. As for the temperature of the lower layers, the thermometer always registered, invariably, four degrees above zero. I observed only that, in the upper sheets, the water was always colder over the shallows than in the open sea.

On January 25, the ocean being utterly deserted, the Nautilus spent the day on the surface, beating the waves with its powerful screw and flinging them up to a great height. How, under such conditions, could it not have been taken for a gigantic cetacean? I spent three-quarters of that day on the platform. I watched the sea. Nothing on the horizon, except, toward four in the afternoon, a long steamer running west on an opposite tack. Its masts were visible for a moment, but it could not make out the Nautilus, which lay too flush with the water. I thought this steamship must belong to the Peninsular and Oriental line, which runs the service from Ceylon to Sydney, calling at King George's Point and Melbourne.

At five in the evening, before that swift twilight that ties day to night in the tropical zones, Conseil and I were dazzled by a curious spectacle.

There is a charming animal whose encounter, according to the ancients, foretold good fortune. Aristotle, Athenaeus, Pliny, and Oppian had studied its tastes and exhausted upon it all the poetry of the scholars of Greece and Italy. They called it Nautilus and Pompylius. But modern science has not ratified their name, and this mollusk is now known as the Argonaut.

Anyone who had consulted Conseil would have learned from that good fellow that the mollusk branch divides into five classes; that the first class, the cephalopods — whose members are sometimes naked, sometimes shelled — includes two families, the dibranchiate and the tetrabranchiate, distinguished by the number of their gills; that the family of the dibranchiates contains three genera, the argonaut, the squid, and the cuttlefish, and that the family of the tetrabranchiates contains but one, the nautilus. And if, after this nomenclature, some unruly mind had confused the argonaut, which is acetabuliferous — that is, a bearer of suckers — with the nautilus, which is tentaculiferous — that is, a bearer of tentacles — he would have been without excuse.

Now, it was a whole troop of these argonauts that was then traveling over the surface of the ocean. We could count several hundred of them. They belonged to the species of the tuberculate argonauts, which is special to the seas of India.

These graceful mollusks moved backward by means of their locomotor tube, driving out through this tube the water they had sucked in. Of their eight tentacles, six — long and slender — floated on the water, while the two others, rounded into paddle shapes, spread to the wind like a light sail. I could see perfectly their spiraling, wavy shell, which Cuvier rightly compares to an elegant little skiff. A real boat, in fact. It carries the animal that secreted it, without the animal being attached to it.

"The argonaut is free to leave its shell," I said to Conseil, "but it never does."

"Just like Captain Nemo," Conseil replied shrewdly. "That's why he would have done better to call his ship the Argonaut."

For about an hour the Nautilus floated in the midst of this troop of mollusks. Then some sudden terror seized them, I know not what. As if at a signal, all the sails were abruptly struck; the arms folded back, the bodies contracted. The shells, capsizing, shifted their center of gravity, and the whole flotilla vanished beneath the waves. It was instantaneous, and never did the ships of a squadron maneuver with more unity.

At that moment night fell all at once, and the swells, barely lifted by the breeze, stretched out peacefully beneath the Nautilus's wales.

The next day, January 26, we cut the equator on the eighty-second meridian, and we passed back into the northern hemisphere.

During that day a formidable troop of sharks escorted us. Terrible animals, which swarm in these seas and make them very dangerous. There were Philippi sharks, brown-backed and whitish-bellied, armed with eleven rows of teeth; ocellated sharks, whose neck is marked with a large black spot ringed with white that looks like an eye; Isabella sharks with a rounded snout strewn with dark points. Often these powerful animals hurled themselves against the salon's glass with a violence hardly reassuring. Ned Land could no longer control himself. He wanted to go up to the surface and harpoon these monsters — especially certain smooth-hound sharks whose mouth is paved with teeth arranged like a mosaic, and great tiger sharks five meters long, which taunted him with a quite particular insistence. But soon the Nautilus, putting on speed, easily left behind the swiftest of these sharks.

On January 27, at the mouth of the vast Gulf of Bengal, we met more than once — a sinister sight! — corpses floating at the surface. They were the dead of the Indian cities, carried by the Ganges out to the open sea, and which the vultures, the country's only undertakers, had not finished devouring. But the sharks were not lacking to help them at their funereal work.

Toward seven in the evening, the Nautilus, half submerged, sailed through a sea of milk. As far as the eye could reach, the ocean seemed turned to milk. Was it the effect of the moon's rays? No, for the moon, barely two days old, was still lost below the horizon in the sun's rays. The whole sky, though lit by the radiance of the stars, seemed black by contrast with the whiteness of the waters.

Conseil could not believe his eyes, and he questioned me about the causes of this singular phenomenon. Luckily, I was in a position to answer him.

"This is what's called a milk sea," I told him, "a vast expanse of white waves often seen off the coasts of Amboina and in these waters."

"But," asked Conseil, "can Master teach me what cause produces such an effect, for this water hasn't turned into milk, I take it!"

"No, my boy, and this whiteness that surprises you is due only to the presence of myriads of tiny infusoria, a sort of little luminous worm, gelatinous and colorless, no thicker than a hair, whose length does not exceed a fifth of a millimeter. Some of these little creatures cling together over a span of several leagues."

"Several leagues!" exclaimed Conseil.

"Yes, my boy, and don't try to reckon the number of these infusoria! You'd never manage it, for, if I'm not mistaken, some navigators have floated on these milk seas for more than forty miles."

I do not know whether Conseil heeded my advice, but he seemed to sink into deep reflection, no doubt trying to calculate how many fifths of a millimeter forty square miles contain. As for me, I went on observing the phenomenon. For several hours the Nautilus cleaved with its ram these whitish waves, and I noticed that it glided noiselessly over this soapy water, as if it were floating in those eddies of foam that the currents and countercurrents of bays sometimes leave between them.

Toward midnight, the sea suddenly took back its ordinary hue; but behind us, all the way to the limits of the horizon, the sky, reflecting the whiteness of the waves, seemed for a long time steeped in the vague glimmers of an aurora borealis.

A New Proposal from Captain Nemo

On February 28, when the Nautilus rose to the surface at noon at 9°4′ north latitude, it came in sight of land lying eight miles to the west. The first thing I made out was a cluster of mountains, about two thousand feet high, whose shapes had been molded into the most fanciful outlines. Once the reading was taken, I went back into the salon, and when the bearing had been plotted on the chart, I recognized that we stood off the island of Ceylon — that pearl hanging from the lower lobe of the Indian peninsula.

I went to the library to look for some book about this island, one of the most fertile on the globe. As it happened, I found a volume by Sirr H. C., esq., titled Ceylon and the Cingalese. Back in the salon, I first noted down the bearings of Ceylon, on which antiquity had lavished so many different names. It lay between 5°55′ and 9°49′ north latitude, and between 79°42′ and 82°4′ longitude east of the Greenwich meridian; its length was two hundred and seventy-five miles; its maximum width, one hundred and fifty miles; its circumference, nine hundred miles; its area, twenty-four thousand four hundred and forty-eight miles — in other words, a little smaller than Ireland.

At that moment Captain Nemo and his second officer appeared.

The captain glanced at the chart. Then, turning to me:

"The island of Ceylon," he said, "a land famous for its pearl fisheries. Would it please you, Professor Aronnax, to visit one of them?"

"Without a doubt, Captain."

"Good. That will be easy enough. Only, if we see the fisheries, we won't see the fishermen. The annual season hasn't opened yet. No matter. I'll give the order to make for the Gulf of Manaar, where we'll arrive during the night."
