The captain said a few words to his second officer, who left at once. Soon the Nautilus sank back into its watery element, and the pressure gauge showed that it was holding at a depth of thirty feet.

With the chart before me, I searched for this Gulf of Manaar. I found it on the ninth parallel, off the northwest coast of Ceylon. It was formed by a long line running out from the little island of Manaar. To reach it, we would have to work our way up the entire western shore of Ceylon.

"Professor," Captain Nemo said to me then, "pearls are fished in the Bay of Bengal, in the Indian Sea, in the seas of China and Japan, in the seas of southern America, in the Gulf of Panama, in the Gulf of California; but it is at Ceylon that the fishing yields the finest results. We're arriving a bit early, no doubt. The fishermen don't gather in the Gulf of Manaar until the month of March, and then, for thirty days, their three hundred boats give themselves over to this lucrative harvest of the sea's treasures. Each boat is crewed by ten rowers and ten fishermen. The fishermen, divided into two teams, dive in turns and go down to a depth of twelve meters by means of a heavy stone that they grip between their feet and that a rope ties back to the boat."

"So," I said, "it's still this primitive method that's in use?"

"Still," Captain Nemo answered, "even though these fisheries belong to the most industrious people on the globe — the English, to whom the Treaty of Amiens ceded them in 1802."

"It seems to me, though, that the diving suit, such as you use it, would render great service in an operation like this."

"Yes, for these poor fishermen can't stay underwater for long. The Englishman Perceval, in his voyage to Ceylon, does speak of an African diver who could stay five minutes without coming up, but I find that hard to believe. I know that some divers reach fifty-seven seconds, and very skilled ones as much as eighty-seven; but they're rare, and once back aboard, these wretched men bleed from the nose and ears, the water tinged with blood. I believe the average time the fishermen can bear is thirty seconds, during which they hurry to cram into a little net all the pearl oysters they can wrench loose; but generally these fishermen don't live to old age. Their eyesight weakens; ulcers break out on their eyes; sores form on their bodies; and often they're even struck down by apoplexy at the bottom of the sea."

"Yes," I said, "it's a sad trade, and one that serves nothing but the satisfaction of a few whims. But tell me, Captain — how many oysters can a boat fish in a day?"

"Forty to fifty thousand or so. It's even said that in 1814, when the English government had fishing done on its own account, its divers, in twenty days of work, brought up seventy-six million oysters."

"At least," I asked, "are these fishermen decently paid?"

"Barely, Professor. In Panama they earn only a dollar a week. Most often, they get a single sou for an oyster that holds a pearl — and how many do they bring up that hold none!"

"A sou to these poor people who make their masters rich! It's disgraceful."

"And so, Professor," Captain Nemo said to me, "you and your companions will visit the bank of Manaar, and if by chance some early fisherman is already there, well, we'll watch him at work."

"Agreed, Captain."

"By the way, Professor Aronnax — you're not afraid of sharks?"

"Sharks?" I cried.

The question struck me as, to say the least, rather pointless.

"Well?" Captain Nemo went on.

"I'll admit, Captain, that I'm not yet very well acquainted with that kind of fish."

"We're used to them, we are," Captain Nemo replied, "and in time you will be too. Besides, we'll be armed, and along the way we might even hunt a shark or two. It's an interesting sport. So then — until tomorrow, Professor, and bright and early."

Having said this in an offhand tone, Captain Nemo left the salon.

If someone invited you to hunt bear in the mountains of Switzerland, you'd say, "Very good! Tomorrow we go bear-hunting." If someone invited you to hunt lion on the plains of the Atlas, or tiger in the jungles of India, you'd say, "Aha! So it seems we're off to hunt tiger or lion!" But if someone invited you to hunt shark in its natural element, you might well ask leave to think it over before accepting the invitation.

As for me, I passed my hand across my forehead, where a few beads of cold sweat had gathered.

"Let's think this over," I told myself, "and take our time. Hunting otters in the underwater forests, as we did in the forests of Crespo Island — that's one thing. But roaming the bottom of the sea when you're all but certain to meet sharks down there — that's another thing entirely! I know that in certain countries, in the Andaman Islands especially, the native divers don't hesitate to attack a shark with a dagger in one hand and a noose in the other; but I also know that many of those who face these formidable animals don't come back alive! Besides, I'm no native diver, and even if I were, I think a slight hesitation on my part, in this case, wouldn't be out of place."

And there I was dreaming of sharks, thinking of those vast jaws bristling with multiple rows of teeth, capable of cutting a man in two. I could already feel a certain ache around my kidneys. And I couldn't stomach the casual ease with which the captain had made this deplorable invitation! You'd have thought he was proposing to go track down some harmless fox in the underbrush.

"Well!" I thought. "Conseil will never agree to come, and that'll excuse me from going along with the captain."

As for Ned Land, I confess I didn't feel quite so sure of his good sense. A danger, however great, always held a certain appeal for his combative nature.

I went back to reading Sirr's book, but I leafed through it absentmindedly. Between the lines I kept seeing jaws opened terrifyingly wide.

Just then Conseil and the Canadian came in, looking calm and even cheerful. They had no idea what was in store for them.

"Well, Professor," Ned Land said to me, "your Captain Nemo — the devil take him! — has just made us a very kind proposal."

"Ah!" I said. "So you know—"

"Begging Master's pardon," Conseil answered, "the commander of the Nautilus has invited us to visit tomorrow, in Master's company, the magnificent fisheries of Ceylon. He did it in excellent terms and behaved like a true gentleman."

"He said nothing more to you?"

"Nothing, Professor," the Canadian answered, "except that he'd already spoken to you about this little outing."

"So he did," I said. "And he gave you no details about—"

"None, Professor. You'll be coming with us, won't you?"

"Me? Of course! I see you've taken a liking to the idea, Master Land."

"Yes! It's curious — very curious."

"Dangerous, perhaps!" I added in an insinuating tone.

"Dangerous," Ned Land answered, "a simple excursion onto an oyster bank!"

Captain Nemo had clearly decided it was pointless to plant the idea of sharks in my companions' minds. I looked at them with a troubled eye, as though they were already missing a limb or two. Should I warn them? Yes, surely, but I hardly knew how to go about it.

"Professor," Conseil said to me, "would Master be willing to give us some details about pearl fishing?"

"About the fishing itself?" I asked. "Or about the incidents that—"

"About the fishing," the Canadian said. "Before setting foot on the ground, it's good to know it."

"Well then! Sit down, my friends, and I'll teach you everything the Englishman Sirr has just taught me."

Ned and Conseil settled onto a divan, and the first thing the Canadian said to me was:

"Professor, what exactly is a pearl?"

"My good Ned," I answered, "to the poet, the pearl is a tear of the sea; to the peoples of the East, it's a drop of dew turned solid; to the ladies, it's a jewel of oblong shape, of a glassy luster, of a mother-of-pearl substance, which they wear on the finger, at the neck, or in the ear; to the chemist, it's a mixture of phosphate and carbonate of lime with a little gelatin; and finally, to the naturalist, it's simply a diseased secretion of the organ that produces mother-of-pearl in certain bivalves."

"Phylum of mollusks," said Conseil, "class of acephalans, order of testaceans."

"Precisely, learned Conseil. Now, among these testaceans, the iridescent sea-ear, the turban shells, the giant clams, the pinna shells — in a word, all those that secrete mother-of-pearl, that is, the blue, bluish, violet, or white substance lining the inside of their shells — are capable of producing pearls."

"Mussels too?" asked the Canadian.

"Yes! The mussels of certain streams of Scotland, Wales, Ireland, Saxony, Bohemia, and France."

"Good! We'll keep an eye out for those from now on," the Canadian answered.

"But," I went on, "the mollusk above all others that distills the pearl is the pearl oyster, the Meleagrina margaritifera, the precious pintadine. The pearl is nothing but a nacreous concretion arranged in a globular form. Either it sticks to the oyster's shell, or it embeds itself in the folds of the animal. On the valves, the pearl is fixed; on the flesh, it's loose. But it always has for its nucleus some small hard body, either a sterile ovule or a grain of sand, around which the nacreous matter is deposited over several years, layer by layer, in thin concentric coats."

"Do you find several pearls in a single oyster?" Conseil asked.

"Yes, my boy. There are certain pintadines that form a veritable jewel-case. People have even cited one oyster — though I take the liberty of doubting it — that contained no fewer than a hundred and fifty sharks."

"A hundred and fifty sharks!" cried Ned Land.

"Did I say sharks?" I exclaimed quickly. "I mean a hundred and fifty pearls. Sharks would make no sense."

"Quite so," said Conseil. "But will Master now teach us the means by which these pearls are extracted?"

"They go about it in several ways, and often, when the pearls stick to the valves, the fishermen tear them loose with pincers. But most commonly, the pintadines are spread out on esparto-grass mats that cover the shore. There they die in the open air, and after ten days they're in a satisfactory state of putrefaction. They're then plunged into vast reservoirs of seawater, after which they're opened and washed. This is where the sorters' double task begins. First, they separate the layers of mother-of-pearl known in the trade as silver-true, white-bastard, and black-bastard, which are delivered in cases of a hundred and twenty-five to a hundred and fifty kilograms. Then they strip out the flesh of the oyster, boil it, and sift it in order to extract even the smallest pearls."

"Does the price of these pearls vary according to their size?" Conseil asked.

"Not only according to their size," I answered, "but also according to their shape, according to their water — that is, their color — and according to their orient — that is, that shimmering, iridescent gleam that makes them so lovely to the eye. The finest pearls are called virgin pearls, or paragons; they form in isolation within the tissue of the mollusk; they're white, often opaque, but sometimes of an opaline transparency, and most commonly spherical or pear-shaped. The spherical ones make bracelets; the pear-shaped ones, pendants — and, being the most precious, they're sold one by one. The others stick to the oyster's shell and, being more irregular, are sold by weight. Finally, in a lower order come the little pearls known as seed pearls; they're sold by measure and serve especially for working embroidery onto church ornaments."

"But this work of separating the pearls by size must be long and difficult," said the Canadian.

"No, my friend. This work is done by means of eleven sieves, or riddles, pierced with a varying number of holes. The pearls that stay in the sieves with twenty to eighty holes are of the first order. Those that don't slip through the riddles pierced with a hundred to eight hundred holes are of the second order. And finally, the pearls for which they use the sieves pierced with nine hundred to a thousand holes make up the seed pearls."

"That's ingenious," said Conseil, "and I see that the dividing and sorting of pearls is done mechanically. And can Master tell us what the working of the pearl-oyster banks brings in?"

"To go by Sirr's book," I answered, "the fisheries of Ceylon are leased out annually for the sum of three million sharks."

"Francs, you mean!" Conseil put in.

"Yes, francs! Three million francs," I went on. "But I believe these fisheries no longer bring in what they once did. The same is true of the American fisheries, which under the reign of Charles the Fifth produced four million francs, now reduced to two-thirds of that. All told, one may put the overall yield of the pearl trade at nine million francs."

"But," Conseil asked, "aren't there a few famous pearls that have been valued at a very high price?"

"Yes, my boy. It's said that Caesar gave Servilia a pearl valued at a hundred and twenty thousand francs in our currency."

"I've even heard tell," said the Canadian, "of a certain lady of ancient times who used to drink pearls in her vinegar."

"Cleopatra," Conseil shot back.

"It must have tasted foul," Ned Land added.

"Detestable, friend Ned," Conseil answered; "but a little glass of vinegar that costs fifteen hundred thousand francs is a tidy price."

"I'm sorry I didn't marry that lady," said the Canadian, working his arm in a rather unsettling way.

"Ned Land the husband of Cleopatra!" cried Conseil.

"Well, I did nearly get married, Conseil," the Canadian answered seriously, "and it's not my fault the business fell through. I'd even bought a pearl necklace for Kat Tender, my fiancée — who married someone else, as it happens. Well, that necklace cost me no more than a dollar and a half, and yet — the Professor will kindly take my word for it — the pearls that made it up wouldn't have passed through the twenty-hole sieve."

"My good Ned," I answered, laughing, "those were artificial pearls, mere glass globules coated on the inside with essence of Orient."

"Essence of Orient?" asked the Canadian. "And what's that worth?"

"Next to nothing! It's nothing but the silvery matter of the bleak's scales, gathered in water and preserved in ammonia. It has no value at all."

"That's maybe why Kat Tender married someone else," Master Land replied philosophically.

"But," I said, "to come back to the pearls of high value, I don't believe any sovereign ever owned one superior to Captain Nemo's."

"This one," said Conseil, pointing to the magnificent jewel enclosed under its glass case.

"Certainly. I'm not mistaken in assigning it a value of two million—"

"Francs!" Conseil said quickly.

"Yes," I said, "two million francs, and no doubt it cost the captain nothing but the trouble of picking it up."

"Ha!" cried Ned Land. "Who's to say we won't run across its equal tomorrow, during our little walk!"

"Bah!" went Conseil.

"And why not?"

"What use would millions be to us aboard the Nautilus?"

"Aboard, none," said Ned Land, "but... elsewhere."

"Oh! Elsewhere!" went Conseil, shaking his head.

"Come to think of it," I said, "Master Land is right. And if we ever bring back to Europe or America a pearl worth a few million, that at least will lend great authenticity — and, at the same time, great value — to the account of our adventures."

"I believe it," said the Canadian.

"But," said Conseil, who always came back to the instructive side of things, "is this pearl fishing dangerous?"

"No," I answered quickly, "especially if one takes certain precautions."

"What's the risk in the trade?" said Ned Land. "Swallowing a few mouthfuls of seawater!"

"Just as you say, Ned. By the way," I said, trying to take on Captain Nemo's offhand tone, "are you afraid of sharks, brave Ned?"

"Me?" the Canadian answered. "A harpooner by trade! It's my job to laugh at them!"

"It's not a matter," I said, "of catching them with a swivel-hook, hoisting them onto a ship's deck, chopping off their tails with an axe, slitting open their bellies, tearing out their hearts, and throwing them back into the sea!"

"Then it's a matter of...?"

"Yes, precisely."

"In the water?"

"In the water."

"My word — with a good harpoon! You know, Professor, these sharks are pretty poorly built beasts. They have to roll over onto their bellies to snap you up, and in the meantime..."

Ned Land had a way of pronouncing the word "snap" that sent a chill down your spine.

"Well, and you, Conseil — what do you think of these sharks?"

"Me?" said Conseil. "I'll be frank with Master."

"That's the spirit," I thought.

"If Master faces the sharks," said Conseil, "I don't see why his faithful servant shouldn't face them right alongside him!"

A Pearl Worth Ten Million

Night came on. I went to bed. I slept badly. Sharks played a leading role in my dreams, and I found it both entirely fitting and entirely unfitting, that old etymology that traces the French word for shark — requin — back to the word requiem.

The next morning, at four o'clock, I was woken by the steward Captain Nemo had assigned specially to my service. I got up quickly, dressed, and went into the salon.

Captain Nemo was waiting for me there.

"Professor Aronnax," he said, "are you ready to leave?"

"I'm ready."

"Then follow me, please."

"And my companions, Captain?"

"They've been told, and they're waiting for us."

"Aren't we going to put on our diving suits?" I asked.

"Not yet. I didn't let the Nautilus come too close to this coast, and we're a fair distance out from the Manaar bank. But I've had the dinghy made ready. It'll carry us to the exact landing point and spare us a rather long swim. It's loaded with our diving gear, which we'll put on the moment this underwater expedition begins."

Captain Nemo led me toward the central staircase, whose steps ended at the platform. Ned and Conseil were there, delighted at the "pleasure outing" being got up. Five of the Nautilus's sailors, oars ready, waited for us in the dinghy, which had been made fast against the side.

The night was still dark. Sheets of cloud covered the sky and let only a few scattered stars show through. I looked toward the land, but I saw only a blurred line closing off three-quarters of the horizon, from southwest to northwest. The Nautilus, having worked its way up the western coast of Ceylon during the night, now lay west of the bay — or rather of the gulf formed by the mainland and the island of Manaar. There, beneath the dark waters, stretched the pearl-oyster bank, an inexhaustible field of pearls more than twenty miles long.

Captain Nemo, Conseil, Ned Land, and I took our places at the stern of the dinghy. The coxswain manned the tiller; his four companions leaned into their oars; the mooring line was cast off, and we pulled away.

The dinghy headed south. The rowers were in no hurry. I noticed that their oar strokes, driven hard beneath the water, came only every ten seconds, in the manner generally used in navies. While the boat ran on under its own way, the drops of water struck the black surface of the swell with a crackle, like spatters of molten lead. A small swell rolling in from the open sea gave the dinghy a slight roll, and a few wave crests slapped against its bow.

We were silent. What was Captain Nemo thinking about? Perhaps about this land he was drawing near — too near for his liking, unlike the Canadian, to whom it still seemed too far off. As for Conseil, he was there as a simple sightseer.

Around half past five, the first tints along the horizon picked out more sharply the upper line of the coast. Fairly flat to the east, it swelled a little toward the south. Five miles still separated us from it, and its shore blurred into the misty waters. Between it and us, the sea was deserted. Not a boat, not a diver. Deep solitude over this meeting place of the pearl fishers. As Captain Nemo had pointed out to me, we were arriving in these waters a month too early.

At six o'clock, day broke all at once, with that suddenness peculiar to the tropics, which know neither dawn nor dusk. The sun's rays pierced the curtain of clouds banked along the eastern horizon, and the radiant star climbed swiftly.

I saw the land distinctly now, with a few trees scattered here and there.

The dinghy drew on toward the island of Manaar, which curved away to the south. Captain Nemo had risen from his bench and was watching the sea.

At a sign from him, the anchor was dropped, and the chain barely ran out, for the bottom lay no more than a meter down, and at this spot it formed one of the highest points of the oyster bank. The dinghy at once swung round under the pull of the ebb tide, which carried it seaward.

"Here we are, Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo said then. "You see this narrow bay. It's right here that, a month from now, the fishing fleets of the exploiters will gather in numbers, and it's these waters their divers will come to search so boldly. This bay is happily arranged for that kind of fishing. It's sheltered from the strongest winds, and the sea here is never very rough — a very favorable condition for the divers' work. Now we'll put on our diving suits, and begin our walk."

I said nothing, and while I stared at these suspect waters, I began, with the help of the boat's sailors, to pull on my heavy sea garment. Captain Nemo and my two companions were dressing as well. None of the Nautilus's men were to go with us on this new excursion.

Soon we were sealed up to the neck in the rubber suit, and straps fixed the air tanks onto our backs. As for the Ruhmkorff apparatus, there was no question of it. Before fitting my head into its copper capsule, I remarked on this to the captain.

"Those devices would be useless to us," he answered. "We won't be going to any great depth, and the sun's rays will be enough to light our way. Besides, it wouldn't be wise to carry an electric lantern down into these waters. Its brightness might unexpectedly attract some dangerous resident of these parts."

While Captain Nemo was speaking these words, I turned toward Conseil and Ned Land. But my two friends had already boxed their heads into the metal helmet, and they could neither hear nor answer.

One last question remained for me to put to Captain Nemo.

"And our weapons?" I asked. "Our rifles?"

"Rifles! What for? Don't your mountain men attack the bear with a dagger in their hand? And isn't steel surer than lead? Here's a good solid blade. Put it in your belt, and let's go."

I looked at my companions. They were armed as we were, and, on top of that, Ned Land was brandishing an enormous harpoon he'd stowed in the dinghy before leaving the Nautilus.

Then, following the captain's example, I let them fit the heavy copper sphere over my head, and our air tanks were immediately switched on.

A moment later, the sailors were setting us down one after another, and, in a meter and a half of water, we planted our feet on smooth sand. Captain Nemo gave us a wave of his hand. We followed him, and down a gentle slope we vanished beneath the waves.

There, the fears that had been haunting my brain left me. I became astonishingly calm. The ease of my movements strengthened my confidence, and the strangeness of the spectacle captured my imagination.

The sun was already sending enough light down under the water. The smallest objects stayed visible. After ten minutes of walking, we were in five meters of water, and the ground had become nearly flat.

At our steps, like flocks of snipe over a marsh, there rose up flights of curious fish of the genus Monopterus, whose members have no fin but the one on the tail. I recognized the javanese, a true serpent eighty centimeters long, with a livid belly, which you'd easily mistake for a conger eel but for the golden lines along its flanks. Among the genus Stromateus, whose body is very flattened and oval, I made out some brightly colored fiatolas carrying their dorsal fin like a scythe — edible fish that, dried and marinated, make an excellent dish known as karawade — then some Tranquebar butterflyfish, belonging to the genus Aspidophoroides, whose body is covered by a scaly armor with eight lengthwise facets.

Meanwhile, the rising sun lit the mass of water more and more. The floor gradually changed. The fine sand gave way to a veritable causeway of rounded rocks, carpeted with mollusks and zoophytes. Among the specimens of these two branches, I noticed some Placuna with thin, unequal valves — a kind of oyster peculiar to the Red Sea and the Indian Ocean — orange lucine clams with an orbicular shell, awl-shaped auger shells, some of those Persian murexes that supplied the Nautilus with such an admirable dye, horned rock shells fifteen centimeters long that reared up beneath the waves like hands ready to seize you, spiny turbinella shells all bristling with prickles, gaping lingula shells, anatina clams — edible shellfish that feed the markets of Hindustan — faintly luminous Pelagia jellyfish, and finally some splendid fan-shaped Oculina corals, magnificent fans that form one of the richest branchings of these seas.

In the midst of these living plants, and under the arbors of hydrophytes, ran clumsy legions of articulates, particularly toothed Ranina crabs, whose shell is a somewhat rounded triangle, coconut crabs special to these waters, and hideous Parthenope crabs, whose look was repellent to the eye. One animal no less loathsome, which I met with several times, was that enormous crab observed by Mr. Darwin, to which nature has given the instinct and the strength needed to feed on coconuts. It climbs the trees along the shore, knocks down the nut, which splits open in its fall, and then opens it with its powerful pincers. Here, beneath these clear waters, this crab scuttled about with unequaled agility, while green turtles, of the kind that frequent the Malabar coast, moved slowly among the shifting rocks.

Around seven o'clock, we at last were treading the pearl-oyster bank, where the oysters breed by the million. These precious mollusks clung to the rocks, held fast by that brown byssus that doesn't allow them to move — a respect in which these oysters are inferior even to the mussel, to which nature has not denied all power of locomotion.

The Meleagrina pintadina — the mother-of-pearl oyster, whose valves are roughly equal — takes the form of a rounded shell with thick walls, very rough on the outside. Some of these shells were leaved and furrowed with greenish bands radiating out from the top. These belonged to the young oysters. The others, with a rough black surface, ten years old and more, measured as much as fifteen centimeters across.

Captain Nemo pointed his hand at this prodigious heap of oysters, and I understood that this mine was truly inexhaustible, for nature's creative force outweighs man's destructive instinct. Ned Land, faithful to that instinct, was busy stuffing the finest of the mollusks into a net he carried at his side.

But we couldn't stop. We had to follow the captain, who seemed to be guiding himself along paths known to him alone. The bottom rose noticeably, and at times my arm, when I raised it, broke the surface of the sea. Then the level of the bank dipped again, capriciously. Often we rounded tall rocks tapered into little pyramids. In their dark crevices, big crustaceans, propped high on their legs like siege engines, watched us with fixed eyes, and under our feet crawled myrians, glycerids, aricians, and other annelids, stretching out their antennae and tentacle-like cirri to absurd lengths.

Just then a vast grotto opened before our steps, hollowed out of a picturesque tumble of rocks hung with all the high-warp tapestries of the underwater flora. At first this grotto looked deeply dark to me. The sun's rays seemed to die out inside it by successive degrees. Its dim transparency was no more than drowned light.

Captain Nemo went in. We went in after him. My eyes soon grew used to that relative darkness. I could make out the strangely twisting spring of the vault, borne on natural pillars set broadly on their granite bases, like the heavy columns of Tuscan architecture. Why was our unfathomable guide drawing us down into the depths of this underwater crypt? I would soon find out.

After going down a fairly steep slope, our feet trod the bottom of a kind of circular well. There Captain Nemo stopped, and with his hand he pointed out to us an object I had not yet noticed.

It was an oyster of extraordinary size, a gigantic tridacna — a holy-water basin that could have held a whole lake of blessed water, a bowl more than two meters across, and therefore larger than the one that adorned the salon of the Nautilus.

I went up to this phenomenal mollusk. By its byssus it clung to a slab of granite, and there it grew all alone in the calm waters of the grotto. I put the weight of this tridacna at three hundred kilograms. Now, such an oyster holds fifteen kilos of flesh, and it would take the stomach of a Gargantua to put away a few dozen of them.

Captain Nemo clearly knew of this bivalve's existence. This was not the first time he'd visited it, and I thought that in leading us to this spot he meant only to show us a natural curiosity. I was mistaken. Captain Nemo had a particular interest in checking the present state of this tridacna.

The mollusk's two valves were half open. The captain went up and slid his dagger between the shells to keep them from snapping shut; then, with his hand, he lifted the membranous, fringe-edged tunic that formed the animal's mantle.

There, between the leafy folds, I saw a loose pearl the size of a coconut. Its globular shape, its perfect clarity, its wonderful orient made it a jewel of priceless value. Carried away by curiosity, I reached out my hand to seize it, to weigh it, to feel it! But the captain stopped me, made a sign of refusal, and, drawing back his dagger with a quick movement, let the two valves suddenly snap shut.

Then I understood what Captain Nemo intended. By leaving that pearl buried under the tridacna's mantle, he was letting it slowly grow. With each year the mollusk's secretion added new concentric layers to it. He alone knew the grotto where this admirable fruit of nature was "ripening"; he alone raised it, so to speak, meaning one day to carry it off to his precious museum. Perhaps he'd even, following the example of the Chinese and the Indians, brought on the making of this pearl by slipping some scrap of glass or metal under the folds of the mollusk, which had little by little coated it in mother-of-pearl. In any case, comparing this pearl to those I already knew, to those that gleamed in the captain's collection, I put its value at ten million francs at the very least. A superb natural curiosity, and no luxury jewel — for I don't know what feminine ear could ever have borne it.

Our visit to the opulent tridacna was over. Captain Nemo left the grotto, and we climbed back up onto the oyster bank, among those clear waters not yet troubled by the labor of the divers.

We walked separately now, like true strollers, each of us stopping or wandering off at the whim of his fancy. For my part, I no longer had any worry about the dangers my imagination had so absurdly exaggerated. The shallows drew noticeably nearer the surface of the sea, and soon, in a meter of water, my head broke the level of the ocean. Conseil rejoined me, and pressing his big helmet against mine, he gave me a friendly greeting with his eyes. But this raised plateau measured only a few fathoms, and soon we had gone back down into our element. I believe I've now earned the right to call it that.

Ten minutes later, Captain Nemo suddenly stopped. I thought he was halting to turn back. No. With a gesture, he ordered us to crouch down beside him at the bottom of a wide crevice. His hand pointed toward a spot in the mass of water, and I looked hard.

Five meters from me, a shadow appeared and sank down to the floor. The uneasy thought of sharks crossed my mind. But I was mistaken, and once again we were not dealing with the monsters of the ocean.

It was a man, a living man, an Indian, a black man, a fisherman, a poor devil no doubt, who had come to glean ahead of the harvest. I could see the bottom of his boat, moored a few feet above his head. He was diving and coming back up, again and again. A stone cut in the shape of a sugarloaf, which he gripped between his feet while a rope tied it to his boat, served to bring him down more quickly to the sea floor. That was the whole of his equipment. Reaching the bottom, at about five meters' depth, he threw himself on his knees and filled his sack with oysters gathered at random. Then he came back up, emptied his sack, hauled his stone up again, and started over — the whole operation lasting no more than thirty seconds.

This diver didn't see us. The shadow of the rock hid us from his gaze. And besides, how would this poor Indian ever have supposed that men, beings like himself, were there beneath the waters, spying on his movements, missing no detail of his fishing?

Several times he came back up like this and dived again. He brought up no more than ten or so oysters on each dive, for they had to be torn from the bank they clung to by their tough byssus. And how many of those oysters were empty of the pearls for which he risked his life!

I watched him with deep attention. His work went along steadily, and for half an hour no danger seemed to threaten him. So I was growing used to the sight of this fascinating fishery when, all at once — at a moment when the Indian was kneeling on the floor — I saw him make a gesture of terror, spring up, and gather himself to shoot back to the surface.

I understood his horror. A gigantic shadow was rising above the wretched diver. It was a large shark, coming on diagonally, its eye ablaze, its jaws open!

I was struck dumb with horror, unable to make a move.

The ravenous animal, with a powerful stroke of its fin, hurled itself at the Indian, who threw himself to one side and dodged the shark's bite — but not the lash of its tail, for that tail, striking him in the chest, stretched him out on the floor.

This scene had barely lasted a few seconds. The shark came back, and rolling over on its back, it was getting ready to cut the Indian in two, when I felt Captain Nemo, posted beside me, rise suddenly. Then, dagger in hand, he walked straight at the monster, ready to fight it hand to hand.

The shark, at the very moment it was about to snap up the wretched fisherman, caught sight of its new adversary, and, turning belly-down again, made straight for him.

I can still see Captain Nemo's stance. Braced down on himself, he waited for the formidable shark with an admirable coolness, and when the beast rushed at him, the captain, throwing himself aside with prodigious quickness, dodged the impact and sank his dagger into its belly. But that wasn't the end of it. A terrible fight began.

The shark had roared, so to speak. Blood poured in streams from its wounds. The sea turned red, and through that opaque murk I could see nothing more.

Nothing more — until, in a clearing, I glimpsed the daring captain clinging to one of the animal's fins, fighting the monster body to body, plowing his dagger into his enemy's belly, yet unable to land the decisive blow, that is, to reach it full in the heart. The shark, thrashing, churned the mass of water with fury, and its wake threatened to knock me off my feet.

I would have liked to run to the captain's aid. But nailed in place by horror, I couldn't stir.

I watched, wild-eyed. I saw the phases of the struggle shift. The captain went down onto the floor, thrown over by the enormous bulk that bore down on him. Then the shark's jaws yawned open like a factory shears, and it would have been all over for the captain if, quick as thought, harpoon in hand, Ned Land had not rushed at the shark and struck it with his terrible point.

The waves filled with a mass of blood. They heaved under the movements of the shark, which beat them with indescribable fury. Ned Land had not missed his mark. This was the monster's death rattle. Struck to the heart, it thrashed in horrible spasms, whose backlash knocked Conseil off his feet.

Meanwhile, Ned Land had pulled the captain free. Nemo, back on his feet without a wound, went straight to the Indian, cut the rope that bound him to his stone with a quick stroke, took him in his arms, and, with a vigorous kick of his heel, rose to the surface of the sea.

We followed, all three of us, and in a few moments, miraculously saved, we reached the fisherman's boat.

Captain Nemo's first care was to call this wretched man back to life. I didn't know whether he'd succeed. I hoped so, for the poor devil hadn't been under long. But the shark's blow with its tail might have struck him a mortal wound.

Happily, under the vigorous rubbing of Conseil and the captain, I saw the drowned man little by little come back to his senses. He opened his eyes. What must his surprise have been — his terror, even — to see the four big copper heads leaning over him!

And above all, what must he have thought when Captain Nemo, drawing a bag of pearls from a pocket of his suit, put it into his hand? This magnificent act of charity, from the man of the waters to the poor Indian of Ceylon, was accepted by the latter with a trembling hand. His frightened eyes showed, moreover, that he didn't know to what superhuman beings he owed both his fortune and his life.

At a sign from the captain, we made our way back to the oyster bank, and, following the route already traveled, after half an hour's walk we came upon the anchor that held the Nautilus's dinghy to the bottom.

Once aboard, each of us, with the sailors' help, shed his heavy copper shell.

Captain Nemo's first word was for the Canadian.

"Thank you, Master Land," he said.

"It's a settling of scores, Captain," Ned Land answered. "I owed you that."

A pale smile slid across the captain's lips, and that was all.

"To the Nautilus," he said.

The boat flew over the waves. A few minutes later, we came upon the shark's carcass, floating.

By the black color marking the tips of its fins, I recognized the terrible melanopterus of the Indian sea, of the species of the true sharks. Its length exceeded twenty-five feet; its enormous mouth took up a third of its body. It was an adult, as could be seen from the six rows of teeth arranged in isosceles triangles along its upper jaw.

Conseil looked at it with a wholly scientific interest, and I'm sure he was filing it away — not without reason — in the class of cartilaginous fish, order Chondropterygii with fixed gills, family Selachii, genus Squalus.

While I was studying this inert mass, a dozen of these voracious melanopters appeared all at once around the boat; but, paying us no mind, they threw themselves on the carcass and fought over the scraps.

At half past eight, we were back aboard the Nautilus.

There I fell to reflecting on the incidents of our excursion to the Manaar bank. Two observations came out of it inevitably. One bore on the unequaled daring of Captain Nemo, the other on his devotion to a human being — one of the representatives of that race he was fleeing beneath the seas. Whatever he might say, this strange man had not yet managed to kill his heart entirely.

When I made this observation to him, he answered in a slightly moved tone:

"That Indian, Professor, is an inhabitant of the land of the oppressed, and I am still, and to my last breath I will remain, a man of that land!"

The Red Sea

Over the course of January 29, the island of Ceylon dropped below the horizon, and the Nautilus, running at twenty miles an hour, slipped into that labyrinth of channels which separates the Maldives from the Laccadives. It even skirted Kittan Island, a bit of land of madreporic origin, discovered by Vasco da Gama in 1499 and one of the nineteen principal islands of that Laccadive archipelago, lying between 10° and 14°30′ north latitude and between 69° and 50°72′ east longitude.

By then we had covered sixteen thousand two hundred and twenty miles, or seven thousand five hundred leagues, since our starting point in the seas of Japan.

The next day, January 30, when the Nautilus rose back to the surface of the ocean, there was no longer any land in sight. She was steering north-northwest, heading toward that Sea of Oman which is hollowed out between Arabia and the Indian peninsula and serves as the outlet of the Persian Gulf.

This was plainly a dead end, with no way through. Where, then, was Captain Nemo taking us? I couldn't have said. And that failed to satisfy the Canadian, who that day asked me where we were going.

"We're going, Master Ned, wherever the captain's whim leads us."

"That whim," the Canadian answered, "can't take us far. The Persian Gulf has no way out, and if we go into it, we won't be long in doubling back on our own tracks."

"Well then, we'll double back, Master Land, and if after the Persian Gulf the Nautilus takes it into her head to visit the Red Sea, the Strait of Bab-el-Mandeb is always there to give her passage."

"I don't need to remind you, Professor," Ned Land replied, "that the Red Sea is just as closed off as the Gulf, since the Isthmus of Suez isn't pierced through yet, and even if it were, a mysterious boat like ours wouldn't risk itself in canals cut up by locks. So the Red Sea still isn't the road that will bring us back to Europe."

"But I never said we'd go back to Europe."

"Then what do you suppose?"

"I suppose that, after visiting these curious waters off Arabia and Egypt, the Nautilus will drop back down through the Indian Ocean, perhaps by way of the Mozambique Channel, perhaps off the Mascarenes, so as to reach the Cape of Good Hope."

"And once at the Cape of Good Hope?" the Canadian asked, with a very particular insistence.

"Well, then we'll push on into that Atlantic we don't yet know. Come now, Ned my friend — are you growing weary of this voyage under the seas? Are you getting jaded by the endlessly changing spectacle of underwater marvels? For my part, I'll see with the keenest regret the end of a voyage that so few men have ever been granted."

"But do you realize, Professor Aronnax," the Canadian answered, "that it will soon be three months we've been imprisoned aboard this Nautilus?"

"No, Ned, I don't realize it, I don't want to realize it, and I count neither the days nor the hours."

"But how does it end?"

"The end will come in its own good time. Besides, there's nothing we can do about it, and we're arguing to no purpose. If you were to come to me, my good Ned, and say, 'A chance to escape is offered to us,' then I'd discuss it with you. But that's not the case, and to speak to you frankly, I don't believe Captain Nemo will ever venture into European waters."

From this short exchange you can see that, fanatic of the Nautilus that I'd become, I had climbed right inside her commander's skin.

As for Ned Land, he ended the conversation with these words, delivered as a sort of monologue: "That's all very fine and good, but to my mind, where there's constraint, there's no more pleasure."

For four days, until February 3, the Nautilus toured the Sea of Oman at various speeds and various depths. She seemed to be moving at random, as though hesitating over which route to take, but she never crossed the Tropic of Cancer.

As we left that sea, we caught a brief glimpse of Muscat, the most important town in the land of Oman. I admired its strange look, ringed by the black rocks against which its houses and forts stand out in white. I made out the rounded dome of its mosques, the elegant point of its minarets, its cool, green terraces. But it was no more than a vision, and the Nautilus soon plunged beneath the dark waves of those waters.

Then she ran for a distance of six miles along the Arabian coasts of Mahrah and Hadhramaut, with their undulating line of mountains relieved here and there by a few ancient ruins. On February 5 we at last entered the Gulf of Aden, a true funnel driven into that neck of Bab-el-Mandeb, which pours the waters of India into the Red Sea.

On February 6 the Nautilus floated in sight of Aden, perched on a promontory joined to the mainland by a narrow isthmus, a sort of unassailable Gibraltar whose fortifications the English rebuilt after seizing it in 1839. I glimpsed the octagonal minarets of that city, which was once, according to the historian Edrisi, the richest and busiest trading depot on the whole coast.

I felt sure that Captain Nemo, having reached this point, would turn back; but I was mistaken, and to my great surprise he did nothing of the sort.

The following day, February 7, we entered the Strait of Bab-el-Mandeb, whose name means, in Arabic, "the Gate of Tears." Twenty miles wide, it is only fifty-two kilometers long, and for the Nautilus running at full speed, crossing it was scarcely the business of an hour. But I saw nothing, not even the island of Perim, whose position the British government has fortified to strengthen Aden. Too many English and French steamers of the lines from Suez to Bombay, to Calcutta, to Melbourne, to Bourbon, to Mauritius were plowing that narrow passage for the Nautilus to attempt to show herself there. So she prudently kept between two waters.

At last, at noon, we were cutting through the swells of the Red Sea.

The Red Sea — that lake famous in biblical tradition, which the rains scarcely freshen, which no important river waters, which an excessive evaporation pumps at unceasingly, and which loses each year a sheet of water a meter and a half deep! Singular gulf, which, closed off and reduced to the conditions of a lake, would perhaps have dried up entirely — inferior in this to its neighbors the Caspian or the Dead Sea, whose levels have dropped only to the point where their evaporation has come to equal exactly the sum of the waters received into them.

This Red Sea is two thousand six hundred kilometers long, with an average width of two hundred and forty. In the time of the Ptolemies and the Roman emperors it was the great commercial artery of the world, and the piercing of the isthmus will restore to it that ancient importance which the Suez railways have already partly brought back.

I would not even try to understand this caprice of Captain Nemo's that could have decided him to draw us into this gulf. But I approved without reservation of the Nautilus's having entered it. She took a moderate pace, now holding to the surface, now diving to avoid some ship, and so I was able to observe both the depths and the surface of this most curious sea.

On February 8, in the earliest hours of the day, Mocha came into view — now a ruined town whose walls fall at the mere sound of a cannon, sheltered here and there by a few green date palms. Once an important city, it had contained six public markets and twenty-six mosques, and its walls, defended by fourteen forts, formed a belt three kilometers around it.

Then the Nautilus drew nearer the African shores, where the sea is considerably deeper. There, between two waters of crystal limpidity, through the open panels, she let us gaze upon splendid thickets of dazzling coral and vast faces of rock clothed in a magnificent green fur of algae and wrack. What an indescribable spectacle, and what a variety of settings and landscapes at the flat crowns of those reefs and volcanic islets that border the Libyan coast! But it was toward the eastern shores that these branching forms appeared in all their beauty, shores the Nautilus was not slow to make for. It was off the coasts of the Tihamah — for there not only did these displays of zoophytes flower below the level of the sea, but they also formed picturesque tangles that unrolled ten fathoms above it; these last more fanciful, but less brightly colored than the ones below, whose freshness was kept up by the moist vitality of the waters.

What charming hours I spent thus at the salon window! What new specimens of the underwater flora and fauna I admired under the blaze of our electric beacon! Agaric-shaped mushroom corals, slate-colored sea anemones, among them the Thalassianthus aster; organ-pipe corals ranged like flutes and only awaiting the breath of the god Pan; shells peculiar to this sea, which settle into the madreporic hollows and whose base is twisted into a short spiral; and finally a thousand specimens of a coral animal I had not yet observed — the common sponge.

The class of sponges, first in the group of polyps, was in fact founded upon this curious product, whose usefulness is beyond dispute. The sponge is not a plant, as some naturalists still hold, but an animal of the lowest order, a coral animal lower even than coral itself. Its animal nature is not in doubt, and one cannot even adopt the opinion of the ancients, who regarded it as a being intermediate between the plant and the animal. I must say, however, that naturalists are not in agreement about the sponge's mode of organization. For some it is a coral animal; for others, such as Monsieur Milne-Edwards, it is an isolated and single individual.

The class of sponges contains about three hundred species, which are found in a great many seas, and even in certain watercourses, where they have been given the name "fluviatile." But their favorite waters are those of the Mediterranean, the Greek archipelago, the coast of Syria, and the Red Sea. There the fine soft sponges reproduce and grow, sponges whose value rises as high as a hundred and fifty francs — the blond sponge of Syria, the hard sponge of Barbary, and so on. But since I could not hope to study these zoophytes in the ports of the Levant, from which we were separated by the impassable Isthmus of Suez, I contented myself with observing them in the waters of the Red Sea.

So I called Conseil to my side, while the Nautilus, at an average depth of eight to nine meters, was slowly grazing all those lovely rocks of the eastern coast.

There grew sponges of every shape — sponges stalked, leafed, globular, fingered. They justified rather exactly those names of baskets, chalices, distaffs, elk's horns, lion's paw, peacock's tail, and Neptune's glove which the fishermen — more poets than the scholars — have given them. From their fibrous tissue, coated with a half-fluid gelatinous substance, there escaped without cease little threads of water which, after carrying life into each cell, were expelled from it by a contractile movement. This substance disappears after the death of the polyp and putrefies, releasing ammonia. Then nothing remains but those horny or gelatinous fibers of which the household sponge is composed, which takes on a reddish tint and is put to various uses, according to its degree of elasticity, permeability, or resistance to soaking.

These coral animals clung to the rocks, to the shells of mollusks, and even to the stems of water plants. They filled the smallest crevices, some spreading flat, others rising up or hanging like coral-like outgrowths. I taught Conseil that these sponges are fished in two ways, either by dredge or by hand. This last method, which requires the use of divers, is the better one, for by sparing the tissue of the coral animal it leaves it a far higher value.

The other zoophytes swarming near the sponges consisted chiefly of jellyfish of a very elegant species; the mollusks were represented by varieties of squid which, according to d'Orbigny, are peculiar to the Red Sea, and the reptiles by virgata turtles, belonging to the genus Chelonia, which furnished our table with a wholesome and delicate dish.

As for the fish, they were numerous and often remarkable. Here are the ones the Nautilus's nets most frequently brought aboard: rays, among them the limma, oval in shape and brick-colored, its body strewn with uneven blue spots and recognizable by its double toothed spine; the arnak with its silvery back; the pastinaca with its stippled tail; and the bockat, vast mantles two meters long that rippled between the waters; the aodon, utterly toothless, a kind of cartilaginous fish akin to the shark; the dromedary trunkfish, whose hump ends in a curved spine a foot and a half long; the ophidion, true morays with silvery tails, bluish backs, and brown pectoral fins bordered with a gray edging; the fiatola, a kind of butterfish zebra-striped with narrow bands of gold and decked out in the three colors of France; the garamit blennies, four decimeters long; superb caranx, decorated with seven crosswise bands of a fine black, blue and yellow fins, and gold and silver scales; centropodes; goldflame mullets with yellow heads; parrotfish, wrasses, triggerfish, gobies, and so forth; and a thousand other fish common to the oceans we had already crossed.

On February 9 the Nautilus was cruising the widest part of the Red Sea, the stretch that runs between Suakin on the western shore and Quonfodah on the eastern, a hundred and ninety miles across.

That day at noon, after taking our position, Captain Nemo came up onto the platform, where I happened to be. I promised myself I would not let him go back down without at least sounding him out about his further plans. He came over the moment he saw me, graciously offered me a cigar, and said:

"Well, Professor, does this Red Sea please you? Have you seen enough of the wonders it hides — its fish and its zoophytes, its flower beds of sponge and its forests of coral? Have you caught sight of the towns scattered along its shores?"

"Yes, Captain Nemo," I answered, "and the Nautilus has lent itself marvelously to the whole study. Ah, she's an intelligent boat!"

"Yes, sir — intelligent, daring, and invulnerable! She fears neither the terrible storms of the Red Sea, nor its currents, nor its reefs."

"Quite so," I said. "This sea is reckoned among the worst of them all, and if I'm not mistaken, in the time of the ancients its reputation was detestable."

"Detestable, Professor Aronnax. The Greek and Latin historians have nothing good to say of it. Strabo tells us it is particularly rough during the season of the Etesian winds and the rains. The Arab geographer Edrisi, who describes it under the name of the Gulf of Colzoum, reports that ships perished in great numbers on its sandbanks, and that no one dared sail it by night. It is, he claims, a sea prone to frightful hurricanes, strewn with inhospitable islands, and one that 'offers nothing good' either in its depths or on its surface. And indeed, that is the opinion you find in Arrian, in Agatharchides, and in Artemidorus."

"You can tell," I replied, "that those historians never sailed aboard the Nautilus."

"True," the captain answered, smiling, "and in that respect the moderns are no further along than the ancients. It took mankind whole centuries to discover the mechanical power of steam! Who knows whether, a hundred years from now, the world will see a second Nautilus? Progress is slow, Professor Aronnax."

"That's true," I said. "Your ship is a century ahead of its time — several centuries, perhaps. What a tragedy that such a secret should die with the man who invented it!"

Captain Nemo made no reply. After a few minutes' silence, he said:

"You were telling me about the opinion the old historians held of the dangers of sailing the Red Sea?"

"I was," I answered. "But weren't their fears exaggerated?"

"Yes and no, Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo replied — and he struck me as a man who knew "his Red Sea" through and through. "What is no longer dangerous for a modern vessel — well rigged, solidly built, master of its own course thanks to obedient steam — offered every kind of peril to the ships of the ancients. Picture those first navigators venturing out in boats made of planks sewn together with palm-fiber rope, caulked with powdered resin, and smeared with the grease of sea-dogs. They hadn't even the instruments to take their bearings; they sailed by dead reckoning through currents they barely knew. Under those conditions, shipwrecks were bound to be many. But in our day the steamers running between Suez and the southern seas have nothing more to fear from the rages of this gulf, monsoons and all. Their captains and passengers no longer prepare for the voyage with propitiatory sacrifices, and on their return they no longer go, decked in garlands and gilded ribbons, to thank the gods in the temple nearby."

"I grant you that," I said, "and steam does seem to have killed off gratitude in the hearts of sailors. But Captain, since you appear to have made a special study of this sea, can you tell me the origin of its name?"

"There are numerous explanations on that score, Professor Aronnax. Would you care to hear the opinion of a fourteenth-century chronicler?"

"Gladly."

"This fanciful fellow claims the name was given after the passage of the Israelites, when Pharaoh perished in the waters that closed again at the voice of Moses:

To mark that marvel, the whole sea Turned red, and ran with crimson dye; And ever after, none knew how To name it but the Red Sea now.

"A poet's explanation, Captain Nemo," I answered, "but I couldn't be satisfied with it. I'll ask you, then, for your own opinion."

"Here it is. To my mind, Professor Aronnax, this name 'Red Sea' should be seen as a translation of the Hebrew word Edrom, and if the ancients gave it that name, it was on account of the peculiar coloring of its waters."

"So far, though, I've seen nothing but clear water, with no particular tint at all."

"No doubt. But as you push toward the head of the gulf, you'll notice that singular appearance. I remember seeing the Bay of Tor entirely red, like a lake of blood."

"And you attribute that color to the presence of a microscopic alga?"

"Yes. It's a purple, mucilaginous matter produced by those tiny plantlets known as trichodesmia, of which it takes forty thousand to fill the space of a single square millimeter. Perhaps you'll come across some when we reach Tor."

"So then, Captain Nemo, this isn't the first time you've traveled the Red Sea aboard the Nautilus?"

"No, sir."

"Then, since you spoke a moment ago of the passage of the Israelites and the disaster of the Egyptians, may I ask whether you've found, beneath these waters, any trace of that great historical event?"

"No, Professor — and for an excellent reason."

"Which is?"

"That the very spot where Moses crossed with all his people is now so silted up that the camels can barely wet their legs there. You'll understand that my Nautilus wouldn't have enough water under her."

"And that spot?" I asked.

"That spot lies a little above Suez, in the arm that once formed a deep estuary, back when the Red Sea reached all the way to the Bitter Lakes. Now, whether the crossing was miraculous or not, the Israelites nonetheless passed there to reach the Promised Land, and Pharaoh's army perished at that exact place. So I believe that excavations carried out in those sands would turn up a great quantity of weapons and implements of Egyptian origin."

"That's obvious," I answered, "and one can only hope, for the archaeologists' sake, that such digs will be made sooner or later, once new towns spring up on that isthmus after the cutting of the Suez Canal. A canal quite useless for a ship like the Nautilus!"

"No doubt, but useful to the whole world," said Captain Nemo. "The ancients understood very well how valuable it would be to their commerce to establish a link between the Red Sea and the Mediterranean; but they never thought of digging a direct canal, and took the Nile as their go-between. In all likelihood the canal joining the Nile to the Red Sea was begun under Sesostris, if we're to believe tradition. What is certain is that, six hundred and fifteen years before Jesus Christ, Necho undertook the works of a canal fed by the waters of the Nile, across that plain of Egypt which faces Arabia. It could be traveled up in four days, and it was wide enough for two triremes to pass abreast. It was continued by Darius, son of Hystaspes, and probably completed by Ptolemy II. Strabo saw it in use for navigation; but the slightness of its gradient between its starting point, near Bubastis, and the Red Sea made it navigable only during a few months of the year. This canal served commerce until the age of the Antonines; then it was abandoned, silted up, restored by order of the Caliph Omar, and finally filled in for good in the year 761 or 762 by the Caliph Al-Mansor, who wanted to cut off supplies to Mohammed-ben-Abdoallah, then in revolt against him. During the Egyptian campaign, your General Bonaparte found traces of these works in the desert of Suez, and, caught by the tide, he nearly perished a few hours before reaching Hadjaroth — the very place where Moses had camped three thousand three hundred years before him."

"Well, Captain, what the ancients didn't dare undertake — this joining of the two seas, which will cut nine thousand kilometers off the route from Cadiz to the Indies — Monsieur de Lesseps has done, and before long he'll have turned Africa into one immense island."

"Yes, Professor Aronnax, and you have every right to be proud of your countryman. He is a man who does a nation more honor than the greatest of its captains! Like so many others, he began amid frustrations and rebuffs, but he triumphed, for he has the genius of willpower. And it is sad to think that this work, which should have been an international undertaking, which would have been enough to make a reign illustrious, will have succeeded only through the energy of a single man. So — all honor to Monsieur de Lesseps!"

"Yes, all honor to that great citizen," I answered, quite taken aback by the tone in which Captain Nemo had just spoken.

"Unfortunately," he went on, "I can't take you through this Suez Canal. But you'll be able to make out the long jetties of Port Said the day after tomorrow, when we're in the Mediterranean."

"In the Mediterranean!" I exclaimed.

"Yes, Professor. Does that surprise you?"

"What surprises me is to think we'll be there the day after tomorrow."

"Really?"

"Yes, Captain — though I ought to be used to being surprised by nothing since I came aboard your ship!"

"But surprised at what, exactly?"

"At the appalling speed you'll have to drive the Nautilus to, if she's to find herself in the open Mediterranean the day after tomorrow, having rounded all of Africa and doubled the Cape of Good Hope!"

"And who told you she'll round Africa, Professor? Who said anything about doubling the Cape of Good Hope?"

"And yet, unless the Nautilus sails on dry land and passes over the isthmus —"

"Or under it, Professor Aronnax."

"Under it?"

"Just so," Captain Nemo replied calmly. "Long ago, nature made beneath this tongue of land what men are only now making on its surface."

"What! There's a passage?"

"Yes, an underground passage that I've named the Arabian Tunnel. It begins below Suez and comes out in the Gulf of Pelusium."

"But this isthmus is nothing but shifting sand?"

"Down to a certain depth. But at only fifty meters you strike an unshakable bed of rock."

"And was it by chance that you discovered this passage?" I asked, more astonished by the moment.

"Chance and reasoning, Professor — and reasoning even more than chance."

"Captain, I'm listening, but my ear resists what it hears."

"Ah, sir! Aures habent, et non audient — that has been true in every age. Not only does this passage exist, but I've made use of it several times. Otherwise I'd never have ventured today into this dead end of the Red Sea."

"Is it indiscreet to ask how you discovered this tunnel?"

"Sir," the captain answered, "there can be nothing secret between people who are never to part."

I let the insinuation pass and waited for Captain Nemo's account.

"Professor," he said, "it was a simple piece of naturalist's reasoning that led me to discover this passage, which I alone know. I had noticed that in the Red Sea and in the Mediterranean there exist a certain number of fish of absolutely identical species — snake-eels, butterfish, rainbow wrasse, parrotfish, sand-smelts, flying fish. Certain of this fact, I asked myself whether there wasn't some communication between the two seas. If there was, the underground current would necessarily run from the Red Sea to the Mediterranean, simply because of the difference in their levels. So I caught a great many fish in the neighborhood of Suez. I slipped a copper ring over their tails and threw them back into the sea. A few months later, off the coast of Syria, I recovered several specimens of my fish, still wearing their telltale rings. The communication between the two seas was thus proved to me. I searched for it with my Nautilus, I found it, I ventured into it — and before long, Professor, you too will have passed through my Arabian Tunnel!"

The Arabian Tunnel

That same day I reported to Conseil and Ned Land the part of the conversation that concerned them directly. When I told them that within two days we would be in the waters of the Mediterranean, Conseil clapped his hands, but the Canadian only shrugged.

"An underwater tunnel!" he cried. "A passage between the two seas! Who ever heard of such a thing?"

"Friend Ned," Conseil replied, "had you ever heard of the Nautilus? No! And yet here it is. So don't shrug so lightly, and don't dismiss a thing just because you've never heard of it."

"We'll see about that!" Ned Land shot back, shaking his head. "After all, I'd like nothing better than to believe in this captain and his passage, and heaven grant that he really does take us into the Mediterranean."

That very evening, at 21°30′ north latitude, the Nautilus, riding on the surface, drew near the Arabian coast. I made out Jeddah, that important trading port for Egypt, Syria, Turkey, and the Indies. I could see fairly clearly the whole sweep of its buildings, the ships moored along the quays, and those whose draft forced them to anchor out in the roadstead. The sun, rather low on the horizon, struck the houses of the town full on and set off their whiteness. Beyond them, a few huts of wood and reeds marked the quarter where the Bedouins lived.

Soon Jeddah faded into the shadows of evening, and the Nautilus slipped back beneath the faintly phosphorescent water.

The next day, February 10, several ships appeared, running on a course opposite to ours. The Nautilus resumed its underwater navigation; but at noon, at the moment of taking our position, the sea being empty, it rose again to its waterline.

With Ned and Conseil, I came and sat on the platform. The coast to the east showed only as a mass barely sketched in a damp haze.

Leaning against the sides of the dinghy, we were chatting about this and that when Ned Land, stretching his hand toward a point on the sea, said to me:

"Do you see something out there, Professor?"

"No, Ned," I answered, "but I don't have your eyes, as you know."

"Look hard," Ned went on. "There, off the starboard bow, about level with the beacon! You don't see a mass that seems to be moving?"

"Yes," I said, after watching closely, "I can make out a sort of long, blackish body on the surface of the water."

"Another Nautilus?" said Conseil.

"No," answered the Canadian, "unless I'm badly mistaken, that's some sea animal."

"Are there whales in the Red Sea?" Conseil asked.

"Yes, my boy," I replied, "you come across them now and then."

"That's no whale," Ned Land put in, never taking his eyes off the thing he'd spotted. "Whales and I are old acquaintances, and I couldn't mistake the look of one."

"Let's wait," said Conseil. "The Nautilus is heading that way, and before long we'll know what to make of it."

Sure enough, that blackish object was soon no more than a mile from us. It looked like a great reef stranded in the open sea. What was it? I still couldn't say.

"Ah! It's moving! It's diving!" cried Ned Land. "A thousand devils! What kind of animal can it be? Its tail isn't forked like a whale's or a sperm whale's, and its fins look like stumps of limbs."

"But then—" I began.

"There now," the Canadian went on, "it's rolled onto its back, and it's lifting its breasts into the air!"

"It's a mermaid," cried Conseil, "a real mermaid, saving Master's presence."

That word mermaid put me on the right track, and I understood that this animal belonged to that order of sea creatures which legend has turned into mermaids, half woman and half fish.

"No," I told Conseil, "it's not a mermaid, but a curious creature of which barely a few specimens survive in the Red Sea. It's a dugong."

"Order Sirenia, group Pisciforma, subclass Monodelphia, class Mammalia, phylum Vertebrata," answered Conseil.

And when Conseil had spoken like that, there was nothing left to add.

Meanwhile Ned Land kept watching. His eyes were shining with greed at the sight of the animal. His hand seemed ready to harpoon it. You would have said he was waiting for the moment to throw himself into the sea and attack it in its own element.

"Oh, Professor," he said to me in a voice trembling with emotion, "I've never killed one of those."

The whole harpooner was in that one word.

At that instant Captain Nemo appeared on the platform. He saw the dugong. He read the Canadian's whole attitude, and, addressing him directly:

"If you were holding a harpoon right now, Master Land, wouldn't it be burning a hole in your hand?"

"Just as you say, sir."

"And it wouldn't displease you to take up your fisherman's trade again for a day, and add this cetacean to the list of those you've already struck?"

"It wouldn't displease me in the least."

"Well then, you may try."

"Thank you, sir," Ned Land replied, his eyes blazing.

"Only," the captain went on, "I urge you not to miss this animal — and that's for your own sake."

"Is this dugong dangerous to attack?" I asked, in spite of the Canadian's shrug.

"Yes, sometimes," the captain answered. "The animal turns on its attackers and capsizes their boat. But for Master Land, there's no fear of that danger. His eye is quick, his arm is sure. If I tell him not to miss this dugong, it's because the creature is rightly considered fine game, and I know Master Land is no enemy of a good cut of meat."

"Ah!" said the Canadian. "So this beast treats itself to the luxury of being good to eat as well?"

"Yes, Master Land. Its flesh — real meat — is extremely prized, and all through Malaysia it's reserved for the tables of princes. That's why this excellent animal is hunted so relentlessly that, like the manatee, its cousin, it grows rarer and rarer."

"Then, Captain," said Conseil in all seriousness, "if by chance this one were the last of its kind, wouldn't it be right to spare it in the interest of science?"

"Perhaps," retorted the Canadian, "but in the interest of the kitchen, it's better to hunt it down."

"Have at it, then, Master Land," answered Captain Nemo.

At that moment seven crewmen, mute and impassive as always, climbed onto the platform. One carried a harpoon and a line like those used by whale fishermen. The dinghy was unshipped, hauled from its socket, and launched into the sea. Six oarsmen took their places on the benches, and the coxswain manned the tiller. Ned, Conseil, and I sat down in the stern.

"Aren't you coming, Captain?" I asked.

"No, sir, but I wish you a good hunt."

The dinghy shoved off and, driven by its six oars, headed quickly toward the dugong, which was floating then some two miles from the Nautilus.

When we had come within a few cable-lengths of the creature, the boat slowed, and the oars dipped noiselessly into the calm water. Ned Land, harpoon in hand, went to take his stand upright at the bow of the dinghy. The harpoon used to strike a whale is ordinarily attached to a very long rope that pays out quickly when the wounded animal drags it along. But here the rope measured no more than ten fathoms or so, and its end was simply made fast to a little barrel that, floating, would mark the dugong's course beneath the water.

I had risen and could clearly study the Canadian's adversary. This dugong — which also goes by the name halicore — closely resembled the manatee. Its oblong body ended in a very long tail fin, and its side flippers ended in what were truly fingers. Its difference from the manatee lay in this: its upper jaw was armed with two long, pointed teeth that formed diverging tusks on either side.

This dugong that Ned Land was preparing to attack had colossal dimensions; its length was at least seven meters. It wasn't moving, and it seemed to be asleep on the surface of the waves — a circumstance that made its capture easier.

The dinghy edged cautiously to within three fathoms of the animal. The oars hung suspended in their rowlocks. I half rose. Ned Land, his body thrown a little back, brandished his harpoon with a practiced hand.

Suddenly a hiss was heard, and the dugong vanished. The harpoon, flung with all Ned's force, had no doubt struck nothing but water.

"A thousand devils!" cried the Canadian in a fury. "I missed it!"

"No," I said, "the animal's wounded — there's its blood — but your weapon didn't stay lodged in its body."

"My harpoon! My harpoon!" cried Ned Land.

The sailors bent to their oars again, and the coxswain steered the boat toward the floating barrel. The harpoon fished up, the dinghy set off in pursuit of the animal.

The dugong came back to the surface from time to time to breathe. Its wound hadn't weakened it, for it shot along with extreme speed. The boat, worked by vigorous arms, flew after it. Several times it closed to within a few fathoms, and the Canadian held himself ready to strike; but the dugong would slip away with a sudden dive, and there was no reaching it.

You can judge the rage that boiled up in the impatient Ned Land. He hurled the most forceful oaths in the English language at the wretched animal. For my part, I'd gotten no further than vexation at seeing the dugong foil all our tricks.

We chased it without letup for an hour, and I was beginning to think it would be very hard to take, when the animal was seized by an unlucky notion of revenge that it would soon have cause to repent. It turned back on the dinghy to attack us in its turn.

This maneuver didn't escape the Canadian.

"Look out!" he said.

The coxswain spoke a few words in his strange language, no doubt warning his men to be on their guard.

The dugong, having come within twenty feet of the dinghy, stopped, snuffed the air sharply through its vast nostrils — pierced not at the tip but at the upper part of its muzzle — then, gathering its momentum, hurled itself at us.

The dinghy couldn't avoid the shock. Half overturned, it shipped a ton or two of water that had to be bailed out; but thanks to the coxswain's skill, taken at an angle instead of head-on, it didn't capsize. Ned Land, clinging to the stem, jabbed harpoon-blow after harpoon-blow into the gigantic animal, which, its teeth sunk into the gunwale, lifted the boat out of the water the way a lion lifts a young deer. We were thrown one on top of another, and I hardly know how the adventure would have ended if the Canadian, relentless against the beast, hadn't at last struck it to the heart.

I heard the grinding of teeth on the metal plating, and the dugong disappeared, dragging the harpoon down with it. But soon the barrel bobbed back to the surface, and a few moments later the animal's body appeared, rolled over on its back. The dinghy came up to it, took it in tow, and made for the Nautilus.

It took powerful tackle to hoist the dugong onto the platform. It weighed five thousand kilograms. It was cut up under the eyes of the Canadian, who insisted on following every detail of the operation. That same day the steward served me at dinner a few slices of that flesh, skillfully prepared by the ship's cook. I found it excellent, and even superior to veal, if not to beef.

The next day, February 11, the Nautilus's larder was enriched again with delicate game. A flock of sea swallows settled on the Nautilus. They were a species of Sterna nilotica, peculiar to Egypt, with a black beak, a gray dappled head, the eye ringed with white spots, the back, wings, and tail grayish, the belly and throat white, and red feet. We also caught a few dozen Nile ducks, wild birds of a high flavor, whose neck and crown are white and speckled with black.

The Nautilus's speed was moderate now. It was moving along at a saunter, so to speak. I noticed that the water of the Red Sea grew less and less salty the closer we came to Suez.

Toward five in the evening, we sighted Cape Ras Muhammad to the north. It's this cape that forms the tip of Arabia Petraea, held between the Gulf of Suez and the Gulf of Aqaba.

The Nautilus entered the Strait of Jubal, which leads to the Gulf of Suez. I could clearly see a high mountain dominating Ras Muhammad between the two gulfs. It was Mount Horeb — that Sinai on whose summit Moses saw God face to face, and which the mind pictures forever crowned with lightning.

At six o'clock, the Nautilus, now floating, now submerged, passed off Tor, set at the bottom of a bay whose waters looked tinged with red — an observation Captain Nemo had already made. Then night fell, in the midst of a heavy silence broken now and then by the cry of a pelican and a few night birds, by the sound of the surf fretted against the rocks, or by the distant groan of a steamer beating the waters of the gulf with its ringing blades.

From eight to nine o'clock, the Nautilus stayed a few meters beneath the surface. By my reckoning, we had to be very near Suez. Through the panels of the salon, I could see the rocky bottom brilliantly lit by our electric light. It seemed to me that the strait was growing narrower and narrower.

At a quarter past nine, the boat having come back to the surface, I climbed onto the platform. Too impatient to pass through Captain Nemo's tunnel, I couldn't keep still, and I sought to breathe the fresh night air.

Soon, in the darkness, I made out a pale fire, half washed out by the haze, glowing a mile from us.

"A floating lighthouse," said a voice near me.

I turned and recognized the captain.

"That's the floating light of Suez," he went on. "It won't be long before we reach the mouth of the tunnel."

"The entrance can't be easy?"

"No, sir. And so I make it my habit to stay in the helmsman's cage and direct the maneuver myself. And now, if you'll go below, Professor Aronnax, the Nautilus is going to sink beneath the waves, and it won't come back to the surface until we've passed through the Arabian Tunnel."

I followed Captain Nemo. The hatch closed, the water tanks filled, and the vessel sank some ten meters.

Just as I was about to head back to my room, the captain stopped me.

"Professor," he said, "would you care to come with me into the pilot's cage?"

"I didn't dare ask you," I answered.

"Come along, then. This way you'll see everything there is to see of a voyage that's at once underground and underwater."

Captain Nemo led me toward the central stairway. Halfway up the steps, he opened a door, followed the upper gangways, and reached the pilot's cage, which, as we know, rose at the end of the platform.

It was a cabin measuring six feet on each side, much like those the helmsmen occupy on the steamboats of the Mississippi or the Hudson. In the middle a wheel was worked, set vertically, geared to the wheel ropes of the rudder that ran all the way to the stern of the Nautilus. Four portholes of lens-shaped glass, set into the walls of the cabin, let the man at the helm look out in every direction.
