The cabin was dark; but soon my eyes grew used to the gloom, and I made out the pilot, a powerful man whose hands rested on the rim of the wheel. Outside, the sea appeared vividly lit by the beacon that shone behind the cabin, at the other end of the platform.

"Now," said Captain Nemo, "let's look for our passage."

Electric wires linked the helmsman's cage with the engine room, and from there the captain could signal to his Nautilus both direction and speed at once. He pressed a metal button, and at once the speed of the propeller dropped sharply.

I watched in silence the high, sheer wall we were skirting just then — the immovable base of the sandy massif of the coast. We followed it that way for an hour, only a few meters off. Captain Nemo never took his eyes off the compass hanging in the cabin between its two concentric rings. At a mere gesture from him, the helmsman altered the Nautilus's course from one instant to the next.

I had placed myself at the port porthole, and I could see magnificent substructures of coral, zoophytes, algae, and crustaceans waving their enormous claws, which reached out from the crevices of the rock.

At a quarter past ten, Captain Nemo took the helm himself. A wide gallery, black and deep, opened before us. The Nautilus plunged boldly into it. An unfamiliar rushing sound made itself heard along its flanks. It was the waters of the Red Sea that the slope of the tunnel was hurling toward the Mediterranean. The Nautilus followed the torrent, swift as an arrow, despite the efforts of its engine, which, to resist, was beating the water with its propeller reversed.

On the narrow walls of the passage I could see nothing now but blazing streaks, straight lines, furrows of fire traced by our speed under the glare of the electricity. My heart was pounding, and I pressed it down with my hand.

At ten thirty-five, Captain Nemo let go of the wheel, and turning to me:

"The Mediterranean," he said.

In less than twenty minutes, the Nautilus, carried along by that torrent, had crossed the Isthmus of Suez.

The Greek Archipelago

The next day, February 12, at daybreak, the Nautilus rose to the surface of the waves. I rushed up onto the platform. Three miles to the south the hazy silhouette of Pelusium took shape. A torrent had carried us from one sea to the other. But that tunnel, easy enough to run down, must surely be impossible to climb back up.

Toward seven o'clock, Ned and Conseil joined me. These two inseparable companions had slept soundly, without troubling themselves in the least over the exploits of the Nautilus.

"Well, Professor," asked the Canadian, in a tone of light mockery, "and this Mediterranean of yours?"

"We're floating on the surface of it, friend Ned."

"What!" said Conseil. "This very night?"

"Yes, this very night, in the space of a few minutes, we crossed that uncrossable isthmus."

"I don't believe a word of it," the Canadian answered.

"Then you're wrong, Master Land," I replied. "That low coast curving away to the south is the coast of Egypt."

"Tell that to someone else, Professor," retorted the stubborn Canadian.

"But since Master says so," Conseil told him, "then Master must be believed."

"What's more, Ned, Captain Nemo did me the honors of his tunnel himself, and I was standing right beside him, in the pilot's cage, while he steered the Nautilus through that narrow passage with his own hands."

"You hear that, Ned?" said Conseil.

"And you, with those sharp eyes of yours," I added, "you can make out the jetties of Port Said stretching out into the sea."

The Canadian looked closely.

"You're right at that," he said. "You're right, Professor, and that captain of yours is a master among men. We're in the Mediterranean. Good. Then let's talk, if you please, about our own little affairs — but in such a way that no one can overhear us."

I saw well enough what the Canadian was driving at. In any case, I thought it was better to talk, since he wanted to, and the three of us went and sat down near the beacon, where we were less exposed to the damp spray of the waves.

"Now then, Ned, we're listening," I said. "What do you have to tell us?"

"What I have to tell you is very simple," the Canadian answered. "We're in Europe, and before Captain Nemo's whims drag us off to the bottom of the polar seas or carry us back to Oceania, I'm asking to leave the Nautilus."

I admit this discussion with the Canadian always put me in an awkward spot. I had no wish whatsoever to hinder my companions' freedom, and yet I felt not the slightest desire to leave Captain Nemo. Thanks to him, thanks to his machine, I was completing my underwater studies day by day, rewriting my book of the ocean depths in the very midst of its element. Would I ever again find such a chance to observe the wonders of the sea? No, surely not! And so I couldn't reconcile myself to the idea of abandoning the Nautilus before our cycle of investigations was done.

"Friend Ned," I said, "answer me honestly. Are you bored aboard? Do you regret that fate threw you into Captain Nemo's hands?"

The Canadian went a few moments without answering. Then, folding his arms:

"Honestly," he said, "I don't regret this voyage under the seas. I'll be glad to have made it. But to have made it, it has to end. That's how I feel."

"It will end, Ned."

"Where and when?"

"Where? I have no idea. When? I can't say — or rather, I suppose it will finish when these seas have nothing more to teach us. Everything that begins in this world necessarily has an end."

"I think as Master does," Conseil put in, "and it's quite possible that after sailing across all the seas of the globe, Captain Nemo will give the three of us the boot."

"The boot!" cried the Canadian. "You mean a boot, I hope!"

"Let's not exaggerate, Master Land," I went on. "We have nothing to fear from the captain, but I don't share Conseil's ideas either. We're masters of the Nautilus's secrets, and I don't expect its commander, just to give us back our freedom, will resign himself to letting those secrets roam the world with us."

"Then what do you hope for?" asked the Canadian.

"That circumstances will arise which we can — which we must — take advantage of, just as well six months from now as this minute."

"Oh ho!" said Ned Land. "And where will we be six months from now, if you please, Professor?"

"Perhaps here, perhaps in China. You know the Nautilus is a swift traveler. It crosses the oceans the way a swallow crosses the air, or an express train crosses the continents. It has no fear of frequented waters. Who's to say it won't come alongside the coasts of France, England, or America, where an escape could be attempted just as favorably as here?"

"Professor Aronnax," the Canadian answered, "your arguments are rotten at the root. You speak in the future: 'We'll be there! We'll be here!' Me, I speak in the present: 'We are here, and we'd better make the most of it.'"

I was hard pressed by Ned Land's logic, and I felt myself beaten on that ground. I no longer knew what arguments to bring forward in my own favor.

"Professor," Ned went on, "let's suppose — impossible as it is — that Captain Nemo offers you your freedom this very day. Would you accept?"

"I don't know," I answered.

"And if he adds that this offer he's making you today, he won't renew it later on, would you accept then?"

I didn't answer.

"And what does friend Conseil think?" asked Ned Land.

"Friend Conseil," the worthy fellow answered calmly, "friend Conseil has nothing to say. He is entirely disinterested in the question. Like his master, like his comrade Ned, he is a bachelor. No wife, no relatives, no children wait for him back home. He is in Master's service, he thinks as Master thinks, he speaks as Master speaks, and, much to his regret, no one can count on him to make a majority. Only two people are here in contention: Master on the one side, Ned Land on the other. That said, friend Conseil is listening, and he stands ready to keep the score."

I couldn't help smiling to see Conseil annihilate his own personality so completely. Deep down, the Canadian must have been delighted not to have him against him.

"Then, Professor," said Ned Land, "since Conseil doesn't exist, let's argue it out between the two of us. I've spoken, you've heard me. What have you got to answer?"

Clearly I had to come to a conclusion, and evasions were repugnant to me.

"Friend Ned," I said, "here is my answer. You're right and I'm wrong, and my arguments can't stand up against yours. There's no counting on Captain Nemo's goodwill. The most ordinary prudence forbids him to set us free. By the same token, prudence demands that we seize the first chance to leave the Nautilus."

"Well said, Professor Aronnax. Now that's talking wisely."

"Only," I said, "one observation, just one. The chance has to be a serious one. Our first attempt to escape has to succeed; because if it fails, we won't get another opening to try again, and Captain Nemo won't forgive us."

"That's all quite true," the Canadian answered. "But your observation applies to any escape attempt, whether it happens in two years or in two days. So the question is always this: if a favorable chance presents itself, we have to seize it."

"Agreed. And now, Ned, will you tell me what you mean by a favorable chance?"

"It would be one that, on some dark night, brings the Nautilus a short distance off a European coast."

"And you'd try to save yourself by swimming for it?"

"Yes, if we were close enough to a shore, and if the ship were floating on the surface. No, if we were far out, and if the ship were running underwater."

"And in that case?"

"In that case, I'd try to get hold of the dinghy. I know how it's handled. We'd slip inside, and with the bolts drawn, we'd rise to the surface without so much as the helmsman, stationed up forward, noticing our escape."

"Good, Ned. Then keep watch for that chance; but don't forget that a failure would be the end of us."

"I won't forget it, Professor."

"And now, Ned, do you want to know my whole thought on your plan?"

"Gladly, Professor Aronnax."

"Well, I think — I won't say I hope — I think that this favorable chance will never present itself."

"Why is that?"

"Because Captain Nemo can't hide from himself that we haven't given up hope of recovering our freedom, and he'll stay on his guard, above all in the seas and within sight of the coasts of Europe."

"I'm of Master's opinion," said Conseil.

"We'll see about that," answered Ned Land, shaking his head with a determined air.

"And now, Ned Land," I added, "let's leave it there. Not another word on any of this. The day you're ready, warn us and we'll follow you. I put myself completely in your hands."

That was how this conversation ended — a conversation that was to have such grave consequences later on. And I must say now that events seemed to confirm my predictions, to the Canadian's great despair. Did Captain Nemo distrust us in these frequented seas, or did he simply want to keep out of sight of the countless ships of every nation that furrow the Mediterranean? I don't know, but most of the time he kept us between two waters and well out from the coasts. Either the Nautilus surfaced, showing nothing but the pilot's cage, or it slipped away to great depths, for between the Greek archipelago and Asia Minor we found no bottom at two thousand meters.

And so it was only through a line of Virgil that I came to know of the island of Karpathos, one of the Sporades, when Captain Nemo quoted it to me, laying his finger on a point of the planisphere:

Est in Carpathio Neptuni gurgite vates Coeruleus Proteus...

It was indeed the ancient dwelling of Proteus, the old shepherd of Neptune's flocks — now the island of Scarpanto, lying between Rhodes and Crete. I saw nothing of it but the granite foundations through the glass of the salon.

The next day, February 14, I resolved to spend a few hours studying the fishes of the Archipelago; but for some reason or other, the panels stayed hermetically shut. Taking the bearings of the Nautilus's course, I noticed that it was heading toward Candia, the ancient island of Crete. At the moment when I had embarked on the Abraham Lincoln, that island had just risen up in its entirety against Turkish despotism. But what had become of the insurrection since that time, I had absolutely no idea, and it certainly wasn't Captain Nemo, cut off from all communication with the land, who could have told me.

So I made no allusion to that event when, that evening, I found myself alone with him in the salon. Besides, he struck me as taciturn, preoccupied. Then, contrary to his habits, he ordered both panels of the salon opened, and, moving from one to the other, he studied the mass of water attentively. To what end? I couldn't guess, and for my part I spent my time studying the fish that passed before my eyes.

Among others, I noticed those aphysa gobies mentioned by Aristotle and commonly known as "sea loaches," which one meets particularly in the salt waters bordering the delta of the Nile. Near them unrolled some half-phosphorescent pagres, a kind of sea bream that the Egyptians ranked among their sacred animals, and whose arrival in the river's waters — announcing its fertile flooding — was celebrated with religious ceremonies. I noted as well some cheilines three decimeters long, bony fish with transparent scales, their livid color mixed with red spots; they are great eaters of marine plants, which gives them an exquisite flavor; and so these cheilines were much sought after by the gourmets of ancient Rome, and their entrails, dressed with the milt of moray eels, the brains of peacocks, and the tongues of flamingos, made up that divine dish which so delighted Vitellius. Another inhabitant of these seas caught my attention and brought back to my mind all the memories of antiquity. This was the remora, which travels attached to the belly of sharks; according to the ancients, this little fish, clinging to the keel of a ship, could stop it in its course, and one of them, holding back Antony's vessel during the Battle of Actium, thus helped Augustus to victory. On what slender threads the destinies of nations hang! I also observed some admirable anthias, which belong to the order of the lutjanids, fish sacred to the Greeks, who credited them with the power to drive sea monsters from the waters they frequented; their name means "flower," and they justified it by their shimmering colors, their shades ranging across the whole scale of red from the paleness of rose to the blaze of ruby, and by the fleeting reflections that shot moiré patterns across their dorsal fins. My eyes couldn't tear themselves away from these marvels of the sea, when suddenly they were struck by an unexpected apparition.

In the midst of the waters, a man appeared — a diver, carrying a leather pouch at his belt. This was no corpse abandoned to the waves. This was a living man, swimming with a vigorous stroke of one arm, vanishing now and again to go breathe at the surface, then plunging back down at once.

I turned toward Captain Nemo, and in a stirred voice:

"A man! A castaway!" I cried. "We must save him at all costs!"

The captain didn't answer me and went to lean against the glass.

The man had come closer, and, his face pressed to the panel, he was looking in at us.

To my utter astonishment, Captain Nemo made him a sign. The diver answered him with his hand, rose immediately toward the surface of the sea, and did not reappear.

"Don't be alarmed," the captain told me. "That's Nicolas, of Cape Matapan, nicknamed the Pesce. He's well known throughout the Cyclades. A bold diver! Water is his element, and he lives in it more than on land, forever going from one island to another, all the way to Crete."

"You know him, Captain?"

"Why not, Professor Aronnax?"

That said, Captain Nemo walked over to a cabinet standing near the left-hand panel of the salon. Beside this cabinet I saw an iron-bound chest, its lid bearing on a copper plate the monogram of the Nautilus, with its motto, Mobilis in Mobili.

At that moment the captain, without concerning himself over my presence, opened the cabinet — a sort of safe that held a great number of ingots.

They were ingots of gold. Where did this precious metal come from, this metal that represented an enormous sum? Where was the captain gathering this gold, and what was he going to do with it?

I didn't utter a word. I watched. Captain Nemo took these ingots one by one and arranged them methodically in the chest, which he filled completely. I estimated that it held then more than a thousand kilograms of gold — that is, close to five million francs.

The chest was securely closed, and the captain wrote an address on its lid in characters that must have belonged to modern Greek.

This done, Captain Nemo pressed a button whose wire connected with the crew's quarters. Four men appeared, and, not without difficulty, they pushed the chest out of the salon. Then I heard them hoisting it by means of tackle up the iron staircase.

At that moment Captain Nemo turned to me:

"You were saying, Professor?" he asked.

"I was saying nothing, Captain."

"Then, Professor, you'll permit me to wish you good evening."

And with that, Captain Nemo left the salon.

I went back to my cabin greatly intrigued, as one can imagine. I tried in vain to sleep. I kept searching for some connection between the appearance of that diver and this chest full of gold. Soon, from certain movements of rolling and pitching, I felt that the Nautilus was leaving the lower layers and coming back to the surface of the waters.

Then I heard the sound of footsteps on the platform. I understood that the dinghy was being unfastened and launched into the sea. It knocked for an instant against the flanks of the Nautilus, and then all sound ceased.

Two hours later, the same sound, the same comings and goings repeated themselves. The boat, hoisted aboard, was fitted back into its socket, and the Nautilus plunged once more beneath the waves.

So those millions had been carried to their destination. To what point on the continent? Who was Captain Nemo's correspondent?

The next day I recounted to Conseil and the Canadian the events of that night, which excited my curiosity to the highest degree. My companions were no less surprised than I was.

"But where does he get these millions?" asked Ned Land.

To that, no answer was possible. I went into the salon after breakfast and set to work. Until five in the evening, I was writing up my notes. At that moment — was I to put it down to some personal disposition? — I felt an extreme heat, and I had to take off my coat of byssus. An incomprehensible effect, for we were not at high latitudes, and besides, the Nautilus, submerged, should have felt no rise in temperature at all. I looked at the pressure gauge. It showed a depth of sixty feet, a depth the warmth of the atmosphere could never have reached.

I went on with my work, but the temperature rose to the point of becoming intolerable.

"Could there be a fire aboard?" I asked myself.

I was about to leave the salon when Captain Nemo came in. He went up to the thermometer, consulted it, and, turning back to me:

"Forty-two degrees," he said.

"I feel it, Captain," I answered, "and if this heat increases even a little, we won't be able to bear it."

"Oh, Professor, this heat won't increase unless we want it to."

"So you can moderate it at will?"

"No, but I can move away from the source that produces it."

"So it's external?"

"Of course. We're floating in a current of boiling water."

"Is it possible?" I cried.

"Look."

The panels opened, and I saw the sea gone entirely white all around the Nautilus. A smoke of sulfurous vapors was unrolling through the middle of the waves, which bubbled like the water of a boiler. I laid my hand against one of the glass panes, but the heat was such that I had to pull it away.

"Where are we?" I asked.

"Near the island of Santorini, Professor," the captain answered, "and precisely in that channel which separates Nea Kameni from Palea Kameni. I wanted to give you the curious spectacle of an undersea eruption."

"I thought," I said, "that the formation of these new islands was finished."

"Nothing is ever finished in volcanic waters," answered Captain Nemo, "and the globe is forever being worked here by its underground fires. Already, in the nineteenth year of our era, according to Cassiodorus and Pliny, a new island, Theia the divine, appeared in the very place where these islets have recently formed. Then it sank beneath the waves, only to show itself again in the year sixty-nine and sink once more. From that time down to our own day, the plutonian labor was suspended. But on February 3, 1866, a new islet, which they named the islet of George, emerged amid the sulfurous vapors near Nea Kameni, and welded itself to it on the sixth of the same month. Seven days later, on February 13, the islet Aphroessa appeared, leaving a channel of ten meters between itself and Nea Kameni. I was in these seas when the phenomenon occurred, and I was able to observe all its phases. The islet Aphroessa, round in shape, measured three hundred feet in diameter by thirty feet in height. It was made of black glassy lavas, mixed with fragments of feldspar. Finally, on March 10, a smaller islet, called Reka, showed itself near Nea Kameni, and since then these three islets, welded together, form no more than a single, selfsame island."

"And the channel where we are at this moment?" I asked.

"Here it is," answered Captain Nemo, showing me a map of the Archipelago. "You see that I've marked the new islets on it."

"But this channel will fill in one day?"

"That's probable, Professor Aronnax, for since 1866, eight little islets of lava have surged up facing the port of Saint Nicholas on Palea Kameni. It's therefore obvious that Nea and Palea will join together before long. If, in the middle of the Pacific, it's the infusoria that build the continents, here it's the eruptive phenomena. Look, Professor, look at the labor being accomplished beneath these waves."

I came back toward the glass. The Nautilus had stopped moving. The heat was becoming intolerable. From white as it had been, the sea was turning red, a coloration due to the presence of an iron salt. Despite the hermetic sealing of the salon, an unbearable sulfurous odor was escaping, and I could make out scarlet flames whose brightness killed the glare of the electric light.

I was drenched in sweat, I was suffocating, I was going to cook. Yes, truly, I felt myself cooking!

"We can't stay any longer in this boiling water," I said to the captain.

"No, that wouldn't be prudent," answered the impassive Nemo.

An order was given. The Nautilus came about and drew away from that furnace it couldn't brave with impunity. A quarter of an hour later, we were breathing at the surface of the waves.

The thought then came to me that if Ned Land had chosen these waters to carry out our escape, we would never have come out alive from this sea of fire.

The next day, February 16, we left that basin which, between Rhodes and Alexandria, counts depths of three thousand meters, and the Nautilus, passing well off Cerigo, left the Greek archipelago behind, after doubling Cape Matapan.

The Mediterranean in Forty-Eight Hours

The Mediterranean — the blue sea above all others, the "Great Sea" of the Hebrews, "the sea" of the Greeks, the mare nostrum of the Romans; fringed with orange groves, aloes, cactus, and maritime pines, sweet with the scent of myrtle, framed by rugged mountains, steeped in a pure and transparent air yet endlessly worked upon by the fires of the earth — is a whole world unto itself. It is here, on its shores and its waters, Michelet says, that a man is tempered anew in one of the mightiest climates on the globe.

But beautiful as it is, I could catch no more than a hurried glimpse of this basin, whose surface covers two million square kilometers. Captain Nemo's personal knowledge failed me here as well, for the enigmatic man did not appear once during this high-speed crossing. I reckon that the Nautilus traveled some six hundred leagues beneath the waves of this sea, and it covered that distance in twice twenty-four hours. Setting out on the morning of February 16 from the waters off Greece, by the 18th, at sunrise, we had cleared the Strait of Gibraltar.

It was plain to me that this Mediterranean, pinched in among the very lands he wished to flee, was hateful to Captain Nemo. Its waves and its breezes carried too many memories back to him, if not too many regrets. Here he no longer had that freedom of movement, that independence of maneuver that the open oceans allowed him, and his Nautilus felt cramped between these close-set shores of Africa and Europe.

And so our speed ran to twenty-five miles an hour — that is, twelve leagues of four kilometers each. It goes without saying that Ned Land, to his great disgust, had to give up his plans for escape. There was no using the dinghy when it was being dragged along at twelve or thirteen meters a second. Leaving the Nautilus under such conditions would have been like jumping from a train going at that speed — a reckless move if ever there was one. Besides, our craft rose to the surface only at night, to renew its supply of air, and it steered solely by the readings of the compass and the log.

So of the interior of this Mediterranean I saw only what the traveler on an express train glimpses of the landscape fleeing before his eyes — the far horizons, and not the foregrounds that flash past like lightning. Even so, Conseil and I were able to observe a few of those Mediterranean fish that the power of their fins kept for a moment or two in the waters of the Nautilus. We kept watch at the windows of the salon, and my notes let me sketch, in a few words, the ichthyology of this sea.

Of the various fish that inhabit it, some I saw clearly, others I only half-glimpsed, to say nothing of those the Nautilus's speed stole from my eyes altogether. Let me be permitted, then, to classify them according to this improvised scheme. It will better convey the swiftness of my observations.

Through the mass of waters, brightly lit by our electric sheets, wound a few of those lampreys a meter long, common to nearly every climate. Oxyrhynchus rays, a kind of skate, five feet across, white-bellied, their backs an ashen gray and speckled, spread themselves out like great shawls swept along by the currents. Other rays flashed by so fast that I could not tell whether they deserved the name of "eagles" the Greeks gave them, or the labels of rat, toad, and bat that modern fishermen have saddled them with. Tope sharks, twelve feet long and especially dreaded by divers, raced one another for speed. Thresher sharks — "sea foxes" — eight feet long and gifted with an extraordinarily keen sense of smell, appeared like great bluish shadows. Gilthead bream, of the sparus family, some of them measuring as much as thirteen decimeters, showed themselves in their livery of silver and azure banded with ribbons, which stood out against the dark tone of their fins — fish consecrated to Venus, their eyes set in a brow of gold; a precious species, at home in every water, fresh or salt, dwelling in rivers, lakes, and oceans, living under every climate, bearing every temperature, and whose race, reaching back to the geological ages of the earth, has kept all its beauty of those first days. Magnificent sturgeons, nine to ten meters long, great travelers, struck the glass of the panels with a powerful tail, showing their bluish backs dotted with small brown spots: they resemble the sharks, though they do not match their strength, and are found in every sea; in spring they love to run up the great rivers, to fight the currents of the Volga, the Danube, the Po, the Rhine, the Loire, the Oder, and they feed on herring, mackerel, salmon, and cod; and though they belong to the class of cartilaginous fish, they are a delicacy — eaten fresh, dried, marinated, or salted, and in the old days they were borne in triumph to the table of the Luculli. But of these varied dwellers in the Mediterranean, the ones I could study to best advantage, whenever the Nautilus drew near the surface, belonged to the sixty-third genus of bony fishes. These were tunny — mackerel-tribe fish, blue-black on the back, their bellies armored in silver, their dorsal rays throwing off gleams of gold. They have a reputation for following in a ship's wake, seeking the cool of its shadow under the fires of a tropical sky, and they did not belie it, escorting the Nautilus as they once escorted the vessels of La Pérouse. For hours on end they matched their speed against our craft. I could not tire of admiring these creatures, truly built for the race — their small heads, their smooth spindle-shaped bodies, which in some ran to more than three meters, their pectoral fins of remarkable power, and their forked tails. They swam in a triangle, like certain flocks of birds whose speed they equaled — which made the ancients say that geometry and strategy were second nature to them. And yet they do not escape the pursuit of the Provençals, who prize them as highly as the peoples of the Propontis and of Italy once did; and it is blindly, heedlessly, that these precious creatures rush in and perish by the thousand in the tuna traps of Marseille.

I will mention, for the record only, those Mediterranean fish that Conseil or I did no more than half-glimpse. There were whitish cusk-eels — pearl-fish — that passed like unseizable vapors; conger-morays, snakes three to four meters long, prettied up in green, blue, and yellow; hakes of the cod family, three feet long, their livers a choice morsel; band-fish, ribbon-like, that floated like fine seaweed; gurnards, which the poets call lyre-fish and sailors call whistlers, their snouts adorned with two triangular, toothed plates that mimic old Homer's instrument; flying gurnards — "swallows" — swimming with the swiftness of the bird whose name they have taken; red-headed soldierfish, their dorsal fins fringed with filaments; shad speckled with black, gray, brown, blue, yellow, and green, sensitive to the silvery ringing of little bells; and splendid turbot — those pheasants of the sea — a kind of diamond-shape with yellowish fins, brown-stippled, whose upper side, the left, is generally marbled in brown and yellow; and, last of all, whole schools of admirable red mullet, true birds of paradise of the ocean, which the Romans paid as much as ten thousand sesterces apiece, and which they killed on their tables so as to follow with a cruel eye their changing colors, from the cinnabar red of life to the pale white of death.

And if I could not observe monkfish, triggerfish, puffers, seahorses, John Dorys, snipefish, blennies, striped mullet, wrasses, smelt, flying fish, anchovies, sea bream, bogues, sea perch, nor any of those chief representatives of the order of flatfish — the dabs, the flounders, the plaice, the soles, the brill — all common to both the Atlantic and the Mediterranean, the blame must fall on the dizzying speed that carried the Nautilus through these teeming waters.

As for the marine mammals, I believe I made out, in passing before the mouth of the Adriatic, two or three sperm whales fitted with a dorsal fin of the physeter kind; a few dolphins of the pilot-whale genus, native to the Mediterranean, the fronts of their heads streaked with little pale lines; and also a dozen seals with white bellies and black coats, known as "monks," which for all the world look like Dominican friars three meters long.

For his part, Conseil believes he caught sight of a turtle six feet across, ornamented with three ridges running lengthwise. I was sorry not to have seen the reptile, for from the description Conseil gave me, I thought I recognized the leatherback, which forms a fairly rare species. For my own part, I noticed only a few loggerheads with elongated shells.

As for the zoophytes, for a few moments I was able to admire a superb orange sea-fir that fastened itself to the glass of the port panel; it was a long slender filament, branching out into endless boughs and ending in the finest lacework the rivals of Arachne ever spun. Unfortunately, I could not fish up this admirable specimen, and no other Mediterranean zoophyte would likely have offered itself to my eyes, if the Nautilus, on the evening of the 16th, had not markedly slackened its speed. This was how it came about.

We were passing then between Sicily and the coast of Tunis. In that narrow space between Cape Bon and the Strait of Messina, the sea floor rises almost abruptly. There a veritable crest has formed, over which only seventeen meters of water remain, while on either side the depth is a hundred and seventy meters. The Nautilus therefore had to maneuver carefully so as not to strike this underwater barrier.

I showed Conseil, on the map of the Mediterranean, the place occupied by this long reef.

"But, if Master doesn't mind my saying so," Conseil observed, "it's like a genuine isthmus joining Europe to Africa."

"Yes, my boy," I answered, "it bars the whole Strait of Libya, and Smith's soundings have proven that the continents were once joined between Cape Boco and Cape Furina."

"I can well believe it," said Conseil.

"I'll add," I went on, "that a similar barrier exists between Gibraltar and Ceuta, which in the geological ages closed off the Mediterranean completely."

"Well now!" said Conseil. "What if some volcanic upheaval were to raise these two barriers back above the waves one day?"

"That's hardly likely, Conseil."

"Still, let Master allow me to finish — if it did happen, it would be a sorry business for Monsieur de Lesseps, who is going to such trouble to cut through his isthmus!"

"I grant you that. But I tell you again, Conseil, that phenomenon will not occur. The violence of the subterranean forces keeps steadily diminishing. The volcanoes, so numerous in the world's first days, are dying out little by little; the internal heat is weakening, the temperature of the globe's lower layers is dropping by an appreciable amount each century — and to our globe's cost, for that heat is its life."

"But the sun—"

"The sun is not enough, Conseil. Can it give heat back to a corpse?"

"Not that I know of."

"Well then, my friend, one day the earth will be that cooled corpse. It will become uninhabitable, and it will be uninhabited, like the moon, which lost its vital heat long ago."

"In how many centuries?" asked Conseil.

"In a few hundred thousand years, my boy."

"Then," replied Conseil, "we have time to finish our voyage — provided Ned Land doesn't meddle in it!"

And Conseil, reassured, went back to studying the shoal that the Nautilus was skimming close over at a moderate speed.

There, on a rocky, volcanic bed, a whole living flora blossomed: sponges, sea cucumbers, glassy cydippes trimmed with reddish cirri and giving off a faint phosphorescence; beroës, commonly known as sea gooseberries, bathed in the shimmerings of a solar spectrum; walking feather stars a meter across, whose crimson reddened the waters; tree-like basket stars of the greatest beauty; peacock-plumes on long stems; a great number of edible sea urchins of varied species; and green sea anemones with grayish trunks and brown disks, lost in their olive-tinted manes of tentacles.

Conseil had busied himself particularly with observing the mollusks and the articulates, and although the nomenclature is a little dry, I don't want to do this good fellow the injustice of leaving out his personal observations.

Among the branch of the mollusks, he lists numerous comb-shaped scallops; donkey's-foot spondylus heaped one upon another; triangular donax clams; three-toothed hyalaea with yellow fins and transparent shells; orange pleurobranchs; sea slugs stippled or sown with greenish points; sea hares, also known as aplysias; dolabellas; fleshy acera; umbrella-shells peculiar to the Mediterranean; sea-ears whose shell yields a much-prized mother-of-pearl; flame-marked scallops; anomias, which the Languedoc folk are said to prefer to oysters; carpet-shells so dear to the people of Marseille; double white fatty venus-clams; a few of those clams that abound on the coasts of North America and command such a considerable trade in New York; scallops with lids of varied colors; date-mussels tucked into their burrows, whose peppery flavor I greatly relished; furrowed venericardias, their shells domed at the top with jutting ribs; sea squirts bristling with scarlet tubercles; carinarias with recurved points, like light little gondolas; crowned pterotracheas; atlantas with spiral shells; gray thetys spotted with white and covered by their fringed mantles; eolids like little slugs; cavolinias crawling on their backs; ear-shells, among them the forget-me-not ear-shell with its oval shell; tawny wentletraps; periwinkles; janthinas; cinerarias; petricolas; lamellarias; cabochons; pandoras; and so on.

As for the articulates, Conseil had, in his notes, very rightly divided them into six classes, three of which belong to the marine world. These are the classes of crustaceans, of cirripedes, and of annelids.

The crustaceans subdivide into nine orders, and the first of these orders comprises the decapods — that is, the animals whose head and thorax are most often fused together, whose mouth apparatus is made up of several pairs of limbs, and which possess four, five, or six pairs of thoracic or walking legs. Conseil had followed the method of our master Milne-Edwards, who makes three sections of the decapods: the brachyurans, the macrurans, and the anomurans. These names are a shade barbarous, but they are exact and precise. Among the brachyurans, Conseil lists amathias, their brows armed with two great diverging spikes; the scorpion inachus, which — I don't know why — symbolized wisdom among the Greeks; masséna and spiny-clawed lambrus crabs, probably strayed onto this shoal, for they ordinarily live at great depths; xanthos; pilumnus crabs; rhomboid crabs; granular calappas — very easy to digest, Conseil notes; toothless corystes; ebalias; cymopolias; woolly dorippes; and so on. Among the macrurans, subdivided into five families — the armored, the burrowers, the crayfish tribe, the prawns, and the ochyzopods — he lists common spiny lobsters, whose flesh is so esteemed in the females; bear-slipper lobsters, or sea-cicadas; shore-dwelling mud-shrimps; and all sorts of edible species — but he says nothing of the crayfish-tribe subdivision that includes the true lobsters, for the spiny lobsters are the Mediterranean's only lobsters. Finally, among the anomurans, he saw common drocinas sheltered behind the abandoned shell they seize for themselves; spiny-fronted homolas; hermit crabs; porcelain crabs; and so on.

There Conseil's work broke off. He had run out of time to complete the class of crustaceans by examining the stomatopods, the amphipods, the homopods, the isopods, the trilobites, the branchiopods, the ostracods, and the entomostracans. And to round off the study of the marine articulates, he would have had to cite the class of cirripedes, which contains the cyclops and the argulus, and the class of annelids, which he would surely have divided into tube-dwellers and dorsibranchs. But the Nautilus, having passed the shoal of the Strait of Libya, resumed its accustomed speed in deeper waters. From then on, no more mollusks, no more articulates, no more zoophytes — barely a few large fish that passed like shadows.

During the night of February 16 to 17, we had entered that second Mediterranean basin, whose greatest depths run to three thousand meters. The Nautilus, driven by its propeller and gliding on its inclined planes, sank down to the sea's lowest layers.

There, in place of nature's marvels, the mass of waters offered my eyes many a moving and terrible scene. For we were now crossing that whole part of the Mediterranean so fertile in disasters. From the Algerian coast to the shores of Provence, how many ships have been wrecked, how many vessels have vanished! The Mediterranean is a mere lake compared to the vast liquid plains of the Pacific, but it is a capricious lake, with changeable waves — today kind and caressing to the frail tartan that seems to float between the double blue of the waters and the sky; tomorrow raging, tormented, torn up by the winds, breaking the strongest ships with its short seas that batter them in quick, hurried blows.

And so, in this rapid passage through the deep layers, how many wrecks I saw lying on the bottom — some already crusted over with corals, others clad only in a coat of rust: anchors, cannon, cannonballs, iron fittings, propeller blades, pieces of machinery, broken cylinders, staved-in boilers, then hulls floating between two waters, some upright, others capsized.

Of these wrecked ships, some had perished by collision, others by striking some granite reef. I saw ships that had gone straight down, their masts erect, their rigging stiffened by the water. They looked as though they were riding at anchor in some immense open roadstead, waiting for the moment to sail. When the Nautilus passed among them and wrapped them in its electric sheets, it seemed those ships were about to salute it with their colors and send it their signal number! But no — nothing but silence and death over that field of catastrophes.

I noticed that the Mediterranean bottom grew more and more cluttered with these grim wrecks as the Nautilus drew near the Strait of Gibraltar. There the coasts of Africa and Europe close in, and in that narrow space collisions are frequent. There I saw numerous iron keels, the fantastic ruins of steamers, some lying on their sides, others standing upright, like formidable beasts. One of these vessels, its flanks torn open, its funnel bent, its paddle wheels stripped down to the frames, its rudder parted from the sternpost and still held by an iron chain, its stern transom eaten away by the sea salts, presented a terrible sight! How many lives shattered in its wreck! How many victims dragged down beneath the waves! Had some sailor aboard survived to tell of that dreadful disaster, or did the waves still keep the secret of the calamity? I don't know why, but the thought came to me that this vessel buried under the sea might be the Atlas, lost with all hands some twenty years before, and never heard of since! Ah, what a sinister tale might be told of these Mediterranean depths — of this vast ossuary, where so many riches have been lost, where so many victims have met their death!

Meanwhile the Nautilus, indifferent and swift, ran at full propeller through the midst of these ruins. On February 18, toward three in the morning, it presented itself at the entrance to the Strait of Gibraltar.

There are two currents there: an upper current, long recognized, which brings the ocean's waters into the basin of the Mediterranean; and then a lower counter-current, whose existence reasoning has demonstrated today. In effect, the sum of the Mediterranean's waters, ceaselessly swelled by the flow of the Atlantic and by the rivers emptying into it, ought to raise the level of this sea year by year, for its evaporation is not enough to restore the balance. And yet it does not, and one has naturally had to admit the existence of a lower current that, through the Strait of Gibraltar, pours the Mediterranean's overflow into the basin of the Atlantic.

A true fact, indeed. And it was this counter-current the Nautilus took advantage of. It advanced rapidly through the narrow pass. For an instant I could make out the admirable ruins of the temple of Hercules, sunk — according to Pliny and Avienus — along with the low island that once bore it up, and a few minutes later we were floating on the waves of the Atlantic.

Vigo Bay

The Atlantic! That vast sheet of water, twenty-five million square miles across, nine thousand miles long and, on average, two thousand seven hundred wide. A great sea almost unknown to the ancients — except perhaps to the Carthaginians, those Dutchmen of antiquity, who on their trading voyages followed the western coasts of Europe and Africa. An ocean whose shores, winding in parallel curves, embrace an immense perimeter, watered by the greatest rivers in the world — the St. Lawrence, the Mississippi, the Amazon, the Plata, the Orinoco, the Niger, the Senegal, the Elbe, the Loire, the Rhine — bringing it the waters of the most civilized nations and the wildest lands alike. A magnificent plain, forever furrowed by the ships of every nation, sheltered under every flag in the world, and closed off at either end by those two dreaded headlands, the terror of navigators — Cape Horn and the Cape of Storms.

The Nautilus was cutting through its waters with the edge of its ram, having covered nearly ten thousand leagues in three and a half months — a run longer than one of the earth's great circles. Where were we bound now, and what did the future hold for us?

Out of the Strait of Gibraltar, the Nautilus had stood for the open sea. It rose back to the surface, and so our daily walks on the platform were restored to us.

I climbed up at once, with Ned Land and Conseil. About twelve miles off, Cape St. Vincent loomed vaguely — the southwestern tip of the Iberian peninsula. A fairly stiff wind was blowing from the south. The sea ran high and heavy, rolling the Nautilus in violent lurches. It was almost impossible to keep one's footing on the platform, which great masses of water swept over every few seconds. So, after a few good breaths of air, we went back down.

I returned to my cabin. Conseil went off to his, but the Canadian, looking distinctly preoccupied, followed me. Our quick dash across the Mediterranean had left him no chance to carry out his plans, and he made little effort to hide his disappointment.

When the door of my cabin had shut, he sat down and looked at me without a word.

"Ned, my friend," I said, "I understand you, but you've nothing to reproach yourself for. Given the way the Nautilus was traveling, to think of leaving it would have been madness."

Ned Land said nothing. His pressed lips and knitted brows marked a man in the grip of a single fixed idea.

"Come now," I went on, "nothing is lost yet. We're working our way up the coast of Portugal. France and England aren't far off — places where we could easily find refuge. If the Nautilus, coming out of Gibraltar, had headed south, if it had carried us off toward those regions where there are no continents, I'd share your worries. But we know now that Captain Nemo doesn't shun the civilized seas, and in a few days, I think you'll be able to act with some measure of safety."

Ned Land only stared at me harder, and at last unsealed his lips.

"It's for tonight," he said.

I sat bolt upright. I confess I was hardly prepared for this announcement. I wanted to answer the Canadian, but the words wouldn't come.

"We agreed to wait for the right circumstances," Ned Land went on. "Well, I've got them. Tonight we'll be only a few miles from the Spanish coast. It's a dark night. The wind's blowing off the sea. I have your word, Professor Aronnax, and I'm counting on you."

As I still kept silent, the Canadian rose and came closer.

"Tonight, at nine o'clock," he said. "I've warned Conseil. By then Captain Nemo will be shut up in his cabin, most likely asleep. Neither the engineers nor the crew can see us. Conseil and I will make our way to the central staircase. You, Professor Aronnax, will wait in the library, two steps from us, for my signal. The oars, the mast, and the sail are all in the dinghy. I've even managed to stow some provisions aboard. I've got hold of a wrench to unscrew the bolts that fasten the dinghy to the hull of the Nautilus. So everything's ready. Until tonight."

"The sea is rough," I said.

"I'll grant you that," the Canadian answered, "but we have to risk it. Freedom is worth the price. Besides, the boat is sound, and a few miles with a following wind are nothing to worry about. Who knows if by tomorrow we won't be a hundred leagues out to sea? Let circumstances favor us, and between ten and eleven o'clock we'll have landed somewhere on solid ground — or we'll be dead. So, God help us, and until tonight!"

With that the Canadian withdrew, leaving me all but stunned. I had imagined that, when the moment came, I'd have time to think, to talk it over. My stubborn companion wouldn't allow me that. And what, after all, would I have said to him? Ned Land was right a hundred times over. This was as good as the right circumstance, and he was seizing it. Could I go back on my word and take on myself the responsibility of ruining my companions' future for a purely personal interest? Might not Captain Nemo, tomorrow, carry us far from any land?

Just then a fairly loud hiss told me the ballast tanks were filling, and the Nautilus sank beneath the waves of the Atlantic.

I stayed in my cabin. I wanted to avoid the captain, to hide from his eyes the emotion that had taken hold of me. What a wretched day I spent that way, torn between the longing to regain my free will and the regret of abandoning this marvelous Nautilus, leaving my undersea studies unfinished! To leave this ocean like that — "my Atlantic," as I liked to call it — without having observed its deepest layers, without having stolen from it those secrets the Indian and Pacific seas had yielded to me! My novel was slipping from my hands at the first volume, my dream was breaking off at its finest moment! What miserable hours dragged by like that, now seeing myself safe ashore with my companions, now wishing — against all reason — that some unforeseen circumstance would prevent Ned Land's plans from coming off.

Twice I went to the salon. I wanted to consult the compass. I wanted to see whether the Nautilus's course was really bringing us closer to the coast, or carrying us away from it. But no. The Nautilus was still holding to Portuguese waters, pointing north as it ran along the shores of the ocean.

So there was nothing for it but to resign myself and get ready to flee. My baggage wasn't heavy. My notes, nothing more.

As for Captain Nemo, I wondered what he'd make of our escape — what anxieties, what harm perhaps it would cause him, and what he'd do if it were either discovered or bungled. Certainly I had no complaint against him; quite the opposite. Never was hospitality more open than his. In leaving him, I couldn't be charged with ingratitude. No oath bound us to him. It was on the force of circumstance alone that he counted, and not on any promise of ours, to keep us with him forever. But this openly avowed intention of holding us prisoner aboard for the rest of our lives justified every attempt we might make to get away.

I hadn't seen the captain since our visit to the island of Santorini. Would chance throw me in his path before our departure? I both wished it and feared it. I listened for the sound of him moving in his cabin, which adjoined mine. Not a sound reached my ear. The room must have been empty.

At which point I began to wonder whether that strange man was even aboard. Ever since the night the dinghy had left the Nautilus on some mysterious errand, my ideas about him had shifted a little. I believed — whatever he might have said — that Captain Nemo must have kept up some kind of relations with the land. Did he never leave the Nautilus? Whole weeks had often passed without my meeting him. What did he do during that time, and while I imagined him a prey to fits of misanthropy, wasn't he off somewhere carrying out some secret act whose nature had escaped me until now?

All these ideas and a thousand others assailed me at once. In the strange situation we were in, the field of conjecture could only be infinite. I felt an unbearable unease. This day of waiting seemed endless. The hours struck too slowly for my impatience.

My dinner was served in my cabin as usual. I ate badly, too preoccupied. I left the table at seven o'clock. A hundred and twenty minutes — I counted them — still separated me from the moment I was to join Ned Land. My agitation kept building. My pulse pounded. I couldn't stay still. I paced back and forth, hoping to calm the turmoil of my mind through movement. The idea of dying in our reckless venture was the least of my worries; but at the thought of our plan being discovered before we'd even left the Nautilus, at the thought of being hauled back before an angry Captain Nemo — or, worse still, one wounded by my desertion — my heart hammered.

I wanted to see the salon one last time. I went along the corridors and came to that museum where I'd passed so many pleasant and profitable hours. I looked at all those riches, all those treasures, like a man on the eve of an eternal exile who is leaving never to return. These wonders of nature, these masterpieces of art, among which my whole life had been concentrated for so many days — I was about to abandon them forever. I would have liked to plunge my gaze through the salon window and out into the waters of the Atlantic; but the panels were sealed tight, and a mantle of iron plate cut me off from that ocean I still didn't know.

Wandering the salon that way, I came near the door set into the cut-off corner, the one that opened onto the captain's cabin. To my great surprise, that door stood ajar. I stepped back involuntarily. If Captain Nemo were in his cabin, he might see me. But hearing no sound, I moved closer. The room was empty. I pushed the door open. I took a few steps inside. The same severe, monkish look as before.

Just then some etchings hanging on the wall, which I hadn't noticed during my first visit, caught my eye. They were portraits — portraits of those great historical figures whose whole existence had been one perpetual devotion to some great human ideal: Kosciuszko, the hero who fell with the cry of Finis Poloniae; Botzaris, the Leonidas of modern Greece; O'Connell, the defender of Ireland; Washington, the founder of the American Union; Manin, the Italian patriot; Lincoln, struck down by the bullet of a defender of slavery; and, last of all, that martyr to the emancipation of the black race, John Brown, hanging from his gallows, just as Victor Hugo's pencil so terribly drew him.

What bond existed between these heroic souls and the soul of Captain Nemo? Could I, at last, draw from this gathering of portraits the mystery of his life? Was he the champion of oppressed peoples, the liberator of enslaved races? Had he played a part in the latest political or social upheavals of the century? Had he been one of the heroes of that terrible American war — a war so lamentable and forever glorious?

All at once the clock struck eight. The stroke of the first hammer-blow on the chime tore me from my musings. I started, as though some invisible eye had been able to plunge into my most secret thoughts, and I hurried out of the cabin.

There, my gaze fell on the compass. Our heading was still north. The log showed a moderate speed, the pressure gauge a depth of about sixty feet. So circumstances were favoring the Canadian's plans.

I went back to my cabin. I dressed warmly — sea boots, an otter-skin cap, a jacket of byssus lined with sealskin. I was ready. I waited. Only the shivering of the propeller broke the deep silence that reigned aboard. I listened, straining my ears. Wouldn't some sudden burst of voices tell me that Ned Land had just been caught in his plans of escape? A mortal anxiety came over me. In vain I tried to recover my composure.

At a few minutes to nine, I pressed my ear to the captain's door. Not a sound. I left my cabin and returned to the salon, which lay in half-darkness, but empty.

I opened the door leading into the library. The same feeble light, the same solitude. I went to take up my post by the door that opened onto the cage of the central staircase. I waited for Ned Land's signal.

At that moment, the shivering of the propeller noticeably slackened, then stopped altogether. Why this change in the Nautilus's movement? Whether the halt helped or hindered Ned Land's designs, I couldn't have said.

The silence was broken now only by the beating of my own heart.

Suddenly, a slight jolt made itself felt. I understood that the Nautilus had come to rest on the ocean floor. My anxiety redoubled. The Canadian's signal didn't reach me. I felt an urge to go join Ned Land and urge him to put off the attempt. I sensed that our navigation was no longer proceeding under ordinary conditions.

At that moment the door of the grand salon opened, and Captain Nemo appeared. He caught sight of me and, without further preamble:

"Ah, Professor," he said in a friendly tone, "I was looking for you. Do you know the history of Spain?"

You could know your own country's history through and through, and in the state I was in — my mind troubled, my head lost — you couldn't have quoted a word of it.

"Well?" Captain Nemo resumed. "Did you hear my question? Do you know the history of Spain?"

"Very poorly," I answered.

"There you have your scholars," said the captain. "They don't know. Well then, sit down," he added, "and I'll tell you a curious episode from that history."

The captain stretched out on a divan, and mechanically I took a seat beside him, in the half-light.

"Professor," he said to me, "listen closely. This story will interest you in a certain respect, for it will answer a question you've no doubt been unable to solve."

"I'm listening, Captain," I said, not knowing where my companion was heading, and wondering whether this incident had anything to do with our plans for flight.

"Professor," Captain Nemo went on, "if you don't mind, we'll go back to the year 1702. You're not unaware that at that time your king, Louis XIV, believing that a mere gesture from a monarch was enough to sink the Pyrenees underground, had imposed his grandson, the Duke of Anjou, on the Spanish. This prince, who reigned more or less badly under the name of Philip V, had, abroad, a formidable adversary to deal with.

"For, the year before, the royal houses of Holland, Austria, and England had concluded a treaty of alliance at The Hague, aiming to wrest the crown of Spain from Philip V and set it on the head of an archduke, on whom they prematurely bestowed the name of Charles III.

"Spain had to resist this coalition. But she was almost entirely without soldiers or sailors. Money, however, she did not lack — provided always that her galleons, laden with the gold and silver of the Americas, made it into her ports. Now, toward the end of 1702, she was expecting a rich convoy, which France had arranged to escort with a fleet of twenty-three ships under Admiral de Château-Renault, since the coalition's navies were then roaming the Atlantic.

"This convoy was supposed to make for Cadiz, but the admiral, having learned that the English fleet was cruising in those waters, resolved to put in at a French port.

"The Spanish commanders of the convoy protested against this decision. They wanted to be taken to a Spanish port, and, failing Cadiz, to the bay of Vigo, on the northwest coast of Spain, which was not blockaded.

"Admiral de Château-Renault was weak enough to obey this injunction, and the galleons entered Vigo Bay.

"Unfortunately, this bay forms an open roadstead that cannot be defended at all. It was therefore urgent to unload the galleons before the coalition fleets arrived, and there would have been time enough for that unloading, had not a wretched question of rivalry suddenly cropped up.

"You're following the chain of events?" Captain Nemo asked me.

"Perfectly," I said, still not knowing what this history lesson was leading up to.

"Then I'll go on. Here is what happened. The merchants of Cadiz held a privilege by which all goods coming from the West Indies were to be received by them. Now, to unload the galleons' ingots at the port of Vigo was to go against their right. So they complained to Madrid, and they obtained from the feeble Philip V an order that the convoy, without proceeding to unload, should remain under seal in the roadstead of Vigo until the enemy fleets had drawn off.

"Now, while this decision was being taken, on October 22, 1702, the English ships arrived in Vigo Bay. Admiral de Château-Renault, despite his inferior forces, fought courageously. But when he saw that the convoy's riches were about to fall into the enemy's hands, he set fire to the galleons and scuttled them, and they went down with their immense treasures."

Captain Nemo had stopped. I confess I still didn't see how this story could interest me.

"Well?" I asked him.

"Well, Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo answered, "we are in that very bay of Vigo, and it rests only with you to penetrate its mysteries."

The captain rose and asked me to follow him. I'd had time to recover myself. I obeyed. The salon was dark, but through the transparent windows the waves of the sea gleamed. I looked.

Around the Nautilus, within a radius of half a mile, the waters appeared saturated with electric light. The sandy bottom was clean and clear. Crew members, dressed in diving suits, were busy clearing away half-rotted casks and staved-in crates, amid wreckage still blackened by fire. From these crates, from these barrels, spilled ingots of gold and silver, cascades of piastres and jewels. The sand was strewn with them. Then, laden with this precious plunder, the men would return to the Nautilus, set down their burden, and go back to that inexhaustible fishery of silver and gold.

I understood. This was the theater of the battle of October 22, 1702. Here was the very spot where the galleons freighted for the Spanish government's account had gone down. Here Captain Nemo came, as his needs required, to cash in the millions with which he ballasted his Nautilus. It was for him, for him alone, that America had yielded up her precious metals. He was the direct and undivided heir to those treasures wrenched from the Incas and the peoples conquered by Hernán Cortés!

"Did you know, Professor," he asked me, smiling, "that the sea held such riches?"

"I knew," I answered, "that the silver held in suspension in its waters is reckoned at two million tons."

"No doubt, but to extract that silver, the costs would outweigh the profit. Here, on the contrary, I have only to gather up what men have lost — and not only in this bay of Vigo, but at a thousand other scenes of shipwreck whose positions my underwater chart has recorded. Do you understand now why I'm rich to the tune of billions?"

"I understand, Captain. Allow me, though, to tell you that in working precisely this bay of Vigo, you've only forestalled the efforts of a rival company."

"And which one?"

"A company that received from the Spanish government the privilege of searching for the sunken galleons. Its shareholders are lured by the bait of an enormous profit, for the value of these shipwrecked riches is estimated at five hundred million."

"Five hundred million!" Captain Nemo replied. "They were here — but they're here no longer."

"Just so," I said. "So a word of warning to those shareholders would be an act of charity. Though who knows whether it would be well received. What gamblers regret above all, as a rule, is not so much the loss of their money as the loss of their wild hopes. Still, I pity them less, after all, than those thousands of unfortunates for whom such riches, fairly distributed, might have done some good — whereas for them they'll remain forever barren!"

No sooner had I voiced this regret than I felt it must have wounded Captain Nemo.

"Barren!" he shot back, kindling. "So you believe, sir, that these riches are lost when it's I who gather them up? Is it for myself, in your opinion, that I take the trouble to collect these treasures? Who tells you I don't put them to good use? Do you think I'm unaware that there are suffering beings, oppressed races on this earth — the wretched to be relieved, the victims to be avenged? Don't you understand?"

Captain Nemo stopped on these last words, regretting perhaps that he had said too much. But I had guessed. Whatever the motives that had forced him to seek independence beneath the seas, above all else he had remained a man! His heart still throbbed for the sufferings of humanity, and his vast charity went out to enslaved races as much as to individuals.

And I understood, then, for whom those millions were destined that Captain Nemo had shipped off when the Nautilus was cruising in the waters of insurgent Crete!

A Vanished Continent

The next morning, February 19, I saw Ned come into my cabin. I had been expecting him. He looked thoroughly crestfallen.

"Well, Professor?" he said.

"Well, Ned, our luck was out yesterday."

"Right! That cursed captain had to stop just at the very hour we were about to slip away from his boat."

"Yes, Ned. He had business with his banker."

"His banker!"

"Or rather his banking house. By which I mean that Ocean out there, where his riches are safer than they'd be in the vaults of any government."

I then told the Canadian about the previous night's events, in the secret hope of nudging him back toward the idea of not abandoning the captain; but the only result of my account was Ned's forcefully expressed regret at not having managed to take a little stroll of his own across the battlefield of Vigo.

"Well," he said, "it's not over yet! It's just one harpoon thrown and missed! Next time we'll pull it off — this very evening, if we have to..."

"Which way is the Nautilus heading?" I asked.

"No idea," Ned answered.

"All right. At noon we'll get our position."

The Canadian went back to Conseil. As soon as I was dressed, I stepped into the salon. The compass was anything but reassuring. The Nautilus's course lay south-southwest. We were turning our backs on Europe.

I waited with a certain impatience for the fix to be marked on the chart. Toward half past eleven the ballast tanks emptied, and our vessel rose to the surface of the Ocean. I rushed up to the platform. Ned Land had beaten me there.

No more land in sight. Nothing but the immense sea. A few sails on the horizon — bound, no doubt, all the way out to Cape San Roque in search of the favorable winds for rounding the Cape of Good Hope. The sky was overcast. A gale was brewing.

Ned, seething, tried to pierce the hazy horizon. He was still hoping that behind all that fog stretched the land he wanted so badly.

At noon the sun showed itself for a moment. The first mate seized on the break in the clouds to take its altitude. Then, as the sea grew heavier, we went back down and the hatch was shut.

An hour later, when I consulted the chart, I saw that the Nautilus's position was marked at 16°17′ longitude and 33°22′ latitude, a hundred and fifty leagues from the nearest coast. There was no thinking of escape now, and I leave you to imagine the Canadian's fury when I gave him the news.

For my own part, I didn't take it too hard. I felt as though a weight that had been pressing on me had lifted, and I was able to go back to my usual work with something like relative calm.

That evening, around eleven o'clock, I had a most unexpected visit from Captain Nemo. He asked me very graciously whether I felt tired after sitting up the night before. I answered that I did not.

"Then, Professor Aronnax, I have a curious excursion to propose."

"Propose away, Captain."

"So far you've only seen the sea floor by day, under the light of the sun. Would it suit you to see it on a dark night?"

"Very gladly."

"This walk will be tiring, I warn you. We'll have to march a long way and climb a mountain. The roads aren't very well kept."

"What you tell me, Captain, only sharpens my curiosity. I'm ready to follow you."

"Come along, then, Professor. We'll go put on our diving suits."

When I reached the changing room, I saw that neither my companions nor any member of the crew was to come with us on this excursion. Captain Nemo hadn't even offered to bring Ned or Conseil.

In a few moments we had climbed into our gear. The air tanks, generously charged, were set on our backs, but the electric lamps were not made ready. I pointed this out to the captain.

"They'd be of no use to us," he answered.

I thought I must have misheard him, but I couldn't repeat my remark, because the captain's head had already vanished into its metal sheath. I finished harnessing myself up, felt an iron-tipped staff placed in my hand, and a few minutes later, after the usual maneuver, we set foot on the bottom of the Atlantic, at a depth of three hundred meters.

Midnight was near. The waters were profoundly dark, but Captain Nemo pointed out to me, far off, a reddish point, a kind of broad glow, shining about two miles from the Nautilus. What that fire was, what fed it, why and how it kept renewing itself within the mass of water, I couldn't have said. In any case, it lit our way — dimly, it's true, but I soon grew used to those peculiar shadows, and I understood, in these circumstances, why the Ruhmkorff lamps were useless.

Captain Nemo and I walked side by side, straight toward the beacon fire. The flat ground rose imperceptibly. We took long strides, helping ourselves along with the staff; but our pace was slow, all in all, for our feet kept sinking into a kind of mud kneaded through with seaweed and strewn with flat stones.

As I went, I heard a sort of crackling overhead. Now and then the noise doubled, becoming a continuous sputter. I soon understood the cause. It was rain, falling hard and pattering on the surface of the waves. Instinctively the thought came to me that I was about to get soaked! By water, in the middle of water! I couldn't help laughing at the absurd idea. But to tell the truth, under the thick coat of a diving suit, you no longer feel the liquid element; you imagine yourself in the middle of an atmosphere only a little denser than the one on land — and that's all there is to it.

After half an hour's walk, the ground turned rocky. Jellyfish, microscopic crustaceans, and sea pens lit it faintly with phosphorescent glimmers. I made out heaps of stones covered by a few million zoophytes and tangles of algae. My foot kept slipping on those slimy carpets of wrack, and without my iron-tipped staff I'd have fallen more than once. When I turned around, I could still see the whitish beacon of the Nautilus, beginning to pale in the distance.

Those piles of stone I've just mentioned were arranged on the ocean floor with a certain regularity I couldn't account for. I made out gigantic furrows that lost themselves in the far darkness, their length beyond any reckoning. Other oddities presented themselves too, things I couldn't bring myself to admit. It seemed to me that my heavy lead soles were crushing a litter of bones that snapped with a dry crack. What, then, was this vast plain I was crossing? I would have liked to question the captain, but his sign language — which let him talk with his companions when they followed him on his undersea excursions — was still incomprehensible to me.

Meanwhile the reddish light that guided us grew stronger and set the horizon ablaze. The presence of this furnace beneath the waters intrigued me to the highest degree. Was it some electrical discharge making itself known? Was I walking toward a natural phenomenon still unknown to the scientists of the land? Or even — for the thought crossed my mind — was a human hand at work in this conflagration? Was it a human hand fanning that blaze? Was I to meet, beneath these deep waters, companions and friends of Captain Nemo, living as he did this strange existence, and to whom he was paying a visit? Would I find down there a whole colony of exiles who, weary of the world's miseries, had sought and found their independence in the deepest reaches of the Ocean? All these wild, inadmissible notions pursued me, and in that state of mind, endlessly overexcited by the parade of marvels passing before my eyes, I would not have been surprised to come upon, at the bottom of that sea, one of those undersea cities Captain Nemo dreamed of!

Our road grew brighter and brighter. The whitening glow radiated from the summit of a mountain some eight hundred feet high. But what I saw was only a reflection, thrown up by the crystal of the layers of water. The furnace, the source of this inexplicable radiance, lay on the far slope of the mountain.

Through the stony labyrinths that seamed the floor of the Atlantic, Captain Nemo advanced without hesitation. He knew this dark road. He had traveled it often, no doubt, and could not lose his way on it. I followed him with unshakable confidence. He seemed to me one of the sea's own genies, and when he strode ahead of me, I admired his tall figure, cut out in black against the glowing background of the horizon.

It was one in the morning. We had reached the first slopes of the mountain. But to tackle them, we had to venture along the difficult paths of a vast thicket.

Yes! A thicket of dead trees, leafless and sapless, trees turned to stone under the action of the water, with here and there a gigantic pine towering above them. It was like a coalfield still standing upright, holding to the sunken ground by its roots, its branches, like the fine cutout of black paper, drawn sharply against the ceiling of the waters. Picture a forest in the Harz Mountains, clinging to the flanks of a slope — but a forest swallowed by the sea. The paths were choked with seaweed and wrack, among which swarmed a whole world of crustaceans. On I went, scaling the rocks, striding over the fallen trunks, snapping the sea vines that swung from tree to tree, startling the fish that flitted from branch to branch. Swept along, I no longer felt tired. I followed my guide, who never tired at all.

What a spectacle! How to convey it? How to paint the look of these woods and rocks in that liquid element — their undersides dark and savage, their tops washed with red tones under a light doubled by the reflecting power of the water? We climbed rocks that then broke away in enormous slabs, tumbling down with the muffled roar of an avalanche. To the right and left yawned shadowy galleries where the eye lost itself. Here vast clearings opened up, as though a human hand had cleared them, and I wondered at times whether some inhabitant of these undersea regions might not suddenly appear before me.

But Captain Nemo climbed on and on. I did not want to fall behind. I followed him boldly. My staff was a real help. A false step would have been dangerous on those narrow ledges hollowed out along the flanks of the chasms; but I trod them with a firm foot, feeling none of the dizzy pull of vertigo. Now I leaped a crevasse whose depth would have made me draw back among the glaciers on land; now I ventured out onto the swaying trunk of a tree flung from one abyss to another, never looking beneath my feet, my eyes only for the wild scenery of this region. Here monumental rocks, tilted on their irregularly cut bases, seemed to defy the laws of balance. Between their stone knees, trees sprang up as though shot out under tremendous pressure, and held up the very rocks that held them. Then natural towers, broad sheer faces cut like the curtain walls of a fortress, leaned at an angle that the laws of gravity would never have permitted on the surface of the land.

And did I not feel that difference myself, owing to the powerful density of the water, when — despite my heavy garments, my copper head, my metal soles — I climbed slopes of impossible steepness, clearing them, so to speak, with the lightness of a mountain goat or a chamois!

In telling the story of this excursion beneath the waves, I feel well enough that no one will believe me! I am the historian of things that seem impossible and yet are real, beyond dispute. I did not dream it. I saw and felt it!

Two hours after leaving the Nautilus, we had crossed the tree line, and a hundred feet above our heads rose the peak of the mountain, its shadow thrown across the dazzling glow of the opposite slope. A few petrified shrubs straggled here and there in grimacing zigzags. Fish rose in masses beneath our steps, like birds startled in tall grass. The rocky mass was pitted with impenetrable crevices, deep grottoes, unfathomable holes, at the bottom of which I could hear formidable things stirring. My blood ran back to my heart when I glimpsed some enormous feeler barring my path, or some terrifying claw snapping shut with a clack in the shadow of the hollows! Thousands of luminous points glittered amid the darkness. They were the eyes of gigantic crustaceans crouched in their dens — giant lobsters rearing up like halberdiers and clashing their legs with a rattle of old iron, titanic crabs trained like cannon on their carriages, and hideous octopuses interlacing their tentacles like a living bramble of serpents.

What was this extravagant world I still knew nothing of? To what order did these jointed creatures belong, for whom the rock formed a kind of second shell? Where had nature found the secret of their sluggish, plantlike existence, and for how many centuries had they lived like this in the lowest layers of the Ocean?

But I could not stop. Captain Nemo, familiar with these dreadful animals, no longer paid them any heed. We had reached a first plateau, where still other surprises awaited me. There stood picturesque ruins that betrayed the hand of man, and no longer that of the Creator. They were vast heaps of stone in which one could make out the vague forms of castles and temples, clothed in a whole world of flowering zoophytes, and over which the algae and wrack, in place of ivy, spread a thick vegetal mantle.

But what was this portion of the globe, swallowed up by cataclysms? Who had set these rocks and stones in place like the dolmens of prehistoric times? Where was I? Where had Captain Nemo's whim carried me?

I longed to ask him. Unable to, I stopped him. I seized his arm. But he, shaking his head and pointing to the last summit of the mountain, seemed to say to me:

"Come! Come on! Come along!"

I followed him in one last effort, and in a few minutes I had scaled the peak, which rose some ten meters above the whole rocky mass.

I looked back at the side we had just crossed. On this face the mountain rose only seven or eight hundred feet above the plain; but on its opposite slope it towered to twice that height above the sunken floor of this part of the Atlantic. My gaze stretched far and took in a vast space lit by a violent blaze. For this mountain was, in fact, a volcano. Fifty feet below the peak, amid a rain of stones and cinders, a broad crater vomited torrents of lava that scattered in a cascade of fire through the mass of water. Set there, this volcano, like an immense torch, lit the plain below all the way to the farthest limits of the horizon.

I said the undersea crater threw up lava, but not flames. Flames need the oxygen of the air, and cannot develop underwater; but streams of lava, which carry within themselves the principle of their own incandescence, can heat to white heat, do battle victoriously with the liquid element, and turn it to vapor on contact. Swift currents swept away all these gases in diffusion, and the lava torrents slid down to the foot of the mountain, like the discharges of Vesuvius over some second Torre del Greco.
