And there, indeed, before my eyes, ruined, wrecked, cast down, lay a destroyed city — its roofs caved in, its temples toppled, its arches wrenched apart, its columns lying on the ground, where you could still feel the solid proportions of a kind of Tuscan architecture; farther off, some remains of a gigantic aqueduct; here the silted-up rise of an acropolis, with the floating outlines of a Parthenon; there the vestiges of a quay, as though some ancient harbor had once sheltered, on the shores of a vanished ocean, the merchant ships and the war triremes; farther still, long lines of crumbled walls, broad deserted streets — a whole Pompeii buried beneath the waters, which Captain Nemo was raising again before my eyes!

Where was I? Where was I? I had to know at any price, I wanted to speak, I wanted to tear off the copper sphere that imprisoned my head.

But Captain Nemo came over and stopped me with a gesture. Then, picking up a piece of chalky stone, he stepped toward a rock of black basalt and traced this single word:

ATLANTIS

What a flash of lightning shot through my mind! Atlantis — the ancient Meropis of Theopompus, the Atlantis of Plato, that continent denied by Origen, Porphyry, Iamblichus, d'Anville, Malte-Brun, and Humboldt, who set its disappearance down to the tales of legend, and admitted by Posidonius, Pliny, Ammianus Marcellinus, Tertullian, Engel, Scherer, Tournefort, Buffon, and d'Avezac — there it was, beneath my very eyes, still bearing the undeniable evidence of its catastrophe! So this was that sunken region that had existed beyond Europe, beyond Asia, beyond Libya, out past the Pillars of Hercules, where that mighty people the Atlanteans had lived — against whom the first wars of ancient Greece were waged!

The historian who set down in his writings the great deeds of those heroic times is Plato himself. His dialogue of Timaeus and Critias was traced, so to speak, under the inspiration of Solon, the poet and lawgiver.

One day Solon was talking with some wise old men of Saïs, a city already eight hundred years old, as its annals engraved on the sacred wall of its temples bore witness. One of these old men told the story of another city, a thousand years older still. This first Athenian city, ninety thousand years old, had been invaded and partly destroyed by the Atlanteans. These Atlanteans, he said, occupied an immense continent larger than Africa and Asia combined, covering an area that ran from the twelfth degree of latitude to the fortieth degree north. Their dominion reached even to Egypt. They tried to force it as far as Greece, but they had to fall back before the indomitable resistance of the Hellenes. Centuries passed. A cataclysm struck — floods, earthquakes. A single night and day were enough to blot out this Atlantis, whose highest peaks — Madeira, the Azores, the Canaries, the Cape Verde islands — still rise above the waves.

Such were the historical memories that Captain Nemo's inscription set throbbing in my mind. And so, led here by the strangest of destinies, I was treading one of the mountains of that continent! My hand was touching those ruins a thousand times ancient, contemporaries of the geological ages! I was walking on the very spot where the contemporaries of the first man had walked! Under my heavy soles I was crushing the skeletons of animals from fabled times, animals that these trees, now turned to stone, had once covered with their shade!

Ah! Why did I not have more time! I would have liked to descend the steep slopes of this mountain, to cross this whole immense continent — which no doubt once joined Africa to America — and to visit those great cities of before the Flood. There, perhaps, spread out beneath my eyes, lay Machimos the warlike and Eusebes the pious, whose giant inhabitants lived for whole centuries, and who had strength enough to heap up these blocks that still resisted the action of the water. One day, perhaps, some eruptive upheaval will bring these sunken ruins back to the surface! Many undersea volcanoes have been reported in this part of the Ocean, and many ships have felt extraordinary tremors as they passed over these tormented depths. Some have heard hollow rumblings that told of the deep struggle of the elements; others have gathered volcanic ash flung up out of the sea. All this ground, right down to the Equator, is still being worked by the plutonic forces. And who knows whether, in some distant age, swollen by volcanic discharges and by successive layers of lava, the peaks of fire-vomiting mountains may not one day break the surface of the Atlantic!

While I mused like this, while I tried to fix in my memory every detail of that magnificent landscape, Captain Nemo — leaning on a moss-grown pillar — stood motionless, as if turned to stone, in a mute rapture. Was he thinking of those vanished generations, asking them the secret of human destiny? Was this the place where this strange man came to steep himself again in the memories of history, and to relive that ancient life — he who wanted nothing of the modern one? What would I not have given to know his thoughts, to share them, to understand them!

We stayed in that spot for a full hour, gazing at the vast plain under the glow of the lava, which now and then flared to a startling intensity. The seething deep within sent quick shudders running across the crust of the mountain. Deep sounds, transmitted with perfect clarity through this liquid element, echoed back with a majestic breadth.

At that moment the moon appeared for an instant through the mass of the waters and cast a few pale rays over the sunken continent. It was only a glimmer, but the effect was beyond describing. The captain rose, took one last look at the immense plain; then with his hand he signaled me to follow him.

We came down the mountain quickly. Once past the mineral forest, I caught sight of the Nautilus's beacon shining like a star. The captain walked straight toward it, and we were back aboard just as the first tints of dawn were whitening the surface of the Ocean.

The Undersea Coal Mines

The next day, February 20, I woke very late. The exhaustion of the night had stretched my sleep until eleven o'clock. I dressed quickly. I was eager to know which way the Nautilus was heading. The instruments told me she was still running south at a speed of twenty miles an hour, at a depth of a hundred meters.

Conseil came in. I told him about our nighttime excursion, and since the panels were open, he could still catch a glimpse of that submerged continent.

The Nautilus was in fact skimming barely ten meters above the floor of the Atlantis plain. She glided along like a balloon carried on the wind above the meadows of the earth — though it would be truer to say that we sat in that salon as if in the car of an express train. The nearest features rushing past our eyes were rocks carved into fantastic shapes, forests of trees that had passed from the vegetable kingdom into the mineral, their motionless silhouettes grimacing beneath the waves. There were stony masses buried under carpets of sea squirts and anemones, bristling with tall vertical water-plants, and then blocks of lava strangely twisted, testifying to all the fury of the earth's volcanic upheavals.

While these bizarre landscapes blazed under our electric beams, I told Conseil the story of these Atlanteans, who — on the purely imaginary side of things — inspired so many charming pages in Bailly. I described to him the wars of those heroic peoples. I argued the question of Atlantis like a man who can no longer doubt. But Conseil, distracted, was hardly listening, and his indifference to this historical matter was soon explained to me.

The fact was that a great many fish were catching his eye, and whenever fish went by, Conseil, swept off into the abysses of classification, took leave of the real world. When that happened, I had nothing to do but follow him and take up our ichthyological studies alongside him.

These fish of the Atlantic did not, for that matter, differ noticeably from those we had observed so far. There were rays of gigantic size, five meters long and endowed with such muscular power that it lets them leap clear of the waves; sharks of various species, among them a blue shark fifteen feet long, with sharp triangular teeth, whose transparency made it almost invisible in the water; brown dogfish; humantins shaped like prisms and armored in a warty hide; sturgeons like their cousins of the Mediterranean; and trumpet pipefish a foot and a half long, yellow-brown, fitted with little gray fins, without teeth or tongue, filing past like slender, supple serpents.

Among the bony fish, Conseil noted blackish marlins three meters long, armed on the upper jaw with a piercing sword; weevers, brightly colored, known in Aristotle's day as sea dragons, whose dorsal spines make them very dangerous to seize; then dolphinfish, their brown backs striped with fine blue lines and framed in a border of gold; beautiful sea bream; moonfish, a kind of disc with azure gleams that, lit from above by the sun's rays, looked like patches of silver; and finally swordfish eight meters long, traveling in schools, carrying yellowish fins cut like scythes and long six-foot blades — intrepid creatures, more herbivorous than fish-eating, obeying the least sign from their females like well-trained husbands.

But even as I studied these various specimens of marine life, I did not stop examining the long plains of Atlantis. Now and then some capricious irregularity of the ground forced the Nautilus to slow down, and she would slip with the skill of a whale through narrow bottlenecks between the hills. If the maze grew impassable, the craft rose like a balloon, and once the obstacle was cleared she resumed her swift course a few meters above the floor. An admirable, delightful way to travel, calling to mind the maneuvers of a balloon voyage — with this one difference, that the Nautilus obeyed, passively and precisely, the hand of her helmsman.

Toward four in the afternoon, the terrain — generally made up of a thick ooze mixed with mineralized branches — changed little by little. It grew rockier and seemed strewn with conglomerates, with basaltic tuffs, and here and there with scatterings of lava and sulfurous obsidian. I judged that a region of mountains was about to succeed the long plains, and sure enough, as the Nautilus swung through certain maneuvers, I saw the southern horizon barred by a high wall that seemed to shut off every exit. Its summit clearly rose above the level of the ocean. It had to be a continent, or at the very least an island — one of the Canaries, perhaps, or one of the Cape Verde group. Since our position had not been taken — deliberately, perhaps — I had no idea where we were. In any case, such a wall seemed to me to mark the end of this Atlantis, of which, all told, we had crossed only a tiny portion.

Night did not interrupt my observations. I had stayed on alone. Conseil had gone back to his cabin. The Nautilus, slackening her pace, hovered above the confused masses of the sea floor, now grazing them as though she meant to settle there, now rising capriciously toward the surface of the waves. Through the crystal of the waters I would then catch sight of a few brilliant constellations, and precisely five or six of those zodiacal stars that trail at the tail of Orion.

I would have lingered a long while yet at my window, admiring the beauties of the sea and the sky, when the panels closed. At that moment the Nautilus had come up flush against the high wall. How she would maneuver, I could not guess. I went back to my room. The Nautilus was no longer moving. I fell asleep with the firm intention of waking after a few hours' rest.

But the next morning it was eight o'clock when I returned to the salon. I looked at the pressure gauge. It told me that the Nautilus was floating on the surface of the ocean. What was more, I heard the sound of footsteps on the platform. And yet not a roll betrayed the swell of the waves above.

I climbed to the hatch. It was open. But instead of the broad daylight I expected, I found myself surrounded by deep darkness. Where were we? Had I been mistaken? Was it still night? No! Not a single star was shining, and the night has no such absolute blackness.

I did not know what to think, when a voice said to me:

"Is that you, Professor?"

"Ah, Captain Nemo," I answered, "where are we?"

"Underground, Professor."

"Underground!" I cried. "And the Nautilus is still afloat?"

"She is always afloat."

"But I don't understand."

"Wait a few moments. Our beacon is about to be lit, and if you like a situation made clear, you'll be satisfied."

I set foot on the platform and waited. The darkness was so complete that I could not even make out Captain Nemo. Still, looking up at the zenith, exactly above my head, I thought I caught an uncertain gleam, a kind of half-light filling a circular hole. At that instant the beacon suddenly blazed on, and its brilliance made that vague light vanish.

I looked, after shutting for a moment my eyes dazzled by the electric flood. The Nautilus was stationary. She floated beside a bank arranged like a quay. This sea that bore her up at present was a lake imprisoned within a ring of walls measuring two miles across, or six miles around. Its level — the pressure gauge showed as much — could only be the level outside, for a passage necessarily existed between this lake and the sea. The high walls, sloping inward at their base, curved over into a vault and formed an immense inverted funnel, five or six hundred meters high. At the summit opened a circular mouth through which I had glimpsed that faint clarity, plainly the light of day.

Before examining more closely the interior arrangement of this enormous cavern, before asking myself whether it was the work of nature or of man, I went over to Captain Nemo.

"Where are we?" I said.

"At the very heart of an extinct volcano," the captain answered, "a volcano whose interior the sea invaded after some convulsion of the ground. While you were sleeping, Professor, the Nautilus entered this lagoon through a natural channel that opens ten meters below the surface of the ocean. This is her home port — a safe, convenient, secret harbor, sheltered from every point of the compass. Find me, on the coasts of your continents or your islands, a roadstead to match this refuge against the fury of the hurricanes."

"You're right," I answered. "Here you are safe, Captain Nemo. Who could reach you at the heart of a volcano? But at its summit — didn't I see an opening?"

"Yes, its crater, a crater once brimming with lava, vapors, and flame, and which now gives passage to the life-giving air we breathe."

"But what volcanic mountain is this?" I asked.

"It belongs to one of the many islets with which this sea is sown. A mere reef for ships, for us an immense cavern. Chance let me discover it, and in that, chance served me well."

"But couldn't a person climb down through that mouth that forms the crater of the volcano?"

"No more than I could climb up it. Up to about a hundred feet, the inner base of this mountain can be walked; but above that the walls overhang, and their slopes could never be scaled."

"I see, Captain, that nature serves you everywhere and always. You are safe on this lake, and no one but you can visit its waters. But what good is this refuge? The Nautilus needs no port."

"No, Professor, but she needs electricity to move, elements to produce her electricity, sodium to feed her elements, coal to make her sodium, and coal mines to dig out her coal. Now, precisely here, the sea covers whole forests that were engulfed in geological ages; mineralized now and turned to coal, they are for me an inexhaustible mine."

"So your men, Captain, work here as miners?"

"Exactly. These mines stretch out beneath the waves like the coalfields of Newcastle. It is here that, clad in the diving suit, pick and mattock in hand, my men go to dig out this coal — which I have never had to ask of any mine on land. When I burn this fuel to make sodium, the smoke that escapes through the crater of this mountain still gives it the look of a volcano in eruption."

"And will we see them at work, your crew?"

"No, not this time, at least, for I'm in a hurry to continue our tour of the underwater world. So I'll content myself with drawing on the reserves of sodium I already have. The time to load them — a single day, that is — and we'll be on our way again. If you'd like to explore this cavern and walk around the lagoon, take advantage of this day, Professor Aronnax."

I thanked the captain and went to fetch my two companions, who had not yet left their cabin. I invited them to follow me without telling them where they were.

They climbed onto the platform. Conseil, whom nothing ever astonished, thought it a perfectly natural thing to wake up beneath a mountain after falling asleep beneath the waves. But Ned Land had no other idea in his head than to see whether the cavern offered any way out.

After breakfast, around ten o'clock, we went down onto the bank.

"So here we are on dry land once more," said Conseil.

"I don't call this 'land,'" the Canadian retorted. "And besides, we're not on it, we're under it."

Between the foot of the mountain walls and the waters of the lake stretched a sandy shore that, at its widest, measured five hundred feet. Along this strand one could easily make the circuit of the lake. But the base of the high walls formed a tortured ground, on which lay, in picturesque heaps, volcanic blocks and enormous pumice stones. All these crumbled masses, coated with a polished glaze under the action of the underground fires, blazed at the touch of the beacon's electric jets. The mica-flecked dust of the shore, kicked up by our steps, flew off like a cloud of sparks.

The ground rose noticeably as we drew away from the water's edge, and we soon reached long, winding ramps — genuine steep tracks that let us climb little by little; but one had to walk carefully among these conglomerates, bound together by no cement, and the foot kept slipping on the glassy trachytes, made of crystals of feldspar and quartz.

The volcanic nature of this enormous excavation asserted itself on every side. I pointed it out to my companions.

"Can you picture," I asked them, "what this funnel must have been when it filled with boiling lava, and the level of that incandescent liquid rose to the mouth of the mountain, like molten iron up the sides of a furnace?"

"I picture it perfectly," Conseil answered. "But will Master tell me why the great smelter suspended his operation, and how it comes about that the furnace has been replaced by the peaceful waters of a lake?"

"Most likely, Conseil, because some convulsion produced, below the surface of the ocean, this opening that served as a passage for the Nautilus. Then the waters of the Atlantic rushed into the interior of the mountain. There was a terrible struggle between the two elements, a struggle that ended to Neptune's advantage. But many centuries have passed since then, and the submerged volcano has changed into a peaceful grotto."

"Very good," Ned Land replied. "I accept the explanation, but I regret, for our own sakes, that this opening the Professor speaks of wasn't made above the level of the sea."

"But, friend Ned," Conseil put in, "if this passage hadn't been underwater, the Nautilus couldn't have entered it!"

"And I'll add, Master Land, that the waters wouldn't have rushed in beneath the mountain, and the volcano would have stayed a volcano. So your regrets are wasted."

Our climb went on. The ramps grew steeper and narrower by degrees. Deep gullies cut across them now and then, which had to be crossed. Overhanging masses had to be worked around. We slid along on our knees, we crawled on our bellies. But with Conseil's agility and the Canadian's strength to help, every obstacle was overcome.

At a height of about thirty meters, the nature of the ground changed, without becoming any easier to walk. Black basalts succeeded the conglomerates and trachytes — some spread in sheets all pocked with blowholes, others forming regular prisms, arranged like a colonnade that bore up the springings of this immense vault, an admirable specimen of nature's architecture. Then, between these basalts, wound long flows of cooled lava, encrusted with streaks of bitumen, and here and there spread broad carpets of sulfur. A stronger daylight, entering through the crater above, flooded with a hazy clarity all these volcanic ejecta, forever buried in the bosom of the extinct mountain.

Before long, however, our upward march was halted, at a height of about two hundred and fifty feet, by impassable obstacles. The inner curve of the vault came back in an overhang, and the climb had to turn into a walk around the rim. At this last level the vegetable kingdom began to contend with the mineral. A few shrubs, and even certain trees, sprang from the crevices of the wall. I recognized spurges letting their caustic sap run out. Heliotropes, quite unfit to justify their name, since the sun's rays never reached them, drooped sadly their clusters of flowers with colors and scents half faded away. Here and there a few chrysanthemums pushed up timidly at the foot of long-leaved aloes, sickly and forlorn. But between the lava flows I caught sight of little violets, still perfumed with a faint scent, and I confess I breathed them in with delight. Fragrance is the soul of the flower, and the flowers of the sea, those splendid water-plants, have no soul!

We had reached the foot of a clump of sturdy dragon trees, which were prying apart the rocks with the effort of their muscular roots, when Ned Land cried out:

"Ah! Professor, a beehive!"

"A beehive!" I replied, with a gesture of perfect disbelief.

"Yes! a beehive," the Canadian repeated, "and bees buzzing around it."

I went closer and had to face the facts. There, at the mouth of a hole bored into the trunk of a dragon tree, were several thousand of these ingenious insects, so common all through the Canaries, and whose product is especially prized there.

Naturally the Canadian meant to lay in a store of honey, and it would have been churlish of me to object. A certain quantity of dry leaves mixed with sulfur was set alight under the spark of his tinderbox, and he began smoking out the bees. The buzzing died away little by little, and the gutted hive yielded several pounds of fragrant honey. Ned Land filled his knapsack with it.

"Once I've mixed this honey with breadfruit paste," he told us, "I'll be in a position to offer you a scrumptious cake."

"By heaven!" said Conseil, "it'll be gingerbread."

"Gingerbread it is," I said, "but let's get on with this interesting walk."

At certain turns of the path we were now following, the lake came into view in its whole extent. The beacon lit up entirely its tranquil surface, which knew neither ripples nor swell. The Nautilus kept a perfect stillness. On her platform and along the bank the men of her crew were moving about, black shadows sharply cut out in that luminous atmosphere.

Just then we were rounding the highest crest of these first tiers of rock that held up the vault. I saw then that the bees were not the only representatives of the animal kingdom inside this volcano. Birds of prey soared and wheeled here and there in the shadow, or fled from their nests perched on points of rock. These were white-bellied hawks, and screeching kestrels. On the slopes there also scampered off, with all the speed of their long legs, fine plump bustards. I leave you to imagine how the Canadian's appetite was kindled at the sight of this tasty game, and how he regretted not having a rifle in his hands. He tried to make stones do the work of buckshot, and after several fruitless attempts he managed to wound one of these magnificent bustards. To say that he risked his life twenty times to get hold of it is only the plain truth, but he managed so well that the creature went to join the honeycakes in his bag.

We then had to go back down toward the shore, for the crest was becoming impassable. Above us the gaping crater looked like the wide mouth of a well. From this spot the sky could be made out quite clearly, and I watched clouds go racing by, tousled by the west wind, trailing their misty rags right down to the summit of the mountain. Sure proof that these clouds hung at no great height, for the volcano rose no more than eight hundred feet above the level of the ocean.

Half an hour after the Canadian's last exploit, we had regained the inner shore. Here the flora was represented by broad carpets of that samphire, a small umbelliferous plant very good for pickling, which also goes by the names of sea-fennel and rock-samphire. Conseil gathered a few bunches of it. As for the fauna, it counted crustaceans of every sort by the thousand: lobsters, brown crabs, prawns, mysid shrimp, sand-hoppers, squat lobsters, and a prodigious number of shellfish — cowries, murex shells, and limpets.

At this spot a magnificent grotto opened up. My companions and I took pleasure in stretching out on its fine sand. Fire had polished its enameled, glittering walls, all dusted with the powder of mica. Ned Land was feeling the walls and trying to sound their thickness. I could not help smiling. The conversation then turned to his eternal projects of escape, and I felt I could, without going too far, hold out this hope to him: that Captain Nemo had gone south only to renew his supply of sodium. So I hoped that now he would head back toward the coasts of Europe and America, which would let the Canadian take up his botched attempt again with more chance of success.

We had been stretched out an hour in this charming grotto. The conversation, lively at the start, was flagging by then. A certain drowsiness was creeping over us. Since I saw no reason to resist sleep, I let myself drift into a deep torpor. I dreamed — one does not choose one's dreams — I dreamed that my existence had been reduced to the vegetative life of a simple mollusk. It seemed to me that this grotto formed the two valves of my shell...

All at once I was awakened by Conseil's voice.

"Alarm! Alarm!" the worthy fellow was shouting.

"What is it?" I asked, half rising.

"The water's coming up on us!"

I sat bolt upright. The sea was pouring like a torrent into our retreat, and since we most decidedly were not mollusks, we had to get out.

In a few moments we were safe on the very top of the grotto.

"What on earth is happening?" asked Conseil. "Some new phenomenon?"

"Why no, my friends," I answered. "It's the tide, only the tide, which nearly caught us out like the hero of Walter Scott! The ocean is swelling outside, and by a perfectly natural law of equilibrium, the level of the lake is rising too. We've got off with a half-bath. Let's go change on the Nautilus."

Three quarters of an hour later we had finished our circular walk and were back on board. The men of the crew were just then finishing loading the stores of sodium, and the Nautilus could have left at once.

Yet Captain Nemo gave no order. Did he mean to wait for night and slip out in secret through his underwater passage? Perhaps.

Be that as it may, the next day the Nautilus, having left her home port, was sailing well clear of any land, a few meters below the waves of the Atlantic.

The Sargasso Sea

The Nautilus had not changed course. For the moment, then, every hope of returning to European waters had to be set aside. Captain Nemo held his heading due south. Where was he taking us? I did not dare imagine it.

That day the Nautilus crossed a singular stretch of the Atlantic Ocean. No one is unaware of the great current of warm water known as the Gulf Stream. After emerging from the channels of Florida, it makes for Spitsbergen. But before it reaches the Gulf of Mexico, at about the forty-fourth degree of north latitude, this current splits into two arms; the main one heads toward the coasts of Ireland and Norway, while the second bends south at the latitude of the Azores, then strikes the shores of Africa and, tracing a long oval, comes back around toward the Antilles.

Now, this second arm — more a collar, really, than an arm — encircles with its rings of warm water that portion of the ocean that is cold, still, and motionless, the region we call the Sargasso Sea. A genuine lake in the middle of the Atlantic, whose circumference the waters of the great current take no less than three years to travel around.

The Sargasso Sea, strictly speaking, covers the entire submerged expanse of Atlantis. Certain authors have even held that the countless weeds strewn across it were torn from the meadows of that ancient continent. It is more likely, however, that this greenery — the algae and the wrack — ripped from the shores of Europe and America, is carried all the way to this zone by the Gulf Stream. That was one of the reasons that led Columbus to suppose the existence of a new world. When the ships of that bold seeker reached the Sargasso Sea, they sailed only with difficulty through the midst of these weeds, which halted their progress to the great terror of the crews, and they lost three long weeks in crossing them.

Such was the region the Nautilus was visiting at that moment — a true meadow, a dense carpet of algae, of floating wrack (Fucus natans), of tropic grapes, so thick and so compact that a vessel's prow could not have parted it without effort. And so Captain Nemo, unwilling to foul his propeller in this weedy mass, kept a few meters below the surface of the waves.

The name Sargasso comes from the Spanish word "sargazzo," meaning kelp. This kelp — the floating or berry-bearing kelp — is what chiefly forms this immense bank. And here, according to the learned Maury, author of The Physical Geography of the Globe, is why these water-plants gather in this tranquil basin of the Atlantic:

"The explanation one can offer," he says, "seems to me to follow from an experiment everyone knows. If you drop fragments of cork, or any floating bodies, into a bowl, and give the water in the bowl a circular motion, you will see the scattered fragments come together in a group at the center of the liquid surface — that is, at the least agitated point. In the phenomenon that concerns us, the bowl is the Atlantic, the Gulf Stream is the circular current, and the Sargasso Sea is the central point where the floating bodies gather."

I share Maury's opinion, and I was able to study the phenomenon in that particular setting, which ships rarely enter. Above us floated bodies of every origin, heaped up among these brownish weeds: tree trunks torn from the Andes or the Rocky Mountains and rafted down by the Amazon or the Mississippi; countless pieces of wreckage; remnants of keels and hulls; smashed-in planking so weighed down by shells and barnacles that it could no longer rise to the surface of the ocean. And time will one day bear out that other opinion of Maury's — that these materials, accumulated in this way over the centuries, will be mineralized by the action of the waters and will go on to form inexhaustible coal beds. A precious reserve that provident nature is laying up against the day when men will have exhausted the mines of the continents.

Amid this inextricable weave of weeds and wrack, I noticed charming star-shaped soft corals in shades of pink, sea anemones trailing their long tresses of tentacles, jellyfish green and red and blue, and above all those great Cuvier's rhizostomes, whose bluish umbrella is fringed with a border of violet.

That whole day of February 22 was spent in the Sargasso Sea, where fish that are fond of marine plants and crustaceans find abundant food. By the next day the ocean had resumed its accustomed look.

From that moment on, for nineteen days — from February 23 to March 12 — the Nautilus, keeping to the middle of the Atlantic, carried us along at a steady speed of a hundred leagues every twenty-four hours. Captain Nemo clearly meant to carry out his underwater itinerary, and I had no doubt that, after rounding Cape Horn, he intended to return toward the southern seas of the Pacific.

Ned Land, then, had been right to fear as much. In these wide seas, empty of islands, there could be no more thought of leaving the ship. Nor was there any way to oppose Captain Nemo's will. The only course was to submit; but what we could no longer expect from force or cunning, I liked to think we might obtain through persuasion. Once this voyage was over, might not Captain Nemo consent to give us our freedom on our sworn oath never to reveal his existence? An oath of honor that we would have kept. But this delicate question would have to be taken up with the captain. And would I even be within my rights to claim that freedom? Had he not himself declared, from the very outset and in the most formal terms, that the secret of his life required our perpetual imprisonment aboard the Nautilus? Might not my silence these four months seem to him a tacit acceptance of the situation? Would returning to the subject not simply rouse suspicions that could harm our plans, if some favorable circumstance should later present itself for taking them up again? I weighed all these reasons, turned them over in my mind, laid them out for Conseil, who was no less at a loss than I was. In short, though I am not easily discouraged, I understood that the chances of ever seeing my fellow men again were dwindling day by day — especially now, with Captain Nemo running so recklessly toward the south of the Atlantic!

During the nineteen days I have mentioned, no particular incident marked our voyage. I saw little of the captain. He was working. In the library I often came upon books he had left lying open — books of natural history above all. My own work on the ocean depths, thumbed through by him, was covered with notes in the margins, notes that sometimes contradicted my theories and my systems. But the captain contented himself with refining my work in this way, and it was rare that he discussed it with me. Sometimes I heard the melancholy strains of his organ, which he played with great feeling — but only at night, in the most secret darkness, when the Nautilus lay sleeping in the ocean's wastes.

During this stretch of the voyage we sailed whole days on the surface of the waves. The sea seemed deserted. Barely a few sailing ships, laden for the Indies, making for the Cape of Good Hope. One day we were chased by the boats of a whaler that no doubt took us for some enormous, high-priced whale. But Captain Nemo would not let these good men waste their time and their labor, and he ended the hunt by diving beneath the waters. The incident seemed to interest Ned Land keenly. I do not think I am mistaken in saying that the Canadian must have regretted that our whale of sheet metal could not be struck dead by those fishermen's harpoons.

The fish that Conseil and I observed during this period differed little from those we had already studied in other latitudes. The chief ones were a few specimens of that fearsome class of cartilaginous fish, divided into three subgenera that number no fewer than thirty-two species: broadband sharks, five meters long, with a head flattened and broader than the body, a rounded tail fin, and a back marked by seven great black bands, parallel and running lengthwise; and then perlon sharks, ash-gray, pierced with seven gill openings and furnished with a single dorsal fin set about the middle of the body.

Great dogfish passed by as well — voracious fish if ever there were any. One has every right to disbelieve the tales of fishermen, but here is what they tell. In the body of one of these animals a buffalo's head and a whole calf were found; in another, two tunas and a sailor in uniform; in another, a soldier with his saber; and in yet another, a horse and its rider. None of this, to tell the truth, is an article of faith. What is certain is that not one of these animals let itself be taken in the Nautilus's nets, so I was unable to verify their voracity.

Elegant, frolicking troops of dolphins kept us company for whole days at a stretch. They went in bands of five or six, hunting in packs like wolves out in the countryside — and no less voracious than the dogfish, if I am to believe a professor from Copenhagen who drew from a dolphin's stomach thirteen porpoises and fifteen seals. This one, it is true, was a killer whale, belonging to the largest known species, whose length sometimes exceeds twenty-four feet. This family of dolphins counts ten genera, and the ones I glimpsed belonged to the genus of bottlenose dolphins, remarkable for an exceedingly narrow snout, four times as long as the skull. Their bodies, measuring three meters, black above, were below a rosy white sown with very sparse little spots.

I will also mention, in these seas, some curious specimens of those fish of the order Acanthopterygii and the family of the drums. Certain authors — more poets than naturalists — claim that these fish sing melodiously, and that their voices together form a concert that no chorus of human voices could equal. I will not say no; but these fish gave us no serenade as we passed, and I regret it.

To finish, then, Conseil classified a great many flying fish. Nothing was more curious than to watch the dolphins give chase to them with marvelous precision. Whatever the range of its flight, whatever trajectory it traced — even above the Nautilus — the luckless fish always found the dolphin's mouth open to receive it. These were either flying gurnards or firewing sea-robins with luminous mouths, which, by night, after tracing streaks of fire across the sky, plunged into the dark waters like so many shooting stars.

Our navigation continued under these conditions until March 13. On that day the Nautilus was put to sounding experiments that interested me keenly.

By then we had traveled nearly thirteen thousand leagues since our departure in the high seas of the Pacific. Our position put us at 45°37′ south latitude and 37°53′ west longitude. These were the very waters where Captain Denham, of the Herald, had let out fourteen thousand meters of sounding line without finding bottom. Here too Lieutenant Parker, of the American frigate Congress, had been unable to reach the ocean floor at fifteen thousand one hundred forty meters.

Captain Nemo resolved to send his Nautilus down to the farthest depth in order to check these various soundings. I made ready to note down all the results of the experiment. The panels of the salon were opened, and the maneuvers began for reaching those prodigiously remote layers.

It will readily be understood that there was no question of diving by filling the ballast tanks. Perhaps they could not have increased the Nautilus's specific gravity enough. Besides, to come back up, that excess of water would have had to be forced out, and the pumps would not have been powerful enough to overcome the outside pressure.

Captain Nemo resolved to seek the ocean floor along a suitably long diagonal, by means of his side planes, which were set at an angle of forty-five degrees with the Nautilus's waterlines. Then the propeller was driven to its maximum speed, and its four blades beat the waters with indescribable violence.

Under this powerful thrust, the Nautilus's hull quivered like a sounding string and sank steadily beneath the waters. The captain and I, posted in the salon, followed the needle of the pressure gauge as it swung rapidly. Soon we passed beyond that habitable zone where most fish reside. If some of these animals can live only at the surface of seas or rivers, others, fewer in number, keep to fairly great depths. Among the latter I observed the sixgill shark, a kind of dogfish equipped with six respiratory slits; the telescope fish, with its enormous eyes; the armored sea-poacher, with its gray thoracic fins and black pectorals, protected by a breastplate of pale-red bony plates; and finally the grenadier, which, living at twelve hundred meters down, was withstanding a pressure of a hundred and twenty atmospheres.

I asked Captain Nemo whether he had observed fish at still more considerable depths.

"Fish?" he answered me. "Rarely. But in the present state of science, what does one suppose — what does one really know?"

"Here is what we know, Captain. We know that as one descends toward the lower layers of the ocean, plant life disappears faster than animal life. We know that where living creatures are still to be found, not a single water-plant grows any longer. We know that scallops and oysters live at two thousand meters of water, and that McClintock, the hero of the polar seas, drew a living starfish from a depth of two thousand five hundred meters. We know that the crew of the Bulldog, of the Royal Navy, fished up a starfish from two thousand six hundred twenty fathoms — more than a league of depth. But perhaps, Captain Nemo, you will tell me that we know nothing?"

"No, Professor," the captain replied, "I would not be so rude. Still, I will ask you how you explain that beings can live at such depths?"

"I explain it by two reasons," I answered. "First, because the vertical currents, set in motion by differences in the salinity and density of the waters, produce a movement that is enough to sustain the rudimentary life of the sea lilies and starfish."

"True," said the captain.

"And second, because if oxygen is the basis of life, we know that the quantity of oxygen dissolved in seawater increases with depth instead of diminishing, and that the pressure of the lower layers helps to compress it there."

"Ah! that is known?" replied Captain Nemo, in a slightly surprised tone. "Well, Professor, one is right to know it, for it is the truth. Indeed, I will add that the swim bladder of fish holds more nitrogen than oxygen when these animals are caught at the surface of the waters, and more oxygen than nitrogen, on the contrary, when they are drawn from the great depths. Which bears out your system. But let us go on with our observations."

My eyes turned back to the pressure gauge. The instrument showed a depth of six thousand meters. Our descent had already lasted an hour. The Nautilus, gliding on its inclined planes, kept sinking. The deserted waters were admirably transparent, of a clarity nothing could paint. An hour later we were at thirteen thousand meters — about three and a quarter leagues — and the ocean floor still gave no sign of itself.

At fourteen thousand meters, however, I made out blackish peaks rising up amid the waters. But these summits might belong to mountains as high as the Himalaya or Mont Blanc, higher even, and the depth of these abysses remained impossible to gauge.

The Nautilus went down lower still, despite the powerful pressures it was undergoing. I felt its plates trembling at the joints of their bolts; its bars bowed; its bulkheads groaned; the windows of the salon seemed to buckle under the pressure of the waters. And this solid vessel would surely have given way if, as its captain had said, it had not been capable of resisting like a solid block.

Skimming the slopes of these rocks lost beneath the waters, I still caught sight of a few shells, some tube worms, living spirorbis, and certain specimens of starfish.

But soon these last representatives of animal life vanished, and below three leagues the Nautilus passed the limits of underwater existence, as a balloon does when it rises through the air above the breathable zones. We had reached a depth of sixteen thousand meters — four leagues — and the flanks of the Nautilus were now bearing a pressure of sixteen hundred atmospheres, that is, sixteen hundred kilograms on every square centimeter of its surface!

"What a place!" I cried. "To travel through these deep regions where man has never come! Look, Captain, look at these magnificent rocks, these uninhabited grottoes, these last recesses of the globe, where life is no longer possible! What unknown sights — and why must we be reduced to keeping nothing of them but the memory?"

"Would it please you," Captain Nemo asked me, "to bring back better than the memory?"

"What do you mean by those words?"

"I mean that nothing is easier than to take a photograph of this undersea region!"

I had not had time to voice the surprise this new proposal caused me before, at a call from Captain Nemo, a lens was brought into the salon. Through the wide-open panels, the liquid medium, lit electrically, spread out with perfect clarity. Not a shadow, not the least dimming of our artificial light. The sun would have been no more favorable to an operation of this kind. The Nautilus, under the thrust of its propeller, held in check by the tilt of its planes, remained motionless. The instrument was aimed at these sites on the ocean floor, and in a few seconds we had obtained a negative of extreme purity.

It is the positive print of it that I give here. In it one sees those primordial rocks that have never known the light of the heavens, those nether granites that form the mighty foundation of the globe, those deep grottoes hollowed out of the stony mass, those profiles of incomparable sharpness whose outer edge stands out in black, as though it were owed to the brush of certain Flemish painters. Then, beyond, a horizon of mountains, an admirable undulating line that composes the backgrounds of the landscape. I cannot describe this whole array of smooth, black, polished rocks, without a trace of moss, without a stain, strangely cut in their forms and solidly set on that carpet of sand which sparkled under the jets of the electric light.

Captain Nemo, however, having finished his operation, said to me:

"Let us go back up, Professor. We must not abuse this situation, nor expose the Nautilus too long to such pressures."

"Let us go back up!" I answered.

"Hold on tight."

I had not yet had time to grasp why the captain gave me this warning when I was flung down onto the carpet.

At a signal from the captain, its propeller engaged and its planes set vertical, the Nautilus, carried up like a balloon into the air, shot upward with dizzying speed. It sliced through the mass of the waters with a resonant shudder. Not a single detail was visible. In four minutes it had covered the four leagues that separated it from the surface of the ocean, and, after breaking the surface like a flying fish, it fell back, sending the waves spurting to a prodigious height.

Sperm Whales and Right Whales

During the night of March 13th to 14th, the Nautilus resumed its course toward the south. I assumed that once we reached the latitude of Cape Horn it would swing west, cross into the Pacific, and finish its voyage around the world. It did nothing of the kind. It kept climbing toward the far southern regions. Where on earth did it mean to go? The Pole? That was madness. I was beginning to think that the captain's recklessness more than justified Ned Land's fears.

For some time now the Canadian had stopped talking to me about his escape plans. He had grown less talkative, almost silent. I could see how heavily this long imprisonment weighed on him. I could feel the anger banking up inside him. Whenever he crossed paths with the captain, his eyes lit with a dark fire, and I was always afraid his natural violence would carry him to some extreme.

That day, March 14th, he and Conseil came looking for me in my cabin. I asked what had brought them.

"Just a simple question, Professor," the Canadian answered.

"Go ahead, Ned."

"How many men do you suppose there are aboard the Nautilus?"

"I couldn't say, my friend."

"It seems to me," Ned Land went on, "that running her doesn't call for a big crew."

"True enough," I replied. "In her present conditions, ten men at most should be enough to handle her."

"Well then," said the Canadian, "why should there be any more than that?"

"Why?" I echoed.

I looked hard at Ned Land, whose intentions were easy enough to guess.

"Because," I said, "if I can trust my instincts, if I've understood the captain's life at all, the Nautilus is not merely a ship. She must also be a refuge for men who, like her commander, have broken off every tie with the land."

"Perhaps," said Conseil, "but surely the Nautilus can only hold so many men. Couldn't Master estimate that maximum?"

"How would I do that, Conseil?"

"By calculation. Given the ship's capacity, which Master knows, and therefore the quantity of air she contains — and knowing, on the other hand, how much each man uses up in the act of breathing — and comparing all that with the fact that the Nautilus has to surface every twenty-four hours..."

Conseil's sentence went on and on, but I saw clearly where he was heading.

"I follow you," I said. "But that calculation, easy enough to set up, can only give a very rough figure."

"Never mind," Ned Land pressed.

"Here's the calculation, then," I answered. "Each man uses up, in one hour, the oxygen contained in a hundred liters of air — so, in twenty-four hours, the oxygen contained in two thousand four hundred liters. We only have to work out how many times over the Nautilus holds two thousand four hundred liters of air."

"Exactly," said Conseil.

"Now," I went on, "the capacity of the Nautilus being fifteen hundred tons, and that of a ton being a thousand liters, the Nautilus holds fifteen hundred thousand liters of air, which, divided by two thousand four hundred..."

I worked it out quickly in pencil.

"...gives a quotient of six hundred and twenty-five. Which is to say that the air held in the Nautilus could, strictly speaking, keep six hundred and twenty-five men alive for twenty-four hours."

"Six hundred and twenty-five!" Ned repeated.

"But you can take it as certain," I added, "that passengers, sailors, and officers all together, we don't make up a tenth of that number."

"Still too many for three men!" muttered Conseil.

"So, my poor Ned, all I can do is advise you to be patient."

"And better than patience," said Conseil, "resignation."

Conseil had found exactly the right word.

"After all," he went on, "Captain Nemo can't keep heading south forever! He'll have to stop sooner or later — if only at the ice barrier — and turn back toward more civilized seas! Then it will be time to take up Ned Land's plans again."

The Canadian shook his head, ran a hand across his forehead, said nothing, and withdrew.

"If Master will allow me a remark," Conseil said to me then, "poor Ned thinks about everything he can't have. Everything from his old life keeps coming back to him. Everything we're denied strikes him as a loss. His old memories weigh on him and his heart is heavy. You have to understand him. What is there for him to do here? Nothing. He's no man of science like Master, and he can't take the same pleasure we do in the marvelous things of the sea. He'd risk anything just to walk into a tavern back home!"

There was no denying that the monotony aboard must have felt unbearable to the Canadian, a man used to a free and active life. The events that could stir him were rare. That day, though, one incident came to remind him of his glory days as a harpooner.

Around eleven in the morning, running along the surface of the ocean, the Nautilus fell in with a pod of right whales. The encounter didn't surprise me, for I knew that these animals, hunted to the brink, have taken refuge in the basins of the high latitudes.

The part the whale has played in the world of the sea, and its influence on geographical discovery, has been considerable. It was the whale that drew after it, first the Basques, then the Asturians, the English, and the Dutch, emboldening them against the dangers of the ocean and leading them from one end of the earth to the other. Whales like to haunt the southern and northern seas. Old legends even claim that these cetaceans led fishermen to within a mere seven leagues of the North Pole. If that is not yet true, it will be one day, and it is probably in just this way — by hunting the whale into the Arctic or Antarctic regions — that men will reach that unknown point of the globe.

We were sitting on the platform in a calm sea. But October in these latitudes gave us fine autumn days. It was the Canadian — he couldn't be mistaken about it — who sighted a whale on the horizon to the east. Watching closely, you could see its blackish back rise and fall above the waves, five miles off the Nautilus.

"Ah!" cried Ned Land, "if I were aboard a whaler, there's an encounter that would do my heart good! That's a big animal! Look at the power in it — the columns of air and vapor its blowholes throw up! A thousand devils! Why must I be chained to this sheet of metal?"

"What, Ned," I answered, "you still haven't gotten over your old fishing notions?"

"How could a whaler forget his old trade, Professor? How does a man ever tire of the thrill of a hunt like that?"

"You've never fished these seas, Ned?"

"Never, Professor. Only the northern seas — the Bering Strait as much as the Davis Strait."

"Then the southern right whale is still unknown to you. What you've hunted up to now is the common right whale, and it would never risk crossing the warm waters of the Equator."

"Ah, Professor, what are you telling me?" the Canadian replied in a distinctly skeptical tone.

"I'm telling you how it is."

"Come now! Take me, the man talking to you — back in sixty-five, two and a half years ago, off Greenland, I brought in a whale that still carried in its flank the punch-stamped harpon of a Bering whaler. Now I ask you — how, after being struck west of America, could the animal have come to be killed in the east, unless, after rounding either Cape Horn or the Cape of Good Hope, it had crossed the Equator?"

"I'm of Ned's mind," said Conseil, "and I'm waiting to hear what Master will answer."

"Master will answer, my friends, that whales keep to certain seas, according to their species, and don't leave them. And if one of these animals came from the Bering Strait to the Davis Strait, it's quite simply because there's a passage from one sea to the other, whether along the coasts of America or of Asia."

"Am I supposed to believe that?" the Canadian asked, closing one eye.

"You're supposed to believe Master," said Conseil.

"In that case," the Canadian went on, "since I've never fished these waters, I don't know the first thing about the whales that frequent them?"

"That's what I told you, Ned."

"All the more reason to make their acquaintance," Conseil replied.

"Look! Look!" cried the Canadian, his voice trembling. "She's coming closer! She's heading straight for us! She's mocking me! She knows there's nothing I can do to her!"

Ned stamped his foot. His hand quivered as it brandished an imaginary harpoon.

"These cetaceans," he asked, "are they as big as the ones in the northern seas?"

"Just about, Ned."

"Because I've seen big whales, Professor — whales that measured as much as a hundred feet long! I've even been told that the Hullamock and the Umgallick of the Aleutian Islands sometimes topped a hundred and fifty feet."

"That strikes me as exaggerated," I answered. "Those animals are only rorquals, fitted with dorsal fins, and like the sperm whales they're generally smaller than the common right whale."

"Ah!" cried the Canadian, whose eyes never left the ocean, "she's coming nearer, she's coming into the Nautilus's waters!"

Then, picking up the conversation again:

"You talk about the sperm whale," he said, "as if it were some little beast! And yet there are stories of gigantic sperm whales. They're intelligent cetaceans. Some of them, they say, cover themselves with seaweed and wrack. People take them for little islands. They land on them, they set up camp, they light a fire..."

"They build houses," said Conseil.

"Yes, joker," Ned Land replied. "Then one fine day the animal dives and drags all its inhabitants down to the bottom of the abyss."

"Just like in the voyages of Sinbad the Sailor," I put in, laughing.

"Ah, Master Land, it seems you're fond of tall tales! What sperm whales you have! I hope you don't believe in them yourself!"

"Professor," the Canadian answered seriously, "when it comes to whales, you have to believe anything!"

"Look at her go, this one! Look how she slips away!"

"They say those animals can travel around the world in a fortnight."

"I won't say no."

"But what you probably don't know, Professor Aronnax, is that at the beginning of the world, whales moved faster still."

"Oh, really, Ned? And why is that?"

"Because in those days their tails ran crosswise, like a fish's — that is, the tail, flattened vertically, struck the water from left to right and right to left. But the Creator, noticing they went too fast, gave their tails a twist, and ever since, they've beaten the waves up and down, at the cost of their speed."

"Well now, Ned," I said, borrowing one of the Canadian's own phrases, "am I supposed to believe that?"

"Not too much," Ned Land answered, "and no more than if I told you there are whales three hundred feet long weighing a hundred thousand pounds."

"That is a great deal, indeed," I said. "Still, one has to admit that certain cetaceans reach a considerable size, since they're said to yield as much as a hundred and twenty tons of oil."

"That I've seen," said the Canadian.

"I readily believe it, Ned, just as I believe that some whales equal a hundred elephants in bulk. Imagine the effect of a mass like that launched at full speed!"

"Is it true," asked Conseil, "that they can sink ships?"

"Ships — I don't think so," I answered. "And yet the story goes that in 1820, in these very southern seas, a whale hurled itself at the Essex and drove it backward at a speed of four meters a second. The sea poured in through the stern, and the Essex went down almost at once."

Ned gave me a mocking look.

"For my part," he said, "I once took a whale's tail — in my boat, needless to say. My mates and I were flung six meters into the air. But next to the Professor's whale, mine was no more than a calf."

"Do these animals live a long time?" Conseil asked.

"A thousand years," the Canadian answered without hesitation.

"And how do you know that, Ned?"

"Because that's what they say."

"And why do they say it?"

"Because that's what they know."

"No, Ned, they don't know it — they suppose it, and here's the reasoning they lean on. Four hundred years ago, when fishermen first hunted whales, these animals ran larger than they grow today. So it's supposed, reasonably enough, that the smaller size of today's whales comes from their not having had the time to reach their full growth. That's what made Buffon say these cetaceans could, and even should, live a thousand years. You follow?"

Ned Land didn't follow. He was no longer listening. The whale kept coming closer. He devoured it with his eyes.

"Ah!" he cried, "it's not one whale anymore, it's ten, it's twenty, it's a whole pod! And to be able to do nothing! To be stuck here, hand and foot bound!"

"But, friend Ned," said Conseil, "why not ask Captain Nemo's permission to hunt?..."

Conseil hadn't finished his sentence before Ned Land had dropped down through the hatch and gone running in search of the captain. A few moments later, the two of them reappeared on the platform.

Captain Nemo studied the pod of cetaceans sporting on the waters a mile off the Nautilus.

"Those are southern right whales," he said. "There's a whole whaling fleet's fortune out there."

"Well then, sir," the Canadian asked, "couldn't I give chase to them, if only so as not to forget my old trade as a harpooner?"

"What would be the point," Captain Nemo answered, "of hunting merely to destroy? We have no use for whale oil aboard."

"And yet, sir," the Canadian pressed, "in the Red Sea you gave us leave to go after a dugong!"

"That was a matter of getting fresh meat for my crew. Here, it would be killing for the sake of killing. I'm well aware that's a privilege reserved to man, but I don't hold with such murderous pastimes. In destroying the southern right whale and the common right whale — harmless, good creatures — your kind, Master Land, commit a blamable act. That's how they've already emptied the whole of Baffin Bay, and how they'll wipe out a class of useful animals. So leave these poor cetaceans in peace. They have quite enough to deal with in their natural enemies — the sperm whales, the swordfish, and the sawfish — without your meddling too."

I leave you to imagine the face the Canadian made during this lecture on morals. To give reasons of that kind to a hunter was to waste one's breath. Ned Land stared at Captain Nemo and plainly didn't grasp what he was trying to tell him. And yet the captain was right. The barbarous, thoughtless zeal of the fishermen will one day make the last whale vanish from the ocean.

Ned Land whistled his Yankee Doodle through his teeth, shoved his hands in his pockets, and turned his back on us.

Meanwhile Captain Nemo went on watching the pod of cetaceans, and, turning to me:

"I was right to say that, without counting man, whales have enemies enough. These are going to have a hard fight on their hands before long. Do you see, Professor Aronnax, eight miles off to leeward, those blackish points moving about?"

"Yes, Captain," I answered.

"Those are sperm whales — terrible animals that I've sometimes met in pods of two or three hundred! As for those, cruel and destructive brutes, people are right to exterminate them."

The Canadian turned around sharply at these last words.

"Well, Captain," I said, "there's still time, in the very interest of the right whales..."

"No need to put ourselves at risk, Professor. The Nautilus will be enough to scatter these sperm whales. She's armed with a steel ram every bit as good as Master Land's harpoon, I fancy."

The Canadian didn't trouble to hide a shrug. Attack cetaceans with a ram! Who had ever heard of such a thing?

"Wait, Professor Aronnax," said Captain Nemo. "We'll show you a hunt you don't know yet. No mercy for these ferocious cetaceans. They're nothing but mouth and teeth!"

Mouth and teeth! You couldn't better describe the great-headed sperm whale — the cachalot macrocéphalus — whose length sometimes exceeds twenty-five meters. This cetacean's enormous head takes up about a third of its body. Better armed than the whale, whose upper jaw carries only baleen, it is fitted out with twenty-five massive teeth, twenty centimeters high, cylindrical and coning to a point at the tip, each weighing two pounds. It's in the upper part of that huge head, in great cavities partitioned off by cartilage, that some three or four hundred kilograms of that precious oil are stored — the stuff called spermaceti, or "whale-white." The sperm whale is an ungainly animal, more tadpole than fish, as Frédol remarks. It is poorly built, "botched," so to speak, along the whole left side of its frame, seeing hardly at all except out of the right eye.

Meanwhile the monstrous pod kept drawing nearer. It had spotted the right whales and was preparing to attack them. You could tell in advance that the sperm whales would win — not only because they're better built for attack than their harmless adversaries, but because they can stay longer beneath the waves without coming up to breathe at the surface.

There was not a moment to lose in going to the whales' rescue. The Nautilus dropped to mid-depth. Conseil, Ned, and I took our places at the windows of the salon. Captain Nemo went to stand beside the helmsman, to work his machine like an engine of destruction. Soon I felt the beat of the propeller quicken and our speed pick up.

The battle between the sperm whales and the right whales had already begun when the Nautilus arrived. She maneuvered so as to cut through the pod of great-heads. At first they showed little alarm at the sight of the new monster joining the fray. But soon they had to guard themselves against her blows.

What a fight! Even Ned Land, soon carried away, ended up clapping his hands. The Nautilus was nothing now but a formidable harpoon, wielded by her captain's hand. She flung herself at those fleshy masses and ran them clean through, leaving two writhing halves of animal in her wake. The tremendous blows of the tails against her flanks — she didn't feel them. The shocks she dealt out — no more than that. One sperm whale finished off, she raced to another, pivoting in place so as not to miss her prey, going forward, going astern, obedient to her helm, diving when the cetacean sank into the deeper layers, rising with it when it came back to the surface, striking it head-on or aslant, cutting it or tearing it, in every direction and at every gait, piercing it with her terrible ram.

What a carnage! What an uproar over the surface of the waves! What shrill whistlings, what strange snorts from those terrified animals! In the midst of those normally so peaceful waters, their tails raised veritable swells.

For a whole hour this Homeric massacre dragged on, and the great-heads could not escape it. Several times, ten or a dozen of them together tried to crush the Nautilus under their mass. Through the window you could see their enormous jaws paved with teeth, their formidable eyes. Ned Land, beside himself, threatened them and hurled abuse at them. You could feel them clinging to our machine like hounds fastening on a wild boar in the thickets. But the Nautilus, forcing her propeller, carried them off, dragged them along, or hauled them back up to the higher levels of the water, heedless of their enormous weight or their powerful grip.

At last the mass of sperm whales thinned out. The waters grew calm again. I felt us rising to the surface of the ocean. The hatch was opened, and we rushed out onto the platform.

The sea was covered with mangled corpses. A tremendous explosion could not have split, torn, and shredded those fleshy masses with more violence. We floated among gigantic bodies, bluish along the back, whitish under the belly, and all knotted with enormous protuberances. A few terrified sperm whales fled toward the horizon. The waves were stained red over a stretch of several miles, and the Nautilus floated in the middle of a sea of blood.

Captain Nemo came to join us.

"Well, Master Land?" he said.

"Well, sir," the Canadian answered — his enthusiasm had cooled now — "it's a terrible sight, right enough. But I'm not a butcher, I'm a hunter, and this is nothing but a slaughter."

"It's a massacre of destructive animals," the captain replied, "and the Nautilus is no butcher's knife."

"I'd rather have my harpoon," the Canadian shot back.

"To each his own weapon," the captain answered, looking Ned Land straight in the eye.

I was afraid Ned might let himself be carried away into some act of violence that would have had deplorable consequences. But his anger was turned aside by the sight of a whale the Nautilus was coming alongside just then.

The animal hadn't been able to escape the sperm whales' teeth. I recognized the southern right whale, with its flattened head, entirely black. Anatomically, it differs from the white whale and the Nord-Caper by the fusion of its seven cervical vertebrae, and it carries two ribs more than its kin. The wretched cetacean, lying on its side, its belly riddled with bites, was dead. From the end of its mutilated fin there still hung a little calf it hadn't been able to save from the massacre. Its open mouth let the water run out, murmuring like surf through its baleen.

Captain Nemo brought the Nautilus up close to the animal's carcass. Two of his men climbed onto the whale's flank, and I saw, not without astonishment, that they were drawing off from its teats all the milk they held — the equivalent, that is, of two or three barrels.

The captain offered me a cup of this milk, still warm. I couldn't help showing him my distaste for the drink. He assured me it was excellent, and in no way different from cow's milk.

I tasted it and agreed. So it was a useful reserve for us, since this milk, in the form of salt butter or cheese, was bound to bring a pleasant variety to our usual fare.

From that day on, I noticed with unease that Ned Land's feelings toward Captain Nemo were turning worse and worse, and I resolved to keep a close watch on the Canadian's every move.

The Ice Barrier

The Nautilus had resumed its unwavering course toward the south. It ran down the fiftieth meridian at a considerable speed. Did it mean, then, to reach the Pole? I didn't think so, for up to now every attempt to climb to that point of the globe had failed. And the season, besides, was already far advanced, since March 13 in the antarctic lands answers to September 13 in the northern regions — the start of the equinoctial period.

On March 14 I spotted floating ice at 55° latitude, mere pallid fragments twenty to twenty-five feet across, forming reefs on which the sea broke. The Nautilus was holding to the surface of the ocean. Ned Land, who had already fished in the arctic seas, was familiar with this spectacle of icebergs. Conseil and I were admiring it for the first time.

In the sky, off toward the southern horizon, there stretched a white band of dazzling brilliance. English whalers have given it the name "ice-blink." However thick the clouds may be, they cannot dim it. It announces the presence of a pack, or bank, of ice.

And in fact, larger blocks soon appeared, their gleam shifting with the whims of the mist. Some of these masses showed veins of green, as though copper sulfate had traced their rippling lines. Others, like enormous amethysts, let the light pass right through them. These threw back the rays of day off the thousand facets of their crystals. Those, tinted with the vivid glints of limestone, would have furnished enough stone to build a whole city of marble.

The farther south we went, the more these floating islands grew in number and in size. Polar birds nested there by the thousands. They were petrels, cape pigeons, shearwaters, and they deafened us with their cries. Some of them, taking the Nautilus for the carcass of a whale, came to settle on it and jabbed at its ringing plates with their beaks.

Through all this navigation among the ice, Captain Nemo often stayed out on the platform. He studied these forsaken waters closely. Now and then I saw his calm gaze come alive. Was he telling himself that here, in these polar seas closed to man, he was in his own domain, master of these impassable spaces? Perhaps. But he did not speak. He stood motionless, coming back to himself only when his instincts as a ship-handler took over again. Steering his Nautilus then with a consummate skill, he neatly dodged the shock of these masses, some of which measured several miles in length by a height that ran from seventy to eighty meters. Often the horizon seemed shut off entirely. At the level of the sixtieth degree of latitude, every passage had vanished. But Captain Nemo, searching carefully, soon found some narrow opening through which he slipped boldly, knowing perfectly well, all the same, that it would close again behind him.

And so it was that the Nautilus, guided by that skilled hand, passed all this ice, which Conseil classified — by shape or by size — with a precision that delighted him: icebergs, or mountains of ice; ice-fields, level and boundless expanses; drift-ice, or floating ice; packs, or broken fields, called palchs when they are circular and streams when they are made of elongated pieces.

The temperature was quite low. The thermometer, exposed to the outside air, read two to three degrees below zero. But we were warmly dressed in furs, paid for by the seals and the sea bears. The interior of the Nautilus, evenly heated by its electric apparatus, defied the most intense cold. Besides, it need only have sunk a few meters below the waves to find a bearable temperature there.

Two months earlier, we would have enjoyed perpetual daylight in this latitude; but already the night was falling for three or four hours, and later it would cast six months of shadow over these circumpolar regions.

On March 15 we passed the latitude of the New Shetland Islands and the South Orkneys. The captain told me that in the old days numerous tribes of seals had inhabited these lands; but the English and American whalers, in their rage for destruction, slaughtering the adults and the pregnant females wherever life stirred, had left the silence of death behind them.

On March 16, toward eight in the morning, the Nautilus, following the fifty-fifth meridian, crossed the antarctic polar circle. The ice hemmed us in on every side and closed off the horizon. Yet Captain Nemo went from passage to passage, still climbing.

"But where is he going?" I asked.

"Straight ahead," Conseil answered. "After all, when he can't go any farther, he'll stop."

"I wouldn't swear to it!" I replied.

And to be honest, I'll confess that this adventurous excursion was not at all displeasing to me. Words fail me to say how the beauties of these new regions astonished me. The ice took on superb attitudes. Here its whole mass formed an eastern city, with its minarets and its countless mosques. There, a fallen city, thrown flat as if by a convulsion of the earth. Scenes forever changing under the slanting rays of the sun, or lost in the gray mists amid hurricanes of snow. Then, on all sides, detonations, avalanches, great tumbling of icebergs that shifted the scenery like the landscape of a diorama.

When the Nautilus was submerged at the moment these balances broke, the noise carried under the water with a frightful intensity, and the fall of these masses set up formidable eddies down into the deep layers of the ocean. The Nautilus would then roll and pitch like a ship abandoned to the fury of the elements.

Often, seeing no way out anymore, I thought we were prisoners for good; but with instinct to guide him, on the slightest hint Captain Nemo would discover fresh passages. He was never wrong in reading the thin threads of bluish water that streaked the ice-fields. And so I had no doubt that he had already ventured the Nautilus into the midst of the antarctic seas before.

Yet in the course of that day, March 16, the ice-fields barred our way absolutely. This was not the ice barrier yet, but vast ice-fields cemented together by the cold. This obstacle could not stop Captain Nemo, and he flung himself against the ice-field with a terrible violence. The Nautilus drove in like a wedge into that brittle mass and split it apart with dreadful cracking. It was the ancient battering ram, driven by an infinite power. The shattered ice, hurled high, fell back in hail all around us. By its momentum alone our craft cut itself a channel. Sometimes, carried away by its own drive, it would ride up onto the ice-field and crush it under its weight, or now and then, wedged in beneath the ice-field, it would break the sheet apart with a simple pitching motion that tore long rifts through it.

During these days violent squalls assailed us. In certain thick fogs you could not have seen from one end of the platform to the other. The wind shifted abruptly to every point of the compass. The snow piled up in layers so hard we had to break it with pickaxes. At a temperature of a mere five degrees below zero, every outside part of the Nautilus was coated with ice. Rigging could not have been worked, for every rope would have jammed in the throats of the pulleys. Only a vessel without sails, driven by an electric motor that had no need of coal, could face latitudes as high as these.

Under these conditions the barometer generally held very low. It even dropped to 73°5′. The readings of the compass no longer offered any guarantee. Its frantic needles pointed in contradictory directions as we neared the southern magnetic pole, which does not coincide with the geographic south of the world. Indeed, according to Hansteen, this pole is situated at about 70° latitude and 130° longitude, and according to Duperrey's observations, at 135° longitude and 70°30′ latitude. We then had to take numerous readings from compasses carried to different parts of the ship and strike an average. But often we relied on dead reckoning to plot the course run — a poor method in the midst of these winding passages whose landmarks change without cease.

At last, on March 18, after twenty useless assaults, the Nautilus found itself definitively blocked. These were no longer streams, nor palchs, nor ice-fields, but an endless, motionless barrier formed of mountains welded to one another.

"The ice barrier!" the Canadian said to me.

I understood that for Ned Land, as for every navigator who had gone before us, this was the impassable obstacle. The sun having shown itself for a moment around noon, Captain Nemo obtained a fairly accurate reading that put our position at 51°30′ longitude and 67°39′ southern latitude. This was already a far-advanced point of the antarctic regions.

Of sea, of liquid surface, there was no longer any sign before our eyes. Beneath the ram of the Nautilus there stretched a vast tormented plain, tangled with confused blocks, with all that capricious jumble that marks the surface of a river some time before the ice breaks up, but on gigantic proportions. Here and there, sharp peaks, slender needles rose to a height of two hundred feet; farther off, a line of cliffs cut sheer and clothed in grayish tints, vast mirrors that reflected a few rays of sunlight half drowned in the mists. Then, over this desolate landscape, a savage silence, scarcely broken by the beating wings of the petrels or the shearwaters. Everything was frozen then, even sound.

The Nautilus was thus forced to halt in its adventurous course among the fields of ice.

"Professor," Ned Land said to me that day, "if your captain goes any farther —"

"Well?"

"He'll be a master among men."

"Why, Ned?"

"Because nobody can cross the ice barrier. He's powerful, your captain; but, a thousand devils! he's no more powerful than nature, and where she has set her boundaries, a man has to stop whether he likes it or not."

"True enough, Ned Land, and yet I would have liked to know what lies behind that ice barrier! A wall — that's what irritates me most!"

"Master is right," said Conseil. "Walls were only ever invented to annoy scholars. There ought not to be walls anywhere."

"Bah!" said the Canadian. "Behind that ice barrier we know perfectly well what's there."

"And what is that?" I asked.

"Ice, and more ice!"

"You may be sure of that fact, Ned," I answered, "but I am not. That's exactly why I'd like to go and see."

"Well, Professor," the Canadian answered, "give up the idea. You've reached the ice barrier, which is quite enough, and you'll go no farther — not you, not your Captain Nemo, not his Nautilus. And whether he likes it or not, we'll turn back north, that is, to the land of honest folk."

I have to admit that Ned Land was right, and until ships are built to navigate over fields of ice, they will have to stop before the ice barrier.

And in fact, despite its efforts, despite the powerful means employed to break the ice apart, the Nautilus was reduced to immobility. Ordinarily, one who can go no farther gets off simply by turning back. But here, to turn back was as impossible as to go forward, for the passages had closed behind us, and if our craft stayed stationary at all, it would soon be locked fast. That is exactly what happened toward two in the afternoon, and the young ice formed along its sides with astonishing speed. I had to admit that Captain Nemo's conduct was more than imprudent.
