I was on the platform just then. The captain, who had been studying the situation for some minutes, said to me:

"Well, Professor, what do you think of it?"

"I think we're caught, Captain."

"Caught! And how do you mean that?"

"I mean that we can go neither forward nor back, nor to any side. That, I believe, is what is called 'caught' — at least on the inhabited continents."

"So, Professor Aronnax, you think the Nautilus won't be able to work itself free?"

"Only with difficulty, Captain, for the season is already too far gone for you to count on the ice breaking up."

"Ah, Professor," Captain Nemo replied in an ironic tone, "you'll always be the same! You see nothing but hindrances and obstacles! I tell you, not only will the Nautilus free itself, but it will go farther still!"

"Farther south?" I asked, looking at the captain.

"Yes, Professor, it will go to the Pole."

"To the Pole!" I cried, unable to hold back a movement of disbelief.

"Yes," the captain answered coldly, "to the antarctic pole, to that unknown point where all the meridians of the globe cross. You know that I make the Nautilus do whatever I wish."

Yes! I knew it. I knew this man was bold to the point of recklessness! But to conquer these obstacles that bristle around the South Pole — more inaccessible than the North Pole, which the boldest navigators had not yet reached — was that not an utterly insane undertaking, one that only the mind of a madman could conceive?

It then occurred to me to ask Captain Nemo whether he had already discovered this pole that no human foot had ever trodden.

"No, Professor," he answered me, "and we will discover it together. Where others have failed, I will not fail. Never have I taken my Nautilus so far across the southern seas; but, I repeat, it will go farther still."

"I want to believe you, Captain," I went on in a slightly ironic tone. "I do believe you! Let's go ahead! There are no obstacles for us! Let's break through this ice barrier! Let's blow it up, and if it resists, let's give the Nautilus wings, so it can pass over the top!"

"Over the top, Professor?" Captain Nemo answered calmly. "No, not over the top, but underneath."

"Underneath!" I cried.

A sudden revelation of the captain's plans had just lit up my mind. I understood. The wonderful qualities of the Nautilus were going to serve it once more in this superhuman enterprise!

"I see we're beginning to understand each other, Professor," the captain said to me with a half smile. "You already glimpse the possibility — I would say the success — of this attempt. What is impracticable with an ordinary ship becomes easy for the Nautilus. If a continent emerges at the Pole, it will stop before that continent. But if, on the contrary, it is open sea that washes the Pole, it will go to the Pole itself!"

"Just so," I said, carried along by the captain's reasoning. "If the surface of the sea is solidified by the ice, its lower layers are free, thanks to that providential fact which places the maximum density of seawater at a temperature above its freezing point. And, if I'm not mistaken, the submerged part of this ice barrier is to the emergent part as four is to one?"

"Roughly, Professor. For every foot the icebergs stand above the sea, they have three below it. Now, since these ice mountains do not exceed a height of a hundred meters, they sink only three hundred. And what are three hundred meters to the Nautilus?"

"Nothing, Captain."

"It can even go and seek, at a greater depth, that uniform temperature of the sea's waters, and there we'll defy with impunity the thirty or forty degrees of cold at the surface."

"Right, Captain, quite right," I replied, warming to it.

"The only difficulty," Captain Nemo went on, "will be to stay submerged several days without renewing our supply of air."

"Is that all?" I answered. "The Nautilus has vast tanks; we'll fill them, and they'll furnish us all the oxygen we need."

"Well thought, Professor Aronnax," the captain answered, smiling. "But not wanting to have you accuse me of recklessness, I lay all my objections before you in advance."

"Have you any more to make?"

"Just one. It's possible, if a sea exists at the South Pole, that this sea is entirely frozen over, and that consequently we won't be able to come back up to its surface!"

"Come, Captain, are you forgetting that the Nautilus is armed with a formidable ram, and can't we launch it diagonally against these fields of ice, which will open at the shock?"

"Ah, Professor, you've got ideas today!"

"Besides, Captain," I added, growing more and more enthusiastic, "why shouldn't one meet open sea at the South Pole as well as at the North Pole? The poles of cold and the poles of the earth do not coincide, either in the southern hemisphere or in the northern; and until there is proof to the contrary, we must assume either a continent or an ocean free of ice at these two points of the globe."

"I believe so too, Professor Aronnax," Captain Nemo answered. "I'll only point out to you that after raising so many objections against my project, you're now crushing me with arguments in its favor."

Captain Nemo was right. I had come to outdo him in daring! It was I who was dragging him to the Pole! I was outstripping him, leaving him behind... But no! You poor fool. Captain Nemo knew better than you the pros and cons of the question, and he was amusing himself to see you swept away in dreams of the impossible!

Meanwhile, he had not lost a moment. At a signal the first officer appeared. The two men spoke rapidly in their incomprehensible language, and whether the officer had been warned of it beforehand or found the project workable, he showed no surprise.

But impassive as he was, he showed no more complete impassiveness than Conseil when I told that worthy fellow of our intention to push on to the South Pole. A "Just as Master pleases" greeted my announcement, and I had to be content with that. As for Ned Land, if ever shoulders were raised high, they were the Canadian's.

"You see, Professor," he said to me, "you and your Captain Nemo — I feel sorry for you both!"

"But we'll reach the Pole, Master Ned."

"Possibly, but you won't come back from it!"

And Ned Land withdrew into his cabin, "so as not to do something desperate," he said as he left me.

Meanwhile, the preparations for this bold attempt had begun. The powerful pumps of the Nautilus were forcing air into the tanks and storing it there at high pressure. Around four o'clock, Captain Nemo announced to me that the platform hatches were about to be closed. I threw a last look at the thick ice barrier we were about to cross. The weather was clear, the air fairly pure, the cold very sharp, twelve degrees below zero; but the wind having died down, this temperature did not seem too unbearable.

Ten or so men climbed onto the sides of the Nautilus and, armed with pickaxes, broke the ice around the hull, which was soon cleared. The operation was quickly done, for the young ice was still thin. We all went back inside. The usual tanks filled with this water left free at the waterline. The Nautilus was not slow to descend.

I had taken a place in the salon with Conseil. Through the open window we watched the lower layers of the southern ocean. The thermometer was rising. The needle of the pressure gauge swung across the dial.

At about three hundred meters, just as Captain Nemo had foreseen, we were floating beneath the rippled underside of the ice barrier. But the Nautilus dove lower still. It reached a depth of eight hundred meters. The temperature of the water, which had given twelve degrees at the surface, now registered only eleven. Two degrees already gained. It goes without saying that the temperature inside the Nautilus, raised by its heating apparatus, held at a far higher level. Every maneuver was carried out with extraordinary precision.

"We'll get through, if Master doesn't mind my saying so," Conseil said to me.

"I certainly count on it!" I answered, in the tone of a deep conviction.

Under this open sea, the Nautilus had struck straight for the Pole, without straying from the fifty-second meridian. From 67°30′ to 90°, twenty-two and a half degrees of latitude remained to be crossed — that is, a little more than five hundred leagues. The Nautilus took an average speed of twenty-six miles an hour, the speed of an express train. If it kept it up, forty hours would be enough to reach the Pole.

For part of the night the novelty of the situation held Conseil and me at the salon window. The sea shone under the electric radiance of the beacon. But it was deserted. The fish did not linger in these imprisoned waters. They found there only a passage for going from the antarctic ocean to the open sea of the Pole. Our pace was swift. You could feel it in the shudders of the long steel hull.

Toward two in the morning I went to snatch a few hours' rest. Conseil did the same. Crossing the gangways, I did not meet Captain Nemo. I supposed he was in the helmsman's cage.

The next day, March 19, at five in the morning, I resumed my post in the salon. The electric log told me that the Nautilus had slackened its speed. It was rising now toward the surface, but cautiously, slowly emptying its tanks.

My heart was pounding. Were we going to surface and find the free air of the Pole?

No. A jolt told me that the Nautilus had struck the lower surface of the ice barrier, still very thick, to judge by the dull sound. In fact, we had "touched," to use the sailors' expression, but in reverse and at a depth of a thousand feet. That gave two thousand feet of ice above us, of which one thousand emerged. The ice barrier stood then at a height greater than the one we had measured along its edges. A not very reassuring circumstance.

Through that day the Nautilus began this same experiment several times over, and each time it came up against the wall that ceilinged over it. At certain moments it met the ice at nine hundred meters, which meant twelve hundred meters of thickness, of which two hundred meters rose above the surface of the ocean. That was double its height at the moment the Nautilus had sunk beneath the waves.

I carefully noted these various depths, and in this way I obtained the underwater profile of this range that unfolded beneath the waters.

That evening no change had come in our situation. Still the ice between four hundred and five hundred meters of depth. A clear decrease, but what a thickness still between us and the surface of the ocean!

It was eight o'clock by then. For four hours already the air inside the Nautilus should have been renewed, according to the daily custom aboard. Yet I was not suffering too much, though Captain Nemo had not yet drawn on his tanks for an extra supply of oxygen.

My sleep was troubled that night. Hope and fear besieged me by turns. I got up several times. The Nautilus went on groping. Toward three in the morning I noticed that the lower surface of the ice barrier was met at only fifty meters of depth. A hundred and fifty feet separated us then from the surface of the water. The ice barrier was gradually becoming ice-field again. The mountain was turning back into plain.

My eyes never left the pressure gauge. We kept rising, following a diagonal along the resplendent surface that glittered under the electric rays. The ice barrier sloped away above and below in long ramps. It thinned out mile by mile.

At last, at six in the morning, on that memorable day of March 19, the salon door opened. Captain Nemo appeared.

"The open sea!" he said to me.

The South Pole

I rushed up to the platform. Yes! Open sea. Only a few scattered chunks of ice here and there, a handful of drifting icebergs; far off, a wide expanse of water; a whole world of birds in the air, and myriads of fish beneath the waves, which — depending on the depth — shaded from deep blue to olive green. The thermometer read three degrees Celsius above zero. It was like a kind of relative springtime, sealed away behind that ice barrier whose distant masses stood out along the northern horizon.

"Are we at the Pole?" I asked the captain, my heart pounding.

"I don't know," he answered. "At noon we'll take our bearings."

"But will the sun show itself through these mists?" I said, glancing at the grayish sky.

"However little of it appears, that will be enough for me," the captain replied.

Ten miles from the Nautilus, to the south, a solitary islet rose to a height of two hundred meters. We steered toward it, cautiously, for these waters might be strewn with reefs.

An hour later we had reached the islet. Two hours after that we had finished circling it. It measured four or five miles around. A narrow channel separated it from a considerable stretch of land — a continent, perhaps — whose limits we could not make out.

The existence of this land seemed to bear out Maury's hypotheses. The American engineer had noticed, in fact, that between the South Pole and the sixtieth parallel the sea is covered with floating ice of enormous size, the like of which is never met with in the North Atlantic. From this he drew the conclusion that the Antarctic Circle encloses considerable landmasses, since icebergs cannot form in the open sea but only along coasts. According to his calculations, the mass of ice that wraps the southern pole forms a vast cap whose width must reach four thousand kilometers.

Meanwhile the Nautilus, for fear of running aground, had halted three cable-lengths off a beach dominated by a magnificent heap of rocks. The dinghy was launched. The captain, two of his men carrying the instruments, Conseil, and I climbed aboard it. It was ten o'clock in the morning. I had not seen Ned Land. No doubt the Canadian did not want to eat his own words in the presence of the South Pole.

A few strokes of the oars brought the dinghy onto the sand, where it ran aground. Just as Conseil was about to leap ashore, I held him back.

"Captain," I said to Nemo, "the honor of setting foot first on this land is yours."

"Yes, sir," the captain answered, "and if I do not hesitate to tread this polar soil, it is because, until now, no human being has ever left the print of a footstep here."

With that, he sprang lightly onto the sand. A keen emotion set his heart beating hard. He climbed a rock that ended in an overhang above a little promontory, and there, arms folded, his gaze burning, motionless, silent, he seemed to take possession of these southern regions. After five minutes spent in this rapture, he turned back toward us.

"Whenever you're ready, sir," he called to me.

I stepped ashore, followed by Conseil, leaving the two men in the dinghy.

Over a long stretch, the ground showed a reddish tufa, as though it were made of crushed brick. Scoria, streams of lava, and pumice stones covered it. There was no mistaking its volcanic origin. In certain spots, faint wisps of steam gave off a sulfurous smell, proof that the inner fires still kept their power to erupt. And yet, having climbed a high escarpment, I could see no volcano within a radius of several miles. It is known that in these Antarctic regions James Ross found the craters of Erebus and Terror in full activity on the one hundred and sixty-seventh meridian, at 77°32′ of latitude.

The vegetation of this desolate continent struck me as extremely sparse. A few lichens of the species Usnea melanoxantha spread over the black rocks. Certain microscopic plantlets, rudimentary diatoms — a sort of cell set between two quartz shells — and long fronds of crimson and purple wrack, buoyed up by little swim bladders and flung ashore by the surf, made up the whole meager flora of the region.

The shore was strewn with mollusks: small mussels, limpets, smooth heart-shaped cockles, and above all with clios, their bodies oblong and membranous, the head formed of two rounded lobes. I also saw myriads of those boreal clios, three centimeters long, of which a whale swallows a whole world at every mouthful. These charming pteropods — true butterflies of the sea — brought the open water to life all along the edge of the shore.

Among the other zoophytes there appeared, in the shallows, a few coral-like growths of the kind that, according to James Ross, live in the Antarctic seas down to a depth of a thousand meters; then small soft corals of the species Procellaria pelagica, and a great number of the starfish peculiar to these climates, sea stars that studded the ground like constellations.

But it was in the air that life overflowed. There, by the thousands, flew and fluttered birds of every kind, deafening us with their cries. Others crowded the rocks, watching us pass without fear and pressing familiarly under our feet. These were penguins, as nimble and supple in the water — where they have sometimes been mistaken for darting bonitos — as they are clumsy and heavy on land. They let out strange, harsh cries and gathered in great assemblies, sparing of gesture but lavish with clamor.

Among the birds, I noticed sheathbills, of the wader family, as big as pigeons, white in color, with short conical beaks and eyes ringed with red. Conseil laid in a supply of them, for these fowl, properly prepared, make an agreeable dish. Through the air passed sooty albatrosses with a wingspan of four meters, rightly called the vultures of the Ocean; giant petrels, among them the quebrantahuesos, or bone-breakers, with their arched wings, great eaters of seals; cape petrels, a kind of little duck black and white on the upper body; and finally a whole run of petrels, some whitish with brown-edged wings, others blue and peculiar to the Antarctic seas — the latter "so oily," I said to Conseil, "that the islanders of the Faroes need only fit them with a wick before lighting them."

"A little more," Conseil replied, "and they'd be perfect lamps! After that, we can hardly demand that nature should have gone and fitted them with a wick in advance!"

Half a mile on, the ground was riddled with the nests of penguins, a sort of burrow arranged for laying eggs, out of which streamed great numbers of birds. Captain Nemo later had several hundred of them hunted, for their dark flesh is very edible. They brayed like donkeys. These creatures, the size of a goose, slate-gray on the body, white underneath, and collared with a band of lemon yellow, let themselves be killed by the throw of a stone without even trying to flee.

Still the mist did not lift, and by eleven o'clock the sun had not yet appeared. Its absence troubled me more than a little. Without it, no observations were possible. How then were we to determine whether we had reached the Pole?

When I rejoined Captain Nemo, I found him silently leaning on a piece of rock, gazing up at the sky. He seemed impatient, vexed. But what could be done? This bold and powerful man did not command the sun as he commanded the sea.

Noon came without the day-star's having shown itself for a single instant. One could not even make out the place it occupied behind the curtain of mist. Before long the mist dissolved into snow.

"Until tomorrow," the captain said to me simply, and we made our way back to the Nautilus through swirling gusts of falling snow.

During our absence, the nets had been let out, and I studied with interest the fish that had just been hauled aboard. The Antarctic seas serve as a refuge for a great many migratory species that flee the storms of the lower latitudes only to fall, it is true, into the teeth of porpoises and seals. I noted a few southern cottus, a decimeter long, a kind of whitish cartilaginous fish crossed with livid bands and armed with spines; then some Antarctic chimaeras, three feet long, their bodies very elongated, the skin white, silvery, and smooth, the head rounded, the back fitted with three fins, the snout ending in a trunk that curves back toward the mouth. I tasted their flesh, but found it insipid, despite the opinion of Conseil, who made a very good meal of it.

The snowstorm lasted until the next day. It was impossible to stay on the platform. From the salon, where I was writing up the incidents of this excursion onto the polar continent, I could hear the cries of the petrels and albatrosses sporting in the middle of the tempest. The Nautilus did not stay still; running along the coast, it pushed another ten miles or so to the south, through that half-light the sun left as it grazed the edge of the horizon.

The next day, March 20, the snow had stopped. The cold was a little sharper. The thermometer read two degrees below zero. The fogs lifted, and I hoped that, on that day, our observation could be carried out.

Captain Nemo not having yet appeared, the dinghy took Conseil and me and set us ashore. The nature of the ground was the same — volcanic. Everywhere traces of lava, scoria, and basalt, without my catching sight of the crater that had spewed them out. Here as there, myriads of birds brought this part of the polar continent to life. But this realm they now shared with vast herds of marine mammals that watched us with their gentle eyes. They were seals of various species, some stretched out on the ground, others lying on drifting ice floes, several coming out of the sea or going back into it. They did not flee at our approach, never having had any dealings with man, and I counted enough of them there to stock a few hundred ships.

"My word," said Conseil, "it's a good thing Ned Land didn't come along with us!"

"Why is that, Conseil?"

"Because the mad hunter would have killed everything in sight."

"'Everything' is saying a lot, but I do think, in fact, that we couldn't have kept our friend the Canadian from harpooning a few of these magnificent creatures. Which would have displeased Captain Nemo, for he does not shed the blood of harmless beasts for nothing."

"He's right."

"Certainly, Conseil. But tell me — haven't you already classified these superb specimens of marine fauna?"

"Master knows perfectly well," Conseil replied, "that I'm not very strong on the practical side. Once Master has taught me the name of these animals..."

"They're seals and walruses."

"Two genera belonging to the family of pinnipeds," my learned Conseil hastened to say, "order of the carnivores, group of the unguiculates, subclass of the monodelphians, class of the mammals, phylum of the vertebrates."

"Well done, Conseil," I answered, "but these two genera, seals and walruses, divide into species, and if I'm not mistaken, we'll have the chance to observe them here. Let's walk on."

It was eight o'clock in the morning. We had four hours left to fill before the sun could be usefully observed. I directed our steps toward a broad bay that scooped into the granite cliff of the shore.

There, I can say, as far as the eye could see all around us, the land and the ice floes were crowded with marine mammals, and I found myself instinctively looking for old Proteus, the mythological herdsman who watched over these immense flocks of Neptune. They were seals, above all. They formed distinct groups, males and females, the father keeping watch over his family, the mother nursing her young, a few of the juveniles, already strong, striking out on their own a few paces off. When these mammals wanted to move, they went by little hops produced by the contraction of their bodies, helping themselves along rather awkwardly with their imperfect flipper, which, in the manatee — their cousin — forms a true forearm. I should say that in the water, their element above all others, these animals with their supple spine, narrow pelvis, short close-set fur, and webbed feet swim admirably. At rest and on land, they took on extremely graceful attitudes. And so it was that the ancients, observing their gentle expressions, their eloquent gaze that not even the loveliest gaze of a woman could surpass, their velvety limpid eyes, their charming poses, and poeticizing them in their own fashion, transformed the males into tritons and the females into sirens.

I pointed out to Conseil the considerable development of the cerebral lobes in these intelligent creatures. No mammal, man excepted, has richer brain matter. And so seals are capable of receiving a certain education; they are easily domesticated, and I believe, along with certain naturalists, that suitably trained they could render great service as fishing dogs.

Most of these seals were sleeping on the rocks or on the sand. Among these true seals — which have no external ears, differing in this from the sea lions whose ear is prominent — I observed several varieties of sea leopards, three meters long, white-furred, with bulldog heads, armed with ten teeth in each jaw, four incisors above and below and two great canines cut in the shape of a fleur-de-lis. Among them slid sea elephants, a kind of seal with a short mobile trunk, the giants of the species, which over a girth of twenty feet measured a length of ten meters. They made no movement at our approach.

"They aren't dangerous animals?" Conseil asked me.

"No," I answered, "unless you attack them. When a seal defends its pup, its fury is terrible, and it isn't rare for it to smash a fisherman's boat to pieces."

"It's within its rights," Conseil replied.

"I won't say otherwise."

Two miles farther on, we were brought up short by the headland that sheltered the bay against the south winds. It dropped sheer to the sea and foamed under the surf. Beyond it came bursts of tremendous bellowing, the kind a herd of cattle might produce.

"Well now," said Conseil, "a concert of bulls?"

"No," I said, "a concert of walruses. Are they fighting?"

"They're fighting or they're playing."

"With Master's leave, we ought to see this."

"We ought to, Conseil."

And so there we were, scrambling across the blackish rocks amid unexpected landslides, over stones the ice made dangerously slippery. More than once I went down, at the expense of my back. Conseil, more careful or more sure-footed, barely stumbled at all, and would haul me up, saying:

"If Master would be so good as to spread his legs a little, Master would keep his balance better."

Reaching the upper ridge of the headland, I saw a vast white plain covered with walruses. The animals were playing among themselves. Those were howls of joy, not of anger.

Walruses resemble seals in the shape of their bodies and the arrangement of their limbs. But they lack canines and incisors in the lower jaw, and as for the upper canines, these are two tusks eighty centimeters long that measure thirty-three around at the socket. These teeth, made of a compact, unstreaked ivory harder than an elephant's and slower to yellow, are much sought after. And so the walruses are the target of a reckless hunt that will soon destroy them down to the last one, since the hunters, slaughtering pregnant females and young indiscriminately, kill off more than four thousand of them every year.

Passing close by these curious creatures, I could examine them at my leisure, for they did not stir. Their hide was thick and rough, a tawny tone verging on red, their coat short and sparse. Some measured four meters in length. Calmer and less fearful than their northern cousins, they posted no chosen sentinels to watch the approaches to their camp.

After examining this city of walruses, I thought about turning back. It was eleven o'clock, and if Captain Nemo found conditions favorable for an observation, I wanted to be present for it. Yet I had little hope the sun would show itself that day. Clouds crushed low against the horizon hid it from our eyes. It was as if that jealous star refused to reveal this unreachable point of the globe to human beings.

Still, I made up my mind to head back to the Nautilus. We followed a narrow path that ran along the top of the cliff. At half past eleven we had reached the landing place. The beached dinghy had set the captain ashore. I spotted him standing on a block of basalt, his instruments beside him. His gaze was fixed on the northern horizon, near which the sun was tracing its long, drawn-out arc.

I took my place beside him and waited without a word. Noon came, and, just as the day before, the sun did not appear.

It was a stroke of bad luck. The observation had failed once more. If it was not accomplished tomorrow, we would have to give up for good on fixing our position.

For we were precisely at March 20. Tomorrow, the 21st, the day of the equinox, refraction not counted, the sun would drop below the horizon for six months, and with its disappearance the long polar night would begin. Since the September equinox it had risen out of the northern horizon, climbing in long spirals until December 21. At that point, the summer solstice of these austral regions, it had begun to descend again, and tomorrow it would cast down its last rays.

I shared my observations and my fears with Captain Nemo.

"You were right, Professor Aronnax," he told me. "If tomorrow I don't get the sun's altitude, I won't be able to try again for six months. But then, precisely because the chances of my navigation have brought me into these seas on March 21, my position will be easy to fix — if, at noon, the sun shows itself to us."

"Why is that, Captain?"

"Because when the sun traces such long spirals, it's difficult to measure its height above the horizon exactly, and the instruments are apt to make serious errors."

"So how will you proceed?"

"I'll use nothing but my chronometer," Captain Nemo answered. "If tomorrow, March 21, at noon, the sun's disk — allowing for refraction — is cut exactly in half by the northern horizon, then I'm at the South Pole."

"True enough," I said. "But that claim isn't mathematically rigorous, because the equinox doesn't necessarily fall at noon."

"No doubt, Professor, but the error won't be a hundred meters, and we need no better than that. Until tomorrow, then."

Captain Nemo returned aboard. Conseil and I stayed until five o'clock, pacing the beach, observing and studying. I gathered nothing curious, except for a penguin egg, remarkable for its size, that a collector would have paid more than a thousand francs for. Its isabelline color, the streaks and markings that adorned it like so many hieroglyphs, made it a rare trinket. I placed it in Conseil's hands, and the careful, sure-footed lad carried it back intact to the Nautilus, holding it like a precious piece of China porcelain.

There I set that rare egg beneath one of the museum's display cases. I had a hearty supper of an excellent slice of seal liver, whose taste recalled that of pork. Then I went to bed, not without invoking, like a Hindu, the favors of the radiant star.

The next morning, March 21, at five o'clock, I climbed up onto the platform. There I found Captain Nemo.

"The weather is clearing a little," he told me. "I have good hopes. After breakfast we'll go ashore to choose an observation post."

That settled, I went to find Ned Land. I would have liked to bring him with me. The stubborn Canadian refused, and I could plainly see that his silence, like his foul temper, was growing worse by the day. All in all, I didn't regret his obstinacy in this instance. There truly were too many seals ashore, and it wouldn't do to subject that rash hunter to such temptation.

Breakfast over, I went ashore. The Nautilus had risen a few more miles during the night. It lay well out, a good league from a coast dominated by a sharp peak four or five hundred meters high. The dinghy carried, along with me, Captain Nemo, two of the crew, and the instruments — that is, a chronometer, a telescope, and a barometer.

During our crossing I saw numerous whales belonging to the three species peculiar to the southern seas: the right whale — the "right-whale" of the English — which has no dorsal fin; the humpback, a rorqual with a pleated belly and vast whitish flippers that, despite their name, do not form wings; and the fin-back, a brownish-yellow whale, the liveliest of the cetaceans. This powerful animal can be heard from far off when it hurls up to a great height its columns of air and vapor, which look like whirls of smoke. These various mammals were frolicking in herds through the calm waters, and I could plainly see that this basin at the Antarctic pole now served as a refuge for the cetaceans too fiercely hunted by the whalers.

I also noticed long whitish strings of salps, a kind of clustered mollusk, and large jellyfish that swayed among the swell of the waves.

At nine o'clock we made land. The sky was brightening. The clouds were fleeing to the south. The mists were abandoning the cold surface of the waters. Captain Nemo made for the peak, which he no doubt meant to use as his observatory. It was a grueling climb over sharp lava and pumice stones, through an atmosphere often saturated with the sulfurous fumes of the fumaroles. The captain, for a man unused to treading on land, scaled the steepest slopes with a suppleness and an agility I couldn't match, and that a chamois hunter would have envied.

It took us two hours to reach the summit of this peak, half porphyry, half basalt. From there our eyes took in a vast sea that, toward the north, drew its terminal line sharply against the backdrop of the sky. At our feet, fields dazzling with whiteness. Over our heads, a pale blue, cleared of mist. To the north, the sun's disk like a ball of fire already notched by the edge of the horizon. From the bosom of the waters rose magnificent sheaves of liquid jets, hundreds of them. In the distance, the Nautilus, like a sleeping whale. Behind us, toward the south and east, an immense land, a chaotic heaping-up of rocks and ice whose limit could not be seen.

Reaching the summit of the peak, Captain Nemo carefully took its altitude by means of the barometer, since he would have to account for it in his observation.

At a quarter to noon the sun, now seen by refraction alone, showed itself as a disk of gold and scattered its last rays over this abandoned continent, over these seas that man has never yet furrowed.

Captain Nemo, equipped with a telescope fitted with crosshairs that corrected the refraction by means of a mirror, watched the sun sink little by little below the horizon, following a long-drawn-out diagonal. I held the chronometer. My heart was pounding. If the disappearance of the sun's half-disk coincided with noon on the chronometer, we were at the pole itself.

"Noon!" I cried out.

"The South Pole!" answered Captain Nemo in a grave voice, handing me the telescope, which showed the sun cut precisely into two equal portions by the horizon.

I watched the last rays crown the peak and the shadows climb slowly up its slopes.

At that moment Captain Nemo, resting his hand on my shoulder, said to me:

"Professor, in 1600 the Dutchman Ghéritk, carried off by currents and storms, reached 64° south latitude and discovered the New Shetlands. In 1773, on January 17, the illustrious Cook, following the thirty-eighth meridian, arrived at 67°30′ latitude, and in 1774, on January 30, on the one hundred and ninth meridian, he reached 71°15′ latitude. In 1819 the Russian Bellingshausen found himself on the sixty-ninth parallel, and in 1821 on the sixty-sixth at 111° west longitude. In 1820 the Englishman Brunsfield was stopped at the sixty-fifth degree. That same year the American Morrell, whose accounts are doubtful, working up the forty-second meridian, discovered open sea at 70°14′ latitude. In 1825 the Englishman Powell could get no farther than the sixty-second degree. That same year a simple seal-hunter, the Englishman Weddell, rose to 72°14′ latitude on the thirty-fifth meridian, and to 74°15′ on the thirty-sixth. In 1829 the Englishman Forster, commanding the Chanticleer, took possession of the Antarctic continent at 63°26′ latitude and 66°26′ longitude. In 1831 the Englishman Biscoe, on February 1, discovered Enderby Land at 68°50′ latitude; in 1832, on February 5, Adelaide Land at 67° latitude, and on February 21, Graham Land at 64°45′ latitude. In 1838 the Frenchman Dumont d'Urville, stopped before the ice pack at 62°57′ latitude, charted Louis-Philippe Land; two years later, on a fresh thrust south, he named — at 66°30′, on January 21 — Adélie Land, and eight days after that, at 64°40′, the Clarie Coast. In 1838 the Englishman Wilkes advanced as far as the sixty-ninth parallel on the hundredth meridian. In 1839 the Englishman Balleny discovered Sabrina Land, on the very edge of the polar circle. Finally, in 1842, the Englishman James Ross, aboard the Erebus and the Terror, on January 12, at 76°56′ latitude and 171°7′ east longitude, found Victoria Land; on the 23rd of the same month he charted the seventy-fourth parallel, the highest point reached until then; on the 27th he was at 76°8′, on the 28th at 77°32′, on February 2 at 78°4′, and in 1842 he came back to the seventy-first degree, which he could not pass. Well then — I, Captain Nemo, on this March 21, 1868, have reached the South Pole at the ninetieth degree, and I take possession of this part of the globe, equal to one-sixth of the known continents."

"In whose name, Captain?"

"In my own, Professor!"

And so saying, Captain Nemo unfurled a black flag, bearing a gold N quartered upon its field. Then, turning back toward the sun, whose last rays were licking the horizon of the sea:

"Farewell, sun!" he cried. "Vanish, radiant star! Set beneath this open sea, and let a night six months long spread its shadows over my new domain!"

Accident or Incident?

The next morning, March 22, at six o'clock, the preparations for departure began. The last glimmers of twilight were melting away into night. The cold was biting. The constellations blazed with startling intensity. At the zenith glittered that magnificent Southern Cross, the polestar of the Antarctic regions.

The thermometer read twelve degrees below zero, and when the wind freshened it bit at us in sharp stings. The ice was thickening on the open water. The sea was beginning to freeze over on every side. Great blackish patches, spread across its surface, warned of the young ice about to form. Clearly, the southern basin, frozen through the six months of its winter, was utterly inaccessible. What became of the whales during that season? No doubt they went beneath the ice pack in search of more navigable seas. As for the seals and walruses, used to living in the harshest climates, they stayed on in these frozen waters. These animals have the instinct to dig holes in the ice-fields and keep them permanently open. They come to those holes to breathe; and once the birds, driven off by the cold, have migrated north, these marine mammals remain the sole masters of the polar continent.

Meanwhile the ballast tanks had filled, and the Nautilus was sinking slowly. At a depth of a thousand feet, it came to a stop. Its screw churned the water, and it drove due north at a speed of fifteen miles an hour. Toward evening it was already gliding beneath the immense frozen carapace of the ice pack.

The panels of the salon had been closed as a precaution, since the Nautilus's hull might strike some submerged block. So I spent the day writing up my notes. My mind was entirely given over to my memories of the Pole. We had reached that unreachable point without hardship, without danger, as though our floating railway car had simply slid along the rails of a track. And now the return had truly begun. Would it hold still more surprises like these in store for me? I believed it would, so inexhaustible is the parade of undersea marvels! Even so, in the five and a half months since chance had thrown us aboard this vessel, we had crossed fourteen thousand leagues, and along that course — longer than the earth's own Equator — how many incidents, curious or terrible, had graced our voyage: the hunt in the forests of Crespo, the grounding in the Torres Strait, the coral cemetery, the Ceylon fisheries, the Arabian tunnel, the fires of Santorini, the millions in Vigo Bay, Atlantis, the South Pole! All night long these memories, passing from dream to dream, never let my brain doze for an instant.

At three in the morning I was jolted awake by a violent shock. I had sat bolt upright in my bed and was listening in the darkness when I was flung headlong into the middle of the room. Clearly the Nautilus had struck something and heeled hard over.

I braced myself against the walls and dragged myself along the passageways to the salon, which was lit by its luminous ceiling. The furniture was overturned. Fortunately the display cases, firmly bolted at the base, had held fast. The pictures on the starboard side, following the shift in the vertical, were flattened against the tapestries, while those on the port side had swung a foot clear of the wall at their lower edge. So the Nautilus was lying over on its starboard side — and, what was more, completely motionless.

Inside I could hear footsteps and a confusion of voices. But Captain Nemo did not appear. Just as I was about to leave the salon, Ned Land and Conseil came in.

"What's happened?" I asked at once.

"I came to ask Master the very same thing," Conseil replied.

"A thousand devils!" cried the Canadian. "I know well enough what's happened! The Nautilus has struck, and judging by the list she's taking, I don't think she'll get out of it the way she did the first time, in the Torres Strait."

"But has she at least come back to the surface of the sea?" I asked.

"We don't know," Conseil answered.

"That's easy enough to find out," I said.

I checked the pressure gauge. To my great surprise, it showed a depth of three hundred and sixty meters.

"What does this mean?" I exclaimed.

"We'll have to ask Captain Nemo," said Conseil.

"But where do we find him?" asked Ned Land.

"Follow me," I told my two companions.

We left the salon. In the library, no one. At the central staircase, at the crew's quarters, no one. I supposed Captain Nemo must be stationed in the pilot's cage. The best thing was to wait. We all three went back to the salon.

I'll pass over the Canadian's grumbling. He had every excuse to lose his temper. I let him vent his ill humor to his heart's content, without answering him.

We had been waiting like this for twenty minutes, straining to catch the faintest sounds inside the Nautilus, when Captain Nemo came in. He did not seem to see us. His face, usually so impassive, betrayed a certain uneasiness. He studied the compass and the pressure gauge in silence, then laid his finger on a point of the chart, in that region representing the southern seas.

I did not want to interrupt him. Only some moments later, when he turned toward me, did I say to him — turning back on him an expression he himself had used in the Torres Strait:

"An incident, Captain?"

"No, Professor," he replied. "An accident this time."

"A serious one?"

"Perhaps."

"Is the danger immediate?"

"No."

"The Nautilus has run aground?"

"Yes."

"And this grounding came about...?"

"Through a whim of nature, not the incompetence of men. Not a single error was made in our maneuvers. And yet there's no preventing equilibrium from producing its effects. One may defy the laws of men, but one cannot resist the laws of nature."

An odd moment Captain Nemo had chosen to indulge in this philosophical reflection. In sum, his answer told me nothing.

"May I know, Captain," I asked him, "the cause of this accident?"

"An enormous block of ice, a whole mountain, has turned over," he answered. "When icebergs are undermined at their base by warmer waters or by repeated blows, their center of gravity rises. Then they flip clean over — they capsize. That is what happened. One of these blocks, as it rolled over, struck the Nautilus as she floated beneath the water. Then, sliding under her hull and lifting her with irresistible force, it carried her up into less dense layers, where she now lies on her side."

"But can't we free the Nautilus by emptying her tanks, so as to bring her back into balance?"

"That is exactly what is being done at this moment, Professor. You can hear the pumps at work. Look at the needle of the pressure gauge. It shows that the Nautilus is rising, but the block of ice is rising with her, and until some obstacle checks its upward movement, our position won't change."

Sure enough, the Nautilus was still taking the same list to starboard. No doubt she would right herself once the block itself came to a stop. But at that point, who could say whether we might not strike the underside of the ice pack — whether we might not find ourselves horribly crushed between the two frozen surfaces?

I turned over in my mind all the consequences of our situation. Captain Nemo never took his eyes off the pressure gauge. Since the iceberg's fall, the Nautilus had risen about a hundred and fifty feet, but she still held the same angle with the perpendicular.

Suddenly a slight movement made itself felt through the hull. Clearly the Nautilus was righting herself a little. The objects hanging in the salon were noticeably returning to their normal position. The walls were coming back toward the vertical. Not one of us spoke. With pounding hearts we watched, we felt the vessel come upright. The floor was leveling out beneath our feet. Ten minutes passed.

"At last, we're straight!" I exclaimed.

"Yes," said Captain Nemo, moving toward the salon door.

"But will we float?" I asked him.

"Certainly," he replied, "since the tanks aren't yet empty, and once they are, the Nautilus will have to rise back to the surface of the sea."

The captain went out, and I soon saw that, by his orders, the Nautilus's upward course had been halted. In fact she would soon have struck the underside of the ice pack, and it was better to keep her suspended between two waters.

"That was a close call!" said Conseil then.

"Yes. We might have been crushed between those blocks of ice, or at the very least imprisoned. And then, with no way to renew the air... Yes! That was a close call!"

"If it's over!" murmured Ned Land.

I had no wish to start a pointless argument with the Canadian, and I didn't answer. Besides, at that very moment the panels slid open, and the light from outside burst in through the uncovered window.

We were in open water, as I've said; but at a distance of ten meters, on either side of the Nautilus, rose a dazzling wall of ice. Above and below, the same wall. Above, because the underside of the ice pack stretched out like an immense ceiling. Below, because the capsized block, having gradually slid down, had found on the two side walls a pair of resting points that held it in this position. The Nautilus was imprisoned in a veritable tunnel of ice, roughly twenty meters wide, filled with still water. It would be easy enough for her to get out of it, then, by going either forward or astern, and to pick up again, a few hundred meters lower down, a clear passage beneath the ice pack.

The luminous ceiling had been switched off, and yet the salon still blazed with an intense light. The powerful reverberation of the ice walls was flinging the sheets of the beacon violently back into the room. I could never paint the effect of the voltaic rays on those great, whimsically cut blocks, each angle, each edge, each facet throwing off a different gleam according to the nature of the veins running through the ice. A dazzling mine of gemstones, and above all of sapphires crossing their blue jets with the green jet of emeralds. Here and there, opaline tints of infinite softness ran among burning points like so many diamonds of fire, whose brilliance the eye could not bear. The power of the beacon was multiplied a hundredfold, like that of a lamp shining through the lenticular panes of a first-order lighthouse.

"How beautiful! How beautiful!" cried Conseil.

"Yes!" I said. "It's a magnificent spectacle. Isn't it, Ned?"

"Eh! a thousand devils! yes," Ned Land shot back. "It's superb! I'm furious at being forced to admit it. No one's ever seen anything like it. But this spectacle here may cost us dear. And if I'm to say everything I think, I believe we're looking at things God meant to keep hidden from the eyes of man!"

Ned was right. It was too beautiful. All at once a cry from Conseil made me spin around.

"What is it?" I asked.

"Let Master shut his eyes! Let Master not look!"

As he said this, Conseil clapped his hands hard over his eyelids.

"But what's the matter, my boy?"

"I'm dazzled, blinded!"

My eyes turned involuntarily toward the window, but I couldn't bear the fire that was devouring it.

I understood what had happened. The Nautilus had just gotten under way at high speed. All the quiet flashes of the ice walls had suddenly turned into streaks of lightning. The fires of those myriad diamonds ran together. Carried along by her screw, the Nautilus was traveling through a sheath of lightning.

The panels of the salon then closed again. We kept our hands over our eyes, still steeped in those concentric glimmers that swim before the retina when the sun's rays have struck it too violently. It took a while for the disturbance in our sight to settle.

At last our hands came down.

"My word, I would never have believed it," said Conseil.

"And I still don't believe it!" the Canadian shot back.

"When we get back on land," Conseil added, "so jaded by all these wonders of nature, what will we think of those miserable continents and the little works turned out by the hand of man! No! the inhabited world is no longer worthy of us!"

Such words in the mouth of an unflappable Fleming showed just how far our enthusiasm had come to the boil. But the Canadian didn't fail to toss in his drop of cold water.

"The inhabited world!" he said, shaking his head. "Don't worry, friend Conseil, we won't be going back to it!"

It was then five o'clock in the morning. At that instant a shock came from the bow of the Nautilus. I understood that her ram had just struck a block of ice. It must have been a wrong maneuver, for this undersea tunnel, choked with blocks, made for no easy sailing. So I supposed that Captain Nemo, altering his course, would steer around these obstacles or follow the windings of the tunnel. In any case, the forward march could not be brought altogether to a halt. And yet, against my expectation, the Nautilus took a very pronounced backward movement.

"We're going astern?" said Conseil.

"Yes," I replied. "The tunnel must have no way out on this side."

"And so...?"

"So," I said, "the maneuver is simple enough. We'll retrace our steps and go out by the southern opening. That's all there is to it."

As I spoke this way, I wanted to appear more reassured than I really was. Meanwhile the Nautilus's backward movement was gathering speed, and driving against her screw, she was carrying us along at great pace.

"It'll mean a delay," said Ned.

"What does that matter — a few hours more or less, so long as we get out."

"Yes," Ned Land repeated, "so long as we get out!"

I paced for a while between the salon and the library. My companions sat in silence. Soon I threw myself down on a divan and picked up a book, which my eyes ran over mechanically.

A quarter of an hour later, Conseil came over to me and said:

"Is it very interesting, what Master is reading?"

"Very interesting," I replied.

"I don't doubt it. It's Master's own book that Master is reading!"

"My book?"

Sure enough, I was holding in my hand the work on The Mysteries of the Great Ocean Depths. I hadn't even realized it. I closed the book and resumed my pacing. Ned and Conseil rose to withdraw.

"Stay, my friends," I said, holding them back. "Let's stay together until we're out of this dead end."

"Just as Master pleases," Conseil replied.

A few hours went by. I often watched the instruments hanging on the salon wall. The pressure gauge showed that the Nautilus was holding at a constant depth of three hundred meters; the compass, that she was still steering south; the log, that she was moving at a speed of twenty miles an hour — an excessive speed in so cramped a space. But Captain Nemo knew he couldn't go too fast, and that now the minutes were worth centuries.

At twenty-five minutes past eight, a second shock came. From the stern, this time. I turned pale. My companions had drawn close to me. I had seized Conseil's hand. We questioned one another with our eyes, more directly than if words had put our thoughts into speech.

At that moment the captain came into the salon. I went up to him.

"The way is barred to the south?" I asked him.

"Yes, Professor. The iceberg, in turning over, has closed off every way out."

"We're blocked?"

"Yes."

For Want of Air

So there it was: all around the Nautilus, above and below, an impenetrable wall of ice. We were prisoners of the ice pack. The Canadian brought one of his massive fists down on a table. Conseil said nothing. I looked at the captain. His face had settled back into its usual calm. He stood with his arms crossed, thinking. The Nautilus no longer stirred.

Then the captain spoke.

"Gentlemen," he said in a level voice, "there are two ways to die in the situation we're in."

This inexplicable man had the air of a mathematics professor laying out a proof for his students.

"The first," he went on, "is to be crushed. The second is to suffocate. I leave aside the possibility of starving, because the Nautilus's stores will certainly outlast us. So let us think about our chances of being crushed — or of asphyxiation."

"As for suffocating, Captain," I answered, "that's nothing to fear. Our tanks are full."

"True," said Captain Nemo, "but they'll give us only two days of air. Now, we've been buried under the water for thirty-six hours already, and the Nautilus's heavy atmosphere is asking to be renewed. In forty-eight hours, our reserve will be exhausted."

"Then, Captain, let's be free before forty-eight hours are out!"

"We'll at least attempt it, by cutting through the wall that surrounds us."

"On which side?" I asked.

"The sounding line will tell us. I'm going to ground the Nautilus on the lower shelf, and my men, in their diving suits, will attack the iceberg where its wall is thinnest."

"Can we open the panels of the salon?"

"No harm in it. We're no longer moving."

Captain Nemo went out. Soon a hissing told me that water was pouring into the tanks. The Nautilus sank slowly and came to rest on the bed of ice at a depth of three hundred and fifty meters — the depth at which the lower ice shelf lay submerged.

"My friends," I said, "the situation is grave, but I'm counting on your courage and your energy."

"Professor," the Canadian answered, "this is no moment to pester you with my complaints. I'm ready to do anything for the good of all of us."

"Well said, Ned," I told him, holding out my hand.

"Let me add," he went on, "that I'm as handy with a pick as with a harpoon. If I can be of use to the captain, he can put me to work."

"He won't turn down your help. Come on, Ned."

I led the Canadian to the room where the Nautilus's men were pulling on their diving suits. I passed Ned's offer along to the captain, who accepted it. The Canadian climbed into his sea gear and was ready as quickly as his fellow workers. Each of them carried on his back a Rouquayrol apparatus, into which the tanks had fed a generous supply of pure air. A heavy draw on the Nautilus's reserve, but a necessary one. As for the Ruhmkorff lamps, they were useless here, in water so bright and saturated with electric light.

Once Ned was dressed, I went back into the salon, whose windows were uncovered, and, taking my place beside Conseil, I studied the surrounding layers that held the Nautilus in place.

A few moments later, we saw a dozen crewmen set foot on the ice shelf, Ned Land among them, easy to pick out by his height. Captain Nemo was with them.

Before beginning to cut into the walls, he had soundings taken to make sure the work would be aimed in the right direction. Long probes were driven into the side walls, but after fifteen meters they were still stopped by the thick barrier. There was no point attacking the ceiling above us, since that was the ice pack itself, more than four hundred meters high. Captain Nemo then had the lower surface sounded. There, ten meters of ice separated us from the water. That was the thickness of the ice field. From now on, the task was to cut out a slab equal in area to the Nautilus's waterline — about six thousand five hundred cubic meters to detach, in order to open a hole through which we could descend below the ice field.

The work began at once and was carried on with tireless stubbornness. Rather than dig all the way around the Nautilus, which would have brought greater difficulties, Captain Nemo had the enormous pit traced out eight meters off her port quarter. Then his men bored into it at several points around its circumference at the same time. Soon the picks were biting hard into that dense material, and big blocks were torn loose from the mass. By a curious effect of specific gravity, these blocks, lighter than water, flew up, so to speak, to the roof of the tunnel, which thickened above by as much as it thinned below. But that hardly mattered, as long as the lower wall was growing thinner all the while.

After two hours of hard work, Ned Land came back exhausted. He and his companions were relieved by fresh workers, whom Conseil and I joined. The Nautilus's first officer directed us.

The water struck me as strangely cold, but I warmed up quickly once I was swinging the pick. My movements were quite free, even though I was working under a pressure of thirty atmospheres.

When I came back inside after two hours of work, to take some food and some rest, I found a marked difference between the pure air the Rouquayrol apparatus fed me and the atmosphere of the Nautilus, already thick with carbon dioxide. The air hadn't been renewed in forty-eight hours, and its life-giving qualities were badly weakened. And yet, in a span of twelve hours, we had removed only a layer of ice a meter thick over the marked-out area — about six hundred cubic meters. Assuming the same amount of work were done every twelve hours, it would still take five nights and four days to bring the job to a good end.

"Five nights and four days!" I said to my companions. "And we have only two days' worth of air in the tanks."

"Not to mention," Ned put in, "that once we're out of this cursed prison, we'll still be shut in under the ice pack, with no way to reach the open air."

A fair point. Who then could foresee the least amount of time we would need to win our freedom? Wouldn't we suffocate before the Nautilus could get back to the surface of the waves? Was she doomed to perish in this tomb of ice with everyone she held? The situation looked terrible. But everyone had faced it head-on, and all were resolved to do their duty to the very end.

As I'd expected, another meter-thick layer was carved out of the vast pit during the night. But in the morning, when, sheathed in my diving suit, I moved through the liquid mass in a temperature of six or seven degrees below zero, I noticed that the side walls were slowly closing in. The layers of water farther from the pit, which weren't warmed by the men's labor and the play of the tools, were showing a tendency to solidify. Faced with this new and imminent danger, what became of our chances of rescue, and how could we stop this liquid medium from freezing — a freezing that would burst the walls of the Nautilus like glass?

I didn't tell my two companions of this new danger. What good would it do to risk breaking the spirit they were pouring into the grueling work of our rescue? But when I got back on board, I pointed out this grave complication to Captain Nemo.

"I know," he said, in that calm tone that not even the most terrible circumstances could alter. "It's one more danger, but I see no way to guard against it. Our only chance of salvation is to move faster than the freezing. We have to get there first. That's all."

Get there first! Well — by now I should have been used to this way of talking.

That day I swung the pick doggedly for several hours. The work kept me going. Besides, to work was to leave the Nautilus, to breathe directly the pure air drawn from the tanks and delivered by the apparatus — to escape, for a while, an atmosphere grown thin and foul.

Toward evening, the pit had been dug another meter deeper. When I came back on board, I was nearly asphyxiated by the carbon dioxide that saturated the air. Ah, if only we'd had the chemical means to drive off that noxious gas! Oxygen we did not lack. All this water held a considerable quantity of it, and by breaking it down with our powerful batteries, it would have given us back the life-giving element. I had certainly thought of it — but what was the use, since the carbon dioxide, the product of our own breathing, had invaded every part of the ship? To absorb it, we would have had to fill containers with caustic potash and shake them without stopping. But that substance was lacking on board, and nothing could take its place.

That evening Captain Nemo had to open the valves of his tanks and release a few columns of pure air into the interior of the Nautilus. Without this precaution, we would never have woken up.

The next day, March 26, I took up my miner's work again, starting on the fifth meter. The side walls and the lower surface of the ice pack were visibly thickening. It was plain that they would meet before the Nautilus managed to break free. Despair seized me for a moment. The pick nearly slipped from my hands. What was the use of digging, if I was only going to die smothered, crushed by this water turning to stone — a torment the cruelty of savages could never even have invented? It seemed to me that I was caught between the fearsome jaws of a monster that were closing on me, and there was no resisting them.

Just then Captain Nemo, directing the work, working himself, passed near me. I touched him with my hand and pointed to the walls of our prison. The starboard wall had advanced to less than four meters from the Nautilus's hull.

The captain understood, and signaled me to follow him. We went back on board. Once I'd taken off my diving suit, I went with him into the salon.

"Professor Aronnax," he said, "we must try some heroic measure, or we're going to be sealed into this solidified water as if in cement."

"Yes!" I said. "But what can we do?"

"Ah!" he cried. "If only my Nautilus were strong enough to bear this pressure without being crushed!"

"Well?" I asked, not grasping his idea.

"Don't you see," he went on, "that this freezing of the water would come to our aid? Don't you see that by turning solid, it would burst apart the ice fields that imprison us, the way water, in freezing, bursts apart the hardest stones? Don't you feel that it would be an agent of rescue instead of an agent of destruction?"

"Yes, Captain, perhaps. But whatever resistance to crushing the Nautilus may have, it couldn't withstand that appalling pressure. It would be flattened like a sheet of tin plate."

"I know, Professor. So we mustn't count on the help of nature, but on ourselves. We have to fight this freezing. We have to hold it back. Not only are the side walls closing in, but there's not ten feet of water left ahead of or behind the Nautilus. The freezing is gaining on us from every side."

"How long," I asked, "will the air in the tanks let us breathe on board?"

The captain looked me full in the face.

"The day after tomorrow," he said, "the tanks will be empty."

A cold sweat came over me. And yet, should I have been surprised at his answer? On March 22 the Nautilus had plunged beneath the open waters of the Pole. We were now at the 26th. For five days we had been living on the ship's reserves! And what breathable air remained had to be saved for the workers. Even as I write these things, my impression of them is still so vivid that an involuntary terror takes hold of my whole being, and the air seems to fail in my lungs!

Meanwhile Captain Nemo sat thinking, silent and still. Plainly an idea was crossing his mind. But he seemed to push it away. He kept answering himself no. At last these words escaped his lips:

"Boiling water!" he murmured.

"Boiling water?" I exclaimed.

"Yes, Professor. We're shut up in a relatively confined space. Wouldn't jets of boiling water, injected constantly by the Nautilus's pumps, raise the temperature of this medium and slow its freezing?"

"It's worth trying," I said firmly.

"Then let's try it, Professor."

The thermometer outside then read seven degrees below zero. Captain Nemo led me to the galley, where huge distilling machines were at work, supplying drinking water by evaporation. They were filled with water, and all the electric heat of the batteries was sent through the coils steeped in the liquid. In a few minutes the water had reached a hundred degrees. It was directed to the pumps while fresh water took its place as fast as it was used. The heat the batteries produced was such that cold seawater, drawn up from the sea, arrived boiling at the pump chambers after passing through the machines alone.

The injection began, and three hours later the thermometer outside read six degrees below zero. That was one degree gained. Two hours after that, it read only four.

"We're going to succeed," I said to the captain, after following and checking the progress of the operation by many observations.

"I think so," he answered. "We won't be crushed. Now we have only suffocation to fear."

During the night, the temperature of the water rose to one degree below zero. The injections couldn't carry it any higher. But since seawater doesn't freeze until minus two degrees, I was reassured at last against the dangers of solidifying.

The next day, March 27, six meters of ice had been torn from the pit. Only four meters were left to remove — still forty-eight hours of work. The air on board the Nautilus could no longer be renewed. And so that day kept going from bad to worse.

An intolerable heaviness weighed me down. Toward three in the afternoon, this feeling of anguish was carried to a violent pitch. Yawns dislocated my jaws. My lungs gasped, searching for that vital, oxygen-rich element the breath needs, which was growing scarcer and scarcer. A moral torpor took hold of me. I lay stretched out without strength, all but unconscious. My good Conseil, seized with the same symptoms, suffering the same sufferings, never left my side. He took my hand, he cheered me on, and I heard him still, murmuring:

"Ah, if only I could stop breathing, to leave more air for Master!"

Tears came to my eyes to hear him speak that way.

If our situation, for all of us, was unbearable inside, with what haste, with what joy we pulled on our diving suits to take our turn at the work! The picks rang against the icy layer. Our arms tired, our hands were rubbed raw, but what were those pains, what did those wounds matter? The air of life was reaching our lungs. We could breathe! We could breathe!

And yet no one dragged his work under the water out past his allotted time. His task done, each man handed over to his gasping companions the tank that was to pour life into him. Captain Nemo set the example and was the first to submit to this stern discipline. When his hour came, he yielded his apparatus to another and went back into the ship's foul air, always calm, without a lapse, without a murmur.

That day the usual work was done with even greater vigor. Only two meters were left to cut through over the whole surface. Only two meters separated us from the open sea. But the tanks were nearly empty of air. The little that remained had to be saved for the workers. Not one atom for the Nautilus!

When I came back on board, I was half suffocated. What a night! I couldn't paint it. Such sufferings can't be described. The next day, my breathing was labored. To my headaches were joined dizzying spells of vertigo that made a drunken man of me. My companions felt the same symptoms. Some of the crewmen were rattling in their throats.

That day, the sixth of our imprisonment, Captain Nemo, finding the mattock and the pick too slow, resolved to crush the layer of ice that still separated us from the open water. This man had kept his cool and his energy. By his moral force he mastered his physical pains. He thought, he calculated, he acted.

On his order, the vessel was lightened — that is, raised off the icy bed by a change in specific gravity. Once she floated, she was hauled into position over the huge pit that had been traced out to follow her waterline. Then, as her ballast tanks filled, she came down and fitted herself into the socket.

At that moment the entire crew came back on board, and the double communication door was shut. The Nautilus now rested on the layer of ice that was less than a meter thick, and that the soundings had pierced in a thousand places.

The valves of the tanks were then thrown wide open, and a hundred cubic meters of water rushed in, adding a hundred thousand kilograms to the weight of the Nautilus.

We waited, we listened, forgetting our sufferings, still hoping. We were staking our survival on one last throw.

In spite of the roaring that filled my head, I soon heard a shuddering under the Nautilus's hull. The ship tilted. The ice cracked with a strange, sharp sound, like paper tearing, and the Nautilus dropped.

"We're through!" Conseil murmured in my ear.

I couldn't answer him. I seized his hand. I gripped it in a helpless spasm.

All at once, carried away by her fearful overload, the Nautilus plunged beneath the water like a cannonball — that is, she fell as she would have fallen through empty air.

The whole electric power was thrown onto the pumps, which at once began driving the water out of the tanks. After a few minutes, our fall was checked. Soon the pressure gauge even showed an upward movement. The screw, running at full speed, made the plated hull shudder down to its bolts, and swept us northward.

But how long would this voyage under the ice pack last before we reached open sea? Another day? I'd be dead before then!

Half stretched out on a divan in the library, I was suffocating. My face was purple, my lips blue, my faculties suspended. I could no longer see, no longer hear. Any sense of time had vanished from my mind. My muscles couldn't contract.

How many hours passed like that, I couldn't say. But I was aware of my death agony beginning. I understood that I was going to die.

Suddenly I came to. A few puffs of air worked their way into my lungs. Had we come back up to the surface of the waves? Had we crossed the ice pack?

No! It was Ned and Conseil, my two brave friends, sacrificing themselves to save me. A few atoms of air were still left at the bottom of an apparatus. Instead of breathing it themselves, they had saved it for me, and, while they were suffocating, they poured life into me drop by drop! I tried to push the apparatus away. They held my hands, and for a few moments I breathed with rapture.

My eyes turned to the clock. It was eleven in the morning. We must be at the 28th of March. The Nautilus was traveling at the terrifying speed of forty miles an hour. She was writhing through the water.

Where was Captain Nemo? Had he gone under? Had his companions died with him?

At that moment the pressure gauge showed we were no more than twenty feet from the surface. A single field of ice separated us from the open air. Couldn't it be broken?

Perhaps! In any case, the Nautilus was going to try. I felt her take a slanting position, dropping her stern and raising her ram. A single intake of water had been enough to break her balance. Then, driven by her powerful screw, she attacked the ice field from below like a colossal battering ram. She split it little by little, drew back, charged again at full speed against the field that was tearing apart, and finally, carried by one supreme burst, she flung herself onto the icy surface and crushed it under her weight.

The panel was opened — torn open, one might say — and pure air came flooding into every part of the Nautilus.

From Cape Horn to the Amazon

How I came to be up on the platform, I couldn't say. Perhaps the Canadian had carried me there. But I was breathing — I was drinking in the living air of the sea. Beside me my two companions were drunk on those cool, fresh molecules. Poor wretches too long starved of food can't fling themselves recklessly on the first meal set before them; but we had no need to hold back. We could fill our lungs to bursting with the atoms of that atmosphere, and it was the breeze, the breeze itself, that poured this voluptuous intoxication into us.

"Ah," Conseil kept saying, "oxygen — how good it is! Master needn't be afraid to breathe. There's plenty for everyone."

As for Ned Land, he said nothing, but he opened jaws wide enough to frighten a shark. And what mighty inhalations! The Canadian "drew" like a stove in full blaze.

Our strength came back to us quickly, and when I looked around I saw that we were alone on the platform. Not a single crewman. Not even Captain Nemo. The strange sailors of the Nautilus made do with the air circulating inside; none had come up to revel in the open atmosphere.

The first words I spoke were words of thanks and gratitude to my two companions. Ned and Conseil had stretched out my life through the last hours of that long agony. No amount of gratitude could ever repay such devotion.

"Come now, Professor," Ned Land answered me, "it's not worth talking about! What was there to it, on our part? Nothing at all. It was purely a matter of arithmetic. Your life was worth more than ours. So it had to be preserved."

"No, Ned," I replied, "it wasn't worth more. No one stands above a generous, good-hearted man — and that's what you are."

"All right, all right," repeated the Canadian, embarrassed.

"And you, my brave Conseil, you suffered badly too."

"Not so very badly, if I'm to tell Master the whole truth. I was short a few mouthfuls of air, but I think I'd have gotten used to it. Besides, I kept my eyes on Master, who was fainting away, and that took away any least desire I had to breathe. It fairly cut off my — as they say — my wind..."

Confused at having stumbled into a cliché, Conseil didn't finish.

"My friends," I answered, deeply moved, "we are bound to one another forever, and you have claims on me —"

"Which I intend to abuse," the Canadian shot back.

"Eh?" said Conseil.

"Yes," Ned Land went on, "the claim of dragging you off with me the day I leave this infernal Nautilus."

"For that matter," said Conseil, "are we heading the right way?"

"Yes," I replied, "since we're heading toward the sun, and here the sun means north."

"No doubt," said Ned Land, "but the question is still whether we're making for the Pacific or the Atlantic — that is, for the busy seas or the empty ones."

To that I had no answer, and I feared Captain Nemo might rather be taking us back toward that vast ocean that washes the coasts of both Asia and America at once. He would thus complete his round-the-world voyage beneath the sea and return to those seas where the Nautilus found its most perfect independence. But if we went back to the Pacific, far from any inhabited land, what became of Ned Land's plans?

We were soon to be settled on that important point. The Nautilus was traveling fast. The polar circle was quickly crossed, and the course laid for the promontory of Cape Horn. We were abreast of the American headland on March 31, at seven in the evening.

By then all our past sufferings were forgotten. The memory of that imprisonment in the ice was fading from our minds. We thought only of the future. Captain Nemo no longer appeared, either in the salon or on the platform. The daily position, plotted on the world chart by the first officer, let me determine the exact heading of the Nautilus. And that evening it became clear, to my great satisfaction, that we were coming back north by the Atlantic route.

I told the Canadian and Conseil the result of my observations.

"Good news," the Canadian answered, "but where is the Nautilus going?"

"I couldn't say, Ned."

"Could her captain be meaning, after the South Pole, to take on the North Pole and get back to the Pacific by the famous Northwest Passage?"

"I wouldn't put it past him," said Conseil.

"Well," said the Canadian, "we'll have given him the slip before then."

"In any case," Conseil added, "he's a real master of a man, this Captain Nemo, and we'll never regret having known him."

"Especially once we've left him!" Ned Land retorted.

The next day, April 1, a few minutes before noon, when the Nautilus rose to the surface of the waves, we sighted a coast to the west. It was Tierra del Fuego — the Land of Fire — so named by the first navigators when they saw the many columns of smoke rising from the natives' huts. This Tierra del Fuego forms a vast cluster of islands stretching thirty leagues long and eighty leagues wide, between 53° and 56° of southern latitude and 67°50′ and 77°15′ of western longitude. The coast looked low to me, but far off rose high mountains. I even thought I glimpsed Mount Sarmiento, rising two thousand and seventy meters above sea level — a pyramidal block of schist with a very sharp summit which, depending on whether it is veiled or clear of mist, "foretells fair weather or foul," Ned Land told me.

"A famous barometer, my friend."

"Yes, Professor, a natural barometer that never once deceived me when I sailed the channels of the Strait of Magellan."

Just then the peak stood out sharp against the sky. It was an omen of fine weather. And fine weather it was.

Back beneath the water, the Nautilus drew close to the coast and ran along it only a few miles off. Through the salon windows I saw long lianas and giant kelp — those pear-bearing wracks of which the free polar sea had held a few specimens. With their glossy, slimy filaments, they measured up to three hundred meters in length; genuine cables, thicker than a thumb and very tough, they often serve as mooring lines for ships. Another weed, known as kelp velp, with leaves four feet long, matted into the coral concretions, carpeted the seabed. It served as nest and food to myriads of crustaceans and mollusks — crabs, cuttlefish. There the seals and otters treated themselves to splendid meals, mixing fish flesh with the vegetables of the sea, after the English fashion.

Over these rich, luxuriant depths the Nautilus passed at extreme speed. Toward evening it approached the Falkland archipelago, whose harsh peaks I was able to make out the next day. The sea there was of only moderate depth. I concluded, and not without reason, that these two islands, ringed by a great many islets, had once formed part of the Magellanic lands. The Falklands were probably discovered by the famous John Davis, who gave them the name Davis Southern Islands. Later, Richard Hawkins called them the Maiden Islands, the Isles of the Virgin. They were afterward named the Malouines, at the start of the eighteenth century, by fishermen of Saint-Malo, and finally the Falklands by the English, to whom they belong today.

In these waters our nets brought up fine specimens of seaweed, and in particular a certain kelp whose roots were laden with mussels — the best in the world. Geese and ducks dropped by the dozen onto the platform and soon took their places in the ship's larders. As for fish, I especially noted bony species belonging to the goby genus, and above all some gudgeons two decimeters long, all speckled with whitish and yellow spots.

I also admired numerous jellyfish, and the loveliest of their kind — the chrysaoras peculiar to the seas of the Falklands.
