At times they formed a very smooth hemispherical umbrella streaked with reddish-brown lines and fringed with twelve regular festoons; at other times they were an upturned basket from which broad leaves and long red streamers gracefully spilled. They swam by waving their four leaf-shaped arms and let their opulent mane of tentacles drift and dangle behind them. I would have liked to keep a few samples of these delicate zoophytes; but they are only clouds, shadows, mere appearances, that melt and evaporate out of their native element.

When the last heights of the Falklands had dropped below the horizon, the Nautilus submerged to between twenty and twenty-five meters and followed the American coast. Captain Nemo did not show himself.

Until April 3 we did not leave the waters of Patagonia, now beneath the ocean, now on its surface. The Nautilus passed the wide estuary formed by the mouth of the Plata and, on April 4, found itself abreast of Uruguay, though fifty miles out to sea. Its heading held to the north, following the long windings of South America. By then we had covered sixteen thousand leagues since we first came aboard in the seas of Japan.

Around eleven in the morning, the Tropic of Capricorn was crossed on the thirty-seventh meridian, and we passed well off Cape Frio. Captain Nemo, much to Ned Land's displeasure, had no liking for the neighborhood of these inhabited coasts of Brazil, for he ran at a dizzying speed. Not a single fish, not a single bird, however swift, could keep up with us, and the natural curiosities of these seas escaped all observation.

This pace held up for several days, and on the evening of April 9 we sighted the easternmost point of South America, which forms Cape São Roque. But then the Nautilus veered off again, and went hunting at greater depths for an undersea valley that gouges its way between this cape and Sierra Leone on the African coast. This valley forks at the latitude of the Antilles and ends in the north in an enormous depression nine thousand meters deep. Here the geological cross-section of the ocean shows, as far as the Lesser Antilles, a sheer cliff six kilometers high, cut straight down, and, at the latitude of the Cape Verde Islands, another wall no less formidable — the two of them thus enclosing the whole submerged continent of Atlantis. The floor of this immense valley is broken by a few mountains that lend a picturesque aspect to these submarine depths. I speak of it mainly on the strength of the manuscript charts in the Nautilus's library — charts plainly drawn by Captain Nemo's own hand and mapped from his personal observations.

For two days these deserted and deep waters were visited by means of the inclined planes. The Nautilus made long diagonal tacks that carried it to every level. But on April 11 it rose abruptly, and land reappeared to us at the mouth of the Amazon River — a vast estuary whose outflow is so tremendous that it freshens the sea over a stretch of several leagues.

The equator was crossed. Twenty miles to the west lay the Guianas, French territory where we could have found an easy refuge. But the wind was blowing a stiff gale, and the furious waves would never have let a mere dinghy face them. Ned Land no doubt understood this, for he said nothing to me about it. On my side, I made no allusion to his plans of escape, for I had no wish to push him into some attempt that would inevitably have miscarried.

I easily made up for this delay with some interesting studies. During those two days, the 11th and 12th of April, the Nautilus did not leave the surface of the sea, and its trawl brought up a whole miraculous catch of zoophytes, fish, and reptiles.

A few zoophytes had been dredged up by the trawl's dragging chain. They were, for the most part, beautiful phyctallines belonging to the family of the actinidians, and among other species the Phyctalis protexta, native to this part of the ocean — a small cylindrical trunk trimmed with vertical lines and dotted with red points, crowned by a marvelous bloom of tentacles. As for the mollusks, they consisted of creatures I had already observed: turret shells; porphyry olive shells with regularly crisscrossed lines whose russet markings stood out vividly against a flesh-colored ground; fanciful pteroceras shells like petrified scorpions; translucent hyala; argonauts; cuttlefish excellent to eat; and certain species of squid that the naturalists of antiquity classed among the flying fish, and which are used chiefly as bait for cod fishing.

Of the fish of these waters that I had not yet had occasion to study, I noted several species. Among the cartilaginous ones: pricka lampreys, a kind of eel fifteen inches long, with a greenish head, violet fins, a bluish-gray back, and a silvery-brown belly strewn with bright spots, the iris of the eye ringed with gold — curious animals that the current of the Amazon must have swept out to sea, for they inhabit fresh water; tuberculate rays with a pointed snout, a long slender tail, and armed with a long serrated spine; small sharks a meter long, gray and whitish of skin, whose teeth, set in several rows, curve backward, and which are commonly known as pantouffliers, or "slipper sharks"; bat-fish, a kind of reddish isosceles triangle half a meter across, whose pectoral fins are attached by fleshy extensions that give them the look of bats, but whose horny appendage near the nostrils has earned them the nickname sea unicorns; and finally a few species of triggerfish — the curassavian, whose stippled flanks gleam with a brilliant golden color, and the pale-violet leatherjacket, iridescent in shifting hues like a pigeon's throat.

I'll end this somewhat dry but very exact nomenclature with the series of bony fish I observed: passans, of the aptenote genus, whose snout is very blunt and snow-white, the body painted a fine black, and which are furnished with a very long, very slender fleshy thong; spiny odontognaths, long sardines three decimeters in length, resplendent with a vivid silvery gleam; guaru mackerel, provided with two anal fins; black centronotes with dark tints, caught with lighted torches — long fish two meters in length, with fatty, white, firm flesh that, eaten fresh, tastes like eel, and dried, like smoked salmon; half-red wrasses, clad in scales only at the base of the dorsal and anal fins; chrysopterans, on which gold and silver mingle their sheen with that of the ruby and the topaz; gilthead breams, whose flesh is extremely delicate and whose phosphorescent properties betray them amid the waters; pob breams, with a fine tongue and orange tints; coro croakers with golden tail fins; blackish surgeonfish; four-eyed fish of Surinam; and so on.

This "et cetera" won't keep me from mentioning one more fish that Conseil will remember for a long time, and with good reason.

One of our nets had brought up a sort of very flattened ray which, had its tail been cut off, would have formed a perfect disk, and which weighed some twenty kilograms. It was white below, reddish above, with big round spots of dark blue ringed with black, very smooth of skin, and ending in a two-lobed fin. Laid out on the platform, it thrashed about, tried to flip itself over with convulsive movements, and struggled so hard that a final jerk was about to hurl it back into the sea. But Conseil, who prized his fish, threw himself upon it, and before I could stop him, seized it with both hands.

In an instant there he was, knocked flat on his back, legs in the air, one half of his body paralyzed, and crying out:

"Oh, master! My master! Come help me!"

It was the first time the poor fellow had ever spoken to me without using the third person.

The Canadian and I picked him up, rubbed him down with all our might, and when he came to his senses, that eternal classifier murmured in a broken voice:

"Class of cartilaginous fish, order of chondropterygians with fixed gills, suborder of selacians, family of rays, genus of torpedoes!"

"Yes, my friend," I answered, "it's a torpedo ray that put you in this deplorable state."

"Ah, Master can take my word for it," Conseil shot back, "but I'll have my revenge on this animal."

"And how?"

"By eating it."

Which he did that very evening — but purely out of reprisal, for frankly it was tough.

The luckless Conseil had tangled with a torpedo ray of the most dangerous species, the cumana. This bizarre animal, in a conducting medium such as water, strikes down fish several meters away, so great is the power of its electric organ, whose two main surfaces measure no less than twenty-seven square feet.

The next day, April 12, during the daytime, the Nautilus approached the Dutch coast, toward the mouth of the Maroni. There, several groups of manatees lived in family bands. These were the manatis which, like the dugong and the Steller's sea cow, belong to the order of sirenians. These beautiful animals, peaceable and harmless, six or seven meters long, must have weighed at least four thousand kilograms. I told Ned Land and Conseil that provident nature had assigned these mammals an important role. It is they, in fact, who, like the seals, are meant to graze the submarine meadows and so destroy the masses of weed that clog the mouths of tropical rivers.

"And do you know," I added, "what has come about since men have almost wholly wiped out these useful races? The rotting weeds have poisoned the air, and the poisoned air is the yellow fever that ravages these admirable regions. The venomous vegetation has multiplied beneath these torrid seas, and the evil has spread irresistibly from the mouth of the Río de la Plata all the way to the Floridas!"

And if we're to believe Toussenel, this scourge is nothing yet beside the one that will strike our descendants when the seas are emptied of whales and seals. Then, choked with octopuses, jellyfish, and squid, they will become vast breeding grounds of infection, since their waters will no longer possess "those vast stomachs that God had charged with skimming the surface of the seas."

Meanwhile, without scorning these theories, the crew of the Nautilus took possession of half a dozen manatis. The point, in fact, was to stock the galley with an excellent meat, superior to beef and veal. This hunt was not an interesting one. The manatis let themselves be struck without defending themselves. Several thousand kilos of meat, destined to be dried, were stored aboard.

That same day, a catch made in a singular fashion further swelled the Nautilus's reserves, so teeming with game were these seas. The trawl had brought up in its meshes a number of fish whose heads ended in an oval plate with fleshy borders. These were remoras, of the third family of the subbrachian malacopterygians. Their flattened disk is made up of movable transverse cartilaginous plates, between which the animal can create a vacuum, enabling it to cling to objects the way a suction cup does.

The remora I had observed in the Mediterranean belongs to this species. But the one in question here was the bony-scaled remora, peculiar to this sea. Our sailors, as they took them, dropped them into tubs full of water.

The fishing done, the Nautilus drew nearer to the coast. In this spot a number of sea turtles were sleeping at the surface of the waves. It would have been difficult to seize these precious reptiles, for the slightest noise wakes them, and their sturdy shell is harpoon-proof. But the remora was to carry out this capture with extraordinary sureness and precision. This animal is, in fact, a living fishhook, one that would make the fortune and delight of the naive angler with his line.

The men of the Nautilus fastened to the tail of these fish a ring wide enough not to hamper their movements, and to that ring a long cord made fast at the other end to the ship.

The remoras, tossed into the sea, at once began their work and went to fix themselves to the breastplates of the turtles. Their grip was so tenacious that they would have torn apart sooner than let go. They were hauled aboard, and with them the turtles to which they clung.

In this way we took several loggerheads, a meter across, weighing two hundred kilos. Their shells, covered with large, thin, transparent, brown horny plates flecked with white and yellow, made them very valuable. Besides, they were excellent from the standpoint of eating, as were the green turtles, which are of an exquisite flavor.

This catch ended our stay in the waters off the Amazon, and, when night came, the Nautilus regained the open sea.

The Giant Squid

For several days the Nautilus kept steadily away from the American coast. Clearly it had no wish to venture into the waters of the Gulf of Mexico or the Caribbean Sea. There would have been no shortage of water beneath its keel — the average depth of those seas is eighteen hundred meters — but no doubt those waters, strewn with islands and furrowed by steamers, did not suit Captain Nemo.

On April 16 we raised Martinique and Guadeloupe, at a distance of about thirty miles. For a moment I caught sight of their high peaks.

The Canadian, who had counted on carrying out his plans somewhere in the Gulf — either by making land or by hailing one of the many boats that ply their coasting trade from island to island — was thoroughly put out. Escape would have been quite feasible if Ned Land could have gotten hold of the dinghy without the captain's knowledge. But out on the open ocean, it was no longer to be thought of.

The Canadian, Conseil, and I had a fairly long conversation on the subject. For six months now we had been prisoners aboard the Nautilus. We had covered seventeen thousand leagues, and, as Ned Land pointed out, there was no reason why it should ever end. So he made me a proposal I hadn't expected: to put the question to Captain Nemo point-blank. Did the captain mean to keep us aboard his vessel indefinitely?

Such a move went against my grain. As I saw it, it could lead nowhere. There was nothing to hope for from the commander of the Nautilus — everything, on the contrary, depended on ourselves alone. Besides, for some time now the man had been growing darker, more withdrawn, less sociable. He seemed to be avoiding me. I met him only at rare intervals. Once, he had taken pleasure in explaining the wonders of the deep to me; now he left me to my studies and no longer came to the salon.

What change had come over him? For what reason? I had nothing to reproach myself with. Perhaps our presence aboard weighed on him? Even so, I could hardly hope that he was the kind of man to give us back our freedom.

So I asked Ned to let me think the matter over before we acted. If the move produced no result, it might only revive Nemo's suspicions, make our situation harder, and jeopardize the Canadian's plans. I might add that there was no way I could plead ill health as an argument. Apart from the harsh ordeal of the ice barrier at the South Pole, none of us had ever been better — not Ned, not Conseil, not I. This wholesome food, this healthy air, this regularity of life, this uniformity of temperature, gave sickness no opening; and for a man to whom memories of the land brought no regret, for a Captain Nemo, who is at home wherever he goes, who goes where he pleases, who marches toward his goal by ways mysterious to others though not to himself — such an existence I could understand. But we had not broken with humanity. For my own part, I had no wish to bury my strange and wholly new studies along with myself. I had now earned the right to write the true book of the sea, and that book I wanted to see the light of day sooner rather than later.

There too, in those waters of the Antilles, ten meters below the surface, through the open panels, what a wealth of interesting specimens I had to enter in my daily notes! Among the zoophytes there were siphonophores known as the pelagic Physalia — great oblong bladders with a pearly sheen, spreading their membranes to the wind and letting their blue tentacles trail like threads of silk: charming jellyfish to the eye, true nettles to the touch, distilling a corrosive fluid. Among the articulates there were annelids a meter and a half long, armed with a pink proboscis and equipped with seventeen hundred organs of locomotion; they wound their way beneath the waters, casting off, as they passed, every color of the solar spectrum. In the branch of fishes there were molubar rays — enormous cartilaginous creatures ten feet long and weighing six hundred pounds, with a triangular pectoral fin, a slightly humped back, and eyes set at the outer edges of the front of the head; they floated like the wreckage of a ship and now and then clapped themselves against our window like an opaque shutter. There were American triggerfish, for which nature has ground up nothing but black and white; feathery gobies, long and fleshy, with yellow fins and a jutting jaw; mackerel of sixteen decimeters, with short sharp teeth and small scales, belonging to the albacore species. Then, in swarms, came surmullets girdled with stripes of gold from head to tail, waving their resplendent fins — true masterpieces of the jeweler's art, once dedicated to Diana, especially prized by wealthy Romans, and of which the old proverb ran: "Let him who catches them not eat them!" Finally, golden angelfish, adorned with emerald bands, dressed in velvet and silk, passed before our eyes like lords stepped out of a Veronese painting; spurred sea bream slipped away on the swift stroke of their thoracic fins; fifteen-inch clupanodons wrapped themselves in their phosphorescent glow; gray mullet beat the sea with their thick fleshy tails; red whitefish seemed to mow the waves with their knife-edged pectorals; and silvery moonfish, worthy of their name, rose over the watery horizon like so many pale-shining moons.

How many more marvelous new specimens I might have observed, had the Nautilus not gradually sunk toward the lower strata! Its inclined planes carried it down to depths of two thousand and three thousand five hundred meters. There animal life was represented only by sea lilies, starfish, lovely Medusa's-head feather stars whose straight stalks bore a little calyx, top shells, bloody-toothed shells, and keyhole limpets — littoral mollusks of the larger species.

On April 20 we had risen again to a mean depth of fifteen hundred meters. The nearest land was then the archipelago of the Lucayan Islands, scattered like a heap of paving stones over the surface of the waters. There rose high submarine cliffs, sheer walls built of rough-hewn blocks laid in broad courses, between which opened black holes that our electric beams could not light to the bottom.

These rocks were carpeted with tall grasses, giant kelp, gigantic wrack — a veritable espalier of water plants fit for a world of Titans.

From these colossal plants, Conseil, Ned, and I naturally fell to speaking of the sea's gigantic animals. The one is plainly meant to feed the other. Yet through the windows of the nearly motionless Nautilus, I could still make out on those long filaments nothing but the chief articulates of the brachyuran order — long-legged spider crabs, violet crabs, and clios peculiar to the Caribbean waters.

It was about eleven o'clock when Ned Land drew my attention to a tremendous swarming going on through the great weeds.

"Well," I said, "these are real devilfish caverns, and I wouldn't be surprised to see a few of those monsters here."

"What!" said Conseil. "Squid — plain ordinary squid — of the class of cephalopods?"

"No," I said, "devilfish of great size. But friend Land is surely mistaken, for I don't see anything."

"A pity," Conseil replied. "I'd like to meet one of those devilfish face to face — the ones I've heard so much about, that can drag ships down into the depths. Those beasts are called krak—"

"A crock is more like it," the Canadian cut in dryly.

"—Krakens," Conseil finished, not troubling himself over his companion's little joke.

"You'll never make me believe," said Ned Land, "that such animals exist."

"Why not?" answered Conseil. "We believed in Master's narwhal, after all."

"We were wrong, Conseil."

"No doubt! But others still believe in it, I'm sure."

"Very likely, Conseil. But for my part, I'm quite determined not to admit the existence of such monsters until I've dissected one with my own hands."

"So," Conseil asked me, "Master doesn't believe in giant devilfish?"

"Who the devil ever did believe in them?" cried the Canadian.

"A great many people, friend Ned."

"Not fishermen. Scholars, maybe!"

"Beg your pardon, Ned. Fishermen and scholars both!"

"But I who am speaking to you now," said Conseil, with the most serious air in the world, "remember perfectly well seeing a large boat dragged under the waves by the arms of a cephalopod."

"You saw that?" the Canadian demanded.

"Yes, Ned."

"With your own eyes?"

"With my own eyes."

"Where, if you please?"

"At Saint-Malo," Conseil answered, unruffled.

"In the harbor?" said Ned Land, mocking.

"No, in a church," Conseil replied.

"In a church!" cried the Canadian.

"Yes, friend Ned. It was a painting — a painting of the devilfish in question!"

"Good!" said Ned Land, bursting out laughing. "Monsieur Conseil, pulling my leg!"

"As a matter of fact, he's right," I said. "I've heard of that painting; but the subject it shows is drawn from a legend, and you know what to make of legends where natural history is concerned! Besides, when it comes to monsters, the imagination asks nothing better than to run wild.

"Not only has it been claimed that these devilfish could drag ships under, but a certain Olaus Magnus tells of a cephalopod a mile long that looked more like an island than an animal. There's also the tale of the bishop of Nidros, who one day set up an altar on an immense rock. When his Mass was over, the rock got under way and returned to the sea. The rock was a devilfish."

"And that's all?" asked the Canadian.

"No," I replied. "Another bishop, Pontoppidan of Bergen, likewise speaks of a devilfish on which a whole regiment of cavalry could maneuver!"

"They knew how to lay it on, those bishops of the old days!" said Ned Land.

"And finally, the naturalists of antiquity cite monsters whose maws were like a gulf, and which were too big to pass through the Strait of Gibraltar."

"Now that's more like it!" said the Canadian.

"But in all these stories," Conseil asked, "what is there that's true?"

"Nothing, my friends — nothing, at least, beyond the bounds of the plausible that mounts into fable or legend. Still, the imagination of storytellers needs, if not a cause, at least a pretext. There's no denying that devilfish and squid of very large species exist, though smaller nonetheless than the cetaceans. Aristotle recorded the dimensions of a squid five cubits long — that is, three meters ten. Our fishermen frequently see specimens more than a meter eighty in length. The museums of Trieste and Montpellier preserve skeletons of devilfish that measure two meters. Moreover, by the naturalists' reckoning, one of these animals only six feet long would have tentacles twenty-seven feet long. Which is quite enough to make a formidable monster."

"Are any caught nowadays?" asked the Canadian.

"If fishermen don't catch them, sailors at least see them. A friend of mine, Captain Paul Bos of Le Havre, has often assured me that he met one of these monsters, of colossal size, in the seas of India. But the most astonishing fact of all, the one that no longer allows the existence of these gigantic animals to be denied, took place only a few years ago, in 1861."

"What fact is that?" asked Ned Land.

"Here it is. In 1861, to the northeast of Tenerife — at just about the latitude where we are at this moment — the crew of the dispatch boat Alecton sighted a monstrous squid swimming in its waters. Commander Bouguer drew close to the animal and attacked it with harpoons and gunfire, without much success, for balls and harpoons passed through that soft flesh as through a jelly with no substance to it. After several fruitless attempts, the crew managed to slip a running knot around the mollusk's body. The knot slid down as far as the tail fins and stopped there. They then tried to haul the monster aboard, but its weight was so considerable that it tore away from its own tail under the pull of the rope, and, stripped of that ornament, it vanished beneath the waters."

"There, at last, is a fact," said Ned Land.

"An indisputable fact, my good Ned. And so it was proposed to name this devilfish 'Bouguer's squid.'"

"And how long was it?" the Canadian asked.

"Wasn't it about six meters?" said Conseil, who, posted at the window, was once more examining the crannies of the cliff.

"Precisely," I answered.

"Wasn't its head," Conseil went on, "crowned with eight tentacles that thrashed on the water like a nest of writhing serpents?"

"Precisely."

"Weren't its eyes, set flush with the head, of considerable size?"

"Yes, Conseil."

"And its mouth — wasn't it a real parrot's beak, but a formidable one?"

"Just so, Conseil."

"Well then! No offense to Master," Conseil replied calmly, "but if this isn't Bouguer's squid, here at least is one of its brothers."

I looked at Conseil. Ned Land rushed to the window.

"The frightful beast!" he cried.

I looked in my turn, and I couldn't hold back a shudder of revulsion. Before my eyes writhed a horrible monster, worthy of a place in the legends of the monster-mongers.

It was a squid of colossal dimensions, eight meters long. It was traveling backward with tremendous velocity, straight toward the Nautilus. It stared with its huge fixed eyes of a glaucous green. Its eight arms — or rather its eight feet, rooted in its head, which is what has earned these animals the name cephalopod, "head-foot" — were twice the length of its body and writhed like the hair of the Furies. One could see distinctly the two hundred and fifty suckers arranged along the inner face of the tentacles in the form of half-spherical capsules. From time to time these suckers fastened onto the salon window, drawing a vacuum against the glass. The monster's mouth — a horny beak shaped like a parrot's — opened and closed vertically. Its tongue, a horny substance itself armed with several rows of sharp teeth, came quivering out of that veritable pair of shears. What a whim of nature! A bird's beak on a mollusk! Its body, spindle-shaped and swollen in the middle, formed a fleshy mass that must have weighed twenty to twenty-five thousand kilograms. Its inconstant color, changing with extreme speed according to the creature's irritation, passed in turn from a livid gray to a reddish brown.

What was irritating this mollusk? No doubt the presence of the Nautilus, more formidable than itself, and on which its sucking arms and its mandibles could get no purchase. And yet — what monsters these devilfish are, what vitality the Creator has bestowed on them, what vigor in their movements, given that they possess three hearts!

Chance had brought us face to face with this squid, and I did not mean to let slip the chance to study this specimen of the cephalopods with care. I mastered the horror its appearance stirred in me and, taking up a pencil, I began to draw it.

"Perhaps it's the very same one the Alecton met," said Conseil.

"No," answered the Canadian, "since this one is whole and the other lost its tail!"

"That's no reason," I replied. "The arms and tail of these animals grow back by regeneration, and in seven years the tail of Bouguer's squid has surely had time to sprout again."

"Besides," Ned retorted, "if it isn't this one, maybe it's one of those!"

And in fact, other devilfish were appearing at the starboard window. I counted seven. They formed an escort around the Nautilus, and I heard the grinding of their beaks on the iron hull. We were served to our heart's content.

I went on with my work. These monsters held themselves in our waters with such precision that they seemed motionless, and I could have traced them foreshortened on the glass. In any case, we were moving at a moderate pace.

Suddenly the Nautilus stopped. A shock ran shuddering through its whole frame.

"Have we struck something?" I asked.

"In any case," the Canadian answered, "we'd already be free of it, since we're afloat."

The Nautilus was afloat, no doubt, but it was no longer moving. The blades of its propeller weren't beating the waves. A minute passed. Captain Nemo, followed by his first officer, came into the salon.

I hadn't seen him for some time. He seemed to me somber. Without speaking to us, without perhaps even seeing us, he went to the panel, looked at the devilfish, and said a few words to his officer.

The officer went out. Soon the panels closed again. The ceiling lit up.

I went over to the captain.

"A curious collection of devilfish," I said to him, in the offhand tone a hobbyist might take before the glass of an aquarium.

"Indeed, Professor," he answered me, "and we're going to fight them hand to hand."

I stared at the captain. I thought I hadn't heard him right.

"Hand to hand?" I repeated.

"Yes, Professor. The propeller has stopped. I believe the horny mandibles of one of these squid have gotten caught in the blades. That's what's keeping us from moving."

"And what do you mean to do?"

"Rise to the surface and slaughter every last one of this vermin."

"A difficult undertaking."

"So it is. The electric bullets are powerless against that soft flesh, where they don't meet enough resistance to burst. But we'll attack them with axes."

"And with the harpoon, Captain," said the Canadian, "if you won't refuse my help."

"I accept it, Master Land."

"We'll go with you," I said, and, following Captain Nemo, we made our way toward the central staircase.

There a dozen men, armed with boarding axes, stood ready for the attack. Conseil and I took up two axes. Ned Land seized a harpoon.

By now the Nautilus had returned to the surface. One of the sailors, posted on the topmost rungs, was unscrewing the bolts of the panel. But hardly were the nuts loosened when the panel flew up with tremendous violence, plainly wrenched open by the sucker of a devilfish's arm.

At once one of those long arms slid like a serpent through the opening, and twenty more thrashed above it. With one stroke of his axe, Captain Nemo cut off the formidable tentacle, which slid writhing down the rungs.

Just as we were pressing against one another to reach the platform, two other arms, lashing the air, came down on the sailor standing in front of Captain Nemo and lifted him away with irresistible force.

Captain Nemo let out a cry and hurled himself outside. We rushed after him.

What a scene! The wretched man, seized by the tentacle and glued to its suckers, was being swung through the air at the whim of that enormous trunk. He gasped, he choked, he screamed: "Help! Help me!" Those words, spoken in French, filled me with profound shock! So I had a countryman aboard — several, perhaps! That heart-rending cry I will hear for the rest of my life!

The unfortunate man was lost. Who could tear him from that powerful grip? Yet Captain Nemo had flung himself on the devilfish and, with one blow of his axe, had struck off another of its arms. His first officer struggled furiously against other monsters crawling up the flanks of the Nautilus. The crew fought with their axes. The Canadian, Conseil, and I drove our weapons into those fleshy masses. A violent smell of musk filled the air. It was horrible.

For an instant I thought the poor man, wound in the devilfish's coils, might be torn free of its powerful suction. Seven of the eight arms had been cut away. A single one, brandishing its victim like a feather, writhed in the air. But just as Captain Nemo and his officer flung themselves upon it, the animal shot out a column of blackish liquid, secreted from a pouch in its abdomen. We were blinded by it. When the cloud had cleared, the squid had vanished — and with it my luckless countryman!

What a rage then drove us against those monsters! We were beside ourselves. Ten or twelve devilfish had overrun the platform and the flanks of the Nautilus. We rolled pell-mell among those writhing serpent-stumps that leaped and jerked across the platform in floods of blood and black ink. It seemed as though those slimy tentacles grew back like the heads of the Hydra. At every stroke Ned Land's harpoon plunged into the glaucous eyes of the squid and burst them. But my daring companion was suddenly bowled over by the tentacles of a monster he hadn't been able to dodge.

Ah! how my heart did not break with terror and horror, I cannot say! The formidable beak of the squid had gaped open over Ned Land. The poor man was about to be cut in two. I threw myself to his rescue. But Captain Nemo had beaten me to it. His axe disappeared between the two enormous mandibles, and, miraculously saved, the Canadian rose to his feet and drove his harpoon clear through to the triple heart of the devilfish.

"I owed myself this revenge!" said Captain Nemo to the Canadian.

Ned bowed without answering him.

The battle had lasted a quarter of an hour. The monsters, beaten, mutilated, struck to death, at last gave way and vanished beneath the waves.

Captain Nemo, red with blood, motionless beside the beacon, gazed at the sea that had swallowed one of his companions, and great tears ran from his eyes.

The Gulf Stream

None of us will ever be able to forget that terrible scene of April 20. I wrote it down under the pressure of a violent emotion. Since then I have gone back over the account. I read it aloud to Conseil and the Canadian. They found it accurate as to the facts, but inadequate as to the effect. To paint such scenes as those, one would need the pen of the most illustrious of our poets, the author of The Toilers of the Sea.

I have said that Captain Nemo wept as he gazed out over the waves. His grief was immense. This was the second companion he had lost since our arrival aboard. And what a death! That friend, crushed, smothered, broken in the fearsome arm of a devilfish, ground to pulp between its iron mandibles, would never rest with his shipmates in the peaceful waters of the coral cemetery!

For my own part, in the midst of that struggle, it was the cry of despair torn from the poor wretch that had ripped my heart open. That poor Frenchman, forgetting the artificial language of the crew, had gone back to speaking the tongue of his country and his mother, to hurl out one last appeal! So among this crew of the Nautilus, bound body and soul to Captain Nemo, fleeing like him from all contact with other men, I had a countryman! Was he the only one to represent France in this mysterious brotherhood, evidently made up of men of many different nationalities? It was one more of those insoluble problems that kept rising, endlessly, before my mind.

Captain Nemo went back into his cabin, and I did not see him again for some time. But how sad, how despairing, how irresolute he must have been, if I could judge by the ship itself, of which he was the soul and which received every one of his moods! The Nautilus no longer held any set course. It came and went, it drifted like a corpse at the mercy of the swells. Its propeller had been cleared, and yet it hardly used it. The ship sailed at random. It could not tear itself away from the scene of its last battle, from that sea which had devoured one of its own!

Ten days passed like this. It was only on May 1 that the Nautilus resolutely took up its course north again, after raising the Lucayas at the mouth of the Bahama Channel. We were then following the current of the sea's greatest river, a river with its own banks, its own fish, and its own temperature. I mean the Gulf Stream.

It is a river, in truth, that flows freely through the middle of the Atlantic, its waters never mingling with the waters of the ocean around it. It is a salt river, saltier than the surrounding sea. Its average depth is three thousand feet, its average width sixty miles. In certain places its current runs at a speed of four kilometers an hour. The unvarying volume of its waters is greater than that of all the rivers of the globe combined.

The true source of the Gulf Stream, identified by Commander Maury — its starting point, if you like — lies in the Bay of Biscay. There its waters, still weak in temperature and color, begin to form. It runs south, skirts equatorial Africa, warms its waves in the rays of the torrid zone, crosses the Atlantic, reaches Cape São Roque on the Brazilian coast, and forks into two branches, one of which goes off to saturate itself still further with the warm molecules of the Caribbean Sea. Then the Gulf Stream, charged with restoring the balance between the temperatures and blending the waters of the tropics with the waters of the north, begins its work as a great regulator. Heated white-hot in the Gulf of Mexico, it climbs north along the American coasts, pushes up as far as Newfoundland, is deflected under the thrust of the cold current from Davis Strait, resumes its ocean course by following the rhumb line along one of the globe's great circles, and divides into two arms around the forty-third degree — one of which, helped along by the northeast trade wind, returns to the Bay of Biscay and the Azores, while the other, after warming the shores of Ireland and Norway, runs on beyond Spitsbergen, where its temperature drops to four degrees, to form the open sea of the pole.

It was on this river of the ocean that the Nautilus was now sailing. As it comes out of the Bahama Channel, fourteen leagues wide and three hundred and fifty meters deep, the Gulf Stream moves at a rate of eight kilometers an hour. This speed decreases steadily as the current works its way north, and one can only hope that this regularity holds, for if — as some believe they have observed — its speed and its direction were ever to change, the climates of Europe would be thrown into disturbances whose consequences no one could begin to calculate.

Toward noon I was out on the platform with Conseil. I was explaining the peculiar features of the Gulf Stream to him. When my explanation was done, I invited him to plunge his hands into the current.

Conseil obeyed, and was very much surprised to feel no sensation of either heat or cold.

"That comes," I told him, "from the fact that the temperature of the Gulf Stream's waters, as they leave the Gulf of Mexico, differs very little from the temperature of the blood. This Gulf Stream is a vast heating system, one that lets the coasts of Europe deck themselves in an eternal green. And if Maury is to be believed, the heat of this current, if it could be harnessed entirely, would supply enough calories to keep a river of molten iron as large as the Amazon or the Missouri in a state of fusion."

At that moment the speed of the Gulf Stream was two meters twenty-five per second. Its current is so distinct from the surrounding sea that its compressed waters rise up above the level of the ocean, and a difference in level forms between them and the cold waters around them. Dark, moreover, and very rich in mineral salts, they stand out by their pure indigo against the green waves surrounding them. So sharp, in fact, is the line dividing them that the Nautilus, off the Carolinas, cut through the waves of the Gulf Stream with its ram while its propeller was still churning those of the open ocean.

This current swept along with it a whole world of living creatures. The argonauts, so common in the Mediterranean, traveled here in numerous shoals. Among the cartilaginous fish, the most remarkable were rays whose very slender tails made up almost a third of the body, and which formed vast diamonds twenty-five feet long; then small sharks a meter in length, with big heads, short rounded snouts, and pointed teeth arranged in several rows, their bodies seemingly covered with scales.

Among the bony fish I noted grey wrasses peculiar to these seas; synagris sea breams whose irises blazed like fire; croakers a meter long, with wide mouths bristling with small teeth, that gave off a faint cry; black centronotes, which I have mentioned before; blue dorados picked out in gold and silver; parrotfish, true rainbows of the ocean, that can rival in color the loveliest birds of the tropics; triangle-headed blennies; bluish rhombus flatfish without scales; batrachoids marked with a yellow crosswise band that forms a Greek letter T; swarms of little pointed gobies stippled with brown spots; dipterodons with silvery heads and yellow tails; various samples of salmon; slender, softly gleaming mullet that Lacépède dedicated to the gentle companion of his life; and finally a beautiful fish, the American cavalier, which — decorated with every order and bedecked with every ribbon — frequents the shores of that great nation where ribbons and decorations are held in such modest esteem.

I will add that, during the night, the phosphorescent waters of the Gulf Stream rivaled the electric glare of our beacon, especially in the stormy weather that threatened us so often.

On May 8 we were still abreast of Cape Hatteras, off the coast of North Carolina. The width of the Gulf Stream is there seventy-five miles, and its depth two hundred and ten meters. The Nautilus went on wandering at random. All watchfulness seemed to have been banished from on board. I will admit that, under these conditions, an escape might have succeeded. Indeed, the inhabited shores offered easy refuge everywhere. The sea was ceaselessly furrowed by the many steamers that run the service between New York or Boston and the Gulf of Mexico, and crossed night and day by those little schooners engaged in coastal trade along the various points of the American shore. There was hope of being picked up. It was, then, a favorable opportunity, despite the thirty miles that separated the Nautilus from the shores of the Union.

But one unfortunate circumstance thwarted the Canadian's plans completely. The weather was very bad. We were approaching those waters where storms are frequent, that homeland of waterspouts and cyclones, bred precisely by the Gulf Stream's current. To brave an often raging sea in a frail little boat was to run headlong to certain destruction. Ned Land admitted as much himself. And so he chafed at the bit, gripped by a furious homesickness that flight alone could have cured.

"Professor," he said to me that day, "this has got to end. I want to know where I stand. Your Nemo is pulling away from land and heading north. But I'm telling you plainly — I've had my fill of the South Pole, and I won't follow him to the North Pole."

"What can we do, Ned, when an escape is out of the question at the moment?"

"I keep coming back to my idea. We have to talk to the captain. You said nothing when we were in the seas off your country. I want to speak, now that we're in the seas off mine. When I think that in a few days the Nautilus is going to be off Nova Scotia, and that there, near Newfoundland, a wide bay opens up — that into that bay pours the Saint Lawrence, and that the Saint Lawrence is my river, the river of Quebec, my native city — when I think of that, the fury rises up in my face, my hair stands on end. Listen, Professor, I'd sooner throw myself into the sea! I won't stay here! I'm suffocating!"

The Canadian was clearly at the end of his patience. His vigorous nature could not adjust itself to this prolonged imprisonment. His face was altering by the day. His temper was growing darker and darker. Nearly seven months had gone by without our having had any news from the land. On top of that, Captain Nemo's withdrawal into himself, his changed mood — above all since the battle with the devilfish — his silence, all of it was making me see things in a different light. I no longer felt the enthusiasm of the early days. It took a Fleming like Conseil to accept such a situation, in this element reserved for whales and the other creatures of the sea. Truly, if that good fellow had had gills instead of lungs, I believe he would have made a distinguished fish!

"Well, Professor?" Ned Land went on, seeing that I did not answer.

"Well, Ned, you want me to ask Captain Nemo what his intentions are regarding us?"

"Yes, Professor."

"Even though he has already made them known?"

"Yes. I want to be certain one last time. Speak for me alone, in my name alone, if you like."

"But I hardly ever run into him. He even avoids me."

"That's all the more reason to go and find him."

"I'll put the question to him, Ned."

"When?" the Canadian pressed.

"When I run into him."

"Professor Aronnax, would you like me to go and find him myself?"

"No, let me handle it. Tomorrow —"

"Today," said Ned Land.

"So be it. Today I'll see him," I answered the Canadian, who, had he acted for himself, would certainly have ruined everything.

I was left alone. The request decided upon, I resolved to have done with it at once. I prefer a thing done to a thing still to do.

I went back to my cabin. From there I heard someone pacing in Captain Nemo's. I must not let this chance to meet with him slip by. I knocked at his door. I got no answer. I knocked again, then turned the knob. The door opened.

I went in. The captain was there. Bent over his worktable, he had not heard me. Determined not to leave without questioning him, I came up to him. He raised his head abruptly, knit his brows, and said to me in a fairly harsh tone:

"You, here! What do you want with me?"

"To speak with you, Captain."

"But I am busy, sir, I am working. This freedom I leave you to keep to yourself — may I not have it for myself?"

The welcome was hardly encouraging. But I was resolved to hear everything out, so as to answer everything.

"Captain," I said coldly, "I have to speak to you about a matter I cannot allow myself to put off."

"About what, sir?" he replied ironically. "Have you made some discovery that escaped me? Has the sea yielded up some new secret to you?"

We were far from any common ground. But before I could reply, he showed me a manuscript lying open on his table and said to me in a graver tone:

"Here, Professor Aronnax, is a manuscript written in several languages. It contains the summary of my studies of the sea, and, God willing, it will not perish with me. This manuscript, signed with my name, completed by the story of my life, will be sealed inside a small unsinkable container. The last of us all to survive aboard the Nautilus will throw this container into the sea, and it will go wherever the waves may carry it."

This man's name! His own history, written by his own hand! So his mystery would one day be laid bare? But at that moment I saw in this confidence nothing more than an opening.

"Captain," I answered, "I can only approve of the thought that moves you. The fruit of your studies must not be lost. But the means you are using strikes me as crude. Who knows where the winds will drive this container, into what hands it will fall? Could you not find something better? Can you not, or one of your men —?"

"Never, sir," said the captain sharply, cutting me off.

"But I, and my companions, we are ready to keep this manuscript in reserve, and if you give us back our freedom —"

"Your freedom!" said Captain Nemo, rising to his feet.

"Yes, sir, and that is the subject on which I wanted to question you. For seven months we have been aboard your vessel, and I ask you today, in the name of my companions as much as my own, whether it is your intention to keep us here forever."

"Professor Aronnax," said Captain Nemo, "I will answer you today what I answered you seven months ago: whoever enters the Nautilus must never leave it."

"But this is slavery itself that you are imposing on us!"

"Give it whatever name you please."

"But everywhere a slave keeps the right to win back his freedom! Whatever the means that offer themselves, he may hold them good!"

"That right," answered Captain Nemo, "who denies it to you? Have I ever thought of binding you by an oath?"

The captain looked at me, folding his arms.

"Sir," I said to him, "to come back a second time to this subject would be to neither your taste nor mine. But since we have begun it, let us have it out. I tell you again, it is not only myself that is at issue. For me study is a solace, a powerful diversion, a spur, a passion that can make me forget everything else. Like you, I am a man able to live unknown, obscure, in the fragile hope of one day bequeathing to the future the results of my labors, by means of some hypothetical container entrusted to the chance of the waves and the winds. In a word, I can admire you, I can follow you without displeasure in a role that I understand on certain points; but there are still other aspects of your life that let me glimpse it hedged about with complications and mysteries in which, alone here, my companions and I have no part. And even when our hearts have been able to beat for you, stirred by some of your sorrows or moved by your acts of genius and courage, we have had to choke back within ourselves the least sign of that sympathy which the sight of what is beautiful and good awakens, whether it comes from a friend or from an enemy. Well, it is this feeling — that we are strangers to everything that touches you — which makes our position something unacceptable, impossible, even for me, but impossible above all for Ned Land. Every man, simply by virtue of being a man, is worth thinking about. Have you asked yourself what schemes of vengeance the love of liberty, the hatred of slavery, might give rise to in a nature like the Canadian's — what he might be thinking, attempting, trying?"

I had fallen silent. Captain Nemo rose.

"Let Ned Land think, attempt, try whatever he likes — what is it to me? It was not I who went looking for him! It is not for my pleasure that I keep him aboard! As for you, Professor Aronnax, you are one of those who can understand anything, even silence. I have nothing more to answer you. Let this first time you have taken up this subject be also the last, for a second time I could not even bring myself to listen to you."

I withdrew. From that day on, our situation was very strained. I reported my conversation to my two companions.

"Now we know," said Ned, "that there is nothing to hope for from this man. The Nautilus is drawing near Long Island. We will make our escape, whatever the weather."

But the sky was growing more and more threatening. Symptoms of a hurricane were showing themselves. The atmosphere turned whitish and milky. To the cirrus clouds in their fine sheaves there succeeded, along the horizon, layers of nimbocumulus. Other low clouds went scudding rapidly past. The sea rose and swelled in long heaving rollers. The birds vanished, all but the storm petrels, those friends of foul weather. The barometer fell noticeably and indicated an extreme tension of vapors in the air. The mixture in the storm glass broke down under the influence of the electricity saturating the atmosphere. The struggle of the elements was near.

The storm broke on the day of May 18, just as the Nautilus was afloat off Long Island, a few miles from the New York channels. I am able to describe this struggle of the elements, because instead of fleeing it in the depths of the sea, Captain Nemo, out of some inexplicable whim, chose to face it out at the surface.

The wind was blowing from the southwest, at first a strong gale — that is, at a speed of fifteen meters a second — which rose to twenty-five meters toward three in the afternoon. That is the figure of a tempest.

Captain Nemo, unshakable under the squalls, had taken up his place on the platform. He had lashed himself around the waist to withstand the monstrous waves breaking over him. I had hauled myself up as well and tied myself down, dividing my wonder between this storm and this incomparable man who stood up to it.

The raging sea was swept by great rags of cloud that trailed down into its waves. I could no longer make out any of those small intermediate ripples that form in the bottom of the great troughs. Nothing but long soot-colored undulations, so compact that their crests did not break. Their height kept rising. They lashed each other into a frenzy. The Nautilus, now heeled over on its side, now standing up like a mast, rolled and pitched frightfully.

Toward five o'clock a torrential rain came down, and it beat down neither the wind nor the sea. The hurricane was unleashed at a speed of forty-five meters a second, that is, nearly forty leagues an hour. Under such conditions it topples houses, drives roof tiles through doors, snaps iron gratings, and shifts twenty-four-pounder cannon. And yet the Nautilus, in the midst of the tumult, bore out the words of a certain learned engineer: "There is no well-built hull that cannot defy the sea!" It was no resisting rock, which those waves would have demolished; it was a spindle of steel, obedient and mobile, without rigging, without masts, that braved their fury with impunity.

Meanwhile I studied those unleashed waves attentively. They measured up to fifteen meters in height over a length of a hundred and fifty to a hundred and seventy-five meters, and their speed of propagation, half that of the wind, was fifteen meters a second. Their volume and their power grew with the depth of the water. I understood then the role of these waves, which trap the air within their flanks and force it down to the bottom of the seas, where they carry life along with the oxygen. Their extreme force of pressure — it has been calculated — can rise as high as three thousand kilograms per square foot of the surface they beat against. It was waves like these that, in the Hebrides, shifted a block weighing eighty-four thousand pounds. It was waves like these that, in the storm of December 23, 1864, after leveling part of the town of Yedo, in Japan, and traveling seven hundred kilometers an hour, went and broke that same day on the shores of America.

The intensity of the storm grew with the night. The barometer, as in 1860 at Réunion during a cyclone, fell to 710 millimeters. At the close of day I saw a great ship pass on the horizon, laboring painfully. It was lying to under low steam, trying to hold itself head-on to the sea. It must have been one of the steamers on the lines from New York to Liverpool or Le Havre. It soon disappeared into the darkness.

At ten o'clock in the evening the sky was on fire. The atmosphere was streaked with violent flashes of lightning. I could not bear their brilliance, while Captain Nemo, looking them full in the face, seemed to breathe in the very soul of the storm. A terrible noise filled the air, a complex noise made up of the howling of the crushed waves, the bellowing of the wind, the crash of the thunder. The wind leaped to every point of the horizon, and the cyclone, setting out from the east, came back to it by way of the north, the west, and the south, in the direction opposite to the whirling storms of the southern hemisphere.

Ah, this Gulf Stream! It lived up to its name of king of storms! It is the Gulf Stream that creates these formidable cyclones, by the difference in temperature between the layers of air stacked above its currents.

The rain had given way to a downpour of fire. The droplets of water changed into blazing plumes. You would have thought that Captain Nemo, wanting a death worthy of himself, was seeking to have himself struck by lightning. In one appalling pitch of the ship, the Nautilus reared its steel ram up into the air like the rod of a lightning conductor, and I saw long sparks come streaming from it.

Broken, at the end of my strength, I dragged myself flat on my belly toward the hatch. I opened it and went back down into the salon. The storm was then reaching its maximum of intensity. It was impossible to stand upright inside the Nautilus.

Captain Nemo came back down toward midnight. I heard the ballast tanks fill little by little, and the Nautilus sank gently below the surface of the waves.

Through the open windows of the salon, I saw great terrified fish that passed like phantoms through the burning waters. Some of them were struck by lightning before my very eyes!

The Nautilus went on descending. I thought it would find calm at a depth of fifteen meters. No. The upper layers were too violently agitated. We had to go seek rest as far down as fifty meters, in the very bowels of the sea.

But there, what tranquility, what silence, what a peaceful realm! Who would have said that a terrible hurricane was raging just then on the surface of that ocean?

Latitude 47°24′, Longitude 17°28′

In the wake of that storm we had been thrown back to the east. Every hope of escaping onto the approaches of New York or of the St. Lawrence had vanished. Poor Ned, in despair, shut himself away as thoroughly as Captain Nemo. Conseil and I no longer left each other's side.

I said the Nautilus had been carried off to the east. I should have said, more precisely, to the northeast. For several days it wandered now over the surface of the waves, now beneath, in the midst of those fogs so dreaded by sailors. They are caused chiefly by the melting of the ice, which keeps the air here saturated with moisture. How many ships have been lost in these waters, running in to make out the uncertain lights of the coast! How many disasters owed to these dense, blinding banks of fog! How many shocks upon these reefs whose breaking surf is drowned out by the roar of the wind! How many collisions between vessels, in spite of their running lights, in spite of the warnings of their whistles and their alarm bells!

And so the bottom of these seas had the look of a battlefield, where all these vanquished of the Ocean still lay: some old and already thickly encrusted, others fresh and reflecting the glare of our beacon on their ironwork and their copper hulls. Among them, how many ships lost with all hands, with their crews and their whole crowds of emigrants, at those dangerous points flagged in the statistics — Cape Race, St. Paul's Island, the Strait of Belle Isle, the estuary of the St. Lawrence! And in these last few years alone, how many victims added to those grim records by the lines of the Royal Mail, the Inman, the Montreal — the Solway, the Isis, the Paramatta, the Hungarian, the Canadian, the Anglo-Saxon, the Humboldt, the United States, all run aground; the Arctic, the Lyonnais, sunk in collision; the President, the Pacific, the City of Glasgow, vanished for reasons never known — a scattering of dark wreckage through which the Nautilus now sailed, as though it were passing the dead in review!

On May 15 we were off the southern tip of the Grand Banks of Newfoundland. These banks are a product of the sea's own deposits, an enormous accumulation of organic debris brought either from the Equator by the current of the Gulf Stream, or from the northern pole by that cold countercurrent that runs along the American coast. Here, too, pile up the erratic blocks dragged down by the breakup of the ice. Here has formed a vast ossuary of fish, of mollusks, of zoophytes, which perish here by the billion.

The sea is not especially deep over the Grand Banks. A few hundred fathoms at most. But toward the south there suddenly opens a deep trough, a pit three thousand meters down. Here the Gulf Stream spreads out. Its waters flower wide. It loses its speed and its warmth, but it becomes a sea in its own right.

Among the fish that the Nautilus startled as it passed, I will mention the lumpfish, a meter long, blackish along the back and orange on the belly, which sets its fellows an example of conjugal fidelity they rarely follow; a large unernack, a kind of emerald moray, excellent eating; some big-eyed karraks, whose heads bear a certain resemblance to a dog's; blennies, egg-laying like the snakes; black gobies, or gudgeon, two decimeters long; and long-tailed grenadiers with a silvery gleam, swift fish that had ventured far from the hyperborean seas.

The nets also brought up a bold, brazen fish, powerful and heavily muscled, armed with spines on its head and prickles along its fins — a veritable scorpion two to three meters long, the sworn enemy of the blennies, the cods, and the salmon: it was the sculpin of the northern seas, its body knobbed and warty, brown in color, red at the fins. The fishermen of the Nautilus had some trouble laying hold of this animal, which, thanks to the structure of its gill covers, keeps its breathing organs safe from the drying touch of the air and can survive some time out of the water.

I mention now — for the record — some little boskian fish that keep company with ships for long stretches in the northern seas; some sharp-snouted bleak, peculiar to the North Atlantic; some rascasses; and I come at last to the cods, and chiefly to that species the common cod, which I caught in its favorite haunt, on this inexhaustible bank of Newfoundland.

You might call these cods mountain fish, for Newfoundland is really nothing but an underwater mountain. When the Nautilus opened itself a path through their crowded ranks, Conseil could not hold back an observation:

"That! Cod?" he said. "But I always thought cod were flat, like dabs or soles."

"You innocent!" I cried. "Cod are only flat at the grocer's, where they're shown split open and laid out. In the water they're spindle-shaped, like mullet, and perfectly built for moving."

"I'll take Master's word for it," Conseil replied. "What a swarm — what an anthill of them!"

"Ah, my friend, there'd be far more still, if it weren't for their enemies — the rascasses and men! Do you know how many eggs have been counted in a single female?"

"Let's do the thing properly," Conseil replied. "Five hundred thousand."

"Eleven million, my friend."

"Eleven million. That I will never accept — not unless I count them myself."

"Then count them, Conseil. But you'll come round to believing me a great deal faster. Besides, it's by the thousands that the French, the English, the Americans, the Danes, the Norwegians fish for cod. They're eaten in prodigious quantities, and without these fish's astonishing fertility the seas would soon be stripped bare of them. In England and America alone, five thousand ships manned by seventy-five thousand sailors are employed in the cod fishery. Each ship brings back forty thousand on average, which comes to twenty-five million. And off the coasts of Norway, the same again."

"Fine," Conseil replied. "I'll defer to Master. I won't count them."

"Count what?"

"The eleven million eggs. But I will make one remark."

"And that is?"

"That if every one of those eggs hatched, four cod would be enough to feed England, America, and Norway."

As we skimmed the bottom of the Newfoundland banks, I could see, perfectly clearly, those long lines armed with two hundred hooks apiece that each boat pays out by the dozen. Each line, dragged down at one end by a small grapnel, was held at the surface by a rope made fast to a cork buoy. The Nautilus had to maneuver deftly through the middle of this underwater web.

In any case, it did not linger long in these busy waters. It rose toward the forty-second parallel of latitude. That put us on a level with St. John's, Newfoundland, and with Heart's Content, where the far end of the transatlantic cable comes ashore.

The Nautilus, instead of holding on to the north, took a course to the east, as though meaning to follow that telegraph plateau on which the cable rests, and whose relief repeated soundings have mapped with extreme precision.

It was on May 17, some five hundred miles from Heart's Content, at a depth of two thousand eight hundred meters, that I caught sight of the cable lying on the sea floor. Conseil, whom I had not warned, took it at first for some gigantic sea serpent and was getting ready to classify it by his usual method. But I set the good fellow straight, and to console him for his disappointment I told him various particulars about the laying of this cable.

The first cable was established during the years 1857 and 1858; but after transmitting some four hundred telegrams, it ceased to function. In 1863 the engineers built a new one, measuring three thousand four hundred kilometers and weighing four thousand five hundred tons, which was loaded aboard the Great Eastern. This attempt failed as well.

Now, on May 25, the Nautilus, submerged at a depth of three thousand eight hundred thirty-six meters, found itself precisely at the spot where the break occurred that ruined the enterprise. It was six hundred thirty-eight miles from the coast of Ireland. At two in the afternoon it had been noticed that communications with Europe had just been cut off. The electricians on board resolved to cut the cable before fishing it up, and by eleven that night they had brought up the damaged section. They made a fresh joint and splice; then the cable was lowered again. But a few days later it snapped once more, and could not be recovered from the depths of the Ocean.

The Americans were not discouraged. The daring Cyrus Field, promoter of the enterprise, who was risking his whole fortune on it, called for a new subscription. It was covered at once. Another cable was made, on better principles. The bundle of conducting wires, insulated in a sheath of gutta-percha, was protected by a padding of textile material held within a metal armature. The Great Eastern put to sea again on July 13, 1866.

The operation went well. There was, however, one incident. Several times, as they were paying out the cable, the electricians noticed that nails had recently been driven into it, meant to ruin its core. Captain Anderson, his officers, and his engineers met, deliberated, and posted a notice that if the guilty man were caught aboard, he would be thrown into the sea with no further trial. From that moment the criminal attempts stopped.

On July 23, the Great Eastern was no more than eight hundred kilometers from Newfoundland when it received word by telegraph from Ireland of the armistice concluded between Prussia and Austria after Sadowa. On the 27th, through the fog, it made out the port of Heart's Content. The enterprise had come to a happy end, and in its first dispatch young America sent old Europe these wise words, so rarely understood: "Glory to God in the highest, and on earth peace to men of good will."

I had not expected to find the electric cable in its original state, as it was when it left the workshops that made it. The long serpent, coated with fragments of shell, bristling with foraminifera, was crusted over in a stony sheath that protected it against the boring mollusks. It lay quietly, sheltered from the movements of the sea, and under a pressure favorable to the transmission of that electric spark which passes from America to Europe in thirty-two hundredths of a second. The life of this cable will no doubt be endless, for it has been observed that the gutta-percha sheath actually improves with its long stay in seawater.

Besides, on this so happily chosen plateau, the cable is never sunk to depths great enough to break it. The Nautilus followed it down to its lowest point, four thousand four hundred thirty-one meters down, and there it still rested without any strain of tension. Then we drew near the place where the accident of 1863 had happened.

The ocean floor there formed a valley a hundred and twenty kilometers wide, on which you might have set Mont Blanc without its summit breaking the surface of the waves. This valley is closed off to the east by a sheer wall two thousand meters high. We reached it on May 28, and the Nautilus was now no more than a hundred and fifty kilometers from Ireland.

Was Captain Nemo going to rise and make land on the British Isles? No. To my great surprise, he went back down to the south and returned toward European waters. Rounding the Emerald Isle, I caught a moment's glimpse of Cape Clear and the light of Fastnet, which guides the thousands of ships that put out from Glasgow or Liverpool.

An important question then presented itself to my mind.

Would the Nautilus dare to venture into the English Channel? Ned Land, who had reappeared since we began to close with land, never stopped questioning me. How was I to answer him? Captain Nemo remained invisible. Having let the Canadian glimpse the shores of America, was he now going to show me the coasts of France?

Meanwhile the Nautilus kept dropping steadily toward the south. On May 30 it passed in sight of Land's End, between the outermost point of England and the Scilly Isles, which it left to starboard.

If it meant to enter the Channel, it needed to bear frankly to the east. It did not.

All through the day of May 31, the Nautilus described a series of circles on the sea that intrigued me deeply. It seemed to be searching for a spot it was having some trouble finding. At noon Captain Nemo came up to take the ship's position himself. He did not say a word to me. He seemed to me gloomier than ever. What could be casting him down like this? Was it his nearness to the shores of Europe? Did he feel some resurgence of memory of the country he had left behind? What was he feeling then — remorse, or regret? For a long while this thought occupied my mind, and I had a kind of presentiment that chance would soon betray the captain's secrets.

The next day, June 31, the Nautilus held to the same maneuvers. It was plain that it was trying to fix a precise point in the Ocean. Captain Nemo came up to take the height of the sun, as he had done the day before. The sea was calm, the sky clear. Eight miles to the east, a great steamship stood out on the line of the horizon. No flag flew at its gaff, and I could not make out its nationality.

A few minutes before the sun crossed the meridian, Captain Nemo took his sextant and made his observation with extreme precision. The absolute calm of the waves made his task easier. The Nautilus lay motionless, feeling neither roll nor pitch.

I was on the platform at that moment. When his reading was finished, the captain spoke these words and no more:

"It is here!"

He went back down through the hatch. Had he seen the vessel that was altering its course and seemed to be drawing nearer to us? I could not say.

I returned to the salon. The hatch closed, and I heard the hiss of water in the tanks. The Nautilus began to sink, along a vertical line, for its screw, now checked, gave it no forward motion at all.

A few minutes later it came to rest at a depth of eight hundred thirty-three meters, settling on the bottom.

Then the luminous ceiling of the salon went out, the panels opened, and through the windows I saw the sea brilliantly lit by the rays of the beacon for a radius of half a mile.

I looked out to port, and saw nothing but the immensity of the tranquil waters.

To starboard, on the bottom, there rose a heavy swelling shape that caught my attention. You would have said it was ruins buried under a crust of whitish shells, as under a mantle of snow. Studying this mass closely, I thought I could make out the thickened forms of a ship, stripped of its masts, that must have gone down bow-first. The wreck certainly dated from a distant time. To have become so caked in the limestone of the waters, it had already spent many long years down there on the floor of the Ocean.

What ship was this? Why had the Nautilus come to visit its grave? Had it really been a shipwreck that dragged this vessel under the waves?

I did not know what to think, when, close beside me, I heard Captain Nemo say in a slow voice:

"In her day this ship was called the Marseillais. She carried seventy-four guns and was launched in 1762. On August 13, 1778, commanded by La Poype-Vertrieux, she fought boldly against the Preston. On July 4, 1779, she took part with Admiral d'Estaing's squadron in the capture of Grenada. On September 5, 1781, she joined the Comte de Grasse's action in Chesapeake Bay. In 1794 the French Republic changed her name. On April 16 of that same year she joined Villaret-Joyeuse's squadron at Brest, charged with escorting a convoy of wheat coming from America under the command of Admiral Van Stabel. On the 11th and 12th of Prairial, Year Two, this squadron met the English ships. Professor, today is the 13th of Prairial, the first of June, 1868. Seventy-four years ago to the day, at this very spot, at latitude 47°24′ and longitude 17°28′, this ship, after a heroic fight, dismasted of all three of her masts, the water in her holds, a third of her crew out of action, chose to go down with her three hundred and fifty-six sailors rather than surrender — and nailing her colors to her stern, she vanished beneath the waves to the cry of: Long live the Republic!"

"The Vengeur!" I cried.

"Yes, Professor. The Vengeur! A fine name!" murmured Captain Nemo, folding his arms.

A Massacre

The way he had said it, the suddenness of the whole scene, the history of that patriot ship recounted so coldly at first, and then the emotion with which the strange man had spoken his last words, that name Vengeur — whose meaning could not escape me — all of it came together to strike deep into my mind. My eyes never left the captain. He, his hands stretched out toward the sea, was gazing with a burning eye at that glorious wreck. Perhaps I would never know who he was, where he had come from, where he was going — but more and more I saw the man break free of the scholar. It was no ordinary misanthropy that had shut Captain Nemo and his companions inside the hull of the Nautilus, but a hatred, monstrous or sublime, that time could not weaken.

Was that hatred still hunting for vengeance? The future would soon teach me.

Meanwhile the Nautilus was rising slowly toward the surface, and I watched the dim shapes of the Vengeur disappear little by little. Soon a slight roll told me that we were floating in the open air.

At that moment a muffled detonation reached us. I looked at the captain. The captain did not move.

"Captain?" I said.

He did not answer.

I left him and climbed up onto the platform. Conseil and the Canadian had gotten there ahead of me.

"Where did that shot come from?" I asked.

"A cannon shot," Ned Land answered.

I looked in the direction of the ship I had spotted earlier. It had drawn closer to the Nautilus, and you could see it was cramming on steam. Six miles separated it from us.

"What ship is that, Ned?"

"From her rigging, from the height of her lower masts," the Canadian replied, "I'd bet she's a warship. May she come down on us and sink this damned Nautilus, if that's what it takes!"

"Friend Ned," Conseil replied, "what harm can it do the Nautilus? Will it go and attack us under the waves? Will it cannonade us on the ocean floor?"

"Tell me, Ned," I asked, "can you make out this ship's nationality?"

The Canadian, knitting his brows, lowering his lids, crinkling his eyes at the corners, stared for a few moments at the ship with all the force of his gaze.

"No, Professor," he answered. "I can't tell what nation she belongs to. Her flag isn't hoisted. But I can swear she's a warship, because there's a long pennant streaming from the top of her mainmast."

For a quarter of an hour we went on watching the ship as it bore down on us. Still, I couldn't believe it had recognized the Nautilus at that distance, still less that it knew what this underwater craft was.

Soon the Canadian announced that this ship was a great warship, ram-bowed, a two-decked ironclad. Thick black smoke poured from its two funnels. Its furled sails blended into the line of the yards. Its gaff flew no flag. The distance still made it impossible to make out the colors of its pennant, which streamed like a thin ribbon.

It was coming on fast. If Captain Nemo let it draw near, a chance of rescue was opening for us.

"Professor," Ned Land said to me, "let that ship pass within a mile of us and I'll throw myself into the sea, and I urge you to do the same."

I made no answer to the Canadian's proposal, and I went on watching the ship as it grew larger before my eyes. English, French, American, or Russian — whatever it was, it would surely take us in if we could only reach its side.

"Master will kindly remember," Conseil said then, "that we have some experience at swimming. He can rely on me to tow him toward that ship, should it suit him to follow friend Ned."

I was about to answer when a burst of white vapor spurted from the bow of the warship. Then, a few seconds later, the water — churned up by the fall of some heavy body — splashed over the stern of the Nautilus. A moment after that, a detonation struck my ear.

"What? They're firing on us!" I cried.

"Fine fellows!" muttered the Canadian.

"So they don't take us for castaways clinging to a wreck!"

"Begging Master's pardon... Well!" said Conseil, shaking off the water that a fresh cannonball had sent splashing all the way up to him. "Begging Master's pardon, they've recognized the narwhal, and they're firing on the narwhal."

"But surely they can see," I cried, "that they're dealing with men."

"Maybe that's exactly why!" Ned Land answered, looking at me.

A whole revelation dawned on me. No doubt people now knew what to make of the supposed monster's existence. No doubt, during its clash with the Abraham Lincoln, when the Canadian struck it with his harpoon, Commander Farragut had realized that the narwhal was an underwater boat, more dangerous than any supernatural whale. Yes, that had to be it, and on every sea, no doubt, they were now hunting this terrible engine of destruction!

Terrible indeed, if — as one might well suppose — Captain Nemo was using the Nautilus for a work of vengeance! That night when he shut us up in the cell, in the middle of the Indian Ocean, hadn't he attacked some ship? That man now buried in the coral cemetery — hadn't he been a victim of the collision the Nautilus had caused? Yes, I say it again. That must be how it was. A part of Captain Nemo's mysterious existence was coming to light. And even if his identity was not known, at least the nations banded together against him were now hunting not some fabulous creature, but a man who had sworn them an implacable hatred!

That whole formidable past rose up before my eyes. Instead of finding friends aboard the approaching ship, we could find nothing there but merciless enemies.

Meanwhile the cannonballs were multiplying around us. Some, striking the surface of the water, went ricocheting off to lose themselves at considerable distances. But not one hit the Nautilus.

The ironclad was now no more than three miles off. Despite its violent bombardment, Captain Nemo did not appear on the platform. And yet one of those conical shells, striking the hull of the Nautilus square on, would have been fatal to it.

The Canadian said to me then:

"Professor, we have to try everything to get ourselves out of this fix. Let's signal them! A thousand devils! Maybe they'll understand we're honest folk!"

Ned Land took out his handkerchief to wave it in the air. But he had barely unfurled it when — struck down by an iron hand, for all his prodigious strength — he crumpled to the deck.

"Wretch!" cried the captain. "Do you want me to nail you to the ram of the Nautilus before it hurls itself against that ship?"

Captain Nemo, terrible to hear, was more terrible still to see. His face had gone white with the spasms of his heart, which must have stopped beating for an instant. His pupils had contracted horribly. His voice no longer spoke — it roared. His body bent forward, he was wrenching the Canadian's shoulders beneath his hand.
